Causality and Created Things

Two Theories of Vision: Imprint and Ray
Razi states two theories of vision and defends the ray theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two competing theories of vision presented by Razi and his defense of the ray theory against a key objection.

Core Idea
Razi prefers the ray theory: vision occurs by a ray emitted from the eye.

Razi begins by outlining the two main theories of vision held by ancient philosophers: the imprint theory, where the image of the object impresses itself on the eye, and the ray theory, where a ray from the eye strikes the object. He declares the ray theory closer to truth and immediately addresses a common objection against it, using an analogy with celestial rays to refute it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Note the two theories and the objection.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two theories
Find where Razi states the two theories. Notice which one he prefers.
He says 'الأقرب إلى الحق هو الأخير' (the nearer to truth is the latter).
3
Understand the objection
The objection says if the ray is a body, bodies would overlap; if an accident, accidents cannot transfer. Razi counters by mentioning celestial rays.
He argues that the same principle that allows celestial rays to exist applies to visual rays.
4
Reflect on the defense
Consider why Razi's defense works. He uses analogy: since celestial rays exist without those problems, visual rays can too.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيان ذلك أن الإبصار يكون إما بانطباع شبح المبصر في البصر وإما بوقوع شعاع من البصر على المبصر والأقرب إلى الحق هو الأخير، وينبغي أن لا يلتفت إلى من يبطل القول بالشعاع بأن الشعاع إن كان جسما لزم منه تداخل الأجسام وإن كان عرضا لزم القول بانتقال العرض من محل إلى آخر لأن شعاع النيران كالشمس والقمر والنار موجود يقينا فما يدفع به المحالين هناك يندفع به بعينه ما أورده من الإشكال على الشعاع البصري، ثم إن الشعاع ممتد من ذي الشعاع إلى قابل الشعاع من غير تخلل خلل، خال عن الشعاع أو تراكم باجتماع شعاعين من مأخذ واحد من ذلك الممتد في بعض أجزاء امتداده، بل على هيئة مخروط مستدير حلوه جوفه، رأسه عند ذي الشعاع وقاعدته على سطح قابل الشعاع الكثيف وينعكس منه إذا كان صقيلا إلى ما يحاذيه على زاوية مساوية للزاوية المحاذية بين الشعاع الممتد والسطح الصقيل ونسميه بزاوية الشعاع، وينفذ في القابل الشفاف ذي السطح الصقيل وينعكس عن سطحه وينعطف في تحته إلى جانب ذي الشعاع كلها معا، والانعكاس والانعطاف يكونان بزاويتين مساويتين للزاوية الشعاع قد بين جميع هذا في والشعاع البصري في أكثر الحيوانات محتاج إلى مدد من جنسه أعني إلى شعاع شيء من أجسام ذوي الأشعة ويستعان في تخيل كيفية اتصاله بالمبصرات بتوهم خطوط تخرج من سطح المخروط الشعاعي، ويكون الإبصار بزاوية تحدث من تلك الخطوط عند رأس المخروط، فكلما كان المبصر أقرب إلى البصر تكون تلك الزاوية أوسع فيراه البصر أعظم، وكلما كان أبعد منه تكون تلك الزاوية أضيق فيراه البصر أصغر إلى أن تتقارب الخطوط وتصير عند الحس لتوهم انطباق بعضها على بعض كخط واحد فيراه البصر كالنقطة وبعد ذلك ينمحى أثره فلا يراه أصلا، هذا على رأي القائلين بالشعاع. وأما القائلون بالانطباع فيقولون إن الزاهرية التي تحدث على سطح الرطوبة الجليدية تصغر وتكبر بحسب بعد المرئي وقربه والبصر يدرك المرئي بتلك الزاوية. ولنعد إلى القول بالشعاع ونقول: إذا تقررت هذه القاعدة فاعلم أن النار في الظلمة إذا كانت قريبة من الرائي عند الشعاع في الظلمة الرقيقة إلى الهواء المضئ، لمجاورة النار فرأى البصر ما حولها بمعاونة من نورها وميزها منها فرآها على ما تقتضيها زاوية الإبصار، وإذا كانت بعيدة جدا لم ينفذ الشعاع في الظلمة الكثيفة ولم ير ما حولها من النور المضئ بنورها ورآها وحدها بزاوية أصغر فيراها أصغر كما في سائر المرئيات وإذا لم تكن قريبة ولا بعيدة جدا فإن الشعاع البصري المحاذي لما حولها لم ينفذ نفوذا تاما فلم يميز النار عن الهواء المضئ بها، بل أدركتهما معا جملة واحدة فيراها البصر بزاوية أوسع من الزاوية التي تحدث من المحاذاة وحدها وذلك هو العلة لكونها في الرؤية أعظم مما لو رؤيت في غير الظلمة المذكورة المحاذاة وحدها.
And the explanation of that is that vision occurs either by the imprint of the image of the visible object in the eye or by the fall of a ray from the eye onto the visible object, and the nearer to the truth is the latter. And one should not pay attention to those who invalidate the claim about the ray by saying that if the ray is a body, then overlapping of bodies would follow, and if it is an accident, then the transfer of an accident from one place to another would be required, because the rays of fires like the sun, moon, and fire exist certainly, and that which repels the impossibility there repels precisely the objection he raised concerning the visual ray. Then the ray extends from the possessor of the ray to the receiver of the ray without any interposing gap, free from the ray or accumulation by the meeting of two rays from one source within that extension in some parts of its extension, but rather in the form of a circular cone, its [؟] hollow, its vertex at the possessor of the ray and its base on the surface of the dense receiver of the ray, and it reflects from it if it is polished to what is opposite it at an angle equal to the opposite angle between the extending ray and the polished surface, and we call it the angle of the ray. And it penetrates the transparent receiver with a polished surface and reflects from its surface and bends under it to the side of the possessor of the ray, all together. And reflection and bending occur at two angles equal to the angle of the ray; all this has been explained in [؟]. And the visual ray in most animals is in need of support from its kind, meaning a ray from some of the bodies that possess rays, and one is aided in imagining how it connects with visible things by imagining lines emerging from the surface of the ray-cone. And vision occurs at an angle generated by those lines at the vertex of the cone. Whenever the visible object is closer to the eye, that angle is wider, and the eye sees it larger; and whenever it is farther, that angle is narrower, and the eye sees it smaller, until the lines converge and become at sensation as if they coincide with each other like one line, and the eye sees it like a point, and after that its trace disappears and it does not see it at all. This is according to the view of the proponents of the ray. As for the proponents of imprint, they say that the brightness that occurs on the surface of the icy moisture becomes smaller and larger according to the distance and proximity of the visible object, and the eye perceives the visible object by that angle. And let us return to the claim about the ray and say: when this principle is established, know that fire in darkness, when it is near the viewer during the ray in the thin darkness to the luminous air due to the proximity of the fire, the eye sees what is around it with the aid of its light and distinguishes it from it, and sees it as required by the vision angle. And when it is very far, the ray does not penetrate the dense darkness and does not see what is around it from the luminous light with its light, and sees it alone at a smaller angle and sees it smaller as in other visible things. And when it is neither near nor very far, the visual ray opposite what is around it does not penetrate completely, so it does not distinguish the fire from the air illuminated by it, but rather perceives both together as one whole, and the eye sees it at a wider angle than the angle that arises from the alignment alone, and that is the cause of its appearing larger in vision than if it were seen in the mentioned darkness by alignment alone.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is an extensive explanation of the mechanism of vision. Razi begins by presenting two theories: the imprint theory (intiba') where the image of the object impresses itself on the eye, and the ray theory (shu'a') where a ray emitted from the eye strikes the object. He declares the ray theory closer to the truth. He then defends the ray theory against an objection: if the ray were a body, it would cause interpenetration; if an accident, it would entail accidents transferring locations. He counters by noting that celestial rays (sun, moon, fire) exist without such problems, and the same principle applies. Next, he describes the geometry: the ray extends from the eye to the object in the form of a cone with its vertex at the eye and its base on the object. The cone can reflect off polished surfaces at equal angles. The visual ray requires support from external light sources. Perception occurs at an angle formed at the vertex: nearer objects produce a wider angle and appear larger; farther objects produce a narrower angle and appear smaller. When the angle becomes extremely narrow, the object appears as a point and then disappears. Razi also briefly presents the imprint theory: the brightness on the crystalline humor changes size with distance. Returning to the ray theory, he applies it to explain an optical illusion: fire in darkness appears larger than usual because the eye cannot distinguish the fire from the surrounding illuminated air, so it perceives both together at a wider angle. This causal explanation shows that the apparent size is not an error of sight but a geometric consequence.

Key Terms
الإبصار
الشعاع
الانطباع
شبح المبصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi rejects the imprint theory entirely and only considers the ray theory.
Correction: Razi does not reject the imprint theory; he only says the ray theory is 'nearer to the truth'. Later in the passage he also presents the imprint theory's explanation for apparent size.

Checkpoint
What is Razi's preferred theory of vision?
A) Imprint theory
B) Ray theory
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Razi favors the ray theory of vision and defends it against the objection of body/accident by using the existence of celestial rays as an analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi defends the ray theory because it aligns with his observation of celestial rays. In your own life, are you willing to trust your observations over established objections? What objecting voices do you need to overcome to accept a truth you see clearly?
?
The objection to the ray theory was based on abstract philosophical principles. Are there times when you have dismissed an obvious truth because of a theoretical objection? What would it cost you to revisit that assumption starting today?

Geometric Explanation of the Ray Theory and Apparent Size
Razi describes the geometric model of the visual cone and explains apparent size and the fire-in-darkness illusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the geometric model of the visual ray as a cone, how the angle at the vertex determines perceived size, and how this explains why fire in darkness appears larger.

Core Idea
The visual ray forms a cone; the angle at the eye determines apparent size.

After defending the ray theory, Razi provides a detailed geometric description of how the ray extends from the eye to the object. He explains that it forms a cone, with the vertex at the eye and the base on the object. The angle of this cone determines the apparent size of the object: a wider angle means a larger appearance. He applies this to explain an optical illusion: a fire in darkness appears larger than it should because the eye cannot distinguish it from the illuminated air around it, so the cone includes both, resulting in a wider angle.

Guided Path
1
Read the geometric description
Read the passage describing the cone, its shape, and how the ray extends. Notice the terms: 'makhroot mustadeer' (circular cone), 'ra's' (vertex), 'qaa'idah' (base).
The description includes reflection and bending of rays.
2
Understand the angle of vision
Find the part that explains how the angle at the vertex changes with distance. Closer objects give a wider angle, farther objects give a narrower angle.
Razi uses 'kullama kaana al-mubsir aqraba...'
3
Examine the point-like appearance
When the lines of the cone converge to nearly one line, the object appears as a point, then disappears.
This explains why very distant objects become invisible.
4
Analyze the fire illusion
Razi applies the geometry to explain why fire in darkness looks larger. He says the visual ray cannot penetrate the darkness to distinguish fire from the illuminated air, so it perceives both together at a wider angle.
This shows Razi's use of geometry to explain optical illusions causally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is an extensive explanation of the mechanism of vision. Razi begins by presenting two theories: the imprint theory (intiba') where the image of the object impresses itself on the eye, and the ray theory (shu'a') where a ray emitted from the eye strikes the object. He declares the ray theory closer to the truth. He then defends the ray theory against an objection: if the ray were a body, it would cause interpenetration; if an accident, it would entail accidents transferring locations. He counters by noting that celestial rays (sun, moon, fire) exist without such problems, and the same principle applies. Next, he describes the geometry: the ray extends from the eye to the object in the form of a cone with its vertex at the eye and its base on the object. The cone can reflect off polished surfaces at equal angles. The visual ray requires support from external light sources. Perception occurs at an angle formed at the vertex: nearer objects produce a wider angle and appear larger; farther objects produce a narrower angle and appear smaller. When the angle becomes extremely narrow, the object appears as a point and then disappears. Razi also briefly presents the imprint theory: the brightness on the crystalline humor changes size with distance. Returning to the ray theory, he applies it to explain an optical illusion: fire in darkness appears larger than usual because the eye cannot distinguish the fire from the surrounding illuminated air, so it perceives both together at a wider angle. This causal explanation shows that the apparent size is not an error of sight but a geometric consequence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fire illusion is a mistake of the eye or a trick of perception.
Correction: Razi explains that it is a geometric consequence: the visual cone includes both the fire and the illuminated air, resulting in a wider angle and larger apparent size.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why does a fire in darkness appear larger than it actually is?
A) Because the darkness expands the image
B) Because the visual ray cannot distinguish the fire from the illuminated air, so the cone includes both, making a wider angle
C) Because fire emits a larger impression
D) Because the eye is deceived by the darkness

Key Takeaway
Razi's geometric model of the visual cone explains both normal vision and optical illusions like the apparent enlargement of fire in darkness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi explains that when the fire is at an intermediate distance, your eye does not distinguish it from the surrounding illuminated air, so you perceive it as larger. In your life, where might you be failing to distinguish between two things that appear blended together, leading you to see something as larger or more significant than it is? What would it take to sharpen your perception?
?
The visual cone model shows that your perception of size depends on the angle of vision. How often do you judge the 'size' of a trial or a blessing based on your own limited angle? What would change if you stepped back and considered the wider cone—the greater picture that only Allah sees?
?
Razi's explanation of the fire illusion reveals that what you see is not necessarily reality; it is a geometric outcome of the conditions of perception. What are some 'spiritual illusions' you might be living under—where your distance or closeness to the object affects how you perceive its true nature? How can you realign your vision with truth?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

Senses and the Illusion of Continuity
Razi's second argument: senses mistake succession for continuity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second argument that the senses mistake repeated similar things for a single continuous entity, and why this supports the Ash'ari occasionalist view.

Core Idea
The senses cannot tell the difference between one thing and its identical copy.

Imam Razi presents a powerful argument against relying on sense perception to judge continuity. He points out that when identical items follow each other rapidly, the senses perceive them as one continuous thing. This has profound implications for the Ash'ari doctrine of occasionalism, where both accidents and bodies are recreated moment by moment. Let's examine the argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice how Razi builds his case.
The argument is concise but dense.
2
Understand the key point
Razi claims that the sense cannot distinguish a thing from its like. This is the core premise. How does that lead to error about continuity?
Think about identical twins or identical frames of a film.
3
Follow the analogy
Razi applies the premise first to colors (accidents) and then to bodies. See how the same logic applies to both.
The Ash'ari position holds that both accidents and bodies are recreated at each moment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها: أن الحس قد يجزم بالاستمرار على الشيء مع أنه لا يكون كذلك، لأن الحس لا يفرق بين الشيء ومثله ولذلك يحصل الالتباس بين الشيء ومثله، فبتقدير توالى الأمثال يظن الحس وجوداً واحداً مستمراً ولا يكون كذلك؛ فإن الألوان غير باقية عند أهل السنة، بل يحددها الله تعالى حالا فحالا، مع أن البصر يحكم بوجود لون واحد مستمر، وإذا احتمل ذلك، احتمل أيضاً أن يقال: الأجسام لا تبقى مستمرة، بل الله تعالى مجددها حالا فحالا، لكنها ما كانت متماثلة متوالية يظنها الحس شيئاً واحدا فثبت أن حكم الحس بالبقاء ليس بيقيني.
And the second [argument]: that the sense may be certain of the continuity of a thing while it is not the case, because the sense does not distinguish between a thing and its like, and therefore confusion occurs between a thing and its like. So if it is supposed that similar things follow in succession, the sense imagines a single continuous existence, but it is not so. For colors are not persisting according to the Ahl al-Sunnah, rather Allah creates them moment by moment, while the sight judges the existence of a single continuous color. And if that is possible, it is also possible to say that bodies do not persist continuously, but rather Allah recreates them moment by moment, but because they are similar and successive, the sense imagines them to be one thing. So it is established that the sense's judgment of persistence is not certain.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for the fallibility of the senses: the senses judge continuity where there is only succession of similar things. The author (Imam Razi) argues that the sense cannot distinguish between a thing and its exact likeness, so when similar items follow each other rapidly, the sense perceives them as one continuous entity. He applies this to the Ash'ari doctrine of occasionalism: according to Ahl al-Sunnah, colors (accidents) do not persist but are recreated by God at each moment. Yet the eye judges a single continuous color. By analogy, bodies may also be recreated moment by moment but appear continuous because of their similarity. Therefore, the sense's judgment of persistence (al-baqa') is not certain or reliable. This argument supports the larger kalam project of demonstrating that sense perception cannot be the foundation for knowledge of the persistence of substances or accidents.

Key Terms
استمرار
بقاء
حس
تماثل
تجدد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Razi's argument denies any continuity in objects; it only shows sense perception alone cannot prove continuity.
Correction: The argument does not claim that there is no continuity; it only shows that sense perception cannot be trusted to judge continuity, leaving room for rational or revealed knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the sense judge a single continuous color even though colors are recreated moment by moment?
A) Because the sense is infallible.
B) Because the sense cannot distinguish between a thing and its like.
C) Because colors are actually permanent.
D) Because the sense has direct knowledge of God's action.

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that the senses' judgment of persistence is uncertain because they cannot distinguish between a thing and its identical successor, which supports the occasionalist view that all things are recreated moment by moment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your senses cannot reliably tell you that a color continues from one moment to the next, how often do you trust your senses to judge the constancy of your own faith? What would it mean for your worship if you realized that every moment of your focus in prayer is a fresh gift from Allah, not a continuation of the previous moment? How would you prepare for each moment of salah if you truly believed that Allah creates your attention anew?
?
The passage shows that senses can be deceived by similarity. In your life, what 'similar' things have you mistaken for lasting truth? Are there habits or beliefs you assume are permanent because they feel continuous, that might actually be recreated by Allah each moment? What would change if you examined them as distinct creations?
?
Razi uses the example of colors to argue that what appears continuous may be a series of discrete creations. How does this change your understanding of Allah's involvement in your daily life? Do you live as if each breath is a separate act of creation, or as if it is merely a continuation? Commit to one way you will remind yourself of this lesson tomorrow.

Refuting the Impossibility of Category Change
Razi refutes the doubt about objects changing categories and affirms the impossibility of qalb al-haqa'iq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the doubt that objects could arbitrarily change their natures, and why changing natures (qalb al-haqa'iq) is considered impossible by the mutakallimun.

Core Idea
Changing the very natures of things is impossible according to the mutakallimun.

Razi addresses a skeptical doubt: when you leave your house, how do you know objects haven't changed into something else? He refutes it by appealing to the theological principle that changing natures is impossible. This lesson explores that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt presented
The objector suggests that when you leave your house, objects might have changed into other things (e.g., vessels into scholars, stones into gold) and then reverted. Understand the challenge to certainty.
Notice the examples used: vessels, stones, water.
2
Identify Razi's refutation
Razi responds that most of these possibilities are impossible. For theologians, changing natures (qalb al-haqa'iq) is impossible; for philosophers, substitution of forms is impossible.
The term qalb al-haqa'iq is central.
3
Reflect on the significance
This refutation upholds the reliability of our knowledge about the persistence of objects and shows that the doubt is baseless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : أنى إذا خرجت من دارى فإني أعلم أن ما فيها من الأوانى وغيره لم ينقلب أناسا فضلاء مدققين في علوم المنطق والهندسة ، ولم ينقلب ما فيها من الأحجار ذهبا وياقوتا ، وأنه ليس تحت رحلى ياقوت بمقدار مائة ألف من ، وأن مياه البحار والأودية لم ينقلب أدما ودهنا ، والاحتمال في الكل قائم ، ولا يندفع ذلك بأني إذا نظرت إليها ثانيا وجدتها كما كانت لاحتمال أن يقال إنها انقلبت إلى هذه الصفات في زمان فني عنها ثم عند عودي إليها صارت كما كانت للفاعل المختار أو للشكل الغريب

(1) أقول : أكثر ما ذكر من هذه الاحتمالات محال ؛ فإن قلب الحقائق عند المتكلمين محال . مقدور عليه ، وتبديل هذه الصورة بالصور التي ذكرها عند الفلاسفة ممتنع .
The third: When I leave my house, I know that what is in it of vessels and other things has not turned into virtuous exact scholars in the sciences of logic and geometry, and that what is in it of stones has not turned into gold and ruby, and that under my saddle there is not a ruby of a hundred thousand mans, and that the waters of the seas and valleys have not turned into butter and oil, and the possibility is present in all. And that is not refuted by my looking at them a second time and finding them as they were, because of the possibility that it is said they had turned into these attributes in a time and perished, then at my return to them they became as they were, whether by the free agent or the strange form. (1) I say: Most of what these possibilities mention are impossible. For changing of natures (qalb al-haqa'iq) is impossible according to the mutakallimun. It is within capacity [?], and the substitution of these forms with the forms he mentioned is impossible according to the philosophers.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage refutes the third doubt. The objector argues that our certainty that ordinary objects (vessels, stones, water) remain what they are is not absolute, because God could momentarily change them into something else and then revert them, or the philosophers' strange form could do so. Razi responds by stating that most of these possibilities are impossible. For theologians (mutakallimun), changing the very natures (qalb al-haqa'iq) of things is impossible, and for philosophers, substituting one form for another as described is also impossible. Thus the doubt is baseless.

Key Terms
قلب الحقائق
متكلمون
ممتنع
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God could arbitrarily change the natures of things at any moment, making our knowledge of objects unreliable.
Correction: The mutakallimun hold that changing natures (qalb al-haqa'iq) is impossible (muhal), so such arbitrary changes are not possible even for God.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the status of changing the natures of things (qalb al-haqa'iq) for the mutakallimun?
A) Possible and within God's power
B) Impossible (muhal)
C) Part of the strange form
D) Possible only for philosophers

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the doubt by asserting that changing natures (qalb al-haqa'iq) is impossible for both theologians and philosophers, thus preserving the reliability of our knowledge about the persistence of objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You place great trust in your own senses—but have you ever considered that the very nature of what you see might be impossible to change? What would happen to your faith if you truly believed that God could turn your whole world upside down in an instant? Would you still sleep at night?
?
Razi says that changing natures is impossible. Does your heart rest in the consistency of the world because you know it is maintained by a wise Creator, or do you secretly fear that His power could turn your life into chaos at any moment? How would your daily actions change if you truly internalized that some things are impossible even for God?
?
You witnessed countless things stay the same today—your coffee cup wasn't suddenly filled with gold, your keys didn't turn into birds. But did you thank the One who kept them as they were? What does your lack of gratitude for the stability of the world reveal about your awareness of His care?

Causal Efficacy as a Positive Attribute: A Kalam Critique
The objector analyzes causal efficacy as a positive attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Asharite objector argues that causal efficacy (mu'aththiriyya) cannot be a positive attribute, leading to infinite regress.

Core Idea
Effectingness as a positive attribute leads to contradictions and infinite regress.

The objector presents a sophisticated logical argument against the very concept of causality. By analyzing the possibility of effectingness as a positive attribute, he shows that it cannot exist without leading to absurdity. This lesson unpacks the first half of that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Note the logical divisions used.
The text uses a series of divisions (taqsīmāt).
2
Identify the main division
The argument divides effectingness into positive (thubūtī) and non-positive. Focus on the positive branch.
Notice that the objector first eliminates the positive option.
3
Follow the sub-divisions
The positive attribute is further divided: mental only vs. external existence. Then external divided into identical to relata or extra. Extra leads to infinite regress.
Each division is eliminated in turn.
4
Understand the infinite regress argument
The key point: if effectingness is an extra attribute, it is contingent and needs another cause, leading to an infinite chain.
The argument also considers the nature of relations and substances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أولها : لو افتقر الممكن إلى المؤثر لكانت مؤثرية المؤثر في ذلك الأثر إما أن تكون وصفا ثبوتيا أولا تكون والتسمان باطلان فالقول بالمؤثرية باطل . وإنما قلنا: إنه يستحيل أن يكون ثبوتيا لأن ثبوته إما في الذهن فقط أوفيه وفى الخارج والأول باطل لأن الذي وجد في الذهن ولا يكون مطابقا للخارج جهل كمن اعتقد أن العالم قديم مع أنه لا يكون في نفسه كذلك : فلو كان حكم الذهن بالمؤثرية غير مطابق للخارج كان ذلك الحكم جهلا فلا يكون الشيء في نفسه مؤثراً ، ولأن كون الشىء مؤثرا فى غيره صفة لذلك الشيء وكانت حاصلة قبل الأذهان وصفة الشيء يستحيل قيامه بغيره ، إلا أن يقال : الموجود في الذهن هو العلم بالمؤثر ، ليكن ذلك لا يفيد كما تقدم . وأما الثانى : وهو أن يكون ليه ثبوت فى الخارج فهو إما أن يكون نفس المؤثر والأثر أو أمر مغاير لهما والأول باطل ، لأنا قد تعلم ذات المؤثر وذات الأثر مع الشك في كون ذلك المؤثر مؤثرا في ذلك الأثر ، كما إذا علمنا العالم وعلمنا قدرة الله ، ولكن لا نعلم أن المؤثر فيه قدرة الله إلا ببرهان منفصل والمعلوم مغاير للمجهول ، ولأن مؤثرية قدرة الله تعالى في العالم ليست نفس قدرته ولأن مؤثرية الشيء في الأثر نسبة بينهما والنسبة بين الشيئين تتوقف على وجود المنتسبين، والمتوقف على الشيء مغاير له ، وأما إن كانت المؤثرية أمرا زائدا ، فهو إما أن يكون من العوارض العارضة لذات المؤثر وإما أن لا يكون كذلك بل يكون موجوداً قائماً بنفسه ، لأن كونه عارضا لشيء آخر غير معقول ، وإن كان الأول كان ممكنا لذاته مفتقرا إلى المؤثر مؤثرية المؤثر فيه زائدة عليه ولزم التسلسل وهو محال . وبتقدير تسليمه فالمحال لازم من وجه آخر لأن التسلسل إنما يعقل لو فرضنا أمورا متتالية إلى غير النهاية وذلك يستدعى كون كل واحد متلوا بصاحبه لو لم تكن بينه وبين متلوه غيره ، لكن ذلك محال ، لأن تأثير المتلو في التالى متوسط بينهما ، وقد كان لا متوسط هذا خلف ، وإن كانت المؤثرية جوهراً قائماً بذاته فهو محال ، لأن مؤثرية الشيء في الأثر نسبة بين الأثر والمؤثر والنسبة بين الشيئين لا يعقل أن يكون جوهرا قائماً بالنفس . على تقدير التسليم فالمؤثر في وجود هذا الممكن هذا الجوهر أو ذاك أوهما ، وعلى التقديرات يكون مؤثرية ذات المؤثرة في وجود الممكن زائدة عليه ولزم التسلسل . وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون المؤثرية صفة عدمية لأنها نقيض الامؤثرية التي يصح حملها على العدم ، والمحمول على العدم عدم ونقيض العدم ثبوت ، فالمؤثرية أمر ثبوتى ، ولأن الشيء الذي لا يكون مؤثرا فصار مؤثراً فالمؤثرية حصلت بعد أن لم تكن فهى صفة وجودية، وإلا فليجوز فما إذا صارت الذات عالمة بعد أن لم تكن أن لا يكون العلم أمراً وجودياً وذلك نهاية الجبل له فظهر بما ذكرنا فسادكون المؤثرية صفة ثبوتية وكونها صفة عدمية، فإذا القول بالمؤثرية باطل .
The first of them: If the contingent were in need of an effecter, then the effectingness of the effecter in that effect would either be a positive attribute or not, and both are false, so the claim of effectingness is false. We say: It is impossible for it to be positive, because its positivity is either only in the mind or in both mind and external. The first is false because what exists in the mind and does not correspond to the external is ignorance, like one who believes the world is eternal while it is not so in itself. So if the mind's judgment of effectingness does not correspond to the external, that judgment is ignorance, so the thing is not in itself an effecter. Also, because a thing's being an effecter of another is an attribute of that thing and it exists prior to minds, and it is impossible for an attribute of a thing to subsist in other than it. Unless it is said: what exists in the mind is knowledge of the effecter, but that does not help as before. As for the second: that it has positivity in the external — it is either identical to the effecter and effect or something else, and the first is false because we may know the essence of the effecter and the essence of the effect while doubting that the effecter is effecting in that effect, as when we know the world and know God's power, but we do not know that the effecter in it is God's power except by a separate proof, and the known is other than the unknown. Also, because the effectingness of God's power in the world is not identical to His power, and because a thing's effectingness in the effect is a relation between them, and the relation between two things depends on the existence of the relata, and what depends on a thing is other than it. But if effectingness is an extra thing, then it is either an accident of the essence of the effecter or not, such that it is an independent substance; because its being an accident of something else is inconceivable. If it is the former, it is contingent in itself, needing an effecter, and the effectingness of the effecter in it is additional to it, leading to infinite regress, which is absurd. And if it is granted, the absurdity is necessary from another aspect, because infinite regress is only conceivable if we suppose successive things ad infinitum, which requires each to be followed by its follower without anything between them, but that is impossible because the influence of the precedent on the subsequent is an intermediate between them, yet there was no intermediate — this is a contradiction. And if effectingness is an independent substance, it is impossible because a thing's effectingness in the effect is a relation between effect and effecter, and the relation between two things cannot be an independent substance. On the assumption of granting it, the effecter of the existence of this contingent is either this substance or that or both, and in all cases, the effectingness of the effecting essence in the existence of the contingent is additional to it, leading to infinite regress. And we say: It is not permissible for effectingness to be a negative attribute, because it is the opposite of non-effectingness, which can be predicated of non-existence, and what is predicated of non-existence is non-existence; the opposite of non-existence is existence, so effectingness is a positive matter. Also, because a thing that was not effecting and then becomes effecting — effectingness occurs after it was not, so it is an existential attribute; otherwise, one might permit that when an essence becomes knowing after not being knowing, knowledge is not an existential thing, which is the ultimate of ignorance. Thus it appears from what we have mentioned the corruption of effectingness being a positive attribute and being a negative attribute; therefore the claim of effectingness is false.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents the first of several counterarguments against the concept of causality (mu'aththiriyya). The objector argues that the attribute of being an effecter (mu'aththiriyya) is impossible whether considered as a positive or negative attribute. If positive, it must exist either only in the mind (making it subjective and possibly false) or in reality. If in reality, it could be identical to the effecter/effect (which is false because we can know the effecter without knowing its causal efficacy) or an extra entity. If an extra entity, it leads to infinite regress. If a negative attribute, it is contradictory because the opposite of non-existence is existence. Therefore the entire notion of causality is invalid. The argument uses logical divisions (taqsīmāt) and reasoning typical of kalam to show that the concept of a cause effecting its effect is incoherent, thus undermining the need for a separate cause.

Key Terms
المؤثر
المؤثرية
التسلسل
الثبوتي
النسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the objector reject the possibility of effectingness being purely mental?
A) Because mental existents are always false.
B) Because it would make effectingness a subjective judgment that may not correspond to reality, thus ignorance.
C) Because the mind cannot conceive of causality.
D) Because mental attributes are impossible for external things.

Key Takeaway
Effectingness as a positive attribute leads to either subjective ignorance or infinite regress, making it impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God causing the world, do you unconsciously imagine His power as a 'thing' in Him that does the causing? How does this argument challenge that picture?
?
If causal efficacy is not a positive attribute of the cause, then what is your relationship to the means you use to achieve things? Are you tempted to see your own efforts as independent causes?
?
The argument says that if effectingness were an extra attribute, it would need another cause. Have you ever sought secondary causes (like your skills or resources) as if they were self-sufficient? This passage invites you to see all causality as contingent on God's direct creation — how does that change where you place your trust today?

Causal Efficacy Cannot Be a Negative Attribute
The objector argues that causal efficacy cannot be a negative attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objector argues that causal efficacy cannot be a negative attribute, and how this completes the refutation of causality.

Core Idea
Effectingness as a negative attribute is self-contradictory because its opposite implies existence.

Having dismantled the possibility of effectingness as a positive attribute, the objector now turns to the alternative: that it is a negative attribute (ʿadamiyya). He shows that this too is incoherent, leading to the conclusion that the very concept of causality is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on negative attribute
Read the Arabic from 'وإنما قلنا' to the end, along with its translation. Notice how the argument against the negative attribute is presented.
The argument uses the principle that the opposite of non-existence is existence.
2
Understand the reasoning
The objector argues that effectingness is the opposite of non-effectingness (lā mu'aththiriyya), which can be predicated of non-existence. It follows that effectingness must be positive.
Also, change from non-effecting to effecting implies a new attribute, which must be existential.
3
Reach the conclusion
Since both positive and negative options are false, the original claim that the contingent needs an effecter is undermined. The objector concludes that causality (mu'aththiriyya) is invalid.
This is a powerful attack on the causal principle in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الذهن فقط أوفيه وفى الخارج والأول باطل لأن الذي وجد في الذهن ولا يكون مطابقا للخارج جهل كمن اعتقد أن العالم قديم مع أنه لا يكون في نفسه كذلك : فلو كان حكم الذهن بالمؤثرية غير مطابق للخارج كان ذلك الحكم جهلا فلا يكون الشيء في نفسه مؤثراً ، ولأن كون الشىء مؤثرا فى غيره صفة لذلك الشيء وكانت حاصلة قبل الأذهان وصفة الشيء يستحيل قيامه بغيره ، إلا أن يقال : الموجود في الذهن هو العلم بالمؤثر ، ليكن وقد كان لا متوسط هذا خلف ، وإن كانت المؤثرية جوهراً قائماً بذاته فهو محال ، لأن مؤثرية الشيء في الأثر نسبة بين الأثر والمؤثر والنسبة بين الشيئين لا يعقل أن يكون جوهرا قائماً بالنفس . على تقدير التسليم فالمؤثر في وجود هذا الممكن هذا الجوهر أو ذاك أوهما ، وعلى التقديرات يكون مؤثرية ذات المؤثرة في وجود الممكن زائدة عليه ولزم التسلسل . وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون المؤثرية صفة عدمية لأنها نقيض الامؤثرية التي يصح حملها على العدم ، والمحمول على العدم عدم ونقيض العدم ثبوت ، فالمؤثرية أمر ثبوتى ، ولأن الشيء الذي لا يكون مؤثرا فصار مؤثراً فالمؤثرية حصلت بعد أن لم تكن فهى صفة وجودية، وإلا فليجوز فما إذا صارت الذات عالمة بعد أن لم تكن أن لا يكون العلم أمراً وجودياً وذلك نهاية الجبل له فظهر بما ذكرنا فسادكون المؤثرية صفة ثبوتية وكونها صفة عدمية، فإذا القول بالمؤثرية باطل .
The first of them: If the contingent were in need of an effecter, then the effectingness of the effecter in that effect would either be a positive attribute or not, and both are false, so the claim of effectingness is false. We say: It is impossible for it to be positive, because its positivity is either only in the mind or in both mind and external. The first is false because what exists in the mind and does not correspond to the external is ignorance, like one who believes the world is eternal while it is not so in itself. So if the mind's judgment of effectingness does not correspond to the external, that judgment is ignorance, so the thing is not in itself an effecter. Also, because a thing's being an effecter of another is an attribute of that thing and it exists prior to minds, and it is impossible for an attribute of a thing to subsist in other than it. Unless it is said: what exists in the mind is knowledge of the effecter, but that does not help as before. As for the second: that it has positivity in the external — it is either identical to the effecter and effect or something else, and the first is false because we may know the essence of the effecter and the essence of the effect while doubting that the effecter is effecting in that effect, as when we know the world and know God's power, but we do not know that the effecter in it is God's power except by a separate proof, and the known is other than the unknown. Also, because the effectingness of God's power in the world is not identical to His power, and because a thing's effectingness in the effect is a relation between them, and the relation between two things depends on the existence of the relata, and what depends on a thing is other than it. But if effectingness is an extra thing, then it is either an accident of the essence of the effecter or not, such that it is an independent substance; because its being an accident of something else is inconceivable. If it is the former, it is contingent in itself, needing an effecter, and the effectingness of the effecter in it is additional to it, leading to infinite regress, which is absurd. And if it is granted, the absurdity is necessary from another aspect, because infinite regress is only conceivable if we suppose successive things ad infinitum, which requires each to be followed by its follower without anything between them, but that is impossible because the influence of the precedent on the subsequent is an intermediate between them, yet there was no intermediate — this is a contradiction. And if effectingness is an independent substance, it is impossible because a thing's effectingness in the effect is a relation between effect and effecter, and the relation between two things cannot be an independent substance. On the assumption of granting it, the effecter of the existence of this contingent is either this substance or that or both, and in all cases, the effectingness of the effecting essence in the existence of the contingent is additional to it, leading to infinite regress. And we say: It is not permissible for effectingness to be a negative attribute, because it is the opposite of non-effectingness, which can be predicated of non-existence, and what is predicated of non-existence is non-existence; the opposite of non-existence is existence, so effectingness is a positive matter. Also, because a thing that was not effecting and then becomes effecting — effectingness occurs after it was not, so it is an existential attribute; otherwise, one might permit that when an essence becomes knowing after not being knowing, knowledge is not an existential thing, which is the ultimate of ignorance. Thus it appears from what we have mentioned the corruption of effectingness being a positive attribute and being a negative attribute; therefore the claim of effectingness is false.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents the first of several counterarguments against the concept of causality (mu'aththiriyya). The objector argues that the attribute of being an effecter (mu'aththiriyya) is impossible whether considered as a positive or negative attribute. If positive, it must exist either only in the mind (making it subjective and possibly false) or in reality. If in reality, it could be identical to the effecter/effect (which is false because we can know the effecter without knowing its causal efficacy) or an extra entity. If an extra entity, it leads to infinite regress. If a negative attribute, it is contradictory because the opposite of non-existence is existence. Therefore the entire notion of causality is invalid. The argument uses logical divisions (taqsīmāt) and reasoning typical of kalam to show that the concept of a cause effecting its effect is incoherent, thus undermining the need for a separate cause.

Key Terms
المؤثرية
العدمية
العدم
الثبوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main premise in the argument against effectingness being a negative attribute?
A) Negative attributes cannot be predicated of anything.
B) The opposite of a negative predicated on non-existence must be existential.
C) Change over time requires divine intervention.
D) Effectingness is identical to non-existence.

Key Takeaway
Effectingness cannot be a negative attribute because its logical opposite and its temporal arising both indicate it is existential, and since positive option fails, causality is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that no created thing has any causal power, how would you view the means you use daily—like food to satisfy hunger or medicine to heal? Would you rely on Allah directly, or do you still feel an independent effect in those means? What does that reveal about your iman?
?
This lesson denies effectingness altogether. Does your heart attribute effects to secondary causes in a way that weakens your tawakkul? Think of a recent situation where you felt anxious about an outcome. Whom did you trust to bring it about—Allah alone, or also the apparent cause? Will you change that trust today?
?
The author shows that even the concept of causal efficacy is self-contradictory. If causality is an illusion, then every moment of your life is directly from Allah without any intermediary. How would living with that awareness transform your reaction to both blessings and hardships? Are you ready to accept that nothing effects anything except Allah?

The Timing Dilemma: When Does a Cause Act?
The objector presents a dilemma about when a cause can act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma posed by the second counterargument against causation: if a cause acts when the effect exists, it is redundant; if when it does not exist, there is nothing to act upon.

Core Idea
A cause cannot act when the effect exists (redundant) nor when it doesn't (impossible), so causation is incoherent.

This passage presents a sharp dilemma that challenges the very concept of a cause acting on an effect. The objector forces us to consider: if the cause acts when the effect already exists, it creates nothing new; if it acts when the effect does not exist, there is nothing to act upon. Either way, the notion of causal influence seems to dissolve.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a dilemma with two horns.
2
Identify the two options
The objector says the cause acts either when the effect exists or when it does not. Mark these two options.
3
Understand the rejection of each
Why is the first impossible? Because you cannot bring into existence what already exists. Why is the second false? Because non-existence has no effect to be influenced.
4
Examine the sub-argument
If influence is identical to the effect's coming-to-be, then without an effect there is no influence. If influence is different, the same problem as the first horn arises (infinite regress).
5
Reflect on the conclusion
This dilemma does not deny that events happen, but it questions the logical mechanism of causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها: أن المؤثر إما أن يؤثر في الأثر حال وجود الأثر أو حال عدمه والأول باطل لاستحالة إيجاد الموجود ، والثاني باطل لأن حال العدم لا أثر له ولا تأثير له، لأن التأثير إن كان عين حصول الأثر عن المؤثر لحيث لا أثر فلا تأثير ، وإن كان مغايراً فالكلام فيها كالسكلام في الأول .
And the second of them: That the effecter either effects the effect at the time of the effect's existence or at the time of its non-existence. The first is false due to the impossibility of bringing the existent into existence, and the second is false because at the time of non-existence there is no effect and no influence, because if the influence is identical to the occurrence of the effect from the effecter, then when there is no effect there is no influence; and if it is different, then the discussion about it is like the discussion about the first.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents the second counterargument: causation cannot occur when the effect already exists (since that would be redundant) nor when it does not exist (since there is nothing to be caused). If causation is identical to the effect's coming-to-be, then when the effect is absent there is no causation. If causation is something else, then the same problem of infinite regress arises as in the first argument. Thus the very idea of a cause acting on an effect is incoherent.

Key Terms
المؤثر
الأثر
الوجود
العدم
الإيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that causation is impossible or that effects do not occur.
Correction: The argument only questions the logical mechanism of how causation could occur, not that events happen.

Checkpoint
Why does the objector reject that the cause acts when the effect already exists?
A) Because the effect would be created twice.
B) Because it is impossible to bring the existent into existence.
C) Because the cause must be separate.
D) Because the effect would be pre-eternal.

Key Takeaway
The timing of causation leads to a dilemma: the cause cannot act when the effect exists (redundant) nor when it does not (impossible), revealing a logical tension in the concept of causal influence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience something happening in your life—like a door opening because you pushed it—do you calmly assume the push caused the open, or does this logical dilemma unsettle that assumption? Will you pause today to consider that the true cause behind every effect is Allah alone, and that our ordinary explanations are merely superficial?
?
This dilemma shows that if we think a cause acts only when the effect does not exist, we fall into absurdity. Do you sometimes attribute delays or failures to a lack of a 'cause' in your own efforts? How can this argument remind you that Allah's creation is not constrained by our limited understanding of cause and effect?
?
The argument forces you to admit that you cannot coherently describe how a cause produces an effect. Are you willing to let go of your attachment to explanations and instead trust that Allah's power is directly behind every moment of your existence—even if your mind cannot grasp the mechanism? What would change in your daily reliance on means if you truly internalised this?

The Three Impossibilities of Causal Influence
The objector argues that the cause cannot act on the essence, existence, or the attribution of existence to essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible loci of a cause's influence (essence, existence, or their attribution) and why each is considered impossible in this Kalam argument.

Core Idea
A cause cannot act on essence, existence, or their relation without contradiction.

This passage presents a sophisticated critique of causality by examining where a cause might exert its effect. The objector systematically tests three possibilities—essence, existence, or their attribution—and shows each leads to absurdity, thereby challenging the very notion of efficient causation. Understanding this argument is key to grasping the Ash'ari position on causal relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three possibilities
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objector lists three possible loci: the essence (mahiyya), existence (wujud), or the qualification of essence by existence (ittisaf al-mahiyya bi al-wujud).
This is a classic trilemma.
2
Understand the refutation of acting on essence
The objector uses the example of blackness: if blackness were blackness by another, it would cease to be blackness when that other is absent, which is impossible. Also, the reply about 'perishing' is shown to be self-contradictory.
The key is that an essence cannot depend on an external cause for its very identity.
3
Examine the refutations of acting on existence and on attribution
Acting on existence would make existence dependent on the cause, so it would cease to be existence. Acting on the attribution leads to infinite regress because the attribution itself would need an attribute, and the cycle repeats.
This shows the incoherence of causal influence in all three options.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانها: أن المؤثر إما أن يكون تأثيره فى الماهية أو فى الوجود أو فى اتصاف الماهية بالوجود ، والأول محال ، لان كل ما بالغير يلزم عدمه عند عدم ذلك الغير ، فلو كان كون السواد سوادا بالغير ازم أن لا يكون السواد سواداً عند عدم ذلك الغير وهذا محال ، لأن السواد يستحيل أن يصير غير السواد . لا يقال : نحن لا نقول السواد مع كونه سواداً يصير موصوفاً بأنه ليس بسواد ، بل نقول يفنى السواد ولا ينفى ، لانا نقول إذا قلنا يفنى السواد فهذه قضية ولكل قضية موضوع و محمول لا محالة ، والموضوع لا بد من تقرره حال الحكم بحصول ذلك المحمول أو سلبه عنه ، فإذا قلنا السواد في فالموضوع هو السواد ، فلا بد أن يكون السواد متقرراً حال ذلك الفناء ، وإن كان الفاني هو السواد أيضاً أزم أن يكون السواد متقررا في هذه الحالة ، فيلزم عند صدق قولنا السواد معدوم كون السواد متقررا أو غير متقرر وأما إن قبل المؤثر أثر في الوجود فذلك محال ، وإلا لزم أن لا يبقة الوجود وجودا عند فرض عدم ذلك التأثير وهو محال على ما مر . وأما الثالث : وهو أن يقال المؤثر أثر فى موصوفية الماهية بالوجود فلقول أولا لا يجوز أن تكون موصوفية الماهية بالوجود أمرا وجوديا ، لانها بتقدير أن تكون أمرا وجوديا لم تكن جوهرا قائما بذاته بل تكون صفة للماهية فيلزم موصوفية المساعية بها زائدة عليه ولزم التسلسل، وإذا لم تكن الموصوفية أمرا ثبوتيا استحال جعلها أثر للمؤثر أصلا ، ثم بتقدير أن تكون أمرا ثبوتيا استحال استنادها إلى المؤثر ، لأن المؤثر إما أن يؤثر فى ماهيته أو في وجوده ويعود التقسم المتقدم ، وإذا ثبت أنه لا يجوز استنادا لماهية والوجوب وانتساب أحدهما إلى الآخر إلى المؤثر كانت الموصوفية بالوجود اغنية من المؤثر ، فثبت أن القول بالتأثير باطل .
And the third of them: That the effecter's influence is either on the essence, or on existence, or on the qualification of the essence by existence. The first is impossible, because everything that is by another entails its non-existence at the non-existence of that other. So if blackness's being blackness were by another, it would necessarily not be blackness at the non-existence of that other, which is impossible because blackness cannot become non-blackness. One cannot say: we do not say that blackness while being blackness becomes qualified as non-blackness, but we say blackness perishes and is not negated. For we say: when we say 'blackness perishes', this is a proposition, and every proposition necessarily has a subject and a predicate. The subject must be established at the time of the judgment of the predicate or its negation. So if we say 'blackness perishes', the subject is blackness, so blackness must be established at the time of that perishing. And if the perishing is also blackness, then blackness must be established in that state. Thus, when our statement 'blackness is non-existent' is true, it follows that blackness is either established or not established [which is contradictory]. And if the cause acts on existence, that is impossible; otherwise, existence would not remain existence when that influence is assumed absent, which is impossible as before. As for the third [possibility], that the cause acts on the qualification of the essence by existence, we say: first, it is not permissible for the qualification of the essence by existence to be an existential thing, because if it were existential, it would not be an independent substance but an attribute of the essence, thus requiring another qualification and leading to infinite regress. And if the qualification is not a positive thing, it cannot be made an effect of the cause at all. Then, if it is positive, its attribution to the cause is impossible, because the cause would act either on its essence or on its existence, and the previous division returns. And if it is established that the essence, existence, and the relation of one to the other cannot be attributed to the cause, then the qualification by existence is independent of the cause. Thus it is established that the claim of influence (ta'thir) is false.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents the third counterargument, which examines three possible loci of causation: the essence (mahiyya), the existence (wujud), or the attribution of existence to essence (ittisaf al-mahiyya bi al-wujud). The objector shows each is impossible. Acting on the essence would make the essence dependent on another, leading to contradictions (e.g., blackness ceasing to be blackness). Acting on existence leads to similar absurdity. Acting on the attribution leads to infinite regress because the attribution itself would need an attribute. Thus, the notion of a cause effecting any change is incoherent.

Key Terms
الماهية
الوجود
الموصوفية
التأثير
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies any form of causation or that things have causes.
Correction: The argument does not deny that things have causes but shows the difficulty of conceiving how a cause relates to its effect in terms of essence, existence, or their relation.

Checkpoint
Why does the objector reject the possibility that the cause acts on the essence (mahiyya)?
A) The essence is eternal and cannot be changed.
B) The essence would then be dependent on another for its very identity, leading to absurdity.
C) The essence is unknown.
D) The essence is the same as existence.

Key Takeaway
The claim of causal influence (ta'thir) is false because any proposed locus of action—essence, existence, or attribution—leads to logical contradiction or infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If no created cause can truly effect change in essence, existence, or their relation, then what is the real basis of your confidence in the means you rely on daily? Will you today, in a small matter, consciously attribute the outcome solely to Allah and set aside the illusion of secondary causation?
?
The argument shows that even the concept of a cause 'annihilating' an essence leads to logical contradiction. When you face a loss—of health, wealth, or a loved one—do you see it as caused by something other than Allah? How might this argument reshape your reaction to such events?
?
You have probably thought that your own actions or choices cause results. The passage forces you to re-evaluate: is your 'self' (your essence) truly shaped by external influences? If not, what does that imply about your accountability and your dependence on Allah alone?

The Preponderance of Non-Existence: A Logical Extension
Opening of a fourth counterargument on preponderance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth counterargument in the kalam discussion: if existence requires a preponderator, then non-existence (as a preponderance) would also require one.

Core Idea
If existence needs a cause, non-existence needs it too.

The objector now pushes the causal principle to its breaking point. If every preponderance of one side over another — including the preponderance of existence over non-existence — demands a preponderator, then the same must hold for the opposite case: non-existence over existence. This symmetrical move challenges the consistency of the opponent's position. Let’s follow the logic closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Note the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
The passage is the beginning of a longer argument; we only have this premise.
2
Identify the key terms
Spot the words for preponderance (ترجح), preponderator (مرجح), non-existence (عدم), and existence (وجود).
These terms are central to the debate about causality.
3
Understand the logical move
The objector is applying the same principle symmetrically: if existence needs a cause, then the opposite (non-existence) would also need a cause. This is a strategy to reveal an absurdity or inconsistency.
The argument is incomplete; it likely leads to an infinite regress or contradiction.
4
Connect to broader debate
Consider why this move is a 'counterargument' — it targets the necessity of a preponderator for existence by showing the same logic would demand one for non-existence, which might be problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها: أنه لو افتقر ترجح أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر إلى المرجح لافتقر رجحان العدم على الوجود
And the fourth of them: That if the preponderance of one of the two sides of the contingent over the other were in need of a preponderator, then the preponderance of non-existence over existence would also be in need
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage begins the fourth counterargument. The objector extends the principle: if the contingent's existence requires a preponderator (murjih), then its non-existence, which is also a preponderance of one side, would equally require a preponderator. The argument is incomplete in this window; it likely continues to show that this leads to an infinite chain of preponderators. This is a typical ash'ari argument against the need for a cause for non-existence.

Key Terms
الترجح
المرجح
العدم
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that non-existence does not need a cause because it is merely absence.
Correction: The objector here argues that if existence requires a cause, then non-existence—as a preponderance of one possible state—must equally require a cause by the same principle.

Checkpoint
What does the objector claim would also need a preponderator according to the same principle?
A) The preponderance of existence over non-existence.
B) The preponderance of non-existence over existence.
C) The essence of the contingent.
D) The cause of the cause.

Key Takeaway
The objector extends the causal principle symmetrically: if existence needs a cause, then non-existence must need one too, challenging the opponent's position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something good happens to you, do you ascribe it to Allah with full conviction, but when you lose something, do you hesitate to say it is from Him? This passage forces you to ask: is He the preponderator of both existence and non-existence in your life? Can you accept that your losses are also determined by Him, and if so, how does that change your response to them?
?
The argument says if existence needs a cause, then non-existence equally does. Reflect on a time you experienced a lack or failure. Did you search for a 'why' as intensely as you would for a blessing? How does this passage challenge your belief in Allah's control over everything, including what He does not give?
?
If you truly believed that the absence of something in your life—a job, a relationship, health—was preponderated by Allah just as much as any presence, would you still complain? What would it take for you to thank Him for the non-existence of certain things with the same sincerity as for the existence of others?

Razi's Second Proof: Influence Cannot Exist or Non-Exist
Razi argues that the influencer cannot act on an effect when it exists or when it does not exist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Razi, an influencer cannot influence an effect either when the effect exists or when it does not exist.

Core Idea
An influencer cannot act on an effect that is already there or not there at all.

Razi presents a crisp logical argument to show that causal influence between creatures is impossible. He asks: When does the influencer exert its effect? It has to be either when the effect already exists or when it does not. Both options lead to absurdity. This passage is a classic example of kalam's method of exhausting possibilities (taqsīm).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic once without analyzing. Notice the two-part structure introduced by 'إما ... أو'.
The passage is concise; each horn is stated and then refuted.
2
Identify the two horns
Find the two possibilities: influence at existence and influence at non-existence. See why each is rejected.
The first horn is rejected because you cannot create what already exists. The second because non-existence has no effect to receive influence.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the argument leaves no room for causal influence in creatures, thereby reinforcing divine omnipotence.
This proof is part of a larger discussion on causality in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها : أن المؤثر إما أن يؤثر في الأثر حال وجود الأثر أو حال عدمه والأول باطل لاستحالة إيجاد الموجود ، والثاني باطل لأن حال العدم لا أثر له ولا تأثير له، لأن التأثير إن كان عين حصول الأثر عن المؤثر لحيث لا أثر فلا تأثير ، وإن كان مغايراً فالكلام فيها كالكلام في الأول .
And the second: That the influencer either influences the effect while the effect exists or while it does not exist. The first is false because of the impossibility of bringing into existence what already exists. The second is false because the state of non-existence has no effect and no influence, because if the influence is identical to the effect's occurrence from the influencer, then where there is no effect there is no influence; and if it is different, then the discussion of it is like the discussion of the first.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is the second proof against causal influence in creatures. Razi presents a dichotomy: an influencer either influences its effect when the effect already exists or when it does not exist. The first is impossible because it would mean creating something already created, which is absurd. The second is impossible because in the state of non-existence, there is no effect to receive influence. He then refines the second point: if influence is identical to the effect's coming into being from the cause, then without an effect there is no influence; if influence is something distinct from the effect, then the same problem recurs as in the first horn. Thus, both horns are impossible.

Key Terms
مؤثر
أثر
إيجاد الموجود
حال العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that causal influence can occur at the moment the effect is being created or just after.
Correction: Razi shows that neither moment works: the effect cannot be created if it already exists, and non-existence cannot receive influence. So causal influence is impossible between creatures.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's second proof, why is it impossible for an influencer to affect an effect when the effect does not exist?
A) Because the influencer would be acting on nothing, which is impossible.
B) Because the effect would have to exist to receive influence.
C) Because the state of non-existence has no effect and no influence.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
Razi's second proof shows that causal influence cannot occur at either the time of existence or non-existence, thereby ruling out causal power in creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, you often think 'I did this, so that happened.' After reading Razi's proof, can you still believe that your actions have inherent power? Take a moment to identify one habit where you rely on your own causality and replace it with a conscious acknowledgment that only Allah brings things into existence.
?
If influence cannot occur when an effect exists or when it does not exist, what does that imply for the times you felt you 'caused' something good or bad? Write down a recent event you attributed to a secondary cause and then sincerely say: 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah.'
?
Razi forces us to admit that creatures have no share in causal influence. How does this truth make you feel – uncomfortable or liberated? Spend one minute in silence, then make a du'a asking Allah to increase your reliance on Him alone as the sole influencer.

Razi's Third Proof: Impossibility of Influence on Essence, Existence, or Description
Razi enumerates three possible objects of influence and refutes each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that any causal influence must affect either essence, existence, or the description of essence by existence, and why each option leads to logical absurdity.

Core Idea
Every possible object of influence leads to contradiction.

Razi presents his third proof against the efficacy of causal influences outside of God. He divides the possible objects of influence into three categories: essence, existence, and the qualification of essence by existence. He then shows each leads to an absurdity. Understanding this argument is crucial for grasping the Ash'ari rejection of secondary causality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three possible objects of influence
Read the opening line where Razi enumerates the three categories: essence (māhiyya), existence (wujūd), and the qualification of essence by existence (ittiṣāf al-māhiyya bi'l-wujūd).
This division is exhaustive of all possibilities.
2
Understand the argument against influencing essence
Razi argues that if a thing's essence depends on another, removing that other would destroy the essence. For example, if blackness were blackness by another, it would cease to be blackness when that other is removed—impossible.
This assumes essence is self-identical.
3
Analyze the objection and reply about 'perishing'
An objector might say the thing doesn't change but perishes. Razi replies that the proposition 'blackness perishes' still requires blackness to be present at the moment of perishing, leading to a contradiction.
This refines the understanding of perishing.
4
See why influencing existence or qualification fails
Influencing existence would make existence contingent, impossible. Influencing qualification leads to infinite regress if the qualification is existent, or impossibility if non-existent.
Both are refuted via the same logic as before.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانها: أن المؤثر إما أن يكون تأثيره فى الماهية أو فى الوجود أو فى اتصاف الماهية بالوجود ، والأول محال ، لان كل ما بالغير يلزم عدمه عند عدم ذلك الغير ، فلو كان كون السواد سوادا بالغير ازم أن لا يكون السواد سواداً عند عدم ذلك الغير وهذا محال ، لأن السواد يستحيل أن يصير غير السواد . لا يقال : نحن لا نقول السواد مع كونه سواداً يصير موصوفاً بأنه ليس بسواد ، بل نقول يفنى السواد ولا ينفى ، لانا نقول إذا قلنا يفنى السواد فهذه قضية ولكل قضية موضوع و محمول لا محالة ، والموضوع لا بد من تقرره حال الحكم بحصول ذلك المحمول أو سلبه عنه ، فإذا قلنا السواد في فالموضوع هو السواد ، فلا بد أن يكون السواد متقرراً حال ذلك الفناء ، وإن كان الفاني هو السواد أيضاً أزم أن يكون السواد متقرراً في هذه الحالة ، فيلزم عند صدق قولنا السواد معدوم كون السواد متقررا أو غير متقرر وأما إن قبل المؤثر أثر في الوجود فذلك محال ، وإلا لزم أن لا يبقة الوجود وجودا عند فرض عدم ذلك التأثير وهو محال على ما مر . وأما الثالث : وهو أن يقال المؤثر أثر فى موصوفية الماهية بالوجود فلقول أولا لا يجوز أن تكون موصوفية الماهية بالوجود أمراً وجوديا ، لانها بتقدير أن تكون أمراً وجوديا لم تكن جوهرا قائما بذاته بل تكون صفة للماهية فيلزم موصوفية الماهية بها زائدة عليه ولزم التسلسل، وإذا لم تكن الموصوفية أمرا ثبوتيا استحال جعلها أثر للمؤثر أصلا ، ثم بتقدير أن تكون أمراً ثبوتيا استحال استنادها إلى المؤثر ، لأن المؤثر إما أن يؤثر فى ماهيته أو في وجوده ويعود التقسم المتقدم ، وإذا ثبت أنه لا يجوز استناد الماهية والوجود وانتساب أحدهما إلى الآخر إلى المؤثر كانت الموصوفية بالوجود مغنية عن المؤثر ، فثبت أن القول بالتأثير باطل .
And another: That the influencer either influences the essence (māhiyya) or existence (wujūd) or the qualification of the essence by existence. The first is impossible, because everything that is [what it is] by another must cease to be when that other ceases. So if blackness were blackness by another, it would follow that when that other ceases, blackness would not be blackness, which is impossible because blackness cannot become non-blackness. It cannot be said: We do not say that blackness while being blackness becomes described as not being blackness, rather we say it perishes (yafnā) and does not remain. For we say: When we say 'blackness perishes,' this is a proposition; every proposition necessarily has a subject and a predicate. The subject must be established at the time of the judgment of the predicate or its negation. So when we say 'blackness perishes,' the subject is blackness, so blackness must be established at the time of that perishing. If what perishes is also blackness, then it becomes necessary that blackness be established in that state. Thus, when we say 'blackness is non-existent,' it entails blackness being established or not established [paradoxically]. As for if the influencer influences existence, that is impossible, otherwise it would follow that existence would not remain existence when that influence is assumed absent, which is impossible as before. As for the third, that the influencer influences the qualification of the essence by existence, we say: First, it is not permissible for the qualification of the essence by existence to be an existent thing, because if it were existent, it would not be a self-subsistent substance but an attribute of the essence, thus requiring a further qualification, leading to infinite regress. And if the qualification were not an affirmative thing, it is impossible to make it an effect of an influencer at all. Then if it were affirmative, it is impossible for it to be derived from the influencer, because the influencer either influences its essence or its existence, and the previous division returns. When it is established that the derivation of essence and existence and the relation of one to the other from the influencer is not possible, the qualification by existence becomes independent of the influencer. Thus it is established that the statement of influence is false.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is the third proof, dividing the possible object of influence into three: the essence (māhiyya), existence (wujūd), or the qualification of essence by existence (ittiṣāf al-māhiyya bi'l-wujūd). Razi refutes each. First, influencing the essence is impossible because if a thing's quiddity depends on another, it would cease to be itself when that other is removed, which is absurd. A potential objection: 'We don't mean the essence changes, but it perishes.' Razi replies that the proposition 'blackness perishes' still requires blackness to be present at the moment of perishing, leading to contradiction. Second, influencing existence directly would mean that existence depends on the influence, so if influence is removed, existence would cease—but then the existence would not be self-subsistent, contradicting its nature. Third, influencing the qualification of essence by existence: if the qualification is an existent attribute, it would be additional to the essence, requiring another qualification ad infinitum. If it is not affirmative, it cannot be an effect. If affirmative, it must be influenced either in its essence or existence, repeating the earlier divisions, leading to impossibility. Hence, all possibilities are refuted.

Key Terms
ماهية
وجود
اتصاف
فناء
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if something perishes rather than changes, it avoids the problem of dependency.
Correction: Razi shows that even perishing requires the thing to be present at the moment of perishing, leading to a contradiction: it must both exist (to be the subject) and not exist (to be perishing).

Checkpoint
Why does Razi reject the possibility that an influencer affects the qualification of essence by existence?
A) Because that qualification is not a real attribute.
B) Because it leads to infinite regress whether the qualification is considered existent or not.
C) Because the qualification is identical to existence itself.
D) Because the essence cannot be qualified by existence.

Key Takeaway
Razi's third proof shows that the concept of causal influence independent of God leads to logical contradictions in every possible scenario.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen that any causal influence independent of God leads to absurdity. But in your daily life, do you act as if natural causes are self-sufficient? When you fall ill, do you truly turn to Allah as the direct creator of your recovery, or do you rely solely on medicine as an independent cause? What would change if you lived with the certainty that every atom of effect is directly from Him?
?
Razi's proof shows that even the relationship between essence and existence cannot be influenced. Consider your own existence: do you feel that your being is borrowed from Allah at every moment? Or do you take your continued existence for granted, as if it were a permanent property? How would a deeper awareness of this constant dependence reshape your worship and reliance on Allah?
