Fourth Proof: Non-Existence Cannot Require a Preponderator
Razi argues that requiring a preponderator for non-existence of a possible leads to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's fourth proof that a preponderator is not needed for non-existence because non-existence cannot be an effect.

Core Idea
Non-existence is pure negation, so it can't be the effect of any cause.

In this passage, Razi presents a concise yet powerful argument against the necessity of a preponderator for non-existence. He reasons that if a preponderator were needed, then non-existence would have to be its effect. But since non-existence is pure negation, it cannot be an effect. This leads to an absurdity, proving the original assumption false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Try to follow the logical flow.
Notice the conditional: 'if...then' and the conclusion that it is impossible.
2
Identify the conditional argument
Razi starts with a conditional: if a preponderator is required for one side of a possible, then it would also be required for non-existence. This sets up a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Grasp the impossibility
The preponderator must have an effect, but non-existence is pure negation, so it cannot be an effect. Therefore, the consequent is impossible, making the antecedent false.
This is the core of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها: أنه لو افتقر ترجح أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر إلى المرجح لافتقر رجحان العدم على الوجود إلى المرجح لكن ذلك محال لأن المرجح مؤثر فى الترجيح والمؤثر لا بد له من أثر والعدم أى محض فيستحيل إسناده إلى المؤثر .
And the fourth: That if the preponderance of one side of the possible over the other required a preponderator (murajjiḥ), then the preponderance of non-existence over existence would require a preponderator, but that is impossible, because the preponderator is an influencer in preponderance, and the influencer must have an effect, and non-existence is pure [negation], so it is impossible to attribute it to the influencer.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is the fourth proof against causal influence. Razi argues that if a possible (mumkin) needed a preponderator (murajjiḥ) to determine one of its two sides (existence or non-existence), then the preponderance of non-existence over existence would also need a preponderator. But this is impossible because the preponderator is an influencer (mu'aththir), and every influencer must have an effect (athar). Non-existence (adam) is pure negation, so it cannot be an effect. Therefore, the original assumption that a preponderator is needed for non-existence is false.

Key Terms
ممكن
ترجح
مرجح
أثر
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The need for a preponderator applies equally to existence and non-existence.
Correction: Razi demonstrates that non-existence cannot be an effect because it is pure negation, so a preponderator cannot influence it.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's fourth proof, why is it impossible for non-existence to require a preponderator?
A) Because non-existence is not a real thing.
B) Because the preponderator would have no effect, as non-existence is pure negation.
C) Because preponderators only apply to existence.
D) Because the possible does not need a preponderator at all.

Key Takeaway
Non-existence is pure negation and thus cannot be the effect of any preponderator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If non-existence is pure negation and cannot be an effect, then your very existence is not a result of chance or a void—it requires a positive cause. Have you truly internalized that you exist because of a specific will and causation, and how should that change your daily gratitude?
?
This proof refutes the idea that the universe could have simply remained non-existent. When you look at the world, do you see it as a passive possibility that happened to become actual, or do you see the hand of a preponderator who chose it over non-existence? How does that affect your trust in God's wisdom?
?
The argument states that non-existence cannot be attributed to an influencer. Yet, in your life, do you sometimes act as if the absence of good in your life is just 'nothing'—a void—or do you realize that every good thing must come from a source? How will you actively attribute blessings to the true influencer?

Razi on the Self-Evidence of the Cause of Non-Existence
Razi responds to the objection that the cause of non-existence is the absence of cause, arguing it is self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi defends the self-evident nature of the principle 'the cause of non-existence is the absence of cause' and why objections to it are mistaken.

Core Idea
The cause of non-existence is self-evident once understood correctly.

In Islamic theology, causal principles are often debated. Here, Razi tackles an objection that seems to deny a basic rule: that non-existence is caused by the absence of its cause. He argues not only that the objection fails but that the principle is self-evident. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening statement: 'The cause of non-existence is the absence of cause.' This is the view that Razi will refute. Notice what it claims about causality and non-existence.
The objector seems to think that non-existence can have a cause, but Razi will show this is misguided.
2
Follow Razi's refutation
Razi gives two arguments against the objection. First, causality is an affirmative attribute, so its subject must exist. Second, non-existence lacks distinctions, so it cannot have cause-effect parts. Trace these arguments in the text.
These refutations show the logical incoherence of the objection.
3
Discover the self-evident answer
Razi then states that the proposition is actually self-evident. He provides a rational proof: a possible exists only when it becomes necessary by a cause; that necessity is an existent attribute requiring an existing subject, which is the cause itself. Thus, the absence of cause leads to non-existence.
Notice how Razi transforms the objection into a self-evident truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : علة العدم عدم العلة . قلت هذا خطأ لأنه العلية مناقضة للإعلية التي هي عدم ، فالعلية ثبوتية فالموصوف بها ثابت ، وإلا فالمعدوم موصوف بالوجود وهو محال ، ولأن العدم لا تميز فيه ولا تعدد ولا هوية فيستحيل جعل بعضه علة والبعض معلولا . والجواب : أن تلك القضية بديهية والتفاوت بينها وبين سائر البديهيات محال في العقل، وإن حاولنا البرهان قلنا : الممكن ما لم يجب لم يوجد وذلك الوجوب لما حصل بعد أن لم يكن كان وصفا وجوديا ويستدعى موصوفاً موجوداً وليس هو ذلك الممكن ، لأنه قبل وجوده معدوم فلابد من شيء آخر يعرض ذلك الوجوب له بالنسبة إلى ذلك الممكن وذلك هو الأثر .
If you say: The cause of non-existence is the absence of cause. I say: This is wrong, because causality (al-illiyya) is the opposite of being uncaused (al-illiyya which is non-existence), so causality is affirmative, and the thing qualified by it is existent. Otherwise, a non-existent would be qualified by existence, which is impossible. And because non-existence has no distinction, no multiplicity, no identity, so it is impossible to make part of it a cause and part an effect. And the answer: That proposition is self-evident, and difference between it and other self-evidents is impossible in the intellect. Even if we attempt a proof, we say: The possible, as long as it is not necessary, does not exist. That necessity, when it occurs after not having been, is an existent attribute and requires an existing subject, and that subject is not that possible, because before its existence it is non-existent, so there must be another thing upon which that necessity occurs relative to that possible, and that is the effect [of the cause].
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and response. The objector says: 'The cause of non-existence is the absence of cause.' Razi first refutes this by arguing that causality (illiyya) is an affirmative attribute; its opposite (being uncaused) is privative. If causality were privative, a non-existent would be described by existence, which is absurd. Also, non-existence has no distinctions, so it cannot be divided into cause and effect. Then he gives a fuller answer: The proposition 'the cause of non-existence is the absence of cause' is actually self-evident (badīhiyya), but one should not think that it conflicts with other self-evident truths. To prove it, he argues: A possible does not exist unless it becomes necessary (wājib) by a cause. That necessity is an existent attribute requiring an existing subject. That subject is not the possible itself (which is non-existent before being caused), so it must be something else—the cause—upon which the necessity occurs. Thus, the effect is that attribute of necessity, not non-existence itself.

Key Terms
علة العدم
علية
بديهة
وجوب
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proposition 'the cause of non-existence is the absence of cause' is false or requires proof.
Correction: Razi argues that the proposition is actually self-evident (badīhi) and provides a rational proof to clarify its meaning, showing it is logically sound.

Checkpoint
How does Razi refute the objection that 'the cause of non-existence is the absence of cause'?
A) By denying that non-existence has any cause.
B) By arguing that causality is an affirmative attribute, so it cannot apply to non-existence.
C) By claiming the proposition is false.
D) By saying the absence of cause is a cause itself.

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that the principle 'the cause of non-existence is the absence of cause' is self-evident and rationally defended.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own existence, do you see yourself as the cause of your own achievements, or do you recognize that you are only possible and that every good in you comes from a cause outside you? What would it mean to live daily with that awareness?
?
Razi says that non-existence has no distinction or identity. In your life, you often think of 'lack' as something real—like a lack of money, love, or success. How does realizing that non-existence is purely privative change the weight you give to what you don't have?
?
If the principle that 'the cause of non-existence is the absence of cause' is self-evident, then why do you still find yourself complaining when things go wrong? Could it be that you are actually complaining about the absence of a cause you wanted, rather than a real evil? How will you realign your heart with this truth?

Refutation of Four Counter-Arguments (Mu'arada) in Kalam Proofs
Razi refutes four objections that attempt to show his reasoning leads to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi systematically refutes four objections that attempt to use his own method of division (taqsīm) against him, revealing that the objections are self-refuting and do not undermine his original proof.

Core Idea
Counter-arguments based on division are self-refuting when applied to self-evident truths.

Razi has presented a powerful divisive argument to establish the need for a preponderator. Opponents attempt to turn the same method against him, applying it to self-evident facts like one's existence at a given time. In this passage, Razi systematically dismantles each counter-argument, showing that the objections actually invalidate self-evident truths, not his proof. You'll see four distinct refutations and a final point about non-existence not needing a preponderator.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of Razi's proof
Razi earlier used a division (taqsīm) to argue that if something does not have a preponderator, it leads to absurdity. Opponents now apply a similar division to self-evident truths to counter him.
Refer to previous lessons on Razi's proof from preponderance (tarjīḥ).
2
Read the first counter-argument and its refutation
The first objection applies division to one's existence at a given time. Razi shows it leads to absurd consequences and concludes that the division invalidates self-evident truths.
Notice Razi does not deny the division; he highlights its consequence.
3
Study the second and third counter-arguments
The second concerns temporal origination (ḥudūth) of a sound; the third divides event into essence, existence, or qualification. Both are shown to invalidate necessary/self-evident truths.
Razi includes a difficulty (ishkāl) after the third refutation.
4
Analyze the fourth counter-argument
The fourth claims non-existence could be preponderant without a preponderator. Razi refutes by stating non-existence is pure negation and cannot be described as preponderant.
This reiterates an earlier point from Razi's proof.
5
Reflect on Razi's overall strategy
Razi does not reject the method of division; he argues that the objections misuse it and end up undermining self-evident truths, thereby protecting his original proof.
Consider why the opponents' arguments are called mu‘āraḍa (counter-arguments).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المعارضة الأولى : فمدفوعة ، لأن ذلك التقسيم قد يتوجه فيما يعلم وجوده بالضرورة كما لو قيل لو كنت أنا موجودا في هذه الساعة لكان كونى فيها إما أن يكون عدميا وهو محال ، لأنه نقيض اللاكون فيها وهو عدمى ونقيض العدم ثبوت، أو يكون ثبوتيا وهو إما عين الذات فيلزم أن لا تبقى الذات عندما يبقى حصوله في تلك الساعة أو زائداً عليه فيكون ذلك الزائد حاصلا في تلك الساعة وازم التسلسل ، ولما كان حصوله فى هذه الساعة يفضى إلى هذه الاقسام الباطلة وجب أن لا يكون له حصول في هذه الساعة فظهر أن هذا التقسيم مبطل للبديهيات . وأما المعارضة الثانية : فهى كذلك أيضا ، لأنه أحداث ، فإن كان في محل البحث ، اليك لا نزاع في الحدوث والتقسيم الذي ذكرتموه يدفعه لأنه يقال إن حدث هذا الصوت مثلا فإما أن يكون حدوثه حال وجوده أو حال عدمه ، فإن حدث حال عدمه وجوده فقد وجد الموجود وإن حدث حال عدمه فقد وجد عند عدمه ، فظهر أن هذا التقسيم مبطل الضروريات . وأما المعارضة الثالثة : فهي أيضاً كذلك لأنه يقال له : إن حدث هذا الصوت لكان الحادث : إما الماهية أو الوجود أو موصوفية الماهية بالوجود ، فان كان الأول فقد انقلب ما ليس بصوت صوتا وإن كان الثاني فقد انقلب ما ليس بوجود وجودا وكذا الثالث فظهر أن هذا التقسيم مبطل للبديهيات وهنا أشكال وهو أن للقادمين فى البديهيات أن يقولوا لما عجزتم عن القدح في مقدمات هذا التقسيم مع أنكم عليتم أن الميجته باطلة لزم منه تطرق القدح إلى البديهيات . وأما المعاوضة الرابعة : فمدفوعة لأن العدم نفى محض فيستحيل وصفه بالرجحان ، فلا جرم لا يفتقر إلى مرجح .
As for the first counter-argument: It is refuted, because that division may apply to what is known to exist necessarily, as if one said: If I exist at this hour, then my being in it is either non-existential—impossible because it is the opposite of my not being in it, which is non-existential, and the opposite of non-existence is existence—or it is existential, and it is either identical to the essence, then it follows that the essence would not remain when its occurrence in that hour remains, or additional to it, then that additional would occur in that hour and entail infinite regress. Since its occurrence in this hour leads to these false divisions, it must be that it does not occur in this hour. So it appears that this division invalidates self-evident truths. As for the second counter-argument: It is also thus, because it concerns events. If it is in the subject under discussion, behold, there is no dispute about temporal origination (ḥudūth), and the division you mentioned refutes it, because it is said: If this sound, for example, occurred, then its occurrence either happens during its existence or during its non-existence. If it occurs during its existence, then the existent has been brought into existence; if it occurs during its non-existence, then it has occurred while it is non-existent. So it appears that this division invalidates necessary truths. As for the third counter-argument: It is also thus, because it is said to him: If this sound occurred, then the event is either the essence, existence, or the qualification of essence by existence. If the first, then what is not sound has turned into sound; if the second, then what is not existence has turned into existence; and similarly the third. So it appears that this division invalidates self-evident truths. And here there is a difficulty: The proponents of the self-evident truths may say: Since you are unable to refute the premises of this division, despite your claim that the [conclusion] is false, it follows that the refutation reaches self-evident truths. As for the fourth counter-argument: It is refuted because non-existence is pure negation, so it is impossible to describe it as being preponderant; therefore, it does not require a preponderator.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage refutes four counter-arguments (mu'arada) leveled against Razi's proofs. The first counter-argument tries to apply Razi's own divisive reasoning to the self-evident fact of one's existence at a given time, showing that it leads to absurdities. Razi responds that such divisions apply to self-evident truths but do not invalidate them; rather, they show that the division itself is flawed. The second counter-argument concerns temporal origination (ḥudūth): if a sound occurs, its occurrence either happens when it exists (impossible) or when it does not exist (impossible). Razi says this division actually refutes the objector's position because it leads to absurdity. The third counter-argument similarly applies the division into essence, existence, or qualification to the occurrence of a sound, again leading to absurdity. Razi acknowledges a difficulty: opponents might say that if one cannot refute the premises of such divisions, yet their conclusion is false, then it demonstrates that self-evident truths are undermined. But Razi does not concede this; he simply states the refutation. The fourth counter-argument claims that non-existence could be preponderant without a preponderator. Razi repeats his earlier point: non-existence is pure negation, so it cannot be described as preponderant, thus no preponderator is needed.

Key Terms
معارضة
تقسيم
بديهيات
ضروريات
حدوث

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Razi's divisive arguments prove too much and can be turned against him to show self-evident truths are false.
Correction: Razi argues that the counter-arguments misuse the division and end up invalidating self-evident truths, not his proof. The method of division is valid; the objections are self-refuting.

Checkpoint
How does Razi respond to the first counter-argument that applies his division to one's existence at a given time?
A) He admits the division is flawed.
B) He argues that the division leads to absurdity only for creatures, not for the self.
C) He states that such divisions invalidate self-evident truths, but that does not disprove his point.
D) He says the division is invalid because it applies to necessary existence.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes four counter-arguments by showing that applying the method of division to self-evident truths leads to absurdity, thereby protecting his original proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Razi uses self-evident truths as a bulwark against flawed objections. What self-evident truths do you rely on in your daily life, and have you ever examined if your actions contradict them?
?
The opponents argued that if a logical division leads to absurdity, then the premises must be doubted. Are there beliefs you hold that you have not subjected to such logical scrutiny because you fear the consequences?
?
Razi refutes four counter-arguments, yet he does not resolve the difficulty he himself raises. How do you handle objections to your faith that you cannot immediately answer? Do you dismiss them, or do you wrestle with them until you find clarity?

The Contingent Requires a Preponderator and the Refutation of the Objection
Imam Razi argues that a contingent must have a preponderator and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a contingent being requires a preponderator and how Imam Razi refutes the objection that non-existence can be explained by absence of cause.

Core Idea
A contingent's existence needs a positive cause, not just the negation of obstacles.

Imam Razi presents a foundational argument in Islamic theology: every contingent (mumkin) must have a preponderator (murajjih) to bring it from non-existence to existence. He then anticipates and refutes a common objection that the cause of non-existence is merely the absence of a cause. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and shows why non-existence cannot serve as a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage may contain OCR uncertainties; focus on the overall argument.
2
Identify the main claim and proof
The passage begins by asserting that a preponderator must have an effect, and non-existence cannot be that effect. Note the reasoning: effecter requires an effect, and non-existence is pure negation.
3
Analyze the objection and refutation
Find the objection that the cause of non-existence is the absence of the cause. Then trace the refutation: causality is a positive attribute, so it cannot inhere in non-existence. Also, non-existence has no distinctions.
The refutation uses logical contradiction.
4
Understand the alternative proof
The author then offers an alternative proof from self-evidence: necessity (wujub) is a positive attribute that must inhere in an existent subject distinct from the contingent itself. This subject is the effect of the preponderator.
This reinforces the necessity of a positive cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى المرجح لكن ذلك محال لأن المرجح مؤثر فى الترجيح والمؤثر لا بد له من أثر والعدم اني محض فيستحيل إسناده إلى المؤثر . فإن قلت : علة العدم عدم العلة . قلت هذا خطأ لأنه العملية مناقضة للأعلية التي هي عدم ، فالعلية ثبوتية فالموصوف بها ثابت ، وإلا فالمعدوم موصوف بالوجود وهو محال ، ولأن العدم لا تميز فيه ولا تعدد ولا هوية فيستحيل جعل بعضه علة والبعض معلولا . والجواب : أن تلك الفضية بديهية والتفاوت بينها وبين سائر البديهيات محال في العقل، وإن حاولنا البرهان قلنا : المملكى ما لم يجب لم يوجد وذلك الوجوب لما حصل بعد أن لم يكى كان وصفا وجوديا ويستدعى موصوفا موجوداً وليس هو ذلك الممكن ، لأنه قبل وجوده معدوم فلابد من شيء آخر يعرض ذلك الوجوب له بالنسبة إلى ذلك المسكي وذلك هو الأثر .
To the preponderator, but that is impossible because the preponderator is an effecter in preponderance, and the effecter must have an effect, and non-existence is pure negation, so relating it to the effecter is impossible. If you say: The cause of non-existence is the absence of the cause. I say: This is a mistake because causality is contradictory to non-causality which is non-existence. Causality is positive, so that which is described by it is existent; otherwise, the non-existent would be described with existence, which is impossible. Also, because non-existence has no distinction, no multiplicity, and no identity, so it is impossible to make some of it a cause and some an effect. The answer: That proposition is self-evident, and difference between it and other self-evident propositions is impossible in the intellect. If we attempt a proof, we say: The contingent, as long as it is not necessary, does not exist. That necessity, when it occurs after it was not, is a positive attribute and requires an existing subject, and it is not that contingent itself, because before its existence it is non-existent. So there must be something else upon which that necessity occurs in relation to that contingent, and that is the effect.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a proof and objection-answer unit. The author first states the argument that a contingent being (mumkin) requires a preponderator (murajjih) to tip the balance from non-existence to existence. He then anticipates an objection: the cause of non-existence is merely the absence of the cause, so no positive effect is needed. He refutes this by arguing that causality (al-'illiyya) is a positive attribute; if non-existence were its subject, non-existence would be described with existence, a contradiction. Non-existence has no distinctions, so it cannot be divided into cause and effect. The author then gives the definitive answer: the proposition 'what is not necessary does not exist' is self-evident. If we insist on a proof, he says: necessity (wujub) is a positive attribute that must inhere in an existent subject, not the contingent itself (which is non-existent before creation). Hence there must be an external cause (the preponderator) that receives this necessity in relation to the contingent—that is the effect (athar).

Key Terms
مرجح
ممكن
أثر
وجوب
علية
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The cause of non-existence is merely the absence of the cause, so no positive effecter is needed.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that causality is a positive attribute and cannot inhere in pure non-existence. Non-existence has no distinctions, so it cannot be divided into cause and effect. Furthermore, the necessity that brings a contingent into existence is a positive attribute that requires an existing subject, proving a real effect.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi reject the claim that the cause of non-existence is the absence of the cause?
A) Because non-existence can be a cause.
B) Because causality is a positive attribute and cannot inhere in pure non-existence.
C) Because the preponderator does not need an effect.
D) Because non-existence has distinctions.

Key Takeaway
A contingent's existence requires a positive cause (the preponderator's effect), not merely the absence of a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every contingent being depends on a preponderator for its existence, then your own existence at this very moment is being sustained by a cause that is not yourself. Are you living as if your existence is self-sufficient, or do you constantly acknowledge your dependence on the One who gives you existence moment by moment? What specific habit can you change today to remind yourself of this dependence?
?
Imam Razi argues that non-existence cannot be a cause because it has no distinctions. Consider the times you have attributed your failures or lack of good deeds to 'circumstances' or 'lack of opportunity'—essentially negating personal responsibility. How does this argument expose the fallacy of blaming non-existence for your spiritual state? What will you actively choose to do this week to be a positive effecter in your own faith?
?
The preponderator's effect is a real, positive attribute that requires an existing subject. When you claim to believe in God as the creator, do you actually feel that His effect is real in your daily life—or is it just a concept? If His effect is real, what evidence of that effect should be visible in your actions, speech, and thoughts? Commit to one concrete action today that makes His causation manifest.

The Mental Status of Causality: Razi's Response
Imam Razi responds to an objection that causality is a relative concept existing only in the mind.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi defends the concept of causality (mu'aththiriyya) as a relative notion that exists in the mind, not externally, and how this does not constitute ignorance.

Core Idea
Causality is a mental relation, not an external attribute — and that's perfectly fine.

In this passage, Imam Razi addresses a key objection against the reality of causality. The objector argues that if causality were merely mental, then our judgments about causes would be ignorant. Razi carefully distinguishes between judging something to exist externally (which can be false) and recognizing its mental existence (which is correct). He also tackles the claim that causality must precede minds and clarifies that causality is distinct from our knowledge of it. This discussion is crucial for understanding how the mind relates to the world in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of Razi's response.
Notice the technical terms like 'amr iḍāfī' and 'iḍāfiyyāt'.
2
Identify the core claim
Razi states causality is a relative matter established in the mind when conceiving an effect from a cause.
Compare to other relatives like fatherhood.
3
Examine the ignorance objection
Understand why non-correspondence to external does not imply ignorance—ignorance is about false judgment of external existence.
Razi uses the example of believing the world eternal as a case of genuine ignorance.
4
Analyze the precedence argument
Razi explains that what precedes minds is the potential for a relation, not the actual mental occurrence.
The actual mental relation cannot exist before the mind.
5
Distinguish causality from its knowledge
Note Razi's final point that causality and knowledge of causality are two distinct mental entities.
Both exist in the mind but are not identical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعارضة الأولى فالمؤرية المذكورة فيها أمر إضافي يثبت في العقل عند تعقل صدور الأمر عن المؤثر، فإن تنقل ذلك يقتضى ثبوت أمر فى العقل هو المؤثرية كما فى سائر الإضافيات . وعدم مطابقته للخارج لا يقتضى كونه جهلا فإن ذلك إنما يكون جهلا إذا حكم بثبوته فى الخارج، ولم يثبت في الخارج اعتقاد كون العالم قديما مع كونه ليس بقديم الذي يمثل به فى الجهل يدل على ما ذكرنا لا على ما أورده في مثاله ، وعدم مطابقته لا يقتضى أيضا أن لا يكون شيء مؤرًا أصلا كما قال ، بل إذا حكم بثبوته في العقل فقط فمطابقته ثبوته في المقل دون الخارج . وقوله : المؤثرية صفة قبل الأذهان وصفة لشيء يستحيل قيامها بغيره . الجوابه أن كون الشيء بحيث لو دقله عاقل حصل لعقله إضافة الك الشيء إلى غيره هو الحاصل قبل الأذهان لا الذى يحصل في العقل ، فإن ذلك يستحيل أن يحصل قبل وجود العقل . وأما قوله : إلا أن يقال الوجود في الذهن هو العلم بالمؤثرية لكن ذلك لا يفيد لما تقدم ، فجوابه الصحيح أن المؤثرية غير العلم بالمؤثرية مع كونهما ثابتين في العقل لاما أحال عليه فيما تقدم ، والقول في باقي كلامه في فساد كون المزثرية ثبوتية ظاهر ما ذكرنا .
As for the first objection: the causality (al-muʾaththiriyya) mentioned in it is a relative matter (amr iḍāfī) that is established in the mind when conceiving the issuing of the thing from the efficient cause (muʾaththir). For reflecting on that necessitates the establishment of a thing in the mind which is causality, just as in other relatives (iḍāfiyyāt). And its non-correspondence to the external does not necessitate that it is ignorance, for that would only be ignorance if one judges that it is established in the external, and it is not established in the external. The belief that the world is eternal while it is not eternal, which he uses as an example of ignorance, indicates what we mentioned, not what he adduced in his example. And its non-correspondence also does not necessitate that there is no cause at all, as he said. Rather, if one judges that it is established only in the mind, then its correspondence is its establishment in the mind, not in the external. And his statement: 'Causality is a quality preceding minds (qabl al-adhhān) and a quality of a thing which it is impossible for it to subsist in another.' The answer is: that a thing being such that if a rational being reflected on it, there would occur in his mind an addition (iḍāfa) of the thing to another, that is what is preceding the minds, not that which occurs in the mind, for that is impossible to occur before the existence of the mind. And as for his statement: 'Unless it is said that existence in the mind is knowledge of causality, but that does not benefit due to what preceded.' The correct answer is that causality is other than knowledge of causality, while both are established in the mind, because he forbade what he forbade previously. And the statement concerning the rest of his speech regarding the invalidity of causality being positive (thubūtiyya) is evident from what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the nature of causality (al-muʾaththiriyya). The objector likely argued that causality is an attribute that must exist externally, and if it is merely mental, then we are ignorant. Imam Razi's answer clarifies that causality is a relative concept (amr iḍāfī) that arises in the mind when we conceive an effect issuing from a cause. It is like other relations (iḍāfiyyāt) that exist only in the mind. He distinguishes between a judgment that something exists in the external (which would be false if it doesn't) and a judgment that it exists only in the mind (which is correct). The objector's example of ignorance (believing the world eternal) involves judging external existence incorrectly, not the case of causality. Then he addresses the claim that causality is 'preceding minds' (qabl al-adhhān) meaning it is a real attribute. Razi answers that what precedes minds is the potentiality that if a rational being reflected, a mental relation would arise; this potentiality is not itself a mental occurrence (which cannot precede the mind). Finally, he corrects the idea that causality is identical to knowledge of causality: they are two distinct mental entities (causality and its knowledge) both established in the mind. The passage shows the author defending the mental status of causality while rejecting objections that would undermine the reality of causal reasoning.

Key Terms
المؤثرية (al-muʾaththiriyya)
إضافي (iḍāfī)
الإضافيات (al-iḍāfiyyāt)
ثبوتي (thubūtī)
قبل الأذهان (qabl al-adhhān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If causality is only mental, then there is no real causality in the world.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, why is the non-correspondence of causality to the external not ignorance?
A) Because causality actually exists externally.
B) Because ignorance only occurs when one falsely judges something to exist externally, not when one judges it to exist only in the mind.
C) Because the objector's example of the eternal world is irrelevant.
D) Because causality is a positive attribute.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi defends causality as a valid mental concept that does not require external existence, distinguishing it from false judgments or ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you attribute a good outcome to your own effort, do you ever pause to consider that the causal link itself may exist only in your mind? How does that thought affect your reliance on your own planning versus reliance on God?
?
Reflect on a time you were absolutely certain that a person or situation was 'the cause' of your happiness or distress. If, as Razi argues, causality is a mental relation, how might that certainty be misplaced? What would it mean to hold that judgment more lightly?
?
If causality is a mental construct, what does that imply for your daily gratitude and supplication? Are you directing your thanks and requests to a God who operates beyond human mental categories, or do you unconsciously limit Him to your own causal frameworks? How can you transform your worship to reflect this understanding?

Razi's Refutation of the Division of Influence
Imam Razi refutes the division of influence into existence or non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the objection that causal influence cannot occur at the time of existence or non-existence of the effect, and the alternative accounts offered by theologians.

Core Idea
Influence is not confined to existence or non-existence; it occurs at the moment of coming-into-being.

Imam Razi continues to defend the possibility of causal influence. The second objection attempts to prove that influence is impossible by dividing it into two exclusive options: either at the time of the effect's existence or its non-existence. Razi shows that this division is false and clarifies the correct relationship between cause and effect, including the views of later theologians.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Razi's initial response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objector divides influence into two impossible cases, and Razi responds with 'it is not so'.
Pay attention to the structure: objection, then refutation.
2
Distinguish between temporal and essential senses
Razi clarifies that if 'place of existence' means time of existence, it is not impossible for cause to influence effect at that time. If it means essential distinction, that is impossible. Understand this key distinction.
3
Explore the alternative accounts
Razi presents the view of some theologians that influence occurs at the moment of origination (ḥudūth), and later theologians who affirm temporal simultaneity with priority. See how these accounts avoid the false division.
This shows the richness of the kalam debate on causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعارضة الثانية قسمة التأثير بأنه يحصل إما في حال وجود الأثر أو فى حال عدمه وهما باطلان فليس كذلك ، لأنه إن أراد بمحال وجود الأثر زمان وجوده فليس مستحيل أن يؤثر المؤثر فى الأثر في زمان وجود الأثر لأن العملة مع معلولها تكون هذه الصفة ، وإن أراد به مغايرته المؤثر للأثر الذاتية فذلك مستحيل ، وإنما يؤثر فيه لا من حيث هو موجود ولا من حيث هو معدوم ، وبعض المتكلمين يقولون المؤثر يؤثر حال حدوث الأثر فإنها ليست بحال الوجود ولا بحال العدم . وقوله في الجواب : إن هذه القسمة مبطلة للضروريات باطل ودال على تحيره في أمثال هذه المواضع وقد يمكن أن يقال ما يقول المتأخرون من المتكلمين الذين يقولون بمقارنة العملة والمعلول في الزمان ، فإنهم يقولون الذي يوجد في الآن الثاني يصدر من موجده في الآن الذى قبله فيكون التأثير سابقاً على الأثر ( بأن ) ويقع بالقياس إلى ما يحصل بعده سواء كان الأمر موجوداً في ذلك الآن بتأثير آخر أو معدوما ويكون الأثر في آن التأثير غير موجود وفي الآن الذي يصير موجوداً لا يكون مقارناً للعدم ذلك
As for the second objection: the division of influence (taʾthīr) into either occurring at the time of the existence of the effect or at its non-existence, both being invalid — it is not so. For if he means by the place of existence of the effect the time of its existence, then it is not impossible for the cause to influence the effect at the time of the effect's existence, because the cause with its effect has this description. And if he means by it the essential (dhātiyya) distinction between the cause and the effect, then that is impossible. Rather, it influences it not insofar as it exists nor insofar as it is non-existent. And some theologians (mutakallimūn) say: the cause influences at the moment of the occurrence (ḥudūth) of the effect, for it is not a state of existence nor a state of non-existence. And his statement in the answer: 'This division invalidates self-evident truths' is false and indicates his confusion in such topics. And it can be said what the later theologians say, those who affirm the temporal simultaneity (muqārana) of cause and effect: they say that what comes into existence in the second instant originates from its originator in the instant before it, so the influence precedes the effect, and it occurs relative to what comes after it, whether the thing exists in that instant by another influence or is non-existent; and the effect at the instant of influence is non-existent, and at the instant it becomes existent it is not simultaneous with non-existence.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the timing of causal influence. The objector divides influence into two possibilities: either it occurs when the effect exists or when it is non-existent, and claims both are impossible. Imam Razi refutes this by distinguishing two senses: (1) If 'place of existence' means the time of existence, it is not impossible for the cause to influence the effect at that time because cause and effect coexist temporally (the cause with its effect). (2) If it means essential distinction, then that is indeed impossible—but the true account is that influence occurs not insofar as the effect exists or does not exist, but at the moment of its coming-into-being (ḥudūth). Some theologians specify that influence happens at the instant of origination, which is neither a state of existence nor non-existence. Razi then criticizes the objector's response (that the division destroys self-evident truths) as false and confused. He offers an alternative account from later theologians: there is temporal simultaneity (muqārana) of cause and effect? Actually he describes a sequence: the effect in the second instant originates from the cause in the prior instant, so influence precedes the effect temporally. The effect at the instant of influence is non-existent, and at the instant it becomes existent it is not coexistent with absolute nothing. This shows a nuanced understanding of causal priority.

Key Terms
التأثير (al-taʾthīr)
وجود الأثر (wujūd al-athar)
عدم (ʿadam)
حدوث (ḥudūth)
مقارنة العلة والمعلول (muqārana al-ʿilla wa al-maʿlūl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that influence cannot occur at the time of existence because the effect would already be there, making influence redundant.
Correction: Razi clarifies that temporal simultaneity is possible: the cause can influence the effect while it exists, not just before or after.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, how can a cause influence an effect at the time of the effect's existence?
A) It cannot; influence must precede existence.
B) By essential distinction between cause and effect.
C) In the sense that the cause and effect coexist in time.
D) Only at the moment of non-existence.

Key Takeaway
Causal influence is not confined to the existence or non-existence of the effect; it occurs at the moment of origination, and later theologians specify a temporal priority that avoids the false dichotomy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read where Razi says: 'Rather, it influences it not insofar as it exists nor insofar as it is non-existent.' Where in your life have you assumed that your influence over a situation only counts when you can see immediate results (existence) or when you have completely given up (non-existence)? Are you neglecting the moment of occurrence—the exact instant of decision or action? What would it mean for you to act at that moment right now?
?
The passage mentions that later theologians say influence precedes the effect in time. Think of one thing you wish to change about yourself. Have you been waiting for the change to happen first before believing it can be influenced? How does this teaching that your influence is earlier than the effect challenge you to start influencing that change before you see any result?
?
Imam Razi accuses his opponent of being confused about self-evident truths. Is there a 'self-evident truth' in your life—something you have taken for granted about how change happens—that this passage calls into question? What would it cost you to admit you might be wrong?

The Two Senses of Necessity and the Homonymy Error
Razi distinguishes essential necessity from consequent necessity and identifies the objector's confusion as homonymy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that the objection that influence cannot be in the essence rests on confusing two senses of necessity.

Core Idea
Necessity is not one; confusing essential and consequent necessity produces false dilemmas.

Imam Razi continues his refutation of objections to causal influence. The third objection claims that influence cannot be in the essence, because if blackness is black by another, it would cease to be black when the other is absent. Razi shows that this reasoning conflates two distinct meanings of 'necessity' — a classic case of homonymy.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's claim: influence cannot be in the essence because that would make blackness depend on another, leading to a contradiction.
Notice the objector assumes a single meaning of necessity.
2
Identify Razi's response
Razi distinguishes between two kinds of necessity: essential necessity (the blackness is black by itself) and consequent necessity (blackness is necessarily black due to a cause).
3
Understand the homonymy critique
Razi charges that the objector commits a sophistry by using the word 'wujūb' (necessity) with two different meanings, a homonymy (lafẓ mushtarak).
This is a key logical fallacy.
4
Apply the distinction
Consider how the distinction resolves the objection: influence can be in the essence without absurdity if we understand the necessity as consequent and not essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في المعارضة الثالثة : فقوله تأثير المؤثر إما في الماهية أو في الوجود أو فى اتصاف الماهية بالوجود يجاب عنه بأنه في الماهية قوله ذلك محال، لأن كون السواد سواداً بالغير يوجب أن لا يكون السواد سوادا عند عدم الغير. جوابه : أنه إذا فرض المسواد وجب سواديته بسبب المرض وجوبا لاحقا مترتبا على الغرض ومع الوجوب يمتنع تأثير المؤثر فيه ، فإنه يكون إيجاداً لما فرض موجوداً أما قبل فرضه سواداً فيمكن أن يوجد المؤثر السواد على سبيل الوجوب ويكون ذلك الوجوب سابقاً وهذه مغالطة من جهة اللفظ المشترك ، لأن الوجوب يدل على المعنيين بالشركة اللفظية ، وأيضا إذا قلنا فني السواد معناه أن السواد الحاصل فى زمان ليس بحاصل فى زمان بعده ويكون حمل غير الحاصل على المتصور منه لا على الموجود الخارجي، فإن الوضع والحمل يكونان في العقول ولا يكونان في الخارج أصلا وهكذا القول في حصول الوجود من موجده ، وإن قيل تأثير المؤثر في جعل الماهية بالوجود كما هو رأى القائلين بأن المعدوم شيء لم يتعلق ذلك بموصوفية الماهية بالوجود لأن ذلك أمر إضافي يحصل بعد اتصافها به، والمراد من تأثير المؤثر هو ضم الماهية إلى الوجود ، ولا يلزم ما ذكره من المحال، وظهر من قوله في الجواب عن هذه المعارضة خبطه وتحيره وقدحه لسبب ذلك تارة فى النظريات وتارة في البديهيات
As for the third objection: his statement that the influence of the cause (taʾthīr al-muʾaththir) is either in the essence (al-māhiyya), or in existence (al-wujūd), or in the qualification (ittiṣāf) of the essence with existence. The answer is regarding its being in the essence: his statement that is impossible, because if blackness is blackness by another, then blackness would not be blackness in the absence of the other. The answer: if blackness is assumed (farḍ) to be blackness due to a cause with a consequent necessity (wujūb lāḥiq) dependent on the purpose, then with necessity it is impossible for the cause to influence it, for that would be a bringing-into-existence (ījād) of what is assumed existent. But before assuming it blackness, it is possible for the cause to bring blackness into existence in a necessary manner, and that necessity would be prior. This is a sophistry (mugālata) due to homonymy (al-lafẓ al-mushtarak), because necessity (al-wujūb) signifies two meanings by homonymy. Also, if we say: the annihilation (fanāʾ) of blackness means that the blackness occurring in a time is not occurring in a time after it, and the attribution (ḥaml) of the non-occurring applies to the mental concept (al-mutaṣawwar) of it, not to the external existent. For positing (waḍʿ) and attribution (ḥaml) occur in minds, not in the external at all. And likewise the statement concerning the obtaining of existence from its originator. And if it is said that the influence of the cause is making the essence possessed of existence (jaʿl al-māhiyya bil-wujūd), as is the view of those who say that non-existence is a thing (al-maʿdūm shayʾ), that does not pertain to the qualification of the essence by existence, because that is a relative matter (amr iḍāfī) that occurs after its being qualified by it. And the intended meaning of the influence of the cause is the conjunction (ḍamm) of the essence with existence, and the impossibility he mentioned does not follow. And it became clear from his statement in answer to this objection that he is confused and perplexed, sometimes impugning theoretical matters (naẓariyyāt) and sometimes self-evident truths (badīhiyyāt).
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the locus of causal influence: does it affect the essence (māhiyya), existence (wujūd), or the qualification of essence by existence? The objector argues that influence in the essence is impossible because if something is black by another, then if that other is absent, it would no longer be black—but that leads to absurdity. Razi responds by distinguishing two senses of necessity (wujūb): one is logical necessity of the essence (e.g., blackness is black by itself), the other is a consequent necessity (wujūb lāḥiq) due to an external cause. The objector confuses these through homonymy (lafẓ mushtarak). Then Razi discusses annihilation (fanāʾ): saying 'blackness perishes' means it is not present in a later time, and that attribution is mental, not external. He then addresses the view of those who say non-existence is a thing (maʿdūm shayʾ): influence is conjoining essence with existence, and that is a relative matter after the conjunction, so no absurdity arises. Finally, Razi accuses the objector of inconsistency—sometimes attacking theoretical matters, sometimes self-evident truths—showing his confusion.

Key Terms
الوجوب (al-wujūb)
اللفظ المشترك (al-lafẓ al-mushtarak)
الماهية (al-māhiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an attribute is derived from a cause, the thing cannot have that attribute independently.
Correction: Razi shows that necessity can be consequent upon a cause without making the attribute part of the essence. The thing can still be what it is once the cause acts.

Checkpoint
What does Imam Razi identify as the cause of the confusion in the third objection?
A) A misunderstanding of the nature of existence.
B) Homonymy (lafẓ mushtarak) of the word 'necessity' (wujūb).
C) The assumption that non-existence is a thing.
D) The denial of causal influence.

Key Takeaway
Razi exposes the objector's fallacy by distinguishing two meanings of necessity, showing that influence in the essence does not lead to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's actions, do you ever unconsciously impose the same kind of necessity that you see in created causes? How does Razi's distinction challenge you to reexamine your assumptions today? What will you do to guard against this confusion?
?
Have you ever faced a spiritual dilemma where you thought if something is caused by God, it must lack reality or independence? How can this lesson free you from such false dilemmas, and what concrete step will you take to apply this understanding in your relationship with God?

Mental Attribution and the Conjunction of Essence with Existence
Razi explains the mental nature of attribution and the meaning of influence as conjunction, then accuses the objector of inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends the view that causal influence involves conjoining essence with existence, and that attributions like annihilation are mental, not external.

Core Idea
Attribution happens in the mind, not in the world; influence is the conjunction of essence with existence.

After clarifying the necessity confusion, Razi addresses two more issues: the annihilation of blackness and the view that influence makes an essence exist. He argues that statements like 'blackness perishes' are mental attributions, and that the cause's influence is simply the conjunction of essence and existence — a relative matter that evades the earlier objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the annihilation argument
Razi explains that saying 'blackness perishes' means blackness that exists at one time does not exist at a later time; this attribution is mental, not external.
2
Understand mental vs external attribution
Razi asserts that positing (waḍʿ) and attribution (ḥaml) occur in minds only, not in external reality. This applies to statements about annihilation and also to the obtaining of existence from its originator.
3
Grasp the view of those who say non-existence is a thing
Some philosophers hold that influence is making the essence possessed of existence. Razi explains that this does not involve qualifying the essence with existence before influence; rather, influence is simply the conjunction (ḍamm) of essence with existence, a relative matter after the fact.
4
Note Razi's final accusation
Razi concludes that the objector's arguments show confusion — sometimes attacking theoretical matters, sometimes self-evident truths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi says that all attributions like 'this perishes' are mental, not in external reality. When you label someone as 'a failure' or 'a success', do you realize that this attribution is a mental construct? How might this awareness change the weight you give to such labels?
?
If causal influence is nothing but the conjunction of essence with existence by Allah, then no secondary cause truly 'does' anything on its own. In your daily life, when you rely on a tool, a person, or your own skill, do you honestly attribute the outcome to that secondary cause or to the One who conjoins? What would it take to shift your reliance?
?
Razi defends the view that non-existence can be a thing (al-maʿdūm shayʾ) in the mind. When you fear losing something, you are imagining its non-existence. Is that mental non-existence as real to you as the thing's presence? How does that fear affect your trust in Allah's provision?

Razi on Non-Existence Requiring a Cause
Imam Razi rebuts the objection that non-existence cannot require a cause, distinguishing mental from external non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the objection that non-existence cannot require a cause by distinguishing between mental and external non-existence, and why the non-existence of a possible can have a mental cause.

Core Idea
Non-existence is not always pure negation; mental non-existence can require a mental cause.

In this passage, Imam Razi addresses a sophisticated objection: if non-existence is pure nothing, how could it need a cause? The objector thinks this is impossible. Razi's response clarifies that the non-existence of a contingent possible is not pure negation because it is considered in the mind, and thus its cause can be mental. This lesson examines his nuanced argument and its implications for causality in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objector's claim that non-existence (ʿadam) is pure negation (nafy maḥḍ) and thus cannot require a preponderator (murajjiḥ).
Pay attention to the distinction Razi introduces.
2
Understand the key distinction
Razi argues that the non-existence of a possible with equal sides (al-mumkin al-mutasāwī al-ṭarafayn) is not pure negation because its two sides are equal only in the mind. This makes the non-existence a mental concept.
3
Follow the logic of mental preponderance
See how the non-existence of the cause (ʿadam al-ʿilla) is also not pure negation and can serve as a mental cause for the non-existence of the effect. This shows that mental causality is plausible.
4
Examine the criticism of the additional argument
Razi notes that the objector's further argument about causality contradicting causality contains an error, and that the objector's own answer actually reinforces the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعارضة الرابعة فقوله : افتقار العدم إلى مرجيح محال لأن العدم نفى محض ليس بشيء لأن عدم الممكن المتساوى الطرفين ليس نفيا محضا وتساوى طرفى وجوده وعدمه لا يكون إلا في العقل المرجح لا يكون إلا عقليا وعدم العلة ليس بنفى محض وهو يسكنى فى الترجيح العقلى ، ولكونه ممتازاً عن عدم المعلول في العقل يجوز أن يعلل هذا العدم بذلك العدم في العقل وقوله : العلمية مناقضة للأعلية إلى آخره فقد من وجه الغلط فيه وجوابه عن هذه المعارضة ليس بجواب عنها إنما هو تأكيد للمعارضة
As for the fourth objection: his statement that the need of non-existence (al-ʿadam) for a preponderator (murajjiḥ) is impossible because non-existence is pure negation (nafy maḥḍ) which is not a thing — the answer: the non-existence of the possible (al-mumkin) that is equal in both directions (al-mutasāwī al-ṭarafayn) is not pure negation, and the equality of the two sides of its existence and non-existence only occurs in the mind; the preponderator (al-murajjiḥ) can only be mental. And the non-existence of the cause (ʿadam al-ʿilla) is not pure negation, and it is used in mental preponderance. And because it is distinguished from the non-existence of the effect in the mind, it is permissible that this non-existence be caused by that non-existence in the mind. And his statement: 'Causality (al-ʿilliyya) contradicts causality...' etc. — the source of error in it has been identified. And his answer to this objection is not an answer to it; rather it is a reinforcement of the objection.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning whether non-existence (ʿadam) can require a cause. The objector argues that non-existence is pure negation (nafy maḥḍ), not a thing, so needing a preponderator is impossible. Imam Razi responds by distinguishing: the non-existence of a possible that is equally balanced between existence and non-existence is not pure negation, because its two sides are equal only in the mind; hence the preponderator is also mental. Moreover, the non-existence of a cause (e.g., the absence of a necessary condition) is also not pure negation; it can be used in mental preponderance and can serve as a mental cause for the non-existence of the effect. Razi then criticizes the objector's additional argument about causality contradicting causality, stating that the error has been pointed out, and the objector's own answer actually reinforces the objection rather than refuting it. This shows the author's style of exposing weaknesses in the opponent's reasoning.

Key Terms
العدم (al-ʿadam)
نفي محض (nafy maḥḍ)
مرجح (murajjiḥ)
الممكن المتساوى الطرفين (al-mumkin al-mutasāwī al-ṭarafayn)
الترجيح العقلي (al-tarjīḥ al-ʿaqlī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence is absolute nothing and cannot have a cause.
Correction: Non-existence of a contingent is a mental concept that can have a mental cause.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi say that the non-existence of a possible with equal sides is not pure negation?
A) Because it actually exists externally.
B) Because its equality of sides is a mental consideration, making the non-existence a mental entity.
C) Because non-existence can only be caused externally.
D) Because pure negation does not require a cause.

Key Takeaway
Non-existence can require a cause if it is a mental non-existence, as in the case of a contingent with equal sides.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever dismiss your own inner doubts or mental struggles as 'not real' because they are not physical? How does Razi's recognition of mental non-existence as a genuine reality challenge that dismissal?
?
When you think about your own existence, do you see yourself as equally balanced between being and non-being? What does it mean for you that a mental cause—perhaps God's will—tipped the scale?
?
Razi argues that the non-existence of a cause can cause non-existence in the mind. How might your own failures or sins (mental non-existence of virtues) have been caused by the absence of a good intention? What does that imply about your responsibility to cultivate mental causes?

Al-Razi's Refutation of the Second Objection on Influence
Al-Razi refutes the second objection by clarifying the meaning of 'state' in the division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's refutation of the objection that influence must occur in a state of existence or non-existence, and how he distinguishes different meanings of 'state'.

Core Idea
Influence is not confined to existence or non-existence; it can occur at the moment of origination.

The objection attempted to disprove the need for an influencer by dividing influence into two impossible states. Al-Razi shows the division is flawed because it equivocates on 'state' and ignores the moment of origination. This lesson examines his reasoning and the alternative view of cause-effect simultaneity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the passage and identify the objector's division: influence must occur either when the effect exists or when it does not exist, both claimed impossible.
The objector thinks this undermines the proof for an influencer.
2
Analyze Al-Razi's critique
Notice how Al-Razi distinguishes two meanings of 'state of existence': temporal existence vs. essential otherness. He shows influence is possible in the first sense.
The second sense is impossible, but influence does not require it.
3
Explore the alternative view
Read the part about late theologians who hold temporal simultaneity: cause precedes effect in time, so influence is prior to the effect's existence.
This provides another way the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعارضة الثانية قسمة التأثير بأنه يحصل إما في حال وجود الأثر أو في حال عدمه وهما باطلان فليس كذلك، لأنه إن أراد بحال وجود الأثر زمان وجوده فليس مستحيل أن يؤثر المؤثر في الأثر في زمان وجود الأثر لأن العلة مع معلولها تكون بهذه الصفة، وإن أراد به مغايرة المؤثر للأثر الذاتية فذلك مستحيل، وإنما يؤثر فيه لا من حيث هو موجود ولا من حيث هو معدوم، وبعض المتكلمين يقولون المؤثر يؤثر حال حدوث الأثر فإنها ليست بحال الوجود ولا بحال العدم. وقوله في الجواب: إن هذه القسمة مبطلة للضروريات باطل ودال على تحيره في أمثال هذه المواضع، وقد يمكن أن يقال ما يقول المتأخرون من المتكلمين الذين يقولون بمقارنة العلة والمعلول في الزمان، فإنهم يقولون الذي يوجد في الآن الثاني يصدر من موجده في الآن الذي قبله فيكون التأثير سابقاً على الأثر، ويقع بالقياس إلى ما يحصل بعده سواء كان الأمر موجوداً في ذلك الآن بتأثير آخر أو معدوماً، ويكون الأثر في آن التأثير غير موجود وفي الآن الذي يصير موجوداً لا يكون مقارناً للعدم.
As for the second objection: the division of influence into either occurring in the state of the effect's existence or in the state of its non-existence, both being false—it is not so. For if by 'state of the effect's existence' he means the time of its existence, then it is not impossible for the influencer to influence the effect in the time of the effect's existence, because the cause with its effect has this attribute. And if by it he means the essential otherness of the influencer from the effect, that is impossible. Rather, it influences it not insofar as it exists nor insofar as it does not exist. Some theologians say that the influencer influences at the moment of the effect's occurrence, for that is not a state of existence nor a state of non-existence. His saying in the answer that this division invalidates self-evident truths is false and indicates his confusion in such matters. It is possible to say what late theologians say who hold the simultaneity of cause and effect in time, for they say that what exists in the second moment proceeds from its creator in the moment before it, so the influence is prior to the effect, and is measured relative to what occurs after it, whether the thing exists in that moment by another influence or is non-existent, and the effect at the moment of influence is non-existent, and at the moment it becomes existent it is not concomitant with non-existence.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the second objection against the proof of the need for an influencer. The objector divides influence into either occurring when the effect exists or when it does not, and claims both are impossible. Al-Razi explains that if 'state of existence' means the time at which the effect exists, it is not impossible for the cause to influence it at that same time, as cause and effect are simultaneous. If 'state of existence' means essential otherness, that is impossible, but influence is not conditioned by existence or non-existence in that sense. Some theologians say the influence occurs at the moment of the effect's origination, which is neither existence nor non-existence. The objector's claim that this division invalidates self-evident truths is false, showing his confusion. Late theologians who affirm temporal simultaneity of cause and effect argue that what exists at T2 proceeds from its creator at T1, so influence precedes the effect, and the effect is non-existent at the moment of influence but becomes existent without being accompanied by non-existence. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
تأثير
مؤثر
أثر
علة
معلول
حدوث
وجود
عدم
مقارنة
زمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that influence must be temporally prior to the effect's existence, and cannot occur when the effect exists.
Correction: Al-Razi clarifies that influence can be simultaneous with the effect's existence (temporal state) or occur at the moment of origination, which is neither existence nor non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Razi, what is the mistake in the second objection's division of influence?
A) It assumes influence must be prior to the effect.
B) It claims influence cannot occur when the effect exists.
C) It divides influence into existence and non-existence states but does not consider the moment of origination.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi refutes the objection by showing that influence can occur at the moment of origination, which is neither existence nor non-existence, and can also be simultaneous with the effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi criticizes the objector for claiming his division invalidates self-evident truths, calling it 'a sign of confusion'. In your own faith, are there areas where you have accepted a false dilemma (e.g., either God controls everything or humans have free will) without examining the middle ground? Will you commit to studying the nuanced positions of scholars like Al-Razi before making judgments?
?
If influence can occur at the moment of the effect's origination—not before or after—what does that mean for your daily reliance on causes and effects? Do you truly see God as the direct creator of each moment, or do you functionally assume that events happen on their own? How does this lesson challenge you to live with greater awareness of God's ongoing creation?

Refuting the Objection: Influence on Essence or Existence
Al-Razi refutes the objection that influence must be on essence, existence, or their connection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi refutes the claim that an influencer's effect must be on the essence, existence, or their connection, and why the confusion arises from the ambiguous term 'wujub'.

Core Idea
The term 'wujub' (necessity) is ambiguous, causing the objection to fail.

Al-Razi tackles a sophisticated objection that attempts to limit causal influence to essence, existence, or the connection between them. He shows that the objection relies on a misunderstanding of necessity and confuses mental constructs with external reality. In this passage, you'll see how he systematically dismantles the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow. Notice how Al-Razi responds to each sub-point of the objection.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main objection and initial response
The objector claims influence must be on essence, existence, or their connection. Al-Razi first addresses influence on essence using the blackness example.
Pay attention to the distinction between prior and subsequent necessity.
3
Understand the fallacy of the ambiguous word 'wujub'
Al-Razi points out that 'wujub' (necessity) has two meanings: logical necessity and a state of being necessary. The objection conflates them.
This is a classic case of equivocation.
4
Examine the alternative view: non-existent as a thing
If one holds that the non-existent is a thing, then influence is the conjunction of essence with existence, not qualification of essence by existence.
This aligns with the Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في المعارضة الثالثة: فقوله تأثير المؤثر إما في الماهية أو في الوجود أو في اتصاف الماهية بالوجود يجاب عنه بأنه في الماهية قوله ذلك محال، لأن كون السواد سواداً بالغير يوجب أن لا يكون السواد سواداً عند عدم الغير. جوابه: أنه إذا فرض المسود وجب سواديته بسبب المرض وجوباً لاحقاً مترتباً على الغرض، ومع الوجوب يمتنع تأثير المؤثر فيه، فإنه يكون إيجاداً لما فرض موجوداً، أما قبل فرضه سواداً فيمكن أن يوجد المؤثر السواد على سبيل الوجوب ويكون ذلك الوجوب سابقاً. وهذه مغالطة من جهة اللفظ المشترك، لأن الوجوب يدل على المعنيين بالشركة اللفظية، وأيضاً إذا قلنا نفي السواد معناه أن السواد الحاصل في زمان ليس بحاصل في زمان بعده، ويكون حمل غير الحاصل على المتصور منه لا على الموجود الخارجي، فإن الوضع والحمل يكونان في العقول ولا يكونان في الخارج أصلاً، وهكذا القول في حصول الوجود من موجده. وإن قيل تأثير المؤثر في جعل الماهية بالوجود كما هو رأي القائلين بأن المعدوم شيء، لم يتعلق ذلك بموصوفية الماهية بالوجود لأن ذلك أمر إضافي يحصل بعد اتصافها به، والمراد من تأثير المؤثر هو ضم الماهية إلى الوجود، ولا يلزم ما ذكره من المحال. وظهر من قوله في الجواب عن هذه المعارضة خبطه وتحيره وقدحه لسبب ذلك تارة في النظريات وتارة في البديهيات.
As for the third objection: his statement that the influence of the influencer is either on the essence, or on existence, or on the essence's being qualified by existence—the answer is that [if he says it is] on the essence, his statement that it is impossible, because blackness being blackness by another necessitates that blackness not be blackness when the other is absent—its answer is that when the blackened thing is assumed, its blackness becomes necessary due to the disease with a subsequent necessity depending on the purpose, and with necessity the influence of the influencer on it is impossible, for it would be a bringing into existence of what is assumed to exist. As for before assuming it blackness, it is possible for the influencer to bring blackness into existence in the mode of necessity, and that necessity is prior. This is a fallacy due to the ambiguous word, because 'wujub' indicates two meanings by homonymy. Also, if we say 'negation of blackness' means that the blackness existing at one time does not exist at a later time, and the attribution of the non-existent is based on the conceived [thing] not on the external existent, for positing and attributing occur in the intellects and not in the external at all. Similarly for the coming into existence of existence from its creator. And if it is said that the influence of the influencer is to make the essence with existence, as is the view of those who hold that the non-existent is a thing, that does not pertain to the essence's being qualified by existence, because that is a relative matter that occurs after its qualification by it, and the meaning of the influencer's influence is the conjunction of the essence with existence, and the impossibility he mentioned does not follow. And it appears from his statement in answering this objection his confusion and perplexity, and his undermining sometimes of theoretical matters and sometimes of self-evident truths.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage refutes the third objection concerning the locus of influence: whether it is on the essence, existence, or the connection between them. The objector argues that influence on the essence is impossible because it would make the essence dependent on another—using blackness as an example. Al-Razi responds that if we assume a thing becomes black due to a cause, the blackness is necessary with a subsequent necessity, but prior to that assumption, the influencer can bring about the blackness with a prior necessity. The confusion arises because the term 'wujub' (necessity) is ambiguous, meaning both logical necessity and a state of being necessary. Furthermore, the idea of negating blackness is a mental operation, not about external existence. If one holds that the non-existent is a thing (as some theologians do), then influence is the conjunction of essence with existence, not a qualification of the essence by existence. Thus the objection fails due to equivocation and confusion between mental and external realms.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'wujub' (necessity) unambiguously refers to logical necessity, and that influence on essence is always impossible.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that 'wujub' has two meanings (prior and subsequent necessity), and that influence can occur before the assumption of essence. The objection fails due to equivocation.

Checkpoint
What is the main source of confusion in the third objection according to Al-Razi?
A) The assumption that influence must be on essence.
B) The ambiguity of the term 'wujub' (necessity) which has multiple meanings.
C) The idea that blackness cannot be brought about by another.
D) The denial that the non-existent is a thing.

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because it equivocates on the term 'wujub' and confuses mental constructs with external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Could the confusion you sometimes feel be rooted in ambiguous language rather than a real contradiction? This week, try to identify one such term in your own reasoning and clarify it.
?
Al-Razi points out that confusion arises from treating mental constructs as if they exist in external reality. Where in your understanding of faith and causality might you be confusing what is true in your mind with what is true in the world? Pause now and ask Allah to grant you clarity between the two.
?
The objector's argument seemed airtight until the ambiguity was exposed. Have you ever clung to a strong opinion about a matter of religion only to discover later that you were misunderstanding a term? Are you willing today to re-examine any deeply held belief with the humility of someone who might be confusing two meanings of a single word?

Refuting the Objection: Non-Existence Needs a Cause
Al-Razi refutes the fourth objection regarding non-existence needing a preponderator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that the non-existence of a possible thing is not pure negation and can be subject to intellectual preponderance.

Core Idea
Non-existence can have a cause in the mind, even if not in reality.

Al-Razi addresses a key objection: how can non-existence require a cause if it's just nothing? He responds by distinguishing types of non-existence and showing that intellectual non-existence can serve as a cause. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and clarifies the distinction between pure negation and the qualified non-existence of a possible thing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument and the technical terms.
Notice that the passage starts with the objection and then gives Al-Razi's response.
2
Identify the objection and Al-Razi's key moves
Identify what the fourth objection claims and how Al-Razi counters it. Focus on the phrase 'not pure negation' and the role of the intellect.
The objection says non-existence is pure negation, so it cannot need a preponderator.
3
Understand the distinction between pure negation and intellectual non-existence
Al-Razi argues that the non-existence of a possible being is not pure negation because it is considered in the intellect with equal possibility. This allows an intellectual cause.
This distinction is the heart of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعارضة الرابعة فقوله: افتقار العدم إلى مرجح محال لأن العدم نفي محض ليس بشيء. لأن عدم الممكن المتساوي الطرفين ليس نفياً محضاً، وتساوي طرفي وجوده وعدمه لا يكون إلا في العقل، والمرجح لا يكون إلا عقلياً، وعدم العلة ليس بنفي محض، وهو يستند في الترجيح العقلي، ولكونه ممتازاً عن عدم المعلول في العقل يجوز أن يعلل هذا العدم بذلك العدم في العقل. وقوله: العلمية مناقضة للأعلية إلى آخره فقد من وجه الغلط فيه، وجوابه عن هذه المعارضة ليس بجواب عنها، إنما هو تأكيد للمعارضة.
As for the fourth objection: his statement that the need of non-existence for a preponderator is impossible because non-existence is pure negation and not a thing—[the answer is that] the non-existence of a possible thing with equal two sides is not pure negation, and the equality of the two sides of its existence and non-existence only occurs in the intellect, and the preponderator is only intellectual, and the non-existence of the cause is not pure negation, and it is based on intellectual preponderance. And because it is distinguished from the non-existence of the effect in the intellect, it is permissible for this non-existence to be caused by that non-existence in the intellect. And his statement: 'causality contradicts causality' etc.—the error in it is evident, and his answer to this objection is not an answer to it; rather it is a reinforcement of the objection.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage addresses the fourth objection: the claim that non-existence cannot need a preponderator because it is pure negation. Al-Razi responds that the non-existence of a possible thing (which is inherently balanced between existence and non-existence) is not pure negation; its equal possibility is an intellectual consideration. The preponderator for non-existence is also intellectual. The non-existence of the cause is not pure negation; it can serve as an intellectual cause for the non-existence of the effect because they are distinguished in the intellect. The objector's statement about causality contradicting causality is confused, and his supposed answer is actually a reinforcement of the objection. Thus the objection fails to disprove the need for a preponderator for non-existence.
