Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That non-existence, being nothing, cannot have any cause or preponderator.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that non-existence of a possible thing is not absolute nothingness; it has an intellectual quality (equal possibility) that allows it to be caused intellectually.

Checkpoint
Why does Al-Razi say the non-existence of a possible thing is not pure negation?
A) Because it is a mental consideration of equal possibility.
B) Because it is actual non-existence in the external world.
C) Because it is caused by a physical agent.
D) Because it is the same as the non-existence of the cause.

Key Takeaway
Non-existence can be intellectually caused when considered as the non-existence of a possible thing with equal possibility in the mind.

?

Why Possibility, Not Origination, Is the Cause of Need
Razi presents a proof that origination leads to circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi proves that the need for an efficient cause is due to possibility (imkan) and not temporal origination (huduth).

Core Idea
Origination cannot cause need because it would require a thing to be posterior to itself.

Razi tackles a foundational issue: what makes a thing need a creator? The mainstream earlier view held that it is origination in time. Razi argues that origination is itself a mode of existence and thus already depends on the efficient cause, leading to circularity. He instead identifies possibility as the true cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the thesis and the argument
Read the Arabic passage stating that the cause is possibility, not origination. Then read the translation.
Notice the concise style: Sanusi states his position and immediately gives a proof.
2
Trace the sequence of posteriorities
Razi lists four posteriorities: origination posterior to existence, existence posterior to the effect, effect posterior to need, need posterior to the cause. Identify each step.
This shows that if origination were the cause, a thing would be posterior to itself by several degrees.
3
Understand the logical consequence
If origination were the cause, it would entail infinite regress or circularity, making the argument absurd. Therefore, origination cannot be the cause.
This refutes the earlier mutakallimun view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : علة الحاجة إلى المؤثر الإمكان لا الحدوث
لأن الحدوث كيفية فى وجود الحادث فيكون متأخرا عنه والوجود متأخر عن تأثير القادر فيه المتأخر من احتياج الممكن إليه المتأخر من علة احتياجه إليه، فلو كانت العلة هي الحدوث لزم تأخير الشيء عن نفسه مراتب.
احتجوا بأن علة الحاجة لو كانت هي الإمكان لزم احتياج العدم الممكن إلى المؤثر وهو محال، لأن المؤثر يستدعى حصول الأثر والعدم نفى محض فلا يكون أثرا.
والجواب : ما قيل إن علة العدم عدم العلة وفيه ما فيه.
(1) أقول: الصفة متأخرة بالطبع عن موصوفها، والوجود الموصوف به متأخر عن تأثير موجده بالذات تأخير المعلول عن العلة، وتأثير الموجد متأخر عن احتياج الأثر إليه في الوجود تأخيرا بالطبع واحتياج الأثر متأخر عن علته بالذات وجميعها أربع تأخرات: اثنان بالطبع واثنان بالذات وذلك يقتضى امتناع كون الحدوث علة للاحتياج. وقد قالوا في معارضة الإمكان صفة للممكن فهو متأخر عنه والممكن متأخر عن تأثير المؤثر فيه والتأثير متأخر عن الاحتياج المتأخر عن علتيه وهو فاسد، ولأن الممكن الموصوف بالإمكان ليس متأخرا عن تأثير المؤثر إنما المتأخر عنه وجوده أو عدمه المتأخرين عن ذاته اللذين بسببهما احتاج إلى مؤثر ثم إلى علة الاحتياج والقائلون بكون الإمكان علة الحاجة هم الفلاسفة والمتأخرون من المتكلمين، والقائلون بكون الحدوث علة لهما هم الأقدمون منهم، وقولهم لو كان الإمكان علة الحاجة لزم احتياج العدم إلى المؤثر وهو محال، ليس بشيء لأن عدم المعلول ليس نفيا صرفا ولا مانع من أن يكون معلولا لعدم العلة كما مر القول فيه، وقد تبين أن ذلك مشتمل على فساد.
Issue: The cause of need for the efficient cause is possibility, not origination. Because origination is a mode in the existence of the originated thing, so it is posterior to it, and existence is posterior to the effect of the Powerful on it, which is posterior to the need of the possible for Him, which is posterior to the cause of its need for Him. So if the cause were origination, a thing's being posterior to itself by several degrees would be necessary. They objected: If the cause of need were possibility, then the need of possible non-existence for the efficient cause would be necessary, which is absurd, because the efficient cause requires the obtaining of the effect, and non-existence is pure negation, so it cannot be an effect. The answer: What has been said that the cause of non-existence is the non-existence of the cause—but there is something in that. (1) I say: The attribute is naturally posterior to its subject, and the existence qualified by it is posterior in essence to the effect of its originator, as the effect is posterior to the cause, and the effect of the originator is naturally posterior to the need of the effect for it in existence, and the need of the effect is posterior in essence to its cause. All of these are four posteriorities: two by nature and two by essence, and that entails the impossibility of origination being the cause of need. And they have said in objection: possibility is an attribute of the possible, so it is posterior to it, and the possible is posterior to the effect of the efficient cause in it, and the effect is posterior to the need, which is posterior to its two causes – and this is invalid. And because the possible qualified by possibility is not posterior to the effect of the efficient cause; rather, what is posterior to it is its existence or its non-existence, which are posterior to its essence, and because of them it needed an efficient cause and then a cause of need. Those who say that possibility is the cause of need are the philosophers and the later mutakallimun, and those who say that origination is the cause for both are the earlier ones. And their statement that if possibility were the cause of need, then the need of non-existence for the efficient cause would follow, which is absurd – this is nothing because the non-existence of the effect is not pure negation, and there is no impediment to its being an effect of the non-existence of the cause, as has been discussed. And it has become clear that this entails corruption.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the cause of a thing's need for an efficient cause (Creator) is its possibility, not its temporal origination (huduth). Razi first presents a sequence of posteriorities: if origination were the cause, there would be circularity because origination is a mode of existence, and existence depends on the efficient cause, which depends on need, which depends on the cause of need. Next, he presents an objection: if possibility is the cause, then possible non-existents would need a cause, which is absurd because non-existence cannot receive a cause's effect. The answer is that the cause of non-existence is the non-existence of the cause, though this answer is not fully satisfactory ('fihi ma fihi'). The long footnote elaborates: there are four posteriorities (two natural, two essential), and origination cannot be the cause because it is posterior to existence. The objection that possibility is posterior to the possible and thus cannot be a cause is refuted: what is posterior is existence or non-existence, not possibility itself. The footnote also identifies the schools: philosophers and later mutakallimun hold possibility as cause; earlier mutakallimun hold origination. The claim that non-existence cannot be an effect is rejected because non-existence can be the effect of the non-existence of the cause.

Key Terms
علة الحاجة
المؤثر
الإمكان
الحدوث
تأخير الشيء عن نفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Razi reject origination (huduth) as the cause of need for an efficient cause?
A) Because origination is an attribute of the possible, not the efficient cause.
B) Because it would lead to a thing being posterior to itself by several degrees.
C) Because origination is not a real attribute.
D) Because the efficient cause does not cause origination.

Key Takeaway
The cause of need for a creator is the possibility of the thing, not its origination, because origination would entail circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If possibility alone is why you need God, then your very existence at this moment declares your absolute dependence on Him. Have you truly lived today as though you could cease to exist at any instant without His sustaining power?
?
Razi argues that origination cannot be the cause of need—it is too derivative. Consider: are you grounding your need for God in something superficial like your birth, your sin, or your good deeds? Or do you recognize that your mere possibility (the fact that you are not necessary) is the deepest, most constant reason you belong to Him? What would change if you felt this foundational need every second?
?
The absurdity Razi exposes is that if origination caused need, a thing would be posterior to itself. Your life, if you trace its cause back, leads to God. But do you ever act as if you are the origin of your own actions—thinking your choices create yourself without need of Him? In what area of your life today are you ignoring your built-in possibility and pretending that you are self-sufficient?

Objection and Answer: Non-Existence and the Cause of Need
Razi presents and answers an objection against his view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that possibility leads to non-existence needing a cause, and Razi's response that non-existence can be an effect of the non-existence of its cause.

Core Idea
Non-existence can be an effect of the non-existence of its cause.

Razi's view that possibility is the cause of need faces a strong objection: if everything possible needs a cause, then possible non-existents would also need a cause, but non-existence cannot receive causation. Razi's answer points to the adage 'the cause of non-existence is the non-existence of the cause,' though he admits it is not entirely satisfying. The footnote he adds later refines the discussion, detailing four posteriorities and identifying the schools.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection that if possibility were the cause, possible non-existents would need an efficient cause, which is absurd.
Non-existence is pure negation and cannot be an effect.
2
Read the initial answer
The answer: 'the cause of non-existence is the non-existence of the cause.' But Razi adds 'fihi ma fihi' (there is something in it), indicating it is not fully satisfactory.
This answer is a classical move but Razi is critical.
3
Explore the footnote's elaboration
The footnote discusses four posteriorities (two natural, two essential) and refines the argument. It also identifies the schools: philosophers and later mutakallimun say possibility is cause; earlier ones say origination.
This footnote shows Razi's deeper analysis.
4
Understand why the objection fails
The footnote argues that non-existence of the effect is not pure negation; it can be an effect of the non-existence of the cause. Thus the objection collapses.
This resolves the issue for Razi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : علة الحاجة إلى المؤثر الإمكان لا الحدوث
لأن الحدوث كيفية فى وجود الحادث فيكون متأخرا عنه والوجود متأخر عن تأثير القادر فيه المتأخر من احتياج الممكن إليه المتأخر من علة احتياجه إليه، فلو كانت العلة هي الحدوث لزم تأخير الشيء عن نفسه مراتب.
احتجوا بأن علة الحاجة لو كانت هي الإمكان لزم احتياج العدم الممكن إلى المؤثر وهو محال، لأن المؤثر يستدعى حصول الأثر والعدم نفى محض فلا يكون أثرا.
والجواب : ما قيل إن علة العدم عدم العلة وفيه ما فيه.
(1) أقول: الصفة متأخرة بالطبع عن موصوفها، والوجود الموصوف به متأخر عن تأثير موجده بالذات تأخير المعلول عن العلة، وتأثير الموجد متأخر عن احتياج الأثر إليه في الوجود تأخيرا بالطبع واحتياج الأثر متأخر عن علته بالذات وجميعها أربع تأخرات: اثنان بالطبع واثنان بالذات وذلك يقتضى امتناع كون الحدوث علة للاحتياج. وقد قالوا في معارضة الإمكان صفة للممكن فهو متأخر عنه والممكن متأخر عن تأثير المؤثر فيه والتأثير متأخر عن الاحتياج المتأخر عن علتيه وهو فاسد، ولأن الممكن الموصوف بالإمكان ليس متأخرا عن تأثير المؤثر إنما المتأخر عنه وجوده أو عدمه المتأخرين عن ذاته اللذين بسببهما احتاج إلى مؤثر ثم إلى علة الاحتياج والقائلون بكون الإمكان علة الحاجة هم الفلاسفة والمتأخرون من المتكلمين، والقائلون بكون الحدوث علة لهما هم الأقدمون منهم، وقولهم لو كان الإمكان علة الحاجة لزم احتياج العدم إلى المؤثر وهو محال، ليس بشيء لأن عدم المعلول ليس نفيا صرفا ولا مانع من أن يكون معلولا لعدم العلة كما مر القول فيه، وقد تبين أن ذلك مشتمل على فساد.
Issue: The cause of need for the efficient cause is possibility, not origination. Because origination is a mode in the existence of the originated thing, so it is posterior to it, and existence is posterior to the effect of the Powerful on it, which is posterior to the need of the possible for Him, which is posterior to the cause of its need for Him. So if the cause were origination, a thing's being posterior to itself by several degrees would be necessary. They objected: If the cause of need were possibility, then the need of possible non-existence for the efficient cause would be necessary, which is absurd, because the efficient cause requires the obtaining of the effect, and non-existence is pure negation, so it cannot be an effect. The answer: What has been said that the cause of non-existence is the non-existence of the cause—but there is something in that. (1) I say: The attribute is naturally posterior to its subject, and the existence qualified by it is posterior in essence to the effect of its originator, as the effect is posterior to the cause, and the effect of the originator is naturally posterior to the need of the effect for it in existence, and the need of the effect is posterior in essence to its cause. All of these are four posteriorities: two by nature and two by essence, and that entails the impossibility of origination being the cause of need. And they have said in objection: possibility is an attribute of the possible, so it is posterior to it, and the possible is posterior to the effect of the efficient cause in it, and the effect is posterior to the need, which is posterior to its two causes – and this is invalid. And because the possible qualified by possibility is not posterior to the effect of the efficient cause; rather, what is posterior to it is its existence or its non-existence, which are posterior to its essence, and because of them it needed an efficient cause and then a cause of need. Those who say that possibility is the cause of need are the philosophers and the later mutakallimun, and those who say that origination is the cause for both are the earlier ones. And their statement that if possibility were the cause of need, then the need of non-existence for the efficient cause would follow, which is absurd – this is nothing because the non-existence of the effect is not pure negation, and there is no impediment to its being an effect of the non-existence of the cause, as has been discussed. And it has become clear that this entails corruption.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the cause of a thing's need for an efficient cause (Creator) is its possibility, not its temporal origination (huduth). Razi first presents a sequence of posteriorities: if origination were the cause, there would be circularity because origination is a mode of existence, and existence depends on the efficient cause, which depends on need, which depends on the cause of need. Next, he presents an objection: if possibility is the cause, then possible non-existents would need a cause, which is absurd because non-existence cannot receive a cause's effect. The answer is that the cause of non-existence is the non-existence of the cause, though this answer is not fully satisfactory ('fihi ma fihi'). The long footnote elaborates: there are four posteriorities (two natural, two essential), and origination cannot be the cause because it is posterior to existence. The objection that possibility is posterior to the possible and thus cannot be a cause is refuted: what is posterior is existence or non-existence, not possibility itself. The footnote also identifies the schools: philosophers and later mutakallimun hold possibility as cause; earlier mutakallimun hold origination. The claim that non-existence cannot be an effect is rejected because non-existence can be the effect of the non-existence of the cause.

The Possible Always Needs Its Cause
Razi argues for the perpetual need of the possible for a cause, and refutes two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possible cannot persist without an efficient cause, and how Razi answers key objections.

Core Idea
Possibility is perpetual, so the need for a cause never ends.

Razi tackles a crucial question: once something exists, does it need God to keep it in existence? Many assume that after creation, things can persist on their own. Here, Razi argues that possibility—the essence of creatures—demands a continuous cause. He answers two objections to clarify the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the full argument. Notice the structure: a claim, an objection, an answer, a second objection, and a further clarification.
The footnote (2) is part of the original text.
2
Identify the main argument
Find the core reasoning: why does the possible need a cause for persistence? Because the cause of need is possibility, which is always present.
3
Understand the two objections and answers
The first objection says the possible becomes 'more appropriate' for existence; Razi shows this leads to a contradiction. The second objection says the cause during persistence either produces nothing or something new; Razi redefines influence as sustaining persistence.
Pay attention to the concept of 'obtaining the obtained'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الممكن حال بقائه لا يستغنى عن المؤثر
لأن علة الحاجة الإمكان والإمكان ضرورى اللزوم لماهية الممكن وهي أبدا محتاجة.
لا يقال: إنه حال البقاء أولى بالوجود وتلك الأولوية مانعة من احتياجه إلى المؤثر
لأنا نقول: هذه الأولوية المغنية عن المرجح إن كانت حاصلة حال الحدوث لزم استغناء الممكن من المؤثر حال الحدوث وإلا فهو أمر حادث حال البقاء، فإذا لذا حصل الاستمرار، والشيء حال استمراره مفتقر إلى المرجح.
احتجوا بأن المؤثر حال بقاء الأثر إما أن يكون له أثر أو لا يكون، فإن كان له أثر فذلك الأثر إما الوجود الذي كان حاصلا وهو محال، لأن تحصيل الحاصل محال، أو أمرا جديداً فكان المؤثر مؤثرا في الجديد لا في الباقي، وإن لم يكن له أثر أصلا استحال أن يكون له فيه تأثير.
والجواب: أنا لا نعني بالتأثير تحصيل أمر جديد بل بقاء الأثر لبقاء المؤثر.
(2) أقول: القول بأن الممكن حال بقائه محتاج إلى المؤثر هو قول الحكماء والمتأخرين من المتكلمين وبعض منهم يفرقون بين الموجد وبين المبقى. والاعتراض بأن المؤثر حال البقاء إما أن يكون له في الأثر تأثير أم لا يشتمل على غلط، فإن المؤثر في البقاء لا يكون له أثر البقاء حال العدم، وتحصيل الحاصل إنما لزم منه والحق أن المؤثر يفيد البقاء بعد الإحداث. وقوله: وإن كان أمرا جديدا كان المؤثر مؤثرا في الجديد لا في الباقي جوابه نعم: تأثيره بعد الإحداث في أمر جديد هو البقاء فإنه غير الإحداث فهو مؤثر في أمر جديد صار به باقيا لا في الذي كان باقيا.
Issue: The possible in its state of persistence does not dispense with the efficient cause. Because the cause of need is possibility, and possibility is necessarily inherent in the essence of the possible, and it is always in need. It cannot be said: In the state of persistence it is more appropriate for existence, and that appropriateness prevents its need for the efficient cause. For we say: This appropriateness which dispenses with the preponderating factor—if it is obtained in the state of origination, then the possible would dispense with the efficient cause in the state of origination; otherwise, it is an occurrent matter in the state of persistence. So when persistence occurs, the thing in its state of persistence is in need of the preponderating factor. They objected: The efficient cause in the state of persistence of the effect either has an effect or does not. If it has an effect, then that effect is either the existence which was already obtained—impossible, because obtaining the obtained is impossible—or it is something new, so the efficient cause would be effective in the new, not in the persistent. And if it has no effect at all, it is impossible that it have any influence in it. The answer: We do not mean by 'influence' the obtaining of something new, but rather the persistence of the effect through the persistence of the efficient cause. (2) I say: The statement that the possible in its state of persistence is in need of the efficient cause is the view of the philosophers and the later mutakallimun, and some of them differentiate between the originator and the preserver. The objection that the efficient cause in the state of persistence either has influence in the effect or does not contains an error. For the efficient cause in persistence does not have an effect of persistence in the state of non-existence, and 'obtaining the obtained' applies only to something else. The truth is that the efficient cause brings about persistence after origination. And his statement: 'If it is something new, then the efficient cause is effective in the new, not in the persistent'—its answer is yes: his influence after origination is in something new, which is persistence, for it is different from origination, so he is effective in something new through which the thing becomes persistent, not in that which was already persistent.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the possible remains in need of the efficient cause even after it has come into existence. Razi argues that since the cause of need is possibility, which is an essential attribute of the possible, the need is perpetual. He then refutes an objection: one might say that in the state of persistence, the possible is more appropriate for existence, so it no longer needs a cause. Razi responds: if this appropriateness were present from origination, then the possible would never need a cause at the moment of origination; if it occurs later, it is an accident, and the thing during its persistence still needs a preponderating factor. A second objection is presented: if the cause has an effect during persistence, either that effect is the same existence (which is already obtained, so impossible) or a new existence (so the cause would affect the new, not the persistent). The answer is that 'influence' here means not producing something new but sustaining the persistence of the effect by the persistence of the cause. The footnote clarifies that this view is held by philosophers and later mutakallimun, and the objection is mistaken because the efficient cause in the state of persistence does not act upon non-existence but upon the persistent thing in a new way (persistence as distinct from origination).

الموجد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Once a thing exists, it can persist on its own without a cause.
Correction: Razi shows that because the essence of the possible remains possible, it always needs a cause for both origination and persistence.

Checkpoint
How does Razi answer the objection that the efficient cause during persistence would either have no effect or produce the same existence?
A) He accepts the objection and says persistence is uncaused.
B) He redefines 'influence' as sustaining persistence, not producing something new.
C) He says the efficient cause creates a second, distinct existence.
D) He says the objection only applies to temporal beings, not spiritual ones.

Key Takeaway
The possible's need for its cause is perpetual because its essence remains possible, and the cause sustains persistence without creating a new existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own life, do you deeply feel that your very existence and continued existence every moment depends solely on Allah? Or do you sometimes live as if your existence is self-sustained? What one thing can you do today to remind yourself of your absolute need for Him?
?
The possible always needs its cause—this is a philosophical truth. But do you actually live knowing that without Allah's continuous preservation, you would cease to exist? How would your relationship with Allah change if you truly internalized that He is sustaining you every moment?
?
Razi refutes the idea that once a thing exists, it can persist on its own. In your spiritual life, what areas do you assume you can sustain without constant divine aid—like your faith, your character, your good deeds? What step can you take this week to actively rely on Allah for their persistence?

Why the Contingent Always Needs a Cause
Razi presents the question with objections and answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a contingent being requires a sustaining cause during its persistence, based on its intrinsic possibility, and how objections against this view are refuted.

Core Idea
Possibility, not origination, is the true basis for needing a cause.

This passage confronts a common assumption: that once something exists, it can sustain itself. Imam Razi argues that the contingent's need for a cause is rooted in its very nature of possibility, which never leaves it. He then addresses two sophisticated objections that attempt to show that a persisting thing cannot have an efficient cause, and clarifies what 'influence' really means.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument unfold.
Note the use of key terms like imkan, baqa', and tahsil al-hasil.
2
Identify the core claim and its proof
Find the main thesis: the contingent is never independent of a cause. Then see why: because the cause of need is possibility, which is always present.
Razi's proof is simple: need is tied to essence, not time.
3
Understand the first objection and refutation
The objection claims that during persistence, the thing is more deserving of existence (priority). Razi shows this priority either already existed (making origination independent) or arises later (itself needing a cause).
This refutation uses a dilemma argument.
4
Grasp the second objection and answer
The second objection argues that a persisting cause cannot have an effect without producing something new. Razi answers by redefining influence as sustaining the effect, not creating anew.
This clarifies the Ash'ari doctrine of continuous creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( مسألة : الممكن حال بقائه لا يستغنى عن المؤثر )
الأن علة الحاجة الإمكان والإمكان ضرورى اللزوم لماهية الممكن وهي أبدا محتاجة .
لا يقال : إنه حال البقاء أولى بالوجود وتلك الأولوية مانعة من احتياجه إلى المؤثر
•
لأنا نقول : هذه الأولوية المغنية عن المرجح إن كانت حاصلة حال الحدوث لزم استغناء الممكن من المؤثر حال الحدوث وإلا فهو أمر حادث حال البقاء ، وإلا لَمَّا حصل الاستمرار ، والشيء حال استمراره مفتقر إلى المرجح . احتجوا بأن المؤثر حال بقاء الأثر إما أن يكون له أثر أو لا يكون ، فإن كان له أثر فذلك الأثر إما الوجود الذى كان حاصلا وهو محال ، لأن تحصيل الحاصل محال ، أو أمرا جديداً فكان المؤثر مؤثرا فى الجديد لا في الباقى ، وإن لم يكن له أثر أصلا استحال أن يكون له فيه تأثير .
والجواب : أنا لا نعني بالتأثير تحصيل أمر جديد بل بقاء الأثر لبقاء المؤثر
(Issue: The contingent, during its persistence, is not independent of the efficient cause.)
Because the cause of need is possibility (imkan), and possibility is necessarily concomitant with the essence of the contingent, and it is always in need.
It cannot be said: It is, during persistence, more deserving of existence, and that priority prevents its need for the efficient cause.
•
For we say: This priority that suffices from the preponderant (murajjih) — if it occurs at the time of origination, then the contingent would be independent of the efficient cause at origination; otherwise, it is an incident that occurs during persistence, otherwise, when continuity occurs, a thing during its continuity is in need of the preponderant.
They argued: The efficient cause during the persistence of the effect either has an effect or not. If it has an effect, that effect is either the existence that was already present, which is impossible because the acquisition of the acquired (tahsil al-hasil) is impossible, or a new thing, so the efficient cause is effective in the new, not in the remaining. And if it has no effect at all, it is impossible that it have any influence in it.
The answer: We do not mean by 'influence' the acquisition of a new thing, but rather the persistence of the effect due to the persistence of the efficient cause.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a theological question (mas'ala) about whether a contingent being (mumkin) requires an efficient cause (mu'aththir) while it persists (baqa') after its origination. The author first states the position: the cause of need is possibility (imkan), which is essential to the contingent's nature, so it is always in need. Then an objection is raised: during persistence, the being is more deserving of existence, so that priority (awlawiyya) should make it independent. The author refutes this by noting that if such priority existed at origination, it would make the being independent then, which is false; if it arises only during persistence, it is itself an incident needing a cause. Another objection follows: the efficient cause during persistence either produces an effect or not. If it produces the same existence, that is 'acquisition of the acquired' (tahsil al-hasil), impossible; if a new effect, then it only affects the new, not the persistent; if no effect, then no influence. The answer clarifies that 'influence' does not mean producing something new but rather the persistence of the effect through the persistence of the cause. This refines the Ash'ari doctrine of continuous creation.

Key Terms
إمكان
مؤثر
بقاء
تحصيل الحاصل
مرجح
أولوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Once a thing exists, it no longer needs a cause to remain in existence.
Correction: The need for a cause is based on the thing's intrinsic possibility (imkan), which is always present, not on its temporal origination. Therefore, it needs a cause as long as it exists.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why does the contingent need a cause even after it has come into existence?
A) Because it is temporal.
B) Because of its intrinsic possibility.
C) Because it might cease to exist.
D) Because the cause continues to act.

Key Takeaway
The contingent's need for a cause is perpetual, because its possibility is essential, and its persistence requires a sustaining cause, not a new act of creation.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
The Philosophers' Argument: Possibility Demands Matter
Razi presents the philosophical argument for matter as a necessary substrate for possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument that the possibility (imkan) of an originated thing is a real existential attribute that necessitates a pre-existing substrate (matter).

Core Idea
Possibility is a real property that needs a substrate — matter is that substrate.

This passage contains one of the most debated premises in medieval Islamic philosophy: the claim that possibility (imkan) is an existential attribute distinct from divine power. The philosophers use this to argue for the eternity of matter. Let's read Razi's concise presentation of their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the source text
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Note the key terms: 'possible' (imkan), 'substrate' (mahall), 'matter' (madda).
The passage only argues for matter; duration is mentioned but not proven here.
2
Understand the claim about possibility
The philosophers claim possibility is a real attribute that exists before the thing exists. It is not merely the agent's ability to create.
This is a metaphysical realism about modality.
3
Follow the logical deduction
If possibility were identical to the agent's power, a circularity would result. Therefore possibility must be a distinct attribute requiring a subject (substrate). That subject is matter.
The argument avoids self-dependence (tawqif al-shay' 'ala nafsihi).
4
Reflect on the theological stakes
This argument challenges the Islamic doctrine of creation ex nihilo. If matter is eternal, then God creates from pre-existing stuff.
Razi often critiques such arguments elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : زعمت الفلاسفة أن كل محدث فهو مسبوق بمادة ومدة . أما المادة فلان المحدث مسبوق بالإمكان فهو صفة وجودية مغايرة لصحة اقتدار القادر عليه موقوفة على كونها ممكنة في نفسها ، فلو كان إمكانها نفس صحة اقتدار القادر عليها لزم توقف الشيء على نفسه ، فثبت أن الإمكان صفة موجودة وهى سابقة على وجود الممكن فيستدعى محلا وهو المادة .
Issue: The philosophers claimed that every originated thing is preceded by matter and duration. As for matter, it is because the originated thing is preceded by possibility (imkan). For possibility is an existential attribute distinct from the validity of the powerful agent's power over it, dependent on it being possible in itself. So if its possibility were the same as the validity of the powerful agent's power over it, a thing depending on itself would follow. So it is established that possibility is an existent attribute which precedes the existence of the possible, so it requires a substrate (mahall) which is matter.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage presents a key philosophical argument for the eternity of matter. The philosophers claim that every originated thing (muhdath) is preceded by matter (madda) and duration (mudda). Razi only provides the argument for matter here. The argument proceeds as follows: The originated thing is preceded by possibility (imkan). Possibility is an existential attribute (sifa wujudiyya) that is distinct from the mere validity of the powerful agent's power over it. The thing's being possible is a real property that is prior to its existence. If possibility were identical to the validity of the agent's power (i.e., if the thing's possibility were just the agent's ability to create it), then the thing's existence would depend on itself, which is circular. Therefore, possibility is an attribute that exists before the thing exists, and since an attribute requires a substrate (mahall) to inhere in, that substrate must be matter. This is an argument that matter must be eternal to serve as the substrate for the possibility of originated things. The duration part is not elaborated here.

Key Terms
المحدث
المادة
الإمكان
الصفة الوجودية
صحة اقتدار القادر
المحل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that 'possibility' (imkan) is merely a logical concept with no real existence.
Correction: The philosophers treat possibility as a real existential attribute that exists before the thing itself, requiring a pre-existing substrate (matter).

Checkpoint
Why do the philosophers claim that possibility (imkan) requires a substrate (matter)?
A) Because possibility is a logical property that does not exist in reality.
B) Because possibility is an attribute that exists before the thing, and attributes need a subject to inhere in.
C) Because matter is the cause of possibility.
D) Because possibility is identical to the agent's power.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' argument for eternal matter rests on treating possibility (imkan) as a real existential attribute that must inhere in a pre-existing substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that the philosophers treat 'possibility' as a real attribute that must be rooted in matter. Where do you stand? Do you feel your gut-level assumption about God creating from nothing is shaken? What would it take for you to reject this argument, and how does that push you to understand divine power more deeply?
?
If possibility is a real attribute, then every possible thing in your future—your next decision, your change of heart, your destiny—is somehow rooted in matter. Does that make you feel trapped or liberated? How does this challenge your daily trust in Allah's absolute freedom and your own moral responsibility?
?
This argument claims that matter is eternal. How does that sit with your conviction that Allah is the only eternal? Sit with the discomfort: is it possible that the philosophers are correct about the structure of creation while still affirming the Creator's absolute priority? What are you personally willing to lose or re-examine in your beliefs to follow the truth wherever it leads?

Philosophers' Proof That Origination Requires Matter
Razi presents the philosophers' argument that origination requires matter, based on the existence of possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that every originated thing is preceded by matter, based on the concept of possibility (imkan) as an existent attribute requiring a substrate.

Core Idea
Possibility is an existent attribute that requires a substrate — matter.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents the philosophers' argument for why every originated thing must have preceded matter. The argument hinges on the nature of possibility (imkan), which they claim is a real attribute that needs a locus. This sets up a classic debate in Islamic theology about the relationship between possibility, non-existence, and matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the philosophers deduce that possibility must be an existent attribute.
2
Identify the logical problem
Find the conditional reasoning: if possibility were identical to the power of the agent, then a thing would depend on itself. Understand why that is impossible.
3
Notice the conclusion and the brief answer
The conclusion is that possibility requires a locus (matter). The author gives a brief answer referencing another discussion.
The answer is not elaborated here; it points to an earlier issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : زعمت الفلاسفة أن كل محدث فهو مسبوق بمادة ومدة . أما المادة فلان المحدث مسبوق بالإمكان فهو صفة وجودية مغايرة لصحة اقتدار القادر عليه موقوفة على كونها ممكنة في نفسها ، فلو كان إمكانها نفس صحة اقتدار القادر عليها لزم توقف الشيء على نفسه ، فثبت أن الإمكان صفة موجودة وهى سابقة على وجود الممكن فيستدعى محلا وهو المادة . والجواب عنها ما مر في مسألة المعدوم . أقول : مامر فى مسألة المعدوم أن الإمكان لا يجوز أن يكون ثابتا حال العدم لأن الذوات المعدومة يمتنع عليها التغير والخروج عن الذاتية فلا يمكن أن تتصف بالإمكان ، ثم إنه حكم بصحة في حجتهم الثانية بأنه يقتضى اللا إمكان المحمول عليه النفى فيجب أن يكون ثابتا ، وههنا لم يجعل الإمكان صفة المعدوم ، بل إنما وجب للكونه ثابتا أن يكون الموصوف به موجودا وإن كان ما يثول إليه الممكن معدماً . والتحقيق في هذا الموضع : هو أن الإمكان يقع بالاشتراك اللفظى عندهم على معنيين أحدهما ما يقابل الامتناع وهو عندهم صفة مقلية بوصف بها كل ما عدا الواجب والممتنع من التصورات ، ولا يلزم من اتصاف الماهية بها كونها مادته . والثانى الاستعداد وهو موجود عندهم معدود فى نوع من أنواع جنس الكيف ، وإذا كان موجوداً وعرضاً وغير باق بعد الخروج إلى العقل فيحتاج لا محالة قبل الخروج إلى محل وهو المادة ، فهذا البحث معهم يجب أن يكون في إثبات ذلك العرض ونفيه .
A question: The philosophers claimed that every originated thing is preceded by matter and time. As for matter, [they argued] because the originated is preceded by possibility (imkan). Possibility is an existent attribute distinct from the validity of the power of the capable over it, dependent on its being possible in itself. If its possibility were identical to the validity of the power of the capable over it, then a thing would depend on itself. So it is established that possibility is an existent attribute that precedes the existence of the possible thing, and therefore requires a locus (mahall), which is matter. The answer to it is what has already passed in the question of the non-existent. I say: What has passed in the question of the non-existent is that possibility cannot be established in the state of non-existence because non-existent essences cannot change or go beyond their essence, so they cannot be described with possibility. Then he ruled that their second argument is valid in that it necessitates the impossibility of what carries negation, so it must be established. Here he did not make possibility an attribute of the non-existent; rather, it is only necessary because of its being established that the thing described by it is existent, even if what the possible turns into is non-existent. The precise investigation in this place is that possibility, in their view, is predicated by homonymy (اشتراك لفظي) on two meanings: one is the opposite of impossibility, which according to them is an intellectual attribute describing everything other than the necessary and the impossible of concepts, and describing an essence with it does not necessitate it being its matter. The second is preparedness (isti'dad), which according to them exists and is counted as a type of the genus of quality (kayf), and if it exists and is an accident and does not persist after emergence into actuality, then it inevitably needs, before emergence, a locus, which is matter. So the discussion with them must be about establishing or denying that accident.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and critical analysis of the philosophers' argument that every originated thing is preceded by matter. The philosophers argue that the originated is preceded by possibility (imkan), which is an existent attribute distinct from the mere validity of the power of the agent. If possibility were just that validity, it would lead to self-dependence. Therefore, possibility must be an attribute that requires a substrate, which is matter. Razi then gives a brief answer referencing an earlier discussion on the non-existent (ma'dum). Then he adds a long editorial note (أقول) that clarifies the issues. He explains that in the earlier discussion, it was established that possibility cannot be an attribute of non-existence because non-existent essences cannot change. However, here the argument does not make possibility an attribute of the non-existent but rather of that which is established as existent. Then Razi makes a more precise distinction: the philosophers use 'imkan' in two senses by homonymy: (1) logical possibility opposed to impossibility, which is an intellectual attribute that does not require a material substrate; (2) preparedness (isti'dad), which is an accident existing in the material substrate before emergence. The real debate is about whether such an accident exists or not. Thus, this passage both presents the philosophers' argument and provides Razi's own thorough analysis of the different meanings of possibility.

Key Terms
الإمكان
المادة
صفة وجودية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that possibility is merely a mental concept or the power of the agent.
Correction: The philosophers argue that possibility is an existent attribute distinct from the agent's power, requiring a real substrate.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why does possibility require matter?
A) Because possibility is a non-existent attribute.
B) Because possibility is an existent attribute that needs a substrate.
C) Because matter is the cause of possibility.
D) Because possibility is identical to the power of the agent.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers base their argument for matter on the claim that possibility is a real, pre-existing attribute needing a substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers argue that possibility cannot be identical to God's power, because that would lead to circularity. Do you ever assume that God's power is limited by some pre-existing possibility? How does this argument challenge you to trust in God's absolute ability to create from nothing?
?
If possibility is a real attribute requiring a substrate, then your own existence depended on matter. But as a believer, you affirm creation ex nihilo. How does this tension sit in your heart? Are you willing to let go of any lingering belief that something besides God is necessary for creation? Examine your faith: is it truly in God's sole creative power?

Razi's Analysis: The Two Meanings of Imkan
Razi clarifies that 'imkan' has two meanings and the debate concerns the accident of preparedness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's crucial distinction between logical possibility and preparedness, and why the real debate is about the existence of preparedness as an accident.

Core Idea
The real debate is not about logical possibility but about the accident of preparedness.

After presenting the philosophers' argument, Razi offers his own detailed analysis. He clarifies that the term 'imkan' is ambiguous, having two distinct meanings. Only one of these, preparedness (isti'dad), can serve as the basis for requiring matter. The discussion must therefore focus on whether such an accident exists. This nuanced analysis shows Razi's sophisticated approach to philosophical theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic from 'أقول' to the end, along with the translation. Identify the two meanings Razi distinguishes.
The first meaning is logical possibility, the second is preparedness.
2
Understand why the first meaning does not require matter
Logical possibility is an intellectual attribute that describes essences without needing a material substrate. It does not entail matter.
3
Grasp the nature of preparedness
Preparedness is an accident in the category of quality, existing before actualization. It requires a locus because it is an accident.
4
See the implication for the debate
Razi concludes that the discussion must be about proving or denying this accident of preparedness, not about logical possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : زعمت الفلاسفة أن كل محدث فهو مسبوق بمادة ومدة . أما المادة فلان المحدث مسبوق بالإمكان فهو صفة وجودية مغايرة لصحة اقتدار القادر عليه موقوفة على كونها ممكنة في نفسها ، فلو كان إمكانها نفس صحة اقتدار القادر عليها لزم توقف الشيء على نفسه ، فثبت أن الإمكان صفة موجودة وهى سابقة على وجود الممكن فيستدعى محلا وهو المادة . والجواب عنها ما مر في مسألة المعدوم . أقول : مامر فى مسألة المعدوم أن الإمكان لا يجوز أن يكون ثابتا حال العدم لأن الذوات المعدومة يمتنع عليها التغير والخروج عن الذاتية فلا يمكن أن تتصف بالإمكان ، ثم إنه حكم بصحة في حجتهم الثانية بأنه يقتضى اللا إمكان المحمول عليه النفى فيجب أن يكون ثابتا ، وههنا لم يجعل الإمكان صفة المعدوم ، بل إنما وجب للكونه ثابتا أن يكون الموصوف به موجودا وإن كان ما يثول إليه الممكن معدماً . والتحقيق في هذا الموضع : هو أن الإمكان يقع بالاشتراك اللفظى عندهم على معنيين أحدهما ما يقابل الامتناع وهو عندهم صفة مقلية بوصف بها كل ما عدا الواجب والممتنع من التصورات ، ولا يلزم من اتصاف الماهية بها كونها مادته . والثانى الاستعداد وهو موجود عندهم معدود فى نوع من أنواع جنس الكيف ، وإذا كان موجوداً وعرضاً وغير باق بعد الخروج إلى العقل فيحتاج لا محالة قبل الخروج إلى محل وهو المادة ، فهذا البحث معهم يجب أن يكون في إثبات ذلك العرض ونفيه .
A question: The philosophers claimed that every originated thing is preceded by matter and time. As for matter, [they argued] because the originated is preceded by possibility (imkan). Possibility is an existent attribute distinct from the validity of the power of the capable over it, dependent on its being possible in itself. If its possibility were identical to the validity of the power of the capable over it, then a thing would depend on itself. So it is established that possibility is an existent attribute that precedes the existence of the possible thing, and therefore requires a locus (mahall), which is matter. The answer to it is what has already passed in the question of the non-existent. I say: What has passed in the question of the non-existent is that possibility cannot be established in the state of non-existence because non-existent essences cannot change or go beyond their essence, so they cannot be described with possibility. Then he ruled that their second argument is valid in that it necessitates the impossibility of what carries negation, so it must be established. Here he did not make possibility an attribute of the non-existent; rather, it is only necessary because of its being established that the thing described by it is existent, even if what the possible turns into is non-existent. The precise investigation in this place is that possibility, in their view, is predicated by homonymy (اشتراك لفظي) on two meanings: one is the opposite of impossibility, which according to them is an intellectual attribute describing everything other than the necessary and the impossible of concepts, and describing an essence with it does not necessitate it being its matter. The second is preparedness (isti'dad), which according to them exists and is counted as a type of the genus of quality (kayf), and if it exists and is an accident and does not persist after emergence into actuality, then it inevitably needs, before emergence, a locus, which is matter. So the discussion with them must be about establishing or denying that accident.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and critical analysis of the philosophers' argument that every originated thing is preceded by matter. The philosophers argue that the originated is preceded by possibility (imkan), which is an existent attribute distinct from the mere validity of the power of the agent. If possibility were just that validity, it would lead to self-dependence. Therefore, possibility must be an attribute that requires a substrate, which is matter. Razi then gives a brief answer referencing an earlier discussion on the non-existent (ma'dum). Then he adds a long editorial note (أقول) that clarifies the issues. He explains that in the earlier discussion, it was established that possibility cannot be an attribute of non-existence because non-existent essences cannot change. However, here the argument does not make possibility an attribute of the non-existent but rather of that which is established as existent. Then Razi makes a more precise distinction: the philosophers use 'imkan' in two senses by homonymy: (1) logical possibility opposed to impossibility, which is an intellectual attribute that does not require a material substrate; (2) preparedness (isti'dad), which is an accident existing in the material substrate before emergence. The real debate is about whether such an accident exists or not. Thus, this passage both presents the philosophers' argument and provides Razi's own thorough analysis of the different meanings of possibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all forms of possibility require matter.
Correction: Razi distinguishes logical possibility (no matter needed) from preparedness (requires matter).

Checkpoint
According to Razi, which meaning of imkan is the basis for requiring matter?
A) Logical possibility
B) Preparedness
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Razi clarifies that the philosophers' argument for matter works only if 'imkan' means preparedness, not logical possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own intellectual journey, have you ever conflated the logical possibility of a concept (it can be thought) with the real preparedness of that concept to exist (it has a material basis)? How has that confusion affected your conclusions about theological matters? What will you do to distinguish them more carefully?
?
Razi narrows a grand philosophical debate to a single empirical question: does an accident of preparedness exist? Think of a belief you hold that rests on an unexamined assumption about 'preparedness'—something you assume is ready or possible without verifying its basis. Will you examine that assumption today, even if it means uprooting a comfortable conviction?
?
The philosophers argued that matter is necessary because it hosts the accident of preparedness. But Razi questions whether that accident exists at all. Consider your own life: are there things you have assumed are 'prepared' in you—like readiness for change, repentance, or understanding—that might actually be absent? What would it take to honestly test whether that preparedness is real, and then act accordingly?

Philosophers' Proof for a Pre-Existent Time
The philosophers argue for an eternal time from the priority of non-existence; Razi rebuts with infinite regress and divine temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that every originated thing requires a pre-existing time, and Razi's refutation based on infinite regress and making God temporal.

Core Idea
The priority of non-existence is a real attribute demanding a timeless substrate: time.

The philosophers argue that before anything comes into existence, there is a period of non-existence. This 'before' cannot be nothing – it must be an attribute of something real, which they identify as time. Razi counters by showing that this leads to absurdities: time would need a prior time, and God would be in time.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Carefully read the passage noting how they distinguish between non-existence and the attribute of priority.
Focus on the claim that priority (qabliyya) is an existent attribute.
2
Identify the conclusion
Why do they conclude that time must be beginningless?
3
Study Razi's refutation
Read Razi's answer and see how he uses the same logic to produce contradictions.
Note the infinite regress and the implication about God.
4
Reflect on the argument's weakness
Consider why the philosophers' proof might seem plausible but ultimately fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المدة فقالوا : كل محدث فعد مه قبل وجوده ، فتلك القبلية ليست نفس الاعدم ، فإن العدم قبل كالعدم بعد وليس القبل بعد ، وهى صفة وجودية تستدعى موصوفا موجوداً فقبل ذلك الحادث شيء موصوف بالقبلية لا إلى أول ، فهذا قبليات لا أول لها ، والذى يلحقه القبلية لذاته هو الزمان ، فهنازمان لا أول له . والجواب : أن تقدم عدم الحادث على وجوده لو وجب أن يكون بالزمان لكان تقدم كل واحد من أجزاء الزمان على وجوده بالزمان ، ولكان تقدم البارى تعالى على هذا الجزء من الزمان ، فيلزم أن يكون الله تعالى زمانيا وأن يكون الزمان زمانيا فهما محالان . أقول: إنهم يقولون القبلية والبعدية يلحقان الزمان لذاته ولغير الزمان بسبب الزمان ، والوجود والعدم لما لم يدخل الزمان في مفهومهما احتاجا في صيرورتهما بعد وقبل إلى زمان ، أما أجزاء الزمان فلا تحتاج إلى غير أنفسها ولا العدم بالقياس إليها في كونها بعد أو قبل إلى غيرها ، وأما البارى تعالى وكل ما هو علة الزمان او شرط وجوده فلا يكون في الزمان ولا معه إلا فى التوهم ، حيث يقبها الوهم على الزمانيات فهذا ما قالوه ههنا.
As for the duration (mudda), they said: every originated thing has its non-existence before its existence. That priority (qabliyya) is not the same as non-existence, for non-existence before is like non-existence after, and the 'before' is not 'after'. It is an existent attribute that requires an existent described entity. So before that originated thing there is something described with priority without a first. Thus there are priorities with no first. And that which is described with priority essentially is time. So there is time with no beginning. The answer: if the priority of the non-existence of the originated over its existence must be by time, then the priority of each part of time over its existence would be by time, and the priority of the Creator Most High over this part of time would be by time, necessitating that God be temporal (zamani) and that time be temporal, both of which are impossible. I say: They claim that priority and posteriority attach to time essentially, and to other things through time. Existence and non-existence, since time is not included in their concept, need time to become 'after' and 'before'. As for the parts of time, they need nothing other than themselves, nor does non-existence in relation to them need another to be 'after' or 'before'. As for the Creator Most High and whatever is the cause or condition of time's existence, it is not in time nor with it, except in the imagination (tawahhum) that estimates [things] in relation to temporal things. This is what they said here.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument for a pre-existing infinite time (mudda) followed by a refutation and then a defense by the philosophers. The argument: every originated thing has its non-existence before its existence. This 'priority' (qabliyya) is not the same as non-existence itself, because non-existence before and after are the same, but the 'before' is different. Therefore, priority is an existent attribute requiring a substrate that is described by it. That substrate must be something that exists and is essentially characterized by priority, which is time. Thus, there is an infinite time with no beginning. Razi then gives a brief answer: if this priority implies temporal priority, then the priority of each part of time over its own existence would also be temporal, leading to an infinite regress, and the priority of God over time would make God temporal, which is impossible. Then Razi adds an editorial note (أقول) explaining the philosophers' response: priority and posteriority attach to time essentially, and to other things (like existence and non-existence) only through time. Existence and non-existence are not inherently temporal; they require time to be described as 'before' or 'after'. But the parts of time do not need an external time, nor does the non-existence relative to them. As for God and other causes of time, they are not in time or with time except in imagination when we compare them to temporal things. Thus, the philosophers defend their position by distinguishing between essential and accidental temporal descriptors.

Key Terms
المدة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Priority is the same as non-existence.
Correction: The philosophers argue that priority is a distinct attribute, not identical to non-existence, because non-existence before and after is the same, while priority changes.

Key Takeaway

Philosophers' Defense: Essential vs. Accidental Temporality
Razi explains the philosophers' refined position: priority/posteriority are essential to time, accidental to everything else.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers defend their concept of time by distinguishing between essential temporal attributes and accidental ones.

Core Idea
Priority and posteriority belong to time essentially; all other things have them only through time.

After Razi's objection, the philosophers offer a more nuanced account: priority and posteriority are intrinsic to time itself, not to things like non-existence or God. Existence and non-existence require time to be described as 'before' or 'after', but parts of time do not. God, as the cause of time, is beyond temporal categories except in imagination.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' defense
Read the passage and note the key distinction: time has priority essentially; other things have it by accident.
2
Understand the three cases
Identify the three cases: existence/non-existence, parts of time, and God/the cause of time.
Each case has a different relationship to time.
3
Analyze the essential vs accidental distinction
What does it mean for priority to be essential to time? How does this block the infinite regress?
4
Reflect on God's relation to time
Why do the philosophers say God is only temporally ordered in imagination?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المدة فقالوا : كل محدث فعد مه قبل وجوده ، فتلك القبلية ليست نفس الاعدم ، فإن العدم قبل كالعدم بعد وليس القبل بعد ، وهى صفة وجودية تستدعى موصوفا موجوداً فقبل ذلك الحادث شيء موصوف بالقبلية لا إلى أول ، فهذا قبليات لا أول لها ، والذى يلحقه القبلية لذاته هو الزمان ، فهنازمان لا أول له . والجواب : أن تقدم عدم الحادث على وجوده لو وجب أن يكون بالزمان لكان تقدم كل واحد من أجزاء الزمان على وجوده بالزمان ، ولكان تقدم البارى تعالى على هذا الجزء من الزمان ، فيلزم أن يكون الله تعالى زمانيا وأن يكون الزمان زمانيا فهما محالان . أقول: إنهم يقولون القبلية والبعدية يلحقان الزمان لذاته ولغير الزمان بسبب الزمان ، والوجود والعدم لما لم يدخل الزمان في مفهومهما احتاجا في صيرورتهما بعد وقبل إلى زمان ، أما أجزاء الزمان فلا تحتاج إلى غير أنفسها ولا العدم بالقياس إليها في كونها بعد أو قبل إلى غيرها ، وأما البارى تعالى وكل ما هو علة الزمان او شرط وجوده فلا يكون في الزمان ولا معه إلا فى التوهم ، حيث يقبها الوهم على الزمانيات فهذا ما قالوه ههنا.
As for the duration (mudda), they said: every originated thing has its non-existence before its existence. That priority (qabliyya) is not the same as non-existence, for non-existence before is like non-existence after, and the 'before' is not 'after'. It is an existent attribute that requires an existent described entity. So before that originated thing there is something described with priority without a first. Thus there are priorities with no first. And that which is described with priority essentially is time. So there is time with no beginning. The answer: if the priority of the non-existence of the originated over its existence must be by time, then the priority of each part of time over its existence would be by time, and the priority of the Creator Most High over this part of time would be by time, necessitating that God be temporal (zamani) and that time be temporal, both of which are impossible. I say: They claim that priority and posteriority attach to time essentially, and to other things through time. Existence and non-existence, since time is not included in their concept, need time to become 'after' and 'before'. As for the parts of time, they need nothing other than themselves, nor does non-existence in relation to them need another to be 'after' or 'before'. As for the Creator Most High and whatever is the cause or condition of time's existence, it is not in time nor with it, except in the imagination (tawahhum) that estimates [things] in relation to temporal things. This is what they said here.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument for a pre-existing infinite time (mudda) followed by a refutation and then a defense by the philosophers. The argument: every originated thing has its non-existence before its existence. This 'priority' (qabliyya) is not the same as non-existence itself, because non-existence before and after are the same, but the 'before' is different. Therefore, priority is an existent attribute requiring a substrate that is described by it. That substrate must be something that exists and is essentially characterized by priority, which is time. Thus, there is an infinite time with no beginning. Razi then gives a brief answer: if this priority implies temporal priority, then the priority of each part of time over its own existence would also be temporal, leading to an infinite regress, and the priority of God over time would make God temporal, which is impossible. Then Razi adds an editorial note (أقول) explaining the philosophers' response: priority and posteriority attach to time essentially, and to other things (like existence and non-existence) only through time. Existence and non-existence are not inherently temporal; they require time to be described as 'before' or 'after'. But the parts of time do not need an external time, nor does the non-existence relative to them. As for God and other causes of time, they are not in time or with time except in imagination when we compare them to temporal things. Thus, the philosophers defend their position by distinguishing between essential and accidental temporal descriptors.

Misconception: God is temporally prior to time in a real sense.
Correction: The philosophers say that God is not in time nor with time; any temporal priority is only in the mind when we compare Him to temporal things.

Checkpoint

B) Through time
C) Through priority
D) Through causality

Key Takeaway
The philosophers distinguish essential temporal attributes (of time itself) from accidental ones, placing God and causes of time outside temporal categories except in imagination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You likely think of your life as a sequence of before and after. But if time itself is created and you are dependent on a Creator outside time, does your sense of urgency or patience need recalibrating? Will you consciously remind yourself today that God is not bound by your timeline?
?
If God is not waiting for you to change because He sees all time at once, how does that affect your view of repentance? Are you content to delay, or will you seize this moment as the only time you have?
?
The philosophers say only imagination places God alongside temporal things. Have you ever imagined God as frustrated with your delays, as if He shares your temporality? How can you replace that mental image with the reality of a transcendent Creator who is beyond all waiting?

The Debate on Infinite Accidents
Ma'mar accepts infinite accidents; mutakallimun refute via number theory; Ma'mar counters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate between Ma'mar and the mutakallimun regarding the possibility of infinite series of accidents, and the mathematical and theological arguments involved.

Core Idea
Infinite series are rejected by kalam using number theory, but Ma'mar challenges the premises.

This passage presents a fascinating exchange between Ma'mar, an early mutakallim who accepted infinite regresses, and the majority of mutakallimun who refuted him using a mathematical argument based on halves. Ma'mar's counterarguments introduce sophisticated distinctions about finitude and infinity, including theological examples. Let's explore the arguments step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read Ma'mar's position
Read the passage and understand why Ma'mar accepted infinite accidents. He found the argument for relative accidents compelling and saw no way to avoid the infinite regress.
2
Examine the mutakallimun's argument
Notice the logical chain: every number has a half, half is less than whole, less implies finite, so whole is finite. This refutes the infinite series.
3
Analyze Ma'mar's rebuttal
See how Ma'mar first denies that every number has a half, then challenges the inference from 'less than' to 'finite' using divine attributes and infinite multiplications.
This shows a nuanced understanding of infinity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن معمراً من قدماء المتكلمين أثبت لقوة هذه الحجة هذه الأغراض النسبية ولم يجد دافعا للتسلسلات المذكورة فالتزمها وأثبت أعراضا لا نهاية لها يقوم كل واحد منها بالآخر . وقال المتكلمون : هذا باطل، لأن كل عدد موجود فله نصف ونصفه أقل من كله ، وكلما كان أقل من غيره فهو متناه فنصفه متناه في العدد ، وكل ما نصفه متناه فكله متناه لأنه ضعف المتناهي قال معمر : لا نسلم أن كل عدد فله نصف بل ذلك من خواص العدد المتناهى ، سلمنا لكن لم قلت بأن كل ما كان أقل من غيره فهو متناه ، أليس أن مقدورات الله تعالى أقل من معلوماته وتضعيف الألف مرارا لانهاية لها أقل من تضعيف الألفين مرارا لانهاية لها .
Then Ma'mar, one of the early mutakallimun, affirmed, due to the strength of this argument, these relative accidents, and he found no repulsion of the mentioned infinite regresses, so he committed to them and affirmed accidents with no end, each one subsisting in another. And the mutakallimun said: This is false, because every existing number has a half, and its half is less than the whole, and whatever is less than another is finite, so its half is finite in number, and whatever has a finite half has a finite whole because it is the double of the finite. Ma'mar said: We do not concede that every number has a half; rather, that is a property of finite numbers. [But] we concede [the argument], but why do you say that whatever is less than another is finite? Are not God's powers (maqdūrāt) less than His knowledge (ma‘lūmāt), and the multiplication of one thousand an infinite number of times less than the multiplication of two thousand an infinite number of times?
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents a specific position: Ma'mar (an early mutakallim) accepted the argument for relative accidents and consequently accepted an infinite regress of accidents, each subsisting in another. The mutakallimun object using a mathematical argument: every number has a half, and the half is less than the whole; what is less than another is finite; thus the half is finite, and so the whole (double of the half) is also finite. Therefore, an infinite series is impossible. Ma'mar counters by denying that every number has a half; he claims this is only for finite numbers. He also challenges the premise that anything less than another is automatically finite, using theological examples: God's powers are less than His knowledge, yet both are infinite; similarly, an infinite multiplication of 1000 is less than an infinite multiplication of 2000, yet both are infinite. The exchange shows a sophisticated debate about infinity and finitude.

Key Terms
التسلسل
العدد المتناهي
النصف
مقدورات
معلومات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun's argument proves that an infinite series cannot exist because all numbers are finite.
Correction: The argument specifically uses the property that if a number has a finite half, then the whole is finite; it does not claim that all numbers are finite in themselves.

Checkpoint
What is Ma'mar's first objection to the mutakallimun's argument?
A) He denies that every existing number has a half.
B) He claims that halves are not relevant to accidents.
C) He argues that infinite numbers are impossible.
D) He asserts that the argument only applies to divine attributes.

Key Takeaway
Ma'mar's defense of infinite accidents involves sophisticated objections to the mathematical argument from halves, challenging universal application and the link between being less and being finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ma'mar was willing to accept an infinite regress because he saw no repulsion in it. Is there a belief or teaching in your faith that you have accepted without fully resolving its logical implications? How does that affect your intellectual integrity before God?
?
The mutakallimun argued that 'less than' implies finiteness, but Ma'mar countered with examples from God's attributes. Do you ever treat God's attributes as if they were limited by human logic? In what areas of your life do you need to expand your understanding of God's infinitude?
?
Ma'mar's second concession ('سلمنا') shows he was willing to engage with the argument on its own terms before challenging it. When you encounter a theological position you disagree with, do you first try to understand it fully, or do you dismiss it? What would it cost you to genuinely listen to a challenging argument?

How White and Black Colors Are Generated
Razi explains white and black as products of mixing and interpenetration, with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's explanation of how white and black colors arise from the mixing of air with transparent bodies and the interpenetration of dense bodies, respectively.

Core Idea
White comes from air mixing with transparent bodies; black from dense bodies compacting.

Imam Razi presents a natural explanation for the generation of two fundamental colors: white and black. He uses the example of alum and oak gall to show how black emerges from interpenetration, and notes that mixing colors yields others, like yellow and blue making green. This lesson explores his account within the kalam tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note that the Arabic may contain OCR artifacts; focus on the meaning conveyed by the translation.
The passage is short but dense with physical reasoning.
2
Identify the two processes
Razi explains two distinct processes: one for white (mixing of air with transparent bodies) and one for black (interpenetration of dense bodies). Confirm you can distinguish them.
3
Understand the example
Examine the example of alum (vitriol) and oak gall to see how black is produced. Neither is black alone, but when mixed, alum penetrates the pores and the astringent causes compaction, yielding black.
This demonstrates the principle of interpenetration.
4
Reflect on color mixing
Notice the final remark that mixing colors yields other colors, e.g., yellow and blue produce green. Consider how this fits with the overall explanation.
This shows Razi's broader understanding of color generation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : قد مر أن البياض يحصل من اختلاط الهواء بالأجسام الشفافة ، وكذلك السواد أيضاً يحصل من اندماج أجزاء الأجسام الكثيفة بعضها في بعض ، والدليل عليه أن الواج في غايه النرر لحدته والعفص في غايه القبض وليس أحدهما بأسود ، فإذا امترجا نفذ الزاج فى المسام الصغيرة من العفص وقبضه الفصل بقوة فاندمج بعضها في بعض وحدث السواد ومن تركيبات الألوان تحصل ألوان أخرى كما ان الصفرة والزرقه الخضرة .
I say: It has passed that whiteness is obtained from the mixing of air with transparent bodies, and similarly blackness is also obtained from the interpenetration of parts of dense bodies into one another. The evidence is that alum is in extreme [؟] due to its sharpness, and oak gall is in extreme astringency, and neither of them is black. When they mix, the alum penetrates the small pores of the oak gall, and its astringent part contracts strongly, so some of it interpenetrates into others and blackness occurs. And from combinations of colors, other colors are obtained, such as yellow and blue produce green.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
In this final gloss, Razi explains the physical generation of white and black. White results from air mixing with transparent bodies (as in snow or crushed glass). Black arises from the interpenetration of dense bodies, using the example of alum and oak gall: neither is black alone, but when mixed, the alum penetrates the pores of the oak gall, and the astringent effect causes compaction, producing blackness. He also notes that mixing colors yields other colors, e.g., yellow and blue give green.

سواد
اختلاط
اندماج
زاج
عفص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That black is a simple, primary color that cannot be further analyzed.
Correction: Razi shows that black results from the interpenetration and compaction of dense bodies, as in the example of alum and oak gall, so it is a product of physical combination.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what produces blackness from alum and oak gall?
A) The light from the sun
B) The mixing and compaction caused by the alum penetrating the oak gall
C) The absence of air
D) The transformation of whiteness

Key Takeaway
White and black are not simple colors but arise from physical interactions of bodies: white from air mixing, black from compaction of dense bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi shows that blackness is not a simple color but results from hidden physical interactions. In your own life, where do you tend to settle for simple, surface-level explanations instead of seeking to understand the deeper, complex processes at work? How can you change that habit starting today?
?
The example of alum and oak gall: neither alone is black, but together they produce blackness. Think of a relationship or situation in your life where you avoid 'mixing' with something or someone different from you, fearing it will complicate things. Could it be that such mixing is precisely what Allah uses to bring about new understanding or growth in you? Will you take a step toward that today?
?
Razi's explanation relies on physical processes like mixing and interpenetration. Do you tend to attribute events only to obvious, visible causes, forgetting the intricate systems Allah has put in place? How can you train your mind to acknowledge and appreciate the hidden mechanisms behind everyday phenomena, and what difference would that make in your gratitude and awe toward Allah?
