Eternal Motion and the Principle of Priority
Objection and response about eternal motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the world could have been moving eternally and the response that motion requires priority and is incompatible with an eternal world.

Core Idea
Motion requires being preceded, so an eternal world cannot be in motion.

This lesson addresses a key objection to the argument that the world must have a beginning. If the world is eternal, why could it not have been in motion from eternity? The response invokes the nature of motion: each motion is preceded by another motion, leading to an infinite regress that is impossible. The dialogue clarifies the concept of priority in motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector grants the dichotomy between motion and rest but asks why the world could not have been moving eternally.
2
Understand the response
The response states that motion requires being preceded by another and priority (al-uwaliyya) is incompatible with motion.
The term 'priority' here means that each motion must have a prior motion.
3
Analyze the counter-response
The author questions why priority would preclude a specific motion, implying that the objector's reasoning may be flawed.
The phrase 'محال مكان' is unclear but likely a marginal note.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا الحصر، لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال إنها كانت متحركة . قوله : الحركة تقتضى المسبوقية بالغير والأولية وتنافيها . محال مكان قلنا الأولية تنافى وجود حركة معينة لسكن لم قلت إنها تنافى فى وجود حركة قبل حركة الثاني في أول أما الوجه الثاني وهو أن المجموع فعل فاعل مختار وله أول قلت لا تسلم إنه فعل فاعل مختار . بيانه أن الموجب قد يتخلف عنه الأثر إما لفوات شرط أو الحصول مانع فلم لا يجوز أن يقال المؤثر في وجود هذه الحوادث موجب بالذات إلا أن كل حادث متقدم فتقدمه شرط لأن يصدر من العلة الموجبة حادث آخر بعده بواسطة . سلمنا أنه فعل المختار لكن لا تسلم أن فعل المختار محدث ، وذلك لأن وجود الحادث وصحة تأثير المؤثر فيه عملك أبدا وإلا فقد كان متن ما لذاته ثم انقلاب ممسكا وذلك عمال وإذا كان كل واحد منهما مكنا أولا كان تأثير الصادر في وجود الأثر جائز أزلا، سلمنا أن الاجسام ما كانت متحركة فلم لا يجوز كونها ساكنة .
We grant the dichotomy, but why is it not permissible to say that they were moving? He said: motion requires being preceded by another and priority, and they are incompatible. Impossible [place]. We said: priority precludes the existence of a specific motion — why do you say it precludes the existence of a motion before a motion? As for the second aspect, that the aggregate is an act of a free agent and has a first, we say: we do not grant that it is an act of a free agent. Explanation: the necessary cause may be followed by the effect either due to a missing condition or an obstacle, so why is it not permissible to say that the effecter in the existence of these events is necessary by essence, except that every preceding event's priority is a condition for the necessary cause to produce another event after it by mediation? We grant that it is an act of the free agent, but we do not grant that the act of the free agent is temporal. That is because the existence of the event and the validity of the effecter's influence in it are always [possible?] otherwise it would be [unclear]. And if each of them is possible initially, then the effecter's influence in the existence of the effect is possible eternally. We grant that the bodies were not moving, so why is it not permissible for them to be at rest?
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections and responses regarding the possibility of an eternal world being in motion or at rest. The first objection (سلمنا الحصر) grants the dichotomy of motion/rest but asks why the world could not have been moving. The response (قوله: الحركة تقتضى المسبوقية...) is that motion requires being preceded by something else (meaning each motion must have a prior motion) and that priority (al-uwaliyya) is incompatible with motion. The author then questions this: why would priority preclude a specific motion? He shifts to the second aspect: that the entire world is an act of a free agent (fa'il mukhtar) and must have a beginning. The objector denies that it is an act of a free agent, arguing instead that the cause could be a necessary cause (muijib) that produces effects conditionally — each preceding event is a condition for the next, avoiding an infinite regress. The author then accepts that it is a free action but denies that freedom necessitates temporality; the free agent's ability to act could be eternal. Finally, the objector grants that bodies were not moving but asks why they could not have been at rest (sakina). This sets up further discussion on the next page.

Key Terms
الحركة
المسبوقية
الأولية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An eternal world could be in motion without a beginning.
Correction: Motion requires a prior motion, so an eternal motion would entail an infinite regress of motions, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given against the world being eternally in motion?
A) Motion requires a prior motion, leading to infinite regress.
B) Motion is an accident and cannot be eternal.
C) The world must have a beginning because it is composed.
D) The objector grants the dichotomy.

Key Takeaway
Motion cannot be eternal because each motion requires a prior motion, leading to an impossible infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You assume that some habits or patterns in your life are 'just the way you are' with no beginning. But this lesson shows that every motion requires a prior cause. What is the specific 'prior motion' that started one of your recurring sinful patterns? Will you trace it back today and confront it head-on?
?
The objector thought the world could be in eternal motion without a starting point. Are there areas in your spiritual life where you are waiting for change without taking the 'first motion'? What is one specific action you can take today to break that stasis, knowing that change must begin somewhere?
?
The opponent's statement that motion requires priority implies that nothing moves without a cause. Is your faith in Allah a first cause that moves your actions, or are you content with being moved by worldly influences? Commit to examining one decision today and ensuring its first cause is your love for Allah.

Aggregate as Free Act vs. Necessary Cause with Conditions
Objection and response about the cause of the world's events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the aggregate of events is an act of a free agent and the response that it could be due to a necessary cause operating with conditions.

Core Idea
A necessary cause can produce effects non-immediately if conditions are fulfilled.

This lesson examines whether the world's events are necessarily caused by a free agent or by a necessary cause that operates through conditions. The objector denies that the aggregate is an act of a free agent, arguing instead for a necessary cause where each event's priority is a condition for the next. This opens the possibility of an eternal world with temporal events.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The second aspect: the aggregate is claimed to be an act of a free agent with a first event. The objector denies this.
2
Understand the alternative explanation
The objector proposes that a necessary cause may produce effects conditionally: each preceding event is a condition for the next.
This avoids the need for a first event.
3
Reflect on the implication
If conditions can delay the effect, then an eternal cause could produce temporal events without a beginning.
This is a crucial argument for those who accept the eternity of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Temporality of Free Action and Eternal Rest
Objections and responses on temporality and rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments that free action does not necessarily imply temporality and that the world could have been at rest eternally.

Core Idea
A free agent's act could be eternal, and eternal rest is possible.

This lesson continues the debate by granting that the world is an act of a free agent but denying that this implies a temporal beginning. The objector argues that the free agent's power could be eternal, so the effect could be eternal. Finally, he asks why the world could not have been eternally at rest, challenging the assumption that rest requires a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection on temporality
Granting free agency, the objector denies that it must be temporal.
2
Understand the argument for eternal possibility
The objector says that if both the event and the agent's power are possible eternally, then the effect could be eternal.
The Arabic is unclear but the gist is that eternal possibility allows eternal actuality.
3
Examine the objection on rest
Finally, the objector asks why bodies could not have been eternally at rest, implying that rest is not impossible.

Source Text

Non-Existence as a Condition: Answering the Objection on Beginningless Events
Rebuttal of an objection about non-existence as a condition for existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how non-existence can be a condition for existence without being a cause, resolving an objection about events without a beginning.

Core Idea
Non-existence can be a condition, not a cause, for existence.

This passage tackles a subtle objection: if the non-existence of a prior event cannot cause a later event, how can we explain the dependency? The answer lies in distinguishing between a cause (ʿillah) and a condition (sharṭ). The author uses everyday examples to show that non-existence often serves as a condition, not a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and rebuttal
Read the passage carefully. First, understand the objection that non-existence cannot be a cause. Then, follow the rebuttal that distinguishes condition from cause.
Notice the key Arabic terms: تعليل (causation) and اشتراط (conditioning).
2
Identify the key distinction
The passage argues that while non-existence cannot cause existence, it can be a condition. Understand why this distinction matters in theological reasoning.
3
Examine the examples
Study the two examples: absence of clouds for sunlight, and absence of oiliness for dyeing. How do these illustrate the role of non-existence as a condition?
Reflect on how these examples relate to the original objection about beginningless events.
4
Connect to broader theology
Consider how this distinction preserves the principle that only existence can cause existence, while allowing non-existence to be a necessary condition.
This connects to discussions about divine attributes and the temporality of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حوادث لا أول لها فقد تقدم إبطاله لكنه قال قبل ذلك في المسألة التي ذكر فيها أن مدير العالم واجب الوجود هكذا حال حدوثه ذلك السابق لم يكن النديم مؤثرا بالفعل فى الحادث اللاحق وعند فنائه يصير مؤثرا فيه بالفعل فتلك المؤثرية حكم حادث ولا بد له من مؤثر ، إن كان هو الحادث الذي عدم الآن لزم تحليل الوجود بالعدم وهو محال ، فيقال له لم لا يجوز أن يكون عدم السابق بعد وجوده شرطا لوجود اللاحق ، ولا يلزم من امتناع تعليل الوجود بالعدم امتناع اشتراط الوجود بالعدم، فإن عدم الغيم شرط فى إضاءة وجه الأرض من الشمس وعدم الدسومة شرط في الصباغ الثوب من الصبغ .
As for events with no beginning (ḥawādith lā awwal lahā), its refutation has already preceded. But he said before that in the issue in which he mentioned that the manager of the world is necessarily existent, thus the case of its origination: that prior was not effectively influencing the subsequent event, and upon its perishing it becomes effectively influencing it. So that influencing is an originated ruling and must have an influencer. If it is the event that has now ceased, then analysis of existence by non-existence would follow, which is impossible. So it is said to him: Why is it not permissible that the non-existence of the prior after its existence be a condition (sharṭ) for the existence of the subsequent? And from the impossibility of causing existence by non-existence, it does not follow that it is impossible to condition existence by non-existence. For the absence of clouds is a condition for the illumination of the earth's surface by the sun, and the absence of oiliness is a condition for the dyeing of the cloth by the dye.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection related to events without a beginning. The author previously argued that the non-existence of a prior event cannot cause a subsequent one. The rebuttal points out that non-existence can serve as a condition (sharṭ) without being a cause (ʿillah). The example given is that the absence of clouds is a condition for the sun's light to brighten the earth, and the absence of oiliness is a condition for dye to color cloth. Thus, it is possible for the non-existence of a prior event to be a condition for the existence of a later one, without implying causation from non-existence.

شرط
تعليل
اشتراط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That non-existence cannot be involved in any way in the existence of something else.
Correction: Non-existence can be a condition for existence without being a cause, as shown by natural examples.

Checkpoint

B) The absence of motion for rest
C) The absence of a cause for an effect
D) The absence of knowledge for ignorance

Key Takeaway
Non-existence can serve as a condition (sharṭ) for existence without being a cause (ʿillah), as seen in natural examples like the absence of clouds enabling sunlight.

?
The objection in the lesson assumed that non-existence cannot have any positive role. Reflect: In your reaction to trials, have you ever said, 'Nothing good can come from this loss'? How does this lesson’s distinction between cause and condition challenge that assumption? What would it mean to realign your heart with that truth?

How can you begin to practice that acceptance today?

The Need for a Free Agent: Individual vs Aggregate
Razi critiques the claim that every motion needs a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that the need for a free agent is not self-evident for collective motion, even if it holds for individual motions.

Core Idea
Collective motion may not need a free agent.

In this lesson, we examine Imam Razi's critique of the claim that every motion requires a free agent. He points out that even if individual motions do, the collective or species of motion may not, and the argument presented lacks proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation. Notice the objector's claim and Razi's response.
The passage is from Imam Razi's refutation.
2
Identify the objector's claim
Find where the objector says every motion needs a free agent. Razi says it's not self-evident and lacks evidence.
3
Understand Razi's distinction
Razi grants that individual motions might need a free agent, but for the aggregate or species, the argument by delay does not apply because they have not been proven to be delayed.
This is the key logical move.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this distinction matters for the debate on eternal motion: even if each particular motion needs a cause, the whole series might be explicable without a free agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله في الوجه الثاني من بيان امتناع كون الحركة أزلية أن كل الحركات محتاج إلى موجد مختار فغير بين بنفسه ولم يورد عليه دليلا. وقد يلوح من كلامه عند الاعتراض عليه أنه إنما قيد [؟] موجد مختار لتخلف الحركة عنه وامتناع تخلف المعلول عن العلة الموجبة، ولكنه لو سلم له هذا المسلك في كل واحد من الحركات، أما المجموع والنوع فلم يثبت كونهما متخلفين عن مؤثرهما حتى يسوغ له الدلالة بالتخلف على كون الموجد مختارا. وقد أحال في الجواب بيان امتناع كون الموجد واجبا وكون كل سابق شرطا لحصول اللاحق إلى باب إثبات القادر، وفي ذلك لم يزد على قوله: وأما حوادث لا أول لها فقد تقدم إبطاله. لكنه قال قبل ذلك في المسألة التي ذكر فيها أن مدير العالم واجب الوجود هكذا حال حدوثه: ذلك السابق لم يكن المؤثر بالفعل في الحادث اللاحق، وعند فنائه يصير مؤثرا فيه بالفعل، فتلك المؤثرية حكم حادث ولا بد له من مؤثر، إن كان هو الحادث الذي عدم الآن لزم تحليل الوجود بالعدم وهو محال. فيقال له: لم لا يجوز أن يكون عدم السابق بعد وجوده شرطا لوجود اللاحق، ولا يلزم من امتناع تعليل الوجود بالعدم امتناع اشتراط الوجود بالعدم، فإن عدم الغيم شرط في إضاءة وجه الأرض من الشمس وعدم الدسومة شرط في صباغ الثوب من الصبغ.
As for his statement in the second aspect of explaining the impossibility of motion being eternal, that every motion is in need of a free agent, it is not self-evident and he did not bring evidence for it. And it appears from his speech when objecting to it that he only restricted [to] a free agent because of the delay of motion from it and the impossibility of the effect's delay from the necessitating cause. But if this path is granted to him for each individual motion, as for the aggregate and the species, they have not been proven to be delayed from their effect so that he may use the delay to indicate that the agent is free. And he referred in the answer the explanation of the impossibility of the agent being necessary and of every prior being a condition for the occurrence of the posterior to the chapter on establishing the powerful. And in that he added nothing to his statement: 'As for events without beginning, its refutation has preceded.' But he said before that in the issue in which he mentioned that the director of the world is necessary of existence, such is the state of its occurrence: that prior was not an agent in act for the later event, and at its annihilation it becomes an agent in act, so that agency is an accidental judgment and must have an agent; if it is the event that now ceased, it would necessitate the analysis of existence by non-existence, which is impossible. So it is said to him: Why is it not permissible that the non-existence of the prior after its existence be a condition for the existence of the posterior? And from the impossibility of explaining existence by non-existence does not follow the impossibility of conditioning existence by non-existence, for the non-existence of clouds is a condition for the illumination of the earth's face from the sun, and the non-existence of greasiness is a condition for the dyeing of the garment by the dye.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection regarding the second aspect of the impossibility of motion being eternal. The objector claimed that every motion requires a free agent, but Imam Razi argues this is not self-evident and lacks proof. Even if one accepts the need for a free agent for each individual motion, the aggregate or species of motion may not depend on such an agent in the same way. The objector then attempts to show that the agent must be free by appealing to the delay of the effect, but Razi points out that the collective motion has not been proven to be delayed. The objector refers to another discussion about the impossibility of the agent being necessary, but Razi counters with an example: the non-existence of a prior condition can be a condition for the existence of a subsequent event, without implying that non-existence causes existence. He illustrates with mundane examples: the absence of clouds is a condition for sunlight reaching the earth, and the absence of grease is a condition for dye penetrating cloth.

Key Terms
حركة
أزلية
موجد مختار
علة موجبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, why does the objector's argument about every motion needing a free agent fail for the collective motion?
A) Because collective motions are not delayed from their effect.
B) Because collective motions do not exist.
C) Because the free agent argument only applies to eternal motions.
D) Because the objector did not define motion.

Key Takeaway
Even if individual motions require a free agent, the aggregate or species may not, so the argument for a free agent is incomplete.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector assumed a free agent is needed because of delay, but Razi showed that this doesn't necessarily apply to the whole. Where in your life have you taken a limited truth about God and applied it too broadly? What would it mean to let God be free beyond your categories?
?
Razi points out that the objector's claim is not self-evident. Are there beliefs you hold about God that you've accepted without evidence, simply because they feel intuitive? Will you examine them today?
?
The argument for a free agent from delay works for individual events, but not for the whole system. Do you trust God's wisdom even when the big picture seems to lack the same structure you see in small events? Ask yourself: Am I willing to surrender my need for neat causality in the face of God's absolute sovereignty?

Conditioning by Non-Existence vs Causing by Non-Existence
Razi distinguishes conditioning by non-existence from causation by non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's distinction between something being a condition for existence and being a cause, especially when the condition is non-existence.

Core Idea
Non-existence can be a condition without being a cause.

In this lesson, we delve into Imam Razi's response to an objection about prior motions being conditions. He clarifies that the non-existence of a prior state can be a condition for the existence of a later state without implying that non-existence causes existence, using everyday examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Pay attention to the objection and Razi's counter-example.
2
Identify the objection
Find where the objector argues that the agency of the prior motion is a new accident requiring a cause, and Razi's response about conditioning.
3
Analyze Razi's examples
Razi gives two examples: absence of clouds conditions sunlight, and absence of grease conditions dyeing. Notice how they illustrate the logical point.
4
Understand the logical distinction
The key is that conditioning existence by non-existence is not the same as causing existence by non-existence. The former is acceptable, the latter impossible.

Source Text

Refuting the Argument from Eternal Rest
Imam Razi refutes the claim that eternal rest implies a necessitating cause leading to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the claim that eternal rest implies a necessitating cause leading to infinite regress.

Core Idea
Eternal rest does not force a regress unless conditions are unconditioned.

This lesson examines Imam Razi’s response to a key objection against the temporality of bodies. The objector argued that if rest is eternal, it requires a cause that leads to infinite regress. Razi shows that the objector’s reasoning depends on an unproven assumption about conditions and that the argument does not require bodies to be similar.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: the objector’s claim is stated first, then Razi’s two refutations.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two refutations
Razi gives two responses. First, he demands proof that conditions cannot be conditioned ad infinitum. Second, he dismisses the need to prove body similarity. Find these in the text.
The second refutation begins with 'وقوله: من أراد تعميم الدلالة...'.
3
Reflect on the logical structure
Consider why the objector's infinite regress fails if conditions can be chained. Also, think about why the proof against something that cannot be free from motion/rest does not require similarity of bodies.
This shows Razi’s method of refuting arguments without conceding unnecessary premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في الوجه الثاني: إن السكون إن كان أزليا ولم يكن واجبا لذاته افتقر إلى مؤثر موجب، والموجب إن لم يسكن تأثيره موقوفا على شرط امتنع زواله، وإن كان موقوفا على شرط فذلك الشرط إن كان واجبا امتنع زوال السكون وإن كان ممكنا عاد التقسيم. فيقال له: لا تسلم هذا بعد تسليم كون السكون ثبوتيا إلا بعد بيان امتناع كون كل شرط مشروطا بشرط آخر قبله، لا إلى أول ولم يوجد ذلك البيان في كلامك. وقوله: من أراد تعميم الدلالة فلا بد له من بيان مماثلة الأجسام ليس بوارد لأن الدليل إن صح دل على امتناع وجود مالا ينفك إما عن الحركة أو عن السكون سواء كان ذلك شيئا واحدا أو أشياء متماثلة أو مختلفة، ولو ثبت اتفاق الاتصافات بهما أزلا لشيء لا يخلو عنهما لثبت حدوث ذلك الشيء كيف ما كان.
And he said in the second aspect: 'If rest is eternal and not necessary by itself, it is in need of a necessitating effect; and if the necessitator does not have its effect suspended on a condition, then its removal is impossible; and if it is suspended on a condition, then that condition, if it is necessary, then the removal of the rest is impossible, and if it is possible, the division returns.' So it is said to him: You do not concede this after conceding that rest is affirmative except after explaining the impossibility of every condition being conditioned by another condition before it, without an end, and that explanation is not found in your speech. And his statement: 'Whoever wants to generalize the indication must explain the similarity of bodies' is not valid, because if the proof is valid, it indicates the impossibility of the existence of that which is not free from either motion or rest, whether that is one thing or similar things or different things; and if the agreement of the two descriptions in pre-eternity for something that cannot be free from them is established, then the occurrence of that thing would be established, however it is.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage refutes the second aspect of an argument concerning eternal rest. The objector argued that if rest is eternal and not necessary per se, it requires a necessitating cause. If the cause's effect is unconditional, the rest would never cease; if conditional, the condition leads to an infinite regress. Imam Razi responds that this reasoning only holds if one first proves that every condition cannot itself be conditioned ad infinitum. The objector failed to provide that proof. Furthermore, the objector's claim that one must prove the similarity of bodies to generalize the argument is rejected: the proof against something that cannot be free from motion or rest does not depend on the things being similar—it applies to anything that necessarily has one of these attributes. If it is established that something in pre-eternity has both motion and rest in agreement, then that thing would be temporal (i.e., created), regardless of its nature.

Key Terms
سكون
أزلي
موجب
شرط
مماثلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any eternal attribute must be necessary by itself or else leads to infinite regress.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that the infinite regress only follows if one assumes conditions cannot be conditioned ad infinitum without proof, and the argument does not require similarity of bodies.

Checkpoint
What does Imam Razi say the objector failed to prove in his argument about eternal rest?
A) That rest is an affirmative attribute.
B) That each condition is not itself conditioned without end.
C) That motion is impossible in pre-eternity.
D) That bodies are similar.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the eternal rest argument by pointing out its unproven assumption about conditions and by showing that the argument does not require similarity of bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief, have you ever stopped to examine whether your reasoning relies on an unproven assumption, just as the objector did with infinite regress? Take a moment to identify one such belief in your own life and commit to testing its foundation.
?
Imam Razi did not accept an argument just because it seemed logical; he demanded proof for every step. Is there an area of your faith or practice where you have accepted a claim without requiring solid evidence from revelation or reason? What would it cost you to actually verify it?
?
The objector wanted to impose a condition (similarity of bodies) to limit the proof. Are you quick to set conditions on divine matters—like 'God must act in this way'—without realizing you are limiting the Almighty? Repent of such presumption and submit to His absolute freedom.

The Four Causes and the Efficient Cause Proof
Imam Razi presents the philosophers' four causes and their proof from the efficient cause against the world's origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' four causes of everything originated and their argument from the efficient cause that the world cannot have originated.

Core Idea
Every originated thing needs four causes; the efficient cause leads to an impossible dilemma.

Imam Razi begins his critique of the philosophers by presenting their core causal framework. He states their axiom that every event requires four causes and then focuses on the first aspect — the efficient cause — to show how they argue against the world's temporal beginning. This passage sets up the philosophical challenge that later kalam arguments must answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: a general statement, then a specific argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the four causes
Find the four causes mentioned: الفاعل (efficient), المادة (material), الصورة (formal), الغاية (final). Understand that these are claimed to be necessary for everything originated.
3
Trace the efficient cause argument
The argument runs: if the world is originated, it has an eternal effect. Its origination at a specific time requires a preponderant (مرجح). Either there is a preponderant or not. Both options are deemed impossible, so the world cannot be originated.
This is a dilemma (syllogism).
4
Reflect on the dilemma
Consider why the philosophers reject both options. The first option (existence of a preponderant) is rejected because pure negation (the eternal agent's state before creation) cannot have distinction. The second option (no preponderant) is against the principle that a possibility cannot be preferred without a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفلاسفة فقد قالوا كل محدث فلا بدله من علل أربعة الفاعل والمادة والصورة والغاية ، قالوا ونحن نبين في هذه الجهات امتناع حدوث العالم . أما بالنظر إلى الفاعل فلان العالم لو كان محدثا لكان له مؤثر قديم ، فتخصيص إحداثه بالوقت الذي أحدثه فيه إما أن يكون لمرجح أولا المرجح ، والأول باطل لأن النفى المحض لا يعقل فيه الامتياز ، والثاني باطل لما سبق أن ترجح أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر من غير مرجح محال .
As for the philosophers, they have said: every originated thing must have four causes: the efficient, the material, the formal, and the final. They said: We will demonstrate in these aspects the impossibility of the world's origination. With respect to the efficient cause: If the world were originated, it would have an eternal effect, so the specification of its origination to the time in which it was originated would either have a preponderant or not. The first is false because pure negation admits no distinction. The second is false because it has been established that the preponderance of one of the two sides of a possibility over the other without a preponderant is impossible.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' initial claim and then focuses on the first of their four proofs against the world's having a beginning. Imam Razi begins by stating the philosophical axiom that every originated thing requires four causes: efficient (الفاعل), material (المادة), formal (الصورة), and final (الغاية). They then announce that they will demonstrate the impossibility of the world's being originated by examining each of these aspects. The first proof looks at the efficient cause: if the world were originated, it would need an eternal effect, i.e., an eternal creator. But then the fact that the creator brought it into existence at a specific time rather than another requires a preponderating factor (مرجح) that tips the scales. The philosophers argue that such a preponderant is impossible: either it exists (which would mean the creator had a reason to create at that time, but that would mean the creator's will is dependent on something external, which is impossible for an eternal being) or it does not exist (which would mean the occurrence of one possibility over another without any cause, which is impossible). Thus, they conclude that the world cannot have originated.

Key Terms
علل أربعة
الفاعل
المادة
الصورة
الغاية
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this philosophical argument proves that creation is impossible altogether.
Correction: The argument is specifically against temporal origination (huduth) based on the assumption of an eternal agent, not against creation per se. It is a challenge that kalam theologians later address.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what is the first aspect they examine to prove the impossibility of the world's origination?
A) The material cause
B) The efficient cause
C) The formal cause
D) The final cause

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that because the time of creation would require an impossible preponderant, the world cannot have originated — a challenge answered by kalam theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the philosophers' argument about why the world cannot have a beginning because it would require an impossible reason for the timing, do you feel your certainty about God's existence shaken? Are you willing to admit that your mind may not be able to fully comprehend God's eternal will, and yet still believe? What one step will you take this week to strengthen your trust in God's wisdom beyond your own logic?
?
The philosophers assumed that God's action must have a preponderant external to Him. Have you ever caught yourself expecting God to explain His decisions to you before you accept them, such as why He allowed a difficulty in your life at a particular time? Will you commit today to saying 'I believe in God's decree, good and bad alike' without demanding a reason?
?
If you were to debate a philosopher using this argument, you would need to understand the deeper assumption that the preponderant cannot be God's own eternal will. In your own spiritual life, are there areas where you rely on external signs or circumstances rather than the direct command of God? What is one thing you can do this week to act purely out of obedience, without needing a visible reason?

Refuting the Objection on Priority and Time
Imam Razi refutes the objection that priority of non-existence implies eternity of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the objection that priority of non-existence requires the eternity of time by distinguishing between intrinsic and extrinsic priority.

Core Idea
Priority does not always require time; only intrinsic priority does.

In this passage, Imam Razi addresses a subtle objection: if non-existence is prior to existence, doesn't that imply time is eternal? The opponent claims that just as some parts of time are prior to others, the priority of non-existence over existence would require time. Razi responds by showing a flaw in the analogy, clarifying the nature of priority and posteriority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument come through.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Find the line where the opponent says priority of non-existence implies eternity of time, likening it to priority of parts of time.
The opponent's claim is the starting point of the objection.
3
Understand Razi's response
Notice that Razi says priority and posteriority are intrinsic to time, but not to non-existence and existence. Therefore, the priority of non-existence needs a time medium.
This is the key distinction: intrinsic vs. extrinsic priority.
4
Contrast with parts of time
See how parts of time have priority and posteriority built into their concepts, so they don't need an external time.
This contrast shows why the analogy fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله فى الجواب عن الوجه الثانى من المنافضة : وهو أن سبق عدم الجسم على وجوده يقتضى قدم الزمان أن ذلك كنقدم بعض أجزاء الزمان على بعضن ليس وارد عند خصمه ، لأنه يقول التقدم والتأخر يلحقان للزمان اذاته وغيره به فتقدم العدم على الوجود محتاج إلى زمان يقعان فيه لعدم دخول الزمان المقتضى للتقدم وللتأخر فى مفهومهما ، وأما بعض أجزاء الزمان فيتقدم على البعض الآخر لكون التقدم والتأخر داخلين في مفهوم هما .
And his statement in the answer concerning the second face of contradiction: that the priority of the non-existence of a body over its existence necessitates the eternity of time — that this is like the priority of some parts of time over others — is not valid according to his opponent, because he says: priority and posteriority attach to time by itself and to other things through it. So the priority of non-existence over existence needs a time in which they both occur, because time which implies priority and posteriority is not included in their concepts. As for some parts of time, they are prior to other parts because priority and posteriority are included in their concepts.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage deals with the second face of contradiction: the opponent argued that the priority of a body's non-existence over its existence would require the eternity of time, just as some parts of time are prior to others. Imam Razi reports the response: this analogy is not valid according to the opponent's own principles. The opponent holds that priority and posteriority are intrinsic to time (by itself) and only apply to other things through time. Therefore, the priority of non-existence over existence would need a time in which both the non-existence and existence occur, because time itself is not part of the concept of priority and posteriority for non-existence and existence. In contrast, for parts of time, priority and posteriority are built into their concepts. Hence, the objection fails.

Key Terms
سبق
تقدم
تأخر
زمان
عدم
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any priority requires time.
Correction: Only priority that is intrinsic to time requires time; other priorities (like non-existence over existence) need time as a medium, not as an intrinsic property.

Checkpoint
According to the opponent's view as reported in this passage, what is required for the priority of non-existence over existence?
A) The eternity of time
B) A time in which both non-existence and existence occur
C) The priority of parts of time
D) No time is needed

Key Takeaway
The priority of non-existence over existence does not imply the eternity of time because priority and posteriority are not intrinsic to non-existence or existence but require time as a medium.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's existence, do you unconsciously imagine a timeline where He 'exists' after a period of non-existence? How does this passage challenge that subtle assumption, and what would it mean for your tawheed if you truly believed that time is a created thing that does not apply to Allah?
?
In your daily life, you treat time as a container for your actions—rushing, waiting, planning. But if priority and posteriority are not intrinsic to non-existence and existence, what does that say about the ultimate reality of time? How should this reshape your attachment to worldly deadlines and your sense of urgency in worship?
?
The opponent's error was drawing a false analogy between time parts and existence. Where in your own thinking do you take a concept that belongs to creation (like time, causality, or change) and wrongly apply it to Allah? What step will you take this week to actively correct that analogy in your heart?

Refuting the Objection on Motion and Rest Outside Place
Imam Razi refutes the objection that the world cannot be in motion or rest since it is not in a place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the claim that the world cannot be in motion or rest because it is not in a place, by exposing the flaws in the opponents' definition of motion and rest.

Core Idea
Motion and rest depend on definition, not on being in a place.

Imam Razi responds to an objection that since the world is not located in any place, it cannot be described as moving or at rest. The opponents propose a novel definition based on contact and separation of substances. Razi shows that this definition leads to absurd implications and ultimately invalidates the original argument. He then recalls the proper interpretation of motion and rest as 'coming-to-be' (al-husul).

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the beginning where the objection is stated: the world is not in a place, so it cannot be in motion or at rest.
This is the claim that Razi will refute.
2
Examine the opponents' definition
Notice how they define motion and rest in terms of contact and separation of two substances.
They define rest as remaining in contact, motion as separation.
3
Follow Razi's first criticism
Razi argues that this definition is new and unhelpful, and implies that a single body cannot be moving or resting.
4
Follow Razi's second criticism
He points out that if a body moves, its parts remain in contact, so they would be at rest, leading to a contradiction.
5
Follow Razi's third criticism and conclusion
Since the world encompasses all bodies, with no external body, it cannot be moving or resting even if its parts are. The proof collapses. Razi reminds that motion and rest were properly interpreted as 'al-husul'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله في الجواب عن الاعتراض الذى بعده : وهو أن العالم ليس في مكان فلا يكون متحركا ولا ساكنا فإنا إذا فرضنا جوهرين متماسين عنينا بالسكون بقائهما على ذلك الوجه وبالحركة . والهما عنه ، تفسير جديد للحركة والسكون بما لا يفيده ، وذلك القول يقتضى أن الجسم الواحد لا يكون متحركا ولا ساكنا، وأيضا أن الجسم إذا تحرك كانت أجزاؤه ساكنة لبقائها على المماسة وأيضا لما كان العالم عبارة عن جميع الأجسام ولا يمكن أن يكون معه جسم آخر فلا يكون متحركا ولا ما كنا ، وإن كانت أجزاؤه متحركة وساكنة وحينئذ بطل أصل الدليل ومن قبل فسر الحركة والسكون بالحصول .
And his statement in the answer concerning the objection that follows: that the world is not in a place, so it cannot be in motion nor at rest. For if we suppose two substances in contact, we mean by rest their remaining in that state, and by motion their separation from it. This is a new interpretation of motion and rest which does not benefit. And that statement implies that a single body cannot be in motion nor at rest; also that if a body moves, its parts are at rest because they remain in contact; also since the world consists of all bodies and no other body can be with it, it cannot be in motion nor at rest, even if its parts are in motion and at rest. In that case the basis of the proof is invalid, and previously motion and rest were interpreted as al-husul (coming-to-be).
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection that because the world is not in a place, it cannot be described as in motion or at rest. The opponent defines motion and rest in terms of contact and separation of two substances: rest is their remaining in contact, motion is their separation. Imam Razi criticizes this as a novel definition that is unhelpful. He points out the absurd consequences: a single body would be neither moving nor at rest; if a body moves, its parts remain in contact so they would be at rest; and since the world encompasses all bodies, with no external body, it cannot be moving or resting even if its parts are. This undermines the original proof. He reminds that the earlier accepted interpretation of motion and rest was 'al-husul' (attainment/coming-to-be), not this new one.

Key Terms

سكون
مكان
جوهر
متماس
حصول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that motion and rest always require being in a place, so the world cannot move or rest.
Correction: The text shows that the definition of motion and rest matters. The opponents' definition fails, but the correct definition (al-husul) allows for motion and rest without a place.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why can the world not be in motion or at rest?
A) Because it is not a body
B) Because it is not in a place
C) Because it has no parts
D) Because it is eternal

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi refutes the objection by showing that the opponents' definition of motion and rest leads to absurd consequences, and recalls that the proper understanding of motion and rest is based on 'al-husul' (coming-to-be), not on contact and separation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define key concepts in your own life—like success, justice, or faith—do you ever impose definitions that lead to contradictions, just as the opponents did with motion and rest? Where might your own definitions be preventing you from seeing the truth?
?
Razi points out that the opponents' definition, if applied consistently, would make even a single body neither moving nor resting. Are there beliefs you hold that, when applied consistently, would make your own existence or actions impossible? Will you examine them today?
?
The correct definition of motion and rest was already established as 'coming-to-be.' Yet the opponents introduced a new definition out of nowhere. Do you ever reinterpret clear truths to fit your own arguments? What does that reveal about your intentions?

The Void Objection and the Possibility of the World
Imam Razi refutes an objection and introduces the place argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's response to the objection that something cannot exist in a non-existent void, and the initial framing of the place argument.

Core Idea
A body can be in a void; place does not require an extended substance.

This lesson examines an objection against the possibility of the world: how can something exist in non-existence? Imam Razi dismisses it and begins to analyze the relationship between the world and place.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the opponent's objection and Imam Razi's refutation.
The Arabic is corrupt in places, but the core argument is clear.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Understand why the opponent thinks existence in a void is impossible and how Imam Razi counters that a body can be in a void.
Void here means a non-existent place.
3
Note the lacuna
The text mentions missing words about the impossibility of place being a state, indicating a gap in the manuscripts.
This shows the challenges in textual transmission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله في تحقيق إمكان العالم إنه إما أن يكون معدوما أو موجودا ثم اعترض بأن الخيز لوكا، تدميا كان الموجود في المعدوم، وادعى أن ذلك محال ، واعتراضه ذلك باطل لأن ذلك يقتضى كون الجسم في مكان هو أمر عدى وليس ذلك بممتنع ، وقد وقع ههنا فى النسخ التى وقعت إلينا ترك ذكر امتناع كون المكان حالا فكأنه قال يمتنع أن يكون ذلك المتحيز غير العالم لأنه حينئذ يجوز أن يكون داخلا لامتناع كون المكان داخل الممكن ، ولا يجوز أن يكون خارجا لأن خارج العالم لا متحيز وممتنع أن يكون ذلك المتحيز هو العالم لاقتضاء الدور ، فإن العالم يكون فيه وهو في العالم . في متحيز وجوابه : أن الدور يلزم لو كانت لفظة «فى، بمعنى واحد لكنها مهنا تدل بالاشتراك على شغل الحيز وعلى القيام بالمحل فلا يلزم الدور قوله : لو كان المكان جسما لصح عليه الحركة ويكون له مكان آخر ويلزم منه وجود أجسام لا نهاية لها ، ليس بصحيح لأن اللازم منه إما الانتهاء إلى جسم لا يصح عليه الحركة أو وجود أجسام لانهاية لها .
And his statement concerning the verification of the world's possibility: it is either non-existent or existent. Then he objected that the wood ??? was existing in the non-existent, and claimed that is impossible. But his objection is invalid because that requires the body to be in a place which is a non-existent thing, and that is not impossible. And here in the copies that have come to us, the mention of the impossibility of place being a state is omitted, as if he said: it is impossible that that which is extended (al-mutahayyiz) is other than the world, because then it would be possible for it to be inside, due to the impossibility of place being inside the possible, and it cannot be outside because outside the world there is no extended thing; and it is impossible that the extended thing is the world because it would necessitate circularity: the world is in it and it is in the world. And its answer: the circularity would follow if the word 'fi' (in) had a single meaning, but here it indicates by homonymy both occupying space and subsisting in a substrate, so circularity does not follow. His statement: if place were a body, motion would be possible for it and it would have another place, necessitating infinite bodies, is not correct because what follows is either ending at a body that cannot have motion or existence of infinite bodies.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is dense and partially corrupt. It begins with an opponent's argument about verifying the possibility of the world: it is either existent or non-existent. The opponent objects that something like wood cannot exist in a non-existent (possibly meaning a void), and claims that is impossible. Imam Razi dismisses this because a body can be in a place that is non-existent (like a void), which is not impossible. The text then notes a lacuna in the manuscripts about the impossibility of place being a state (حال). The argument proceeds: it is impossible for the extended thing (the world) to be other than the world, because if it were inside, place would be inside the possible (which is impossible); if outside, there is no extended thing outside; and if it is the world itself, that leads to circularity (the world is in itself). The response to the circularity objection is that the word 'fi' (في) has multiple meanings: it can mean 'occupying space' (شغل الحيز) or 'subsisting in a substrate' (القيام بالمحل). Therefore, the world's being in place and place being in the world are not the same relation. Finally, there is a refutation of the claim that if place were a body, it would lead to an infinite regress: either the regress ends at a body that cannot move, or there are infinite bodies, neither of which is necessarily false.

Key Terms
إمكان
مكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A body cannot exist in a void because a void is non-existent.
Correction: Imam Razi argues that a body can be in a non-existent place like a void; this is not rationally impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi consider the opponent's objection invalid?
A) Because the wood is actually in a place that is existent
B) Because a body can be in a non-existent place
C) Because the world is not possible
D) Because the opponent misread the text

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi refutes the objection that a body cannot exist in a void by affirming that a body can occupy a non-existent place without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The opponent assumed that a void cannot contain a body. What assumption about the nature of reality—something you've taken for granted—might actually be false? How would questioning that assumption change your view of Allah's absolute power?

?
Imam Razi did not accept the opponent's objection at face value. When you encounter doubts about your faith, do you passively accept them or actively seek out refutations? Write down one doubt you have and why you have not yet challenged it.

The Place Argument and the Homonymy of 'Fi'
Imam Razi resolves the circularity of place using homonymy and refutes infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's argument about the relationship between the world and place, the circularity objection, and its solution via the multiple meanings of 'fi'.

Core Idea
The word 'fi' has different meanings, avoiding circularity between the world and place.

This lesson continues the discussion on place. The argument considers whether the extended thing can be other than the world, leading to a paradox: if it is the world, then the world is in itself. Imam Razi resolves this by noting that 'fi' can mean either occupying space or subsisting in a substrate. Finally, he refutes the infinite regress objection.

Guided Path
1
Understand the options for the extended thing
The passage presents three possibilities for the extended thing: other than the world (inside or outside) or the world itself, and shows problems with each.
The inside option is impossible because place cannot be inside the possible.
2
Analyze the circularity objection
If the extended thing is the world, then the world is in itself, leading to circularity. Understand why this is a problem.
Circularity (دور) is a logical fallacy.
3
Learn the homonymy solution
The answer distinguishes two meanings of 'fi': occupying space and subsisting in a substrate. The world occupies its place and place subsists in the world, so no circularity.
This is a key terminological distinction in kalam.
4
Examine the infinite regress refutation
The opponent claims that if place is a body, it would lead to infinite bodies. Imam Razi says the regress either ends at an immovable body or goes on infinitely, neither of which is impossible.
Both options are rationally possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And his statement concerning the verification of the world's possibility: it is either non-existent or existent. Then he objected that the wood ??? was existing in the non-existent, and claimed that is impossible. But his objection is invalid because that requires the body to be in a place which is a non-existent thing, and that is not impossible. Commentary
This passage is dense and partially corrupt. It begins with an opponent's argument about verifying the possibility of the world: it is either existent or non-existent. The opponent objects that something like wood cannot exist in a non-existent (possibly meaning a void), and claims that is impossible. Imam Razi dismisses this because a body can be in a place that is non-existent (like a void), which is not impossible. The text then notes a lacuna in the manuscripts about the impossibility of place being a state (حال). The argument proceeds: it is impossible for the extended thing (the world) to be other than the world, because if it were inside, place would be inside the possible (which is impossible); if outside, there is no extended thing outside; and if it is the world itself, that leads to circularity (the world is in itself). The response to the circularity objection is that the word 'fi' (في) has multiple meanings: it can mean 'occupying space' (شغل الحيز) or 'subsisting in a substrate' (القيام بالمحل). Therefore, the world's being in place and place being in the world are not the same relation. Finally, there is a refutation of the claim that if place were a body, it would lead to an infinite regress: either the regress ends at a body that cannot move, or there are infinite bodies, neither of which is necessarily false.

Key Terms
متحيز
مكان
حيز
دور
اشتراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The circularity in 'the world is in itself' is a logical contradiction that cannot be resolved.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that the word 'fi' is homonymous, so the two relations are different; no contradiction.

Checkpoint
How does Imam Razi resolve the circularity problem?
A) By denying that place exists
B) By saying the world is not extended
C) By noting that 'fi' has multiple meanings
D) By accepting the circularity as necessary

Key Takeaway
The circularity of the world being in itself is resolved by recognizing the dual meaning of 'fi', and the infinite regress argument against body-place is not decisive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a apparent contradiction in Islamic teachings, do you immediately assume it is a fatal flaw, or do you pause to examine whether key terms might have multiple meanings? What steps can you take to cultivate the latter habit, as Imam Razi demonstrates here?
?
In your own life, are there areas where you rely on ambiguous definitions to avoid dealing with a deeper truth? What would it cost you to clarify those terms honestly?
?
The argument about infinite regress is dismissed as not decisive. Is there an argument against the existence of God that you have dismissed too quickly without careful analysis? Would you be willing to examine it with the same rigor Imam Razi applies to this objection?

Philosophers' Proofs from Material, Formal, and Final Causes
Imam Razi presents the philosophers' proofs for the world's eternity from material, formal, and final causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three philosophical proofs for the eternity of the world based on material, formal, and final causes, and how each attempts to argue that the world cannot have a temporal beginning.

Core Idea
The philosophers use the concepts of matter, time, and purpose to argue the world must be eternal.

Imam Razi now presents the philosophers' remaining arguments against the world's temporal origination. These proofs draw on the material, formal, and final causes, aiming to show that the world cannot have a beginning. As you read, notice how each argument leads to either an infinite regress or a problematic conception of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three proofs
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Look for the three distinct arguments: material, formal, and final cause proofs.
Each proof begins with 'وأما بالنظر إلى' (as for the consideration of).
2
Examine the material cause argument
Understand why possibility (imkan) requires a stable subject (matter), and why that leads to the conclusion that matter must be eternal to avoid infinite regress.
The key clause is 'فإن كانت حادثة افتقرت إلى مادة أخرى ولزم التسلسل'.
3
Examine the formal cause argument
Analyze how the priority of non-existence over existence is an affirmative attribute that requires an infinite chain of originated things before any first originated thing.
Focus on the concept of 'قبلية' (priority) as a real attribute.
4
Examine the final cause argument
Consider the dilemma: if the originator is free, it needs a purpose (implying deficiency); if necessitating, the effect must be eternal. Both lead to the world's eternity.
This argument uses the disjunction 'إما مختارا وإما موجبا بالذات'.
5
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Remember that these are not conclusive proofs; they are arguments the philosophers raise, which Imam Razi will later refute.
Do not assume these arguments are accepted by the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بالنظر إلى المادة فلان كل محدث فقد كان قبل حدوته ممكنا والإمكان وصف ثبوتى في الممكن فيستدعى موصوفا ثابتا وذلك هو المادة ، ثم هي إن كانت حادثة افتقرت إلى مادة أخرى ولزم التسلسل وإلا لزم قدم المادة . وأما بالنظر إلى الصورة فلان الزمان لا يقبل العدم الزماني لأن كل محدث فعدمه سابق على وجوده فمفهوم ذلك السبق أمر مغاير للعدم ، لأن العدم قد يكون قبل وبعد والقبل لا يكون بعد ، وتلك القبلية صفة ثبوتية فقبل أول الحوادث حادث آخر والكلام فيه كما فى الأول فقبل كل حادث حادث لا إلى أول . وأما بالنظر إلى الغاية فهو أن موجد العالم إن كان مختارا فلابد له من غاية الإيجاد فكان مستكملا بذلك الإيجاد فكان ناقصا لذاته ، وإن لم يكن مختارا لكان موجبا لذاته فيلزم من قدمه قدم الأثر
As for the consideration of the material cause: every originated thing was possible before its origination, and possibility (imkan) is an affirmative attribute in the possible, so it requires a stable subject, which is matter (al-madda). Then, if it is originated, it would need another matter, leading to infinite regress; otherwise, matter would be eternal. As for the consideration of the formal cause: time does not admit temporal non-existence, because every originated thing has its non-existence prior to its existence, and the concept of that priority is something other than non-existence, for non-existence can be before and after, but 'before' is not 'after', and that priority is an affirmative attribute. So before the first of originated things there is another originated thing, and the same applies, so before every originated thing there is another originated thing without first. As for the consideration of the final cause: if the originator of the world is free (mukhtar), it must have a purpose for bringing into existence, so it would be completing itself by that bringing into existence, thus it would be deficient in itself; and if it is not free, it would be necessitating by essence, so from its eternity the effect's eternity would follow.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' three remaining proofs against the world's temporal origination, based on the material, formal, and final causes. First, considering the material cause: every originated thing before its origination is possible, and possibility is an affirmative attribute (وصف ثبوتي) that requires a subject. This subject is matter. If matter itself is originated, it would need another matter, leading to an infinite regress; if not, matter is eternal. Thus, the world's origination requires eternal matter. Second, considering the formal cause: time does not admit temporal non-existence because for every originated thing, its non-existence is prior to its existence. This priority is a real attribute distinct from non-existence. Since 'before' and 'after' are real relations, there must be another originated thing before the first originated thing, leading to an infinite sequence with no first. Third, considering the final cause: the world's originator is either free or necessitating. If free, it must have a purpose, implying that it is completing itself through creation, hence deficient in itself. If necessitating, its eternity necessitates the eternity of its effect, so the world is eternal. In both cases, the world cannot have a beginning.

Key Terms
مادة
صورة
غاية
إمكان
وصف ثبوتي
سبق
غاية الإيجاد
موجب بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These proofs conclusively demonstrate the world is eternal.
Correction: These are arguments raised by the philosophers; Imam Razi will refute them. They are not presented as accepted truth.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' argument from the material cause, what would happen if matter were originated?
A) It would be eternal
B) It would need another matter, leading to infinite regress
C) It would be impossible
D) It would be self-subsistent

Key Takeaway
The philosophers attempt to prove the world's eternity by arguing that otherwise there would be infinite regress in matter, time, or a deficient conception of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
When you consider your own existence as a 'possible' thing, do you feel the weight of contingency? If your existence depends entirely on Allah at every moment, how should that change the way you approach your next decision?

Objection on Regress of Matters and Its Resolution
Imam Razi presents the second objection regarding the need for a matter preceding each originated thing and gives the correct answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second objection about an infinite regress of matters is invalid and how distinguishing between logical possibility and physical preparedness resolves it.

Core Idea
Not all possibility is the same; logical possibility needs no locus, ending the regress.

In this passage, Imam Razi tackles a key objection: if every originated thing requires a preceding matter, doesn't that lead to an infinite chain? He first gives a preliminary answer, then offers the definitive resolution by distinguishing two types of possibility. This lesson will walk through that reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully: every originated thing needs a preceding matter, and if that matter is also originated, a regress follows.
This objection challenges the need for a material substrate.
2
Understand the preliminary answer
The preliminary answer states that possibility (imkan) is not a real existent attribute, so it does not need a locus. Also, the possibility of matter does not require another matter because there are two kinds of possibility.
This sets up the key distinction.
3
Grasp the distinction between logical possibility and physical preparedness
Logical possibility (imkan 'aqli) is a rational consideration of the quiddity's relation to existence. Physical preparedness (isti'dad) is an existent quality that requires a locus.
The first is not real, the second is real and needs a substrate.
4
See how the correct answer resolves the regress
For directly created things (ibda'), there is no prior preparedness; their possibility is understood only at existence. Thus the regress stops.
This is the final resolution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما التشكيك الثاني بأن كل محدث محتاج إلى مادة تسبقه وتكون محلا لإمكانه والمادة وإن حدثت احتاجت إلى مادة تسبقها والجواب عنه بأن الإمكان غير وجودى وأيضاً المادة ممكنة فيلزم أن يقول إمكانها بمادة أخرى ليس بوارد ، لأن الإمكان الذى محله الماهية غير الإمكان الذى محله المادة ، فإن الأول منهما أمر عقلى يعقل عند انتساب الماهية إلى وجودها والثانى عبارة عن الاستعداد و وهو استعداد وجود شيء يكون قبل وجود ذلك الشيء ويحتاج إلى محل لأنه عندهم عرض موجود من جنس الكيف . والجواب الصحيح : أن الأمور الإبداعية لا يتصور فيها استعداد يتقدم وجودها وإمكانها إنما يعقل عند وجودها وهو صفة لماهيتها التى لا توجد قبل وجودها
And as for the second objection: that every originated thing needs a matter that precedes it and is a locus for its possibility, and if the matter is originated, it needs a matter that precedes it. The answer to it is that possibility is not existent, and also the matter is possible, so it would follow that its possibility inheres in another matter; this is not valid, because the possibility whose locus is the quiddity (mahiyya) is different from the possibility whose locus is the matter. The first is a rational thing understood when the quiddity is related to its existence, and the second is an expression for preparedness (isti'dad), and it is a preparedness of existence of a thing that is before the existence of that thing and needs a locus, because according to them it is an existent accident of the genus of quality. And the correct answer: that in matters of direct creation (ibda'), preparedness does not precede their existence, and their possibility is only understood at their existence, and it is an attribute of their quiddity, which does not exist before their existence.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage addresses the second objection: if every originated thing needs a preceding matter to host its possibility, and that matter itself is originated, an infinite regress occurs. The author first gives a preliminary answer: possibility is not a real attribute, and even if matter is possible, its possibility does not require another matter because there are two types of possibility. The first type is logical possibility (imkan 'aqli) that pertains to the quiddity; the second is physical potential (isti'dad) which is an existing quality that needs a locus. The correct answer clarifies that for directly created things (ibda'), such as the world, there is no preceding preparedness; their possibility is a rational attribute of their quiddity that is understood only when they exist, not before.

Key Terms

إمكان
استعداد
ماهية
إبداع
كيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All possibility is the same; therefore an infinite regress is inevitable if every originated thing needs a matter.
Correction: Logical possibility is not a real attribute needing a locus; physical preparedness does, but only for originated things, not for directly created ones. Thus the regress does not apply to ibda'.

Checkpoint
According to the correct answer, why does the second objection not lead to an infinite regress?
A) Because possibility is a positive attribute that can inhere in the quiddity alone
B) Because directly created things have no prior preparedness; their possibility is rational and understood only at existence
C) Because matter is eternal
D) Because possibility is not needed

Key Takeaway
The correct answer to the regress objection is that directly created things have no prior preparedness; their possibility is a rational attribute of their quiddity, understood only at existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Third Objection: Precedence of Non-Existence Over Existence
Imam Razi presents the third objection about precedence of non-existence and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection to the claim that non-existence precedes existence and how Imam Razi refutes it by arguing that precedence is a mental consideration, not a real attribute.

Core Idea
The precedence of non-existence is a mental construct, not a real attribute.

This passage presents the third objection in a series about the temporal precedence of non-existence over existence. The objector argues that such precedence would require an intermediate originated thing. Imam Razi's response distinguishes between real and mental attributes, affirming that the precedence is only in the mind. This clarifies a subtle point about how we conceptualize time and existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note that the text is corrupt in the middle, so the translation is tentative.
The phrase 'ليس نفيد لاسم يسترفون' is likely corrupt; context suggests it means 'it does not benefit them to argue...'
2
Identify the objection
The objection claims that if non-existence precedes existence, that precedence itself would be an originated thing, leading to infinite regress. Find the key phrase: 'يقتضى وجود حادث مثل ذلك الحادث'.
3
Analyze the answer
The answer states that the precedence is not a positive (thubuti) attribute but mental (dhihni). This avoids the regress. The text then discusses a supposed time, but it is dismissed.
The word 'رمان' is likely a corruption of 'زمان' (time).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتشكيك الثالث : بأن سبق العدم على الوجود يقتضى وجود حادث مثل ذلك الحادث. والجواب بأن السابق ليس ثبوتياً أيضاً ليس نفيد لاسم يسترفون بأن ذلك السبق ذهنى يلزم من توهم القدم السابق إلا أنه وجود رمان عندهم يقع فيه العدم السابق والوجود المسبوق ، وهو لم يبطل ذلك
And the third objection: that the precedence of non-existence over existence necessitates the existence of an originated thing like that originated thing. And the answer is that the precedence is not positive; also it does not benefit [؟] they argue that that precedence is mental, and it follows from the supposition of a preceding eternity, except that it is [؟] time (zaman) according to them in which the preceding non-existence and the preceded existence occur, and it does not invalidate that.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This brief passage deals with the third objection: if non-existence precedes existence, then that precedence itself seems to require another originated thing (like a time). The answer is that the precedence is not a real attribute but a mental consideration. The text becomes unclear at the end, but the gist is that the objectors suppose a time in which non-existence and existence are situated, but this is not accepted. The author implies that the supposed precedence is merely conceptual and does not entail a real regress.

Correction: Imam Razi clarifies that the precedence is only a mental consideration (ذهني), not a real attribute, so no real container is required.

Refuting the Need for Pre-Existing Matter in Creation
Author refutes the claim that every temporal thing needs pre-existing matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that every originated thing requires pre-existing matter is invalid for divine creation, and how the concept of possibility differs between essence and physical matter.

Core Idea
Divine creation requires no pre-existing matter or preparedness.

The philosopher's objection that every new thing needs a pre-existing matter leads to an infinite regress. The author examines a flawed response and then provides the correct answer: for things created ex nihilo, possibility is not a real quality in a prior substrate, but is only an attribute conceived at the moment of existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the initial statement: every originated thing needs a matter preceding it and if matter is also originated, it needs further matter, leading to infinite regress.
This objection challenges creation from nothing.
2
Examine a flawed answer
Notice the author presents a flawed answer that tries to distinguish between two types of possibility, then shows why it is not sufficient.
The flawed answer claims impossibility of regress by differentiating possibilities.
3
Learn the correct answer
The correct answer is that for creative (ibdā'ī) things, no preparedness exists before them; their possibility is only conceived at the moment of existence.
4
Distinguish the two meanings of possibility
Understand the distinction between rational possibility (attached to essence) and physical preparedness (attached to matter).
This distinction is key to refuting the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما التشكيك الثاني بأن كل محدث محتاج إلى مادة تسبقه وتكون محلا لإمكانه والمادة وإن حدثت احتاجت إلى مادة تسبقها والجواب عنه بأن الإمكان غير وجودى وأيضاً المادة ممكنة فيلزم أن يقول إمكانها بمادة أخرى ليس بوارد ، لأن الإمكان الذى محله الماهية غير الإمكان الذى محله المادة ، فإن الأول منهما أمر عقلى يعقل عند انتساب الماهية إلى وجودها والثانى عبارة عن الاستعداد و وهو استعداد وجود شيء يكون قبل وجود ذلك الشيء ويحتاج إلى محل لأنه عندهم عرض موجود من جنس الكيف . والجواب الصحيح : أن الأمور الإبداعية لا يتصور فيها استعداد يتقدم وجودها وإمكانها إنما يعقل عند وجودها وهو صفة لماهيتها التى لا توجد قبل وجودها
As for the second doubt: that every originated thing needs a matter that precedes it and is a locus for its possibility, and if the matter itself originated it would need a matter that precedes it—the answer to it is that possibility (al-imkān) is not existent, and also matter is possible, so it would follow that one says its possibility needs another matter; this is not valid, because the possibility whose locus is the essence (al-māhiyya) is not the same as the possibility whose locus is matter. For the first of them is an intellectual thing, conceived when the essence is related to its existence, and the second is an expression for preparedness (al-isti'dād), which is a preparedness for the existence of something that is before the existence of that thing and needs a locus because according to them it is an existent accident of the genus of quality (al-kayf). And the correct answer: that in creative matters (al-umūr al-ibdā'iyya) no preparedness that precedes their existence is conceivable, and their possibility is only conceived at their existence, and it is an attribute of their essence that does not exist before their existence.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage handles the second objection: that every originated thing requires a pre-existing matter to serve as a substrate for its possibility, leading to an infinite regress. The author first refutes a flawed answer that claims possibility is non-existent and that matter's possibility does not need another matter because possibilities are different. He then gives the correct answer: Creative things (things brought into existence from nothing, ibdā'ī) have no preparedness that precedes their existence. Their possibility is not a real quality in a pre-existing substrate; it is only conceived at the moment of existence as an attribute of their essence, which itself is not prior to existence. Thus, no infinite regress of matter is required.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility (imkān) is a real attribute that requires a pre-existing substrate.
Correction: Possibility is an intellectual concept when related to essence, not a real quality; only physical preparedness requires a substrate, but divine creation involves no such preparedness.

Checkpoint

B) It is an intellectual concept conceived only at existence
C) It is a physical preparedness that precedes existence
D) It is eternal and uncreated

Key Takeaway
