Bodies Are Enduring (Baqa') Contrary to al-Nazzam
Al-Razi presents and defends the view that bodies endure, rebutting al-Nazzam's denial, with objections and responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational arguments for the endurance of bodies and the objections raised by al-Nazzam, including the limitations of sensory evidence.

Core Idea
Bodies endure because their possibility remains, but sensory continuity alone cannot prove it.

This passage from Imam al-Razi's work tackles the question of whether physical bodies continue to exist over time. Al-Razi defends the orthodox view that bodies are enduring (baqa'), rejecting al-Nazzam's denial. He presents a logical argument based on intrinsic possibility and addresses key objections, including the unreliability of sensory perception and the nature of identity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
The passage begins by stating the issue: bodies are enduring, contrary to al-Nazzam. Notice the initial argument: if a body can exist at one time, it can continue because its possibility doesn't turn into impossibility.
This is a key logical premise.
2
Examine the objection based on accidents
The author notes that the argument is refuted by the Ash'ari view that accidents do not endure. This introduces a tension.
The objection forces a deeper analysis.
3
Understand why sensory evidence is insufficient
Al-Razi explains that similar successive things can appear continuous to the senses, so we cannot rely on perception to prove endurance. The example of colors is given.
This is crucial for distinguishing between appearance and reality.
4
Analyze the identity argument
The claim 'I am the same as this morning' is based on the rational soul, not the body. Moreover, animal identity includes non-enduring accidents, so overall identity is not permanent.
This shows the complexity of identity and duration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: الاجسام باقية خلافا للنظام. انا أنه يصح وجودها في الزمان الأول فيصح فى الثانى لامتناع الانقلاب من الإمكان الذاتي إلى الامتناع الذاتي وهو منقوض أعلى قول أصحابنا بالأعراض، ولا يمكن الاعتماد فيه على الاستمرار في الحس لما عرفت أن عند تعاقب الأمثال يظنها الحسن واحدا مستمرا، لأنه منقوض بالألوان على قول أصحابها بالأعراض وما يقال أنا أعلم بالضروره أنى أنا الذى كنت بالبكرة فهو بناء على النفس الناطقة ولأن هوية الحيوان المعين ليست عبارة عن الجسم فقط بل لابد فيه من أعراض مخصوصة وهى غير باقية، وإذا كان أحد أجزاء الهوية غير باقية كانت الهوية غير باقية.
Issue: Bodies are enduring, contrary to al-Nazzam. [We argue] that their existence is valid in the first time, so it is valid in the second, due to the impossibility of changing from intrinsic possibility to intrinsic impossibility. But this is refuted by the statement of our companions regarding accidents. And one cannot rely on sensory continuity, because you have known that when similar things succeed one another, the sense supposes them to be one continuous thing. For it is refuted by colors according to the statement of those [companions] about accidents. And what is said, 'I know necessarily that I am the one who was in the morning' is based on the rational soul. And because the identity of a particular animal is not expressed by the body alone, but must involve specific accidents, and these are not enduring. When one part of the identity is not enduring, the identity is not enduring.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of whether bodies endure (baqa') over time, a position al-Razi attributes to his school against al-Nazzam who denies it. The initial argument: if a body exists in one moment, it can exist in the next because its possibility remains intrinsic and cannot turn into impossibility. However, this is objected by analogy with accidents (which do not endure) according to the Ash'ari position. Then the author dismisses reliance on sensory continuity because similar succeeding things appear continuous even if they are not (e.g., colors). The claim of self-identity over time ('I am the same as this morning') is grounded in the rational soul, not the body. Moreover, the identity of a living being includes non-enduring accidents, so the whole identity is not enduring. Thus al-Razi implicitly defends bodily endurance but acknowledges strong objections.

Key Terms
بقاء الأجسام
الإمكان الذاتي
الامتناع الذاتي
هوية الحيوان
النفس الناطقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sensory continuity proves that bodies endure without change.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that similar successive things can appear continuous to the senses even if they are replaced, so sensory continuity is not proof of endurance.

Checkpoint
Why cannot sensory continuity prove the endurance of bodies?
A) Because senses are always mistaken.
B) Because similar successive things can appear as one even if they change.
C) Because bodies are not perceived.
D) Because only the soul endures.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi defends bodily endurance through logical necessity, but acknowledges that sensory experience and identity claims rely on deeper principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often trust your senses to tell you what is real and stable—but al-Razi warns that sensory continuity can deceive. Where in your life are you treating a feeling of continuity (e.g., relationships, faith, identity) as proof of permanence, when in reality it might be a succession of fleeting moments? Will you examine that today?
?
Al-Razi says your identity is not just your body; it includes accidents that do not endure. If your body, emotions, and thoughts change constantly, what part of 'you' is truly constant before Allah? Are you living as if your eternal identity is your changing self, or are you turning your heart toward the unchanging soul?
?
The argument from possibility seems convincing, yet al-Razi himself notes it is refuted. When you rely on a rational proof for a belief, do you prepare yourself to encounter objections that challenge it? Or do you cling to the proof as if it were infallible? How would you respond if a trusted argument turned out to be insufficient?

Bodies Are Enduring: The Kalam Argument Against al-Nazzam
Razi argues for the endurance of bodies and refutes al-Nazzam's view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi argues for the endurance of bodies and why standard arguments face refutations.

Core Idea
Endurance is proven by possibility, but sensory continuity is unreliable.

Imam Razi presents the argument that bodies endure because they exist in the first moment and no intrinsic change prevents a second moment. However, he also notes that this argument is refuted by the Ash'arī position on accidents, and that sensory continuity is deceptive due to successive similars. This lesson walks through the reasoning and its critiques.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Razi states the issue and then immediately gives an argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main argument and refutations
Find the core argument: existence in first moment implies existence in second because possibility cannot turn to impossibility. Then note the refutations: the Ash'ari view on accidents, the illusion of sensory continuity, and the identity argument based on the rational soul.
Razi himself points out flaws in the argument.
3
Understand the role of identity
Consider why the claim 'I am the same as yesterday' does not prove body endurance, since identity includes non-enduring accidents. This ties the discussion to the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الاجسام باقية خلافا للنظام : انا أنه يصح وجودها في الزمان الأول فيصح فى الثانى لامتناع الانقلاب من الإمكان الذاتي إلى الامتناع الذاتي وهو منقوض أعلى قول أصحابنا بالأعراض، ولا يمكن الاعتماد فيه على الاستمرار في الحس لما عرفت أن عند تعاقب الأمثال يظنها الحسن واحدا مستمرا ، لأنه منقوض بالألوان على قول أصحابها بالأعراض وما يقال أنا أعلم بالضروره أنى أنا الذى كنت بالبكرة فهو بناء على النفس الناطقة ولأن هوية الحيوان المعين ليست عبارة عن الجسم فقط بل لابد فيه من أعراض مخصوصة وهى غير باقية ، وإذا كان أحد أجزاء الهوية غير باقية كانت الهوية غير باقية (٢) أقول : هذا النقل من النظام غير معتمد عليه وقال بعضهم إنه قال باحتياج الاجسام إلى المؤثر حال البقاء بذهب وهم النقلة إلى أنه لا يقول ببقائها ، والأولى دعوى الضرورة في بقاء الأجسام ، ولا ينتقض ذلك بما يورد عليه مما مر ذكره فى باب السفسطة ، وقيل إنه قال بذلك لأنه قال بأن الإعدام من المؤثر غير معقول وأنه لاحد الأجسام حتى يقولوا أنه ينتفى بطاريان الضد ولا يقول بثبوت المعدوم حال العدم ، ومذهبه أن الأجسام تنتى عند القسمة ، فلابد له من القول بأنها لا تبق كما قيل في الأعراض .
Issue: Bodies are enduring, contrary to al-Nazzam. [Argument]: We say that it is valid for them to exist in the first moment, so it is valid in the second, because a change from intrinsic possibility to intrinsic impossibility is impossible. But this is refuted by the statement of our companions regarding accidents. And one cannot rely on sensory continuity, because you know that with the succession of similars, the sense perceives them as one continuous thing, and this is refuted by colors according to those who hold the view of accidents. And what is said: 'I know by necessity that I am the one who was yesterday' is based on the rational soul. And because the identity of a particular animal is not merely the body, but must include specific accidents which are not enduring. So if one part of the identity is not enduring, the identity is not enduring. (2) I say: This report from al-Nazzam is not reliable, and some said that he held that bodies need an effecter in the state of endurance, but the transmitters mistakenly thought that he denied their endurance. The better course is to claim necessity in the endurance of bodies, and this is not refuted by what is objected to it from what was mentioned in the chapter on sophistry. And it was said that he held this because he said that non-existence from an effecter is inconceivable, and that bodies have no limit such that they say it is removed by the occurrence of a contrary, and he does not affirm the existence of a non-existent in the state of non-existence. His doctrine is that bodies cease [؟] at division, so he must say that they do not endure, as was said about accidents.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a mas'ala (doctrinal issue) on the endurance (baqā') of bodies, arguing against al-Nazzam. The author's argument: bodies exist in the first moment, and since there is no change from intrinsic possibility to intrinsic impossibility, they can exist in the second moment (i.e., they endure). However, the author then notes that this argument is refuted by the Ash'ari position on accidents. He also rejects reliance on sensory continuity because of the problem of successive similars. The claim of self-identity over time ('I know I am the same as yesterday') is based on the rational soul, not the body. The identity of an animal includes accidents that are not enduring, so the whole identity is not enduring. The marginal note (أقول) clarifies that the report from al-Nazzam may be unreliable; some say he actually held that bodies need an effecter for endurance, not that they perish. The commentator suggests claiming intuitive necessity for endurance and notes that if one denies that non-existence is caused, then bodies must be said to perish at division. The discussion highlights the complexity of the endurance debate and the importance of distinguishing the material body from its accidental identity.

الامتناع الذاتي
تعاقب الأمثال

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that sensory continuity proves endurance without addressing the problem of successive similars.
Correction: The passage explains that when similar things succeed each other, the senses perceive them as one continuous thing, so sensory continuity does not necessarily indicate true endurance.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author gives for doubting the argument from sensory continuity for endurance?
A) The senses are always unreliable.
B) Successive similars can give the illusion of continuity.
C) There is no first moment of existence.
D) The argument is refuted by colors.

Key Takeaway
The endurance of bodies is a rational possibility, but proving it requires careful argument that avoids the pitfalls of sensory illusion and identity composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on your senses to confirm the continuity of your existence, but this lesson shows that sensory perception can be deceived by successive similars. How does this affect your understanding of your accountability before Allah? Are you pinning your spiritual identity on something that will not last?
?
Al-Nazzam's view was misrepresented by transmitters. Have you ever accepted a critique of a belief without verifying its accuracy from the original source? Bodies Can Be Devoid of Accidents: Refuting Ash'ari Analogies
Razi argues for possibility of bodies devoid of accidents and refutes two analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends the possibility of bodies being devoid of accidents by refuting two key analogies from the Ash'ari school.

Core Idea
A body can lack accidents; Ash'ari analogies fail due to missing common principles and differing conditions.

Imam Razi presents a classic kalam argument against the Ash'ari position that bodies must have certain accidents. Using the example of air, he challenges the necessity of color, taste, and smell. He then dissects two analogies offered by the Ash'aris, showing their logical flaws. This lesson walks through these refutations, sharpening your understanding of analogical reasoning (qiyas) in theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation to grasp the flow. Note the two analogies mentioned.
Pay attention to the technical terms like 'al-kawn' and 'al-ittisaf'.
2
Identify the first analogy (color to location)
Find where the Ash'aris analogize color to al-kawn (location). Understand Razi's refutation: no common principle (jami').
The refutation is brief but key: 'الأول خال عن الجامع'.
3
Examine the second analogy (before/after qualification)
This analogy compares the state before acquiring an accident to after. Razi distinguishes: after qualification, removal requires a contrary; before, it does not.
The distinction hinges on the need for a contrary (ضد) after, but not before.
4
Understand the broader consensus
Both Ash'aris and Mu'tazila agree on impossibility after qualification, but for different reasons. Razi uses this to show the flaw in the analogy.
The marginal note clarifies al-Ash'ari's position on creation of similars.
5
Reflect on the conclusiveness
If the distinction holds, Ash'ari argument fails. Even if not, Razi challenges the consensus on impossibility after qualification.
This shows the depth of Razi's critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة الأجسام مجوز خلوها عن الألوان والطعوم والروائح خلافا لأصحابنا : لنا أن الهواء لا لون له ولا طعم له احتجوا بقياس اللون على السكون وبقياس ما قبل الانصاف على ما بعد والأول خال عن الجامع ، وأما الثاني فعندنا مجوز خلوه عما لا يبقى بعد الاتصاف بها ، وأما الباقي فهو لا ينتفى عن المحل إلا بضد يزيله، فإن صح هذا ظهر الفرق وإلا منعنا الحكم في الأصل (۲) أقول : لعل هذا من أبي الحسن الأشعرى ، وقيل لم يكن مراده ما فهم من شأنه أن يحس به من غير مانع يقتضى النفي وإلا لأدى إلى السفسطة ، وادعوا أن أبا الحسن قاس اللون على الكون يعنى لما امتنع خلو الجسم من الكون امتنع خلوه عن اللون قياسا عليه ، ومنع المصنف هذا القياس الخلو حكمى اللون والكون عن الجامع، وأيضاً انفق الفريقان أعى أبا الحسن والمعتزلة على امتناع خلو الجسم عن الأعراض التي هي قارة في الحس كالألوان لا التي غير قارة كالأصوات بعد انصافه بها، أما الأشعرى فلإجراء المادة بخلق أمثالها عقيب زوالها، وأما المعتزلة فلامتناع انتفائها من غير طريان الضد عليها فقاس أبو الحسن ما قبل الاتصاف على ما بعده وقال كما امتنع خلو الجسم عنها بعد الاتصاف امتنع خلوه عنها قبل الانصاف قياسا عليه ، فمنع المصنف هذا القياس بالفرق بين الصورتين وهو أن امتناع الخلو بعد الاتصاف موقوف على طريان الضد وقبل الانصاف ليس هكذا ، فإن صح هذا ظهر الفرق وإلا منعنا الحكم في الأصل وقلنا بجواز الخلو بعد الاتصاف أى خالفنا الاتفاق .
Issue: Bodies are possibly devoid of colors, tastes, and smells, contrary to our companions. Our evidence: air has no color and no taste. They argued by analogy of color with location (al-kawn) and by analogy of what is prior to qualification with what is after. The first [analogy] lacks a common principle. As for the second, we hold it possible for a body to be devoid of that which does not endure after being qualified with it. As for that which endures, it does not cease from the substrate except by a contrary that removes it. If this is valid, the difference appears; otherwise we deny the judgment in the original. (2) I say: This is perhaps from Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. It was said that his intent was not what was understood—that the thing can be sensed without a preventer that necessitates negation—otherwise it would lead to sophistry. They claimed that Abu al-Hasan analogized color to location: just as it is impossible for a body to be devoid of location, so it is impossible for it to be devoid of color. The author refuted this analogy because of the absence of a common principle [between color and location]. Also, both parties—Abu al-Hasan and the Mu'tazila—agree on the impossibility of a body being devoid of accidents that are settled in sense (like colors), not those that are not settled (like sounds), after being qualified with them. For al-Ash'ari, it is because matter continues by creating similars after their cessation; for the Mu'tazila, it is because they cannot cease without the occurrence of a contrary. So Abu al-Hasan analogized what is prior to qualification to what comes after, saying: just as it is impossible for the body to be devoid of them after qualification, so it is impossible for it to be devoid of them before qualification. The author refuted this analogy by distinguishing the two cases: the impossibility after qualification depends on the occurrence of a contrary, whereas before qualification it is not so. If this distinction is valid, the difference appears; otherwise we deny the judgment in the original and say that it is possible to be devoid even after qualification, i.e., we contradict the consensus.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a mas'ala arguing that bodies can be devoid of color, taste, and smell, contrary to the Ash'ari position. The proof is the simple observation that air lacks color and taste. The opponents use two analogies: (1) comparing color to location (al-kawn), and (2) comparing the state before qualification to after qualification. The author refutes the first analogy because there is no common principle between color and location. For the second analogy, he distinguishes: it is possible for a body to be devoid of accidents that do not endure after qualification; but for enduring accidents, they require a contrary to remove them. The marginal note clarifies that this may be from al-Ash'ari, and explains that al-Ash'ari's position is based on the continuity of matter through the creation of similars, while the Mu'tazila base it on the impossibility of cessation without a contrary. The author refutes the analogy by pointing out the difference in the two situations: after qualification, removal requires a contrary; before qualification, there is no such requirement. If this distinction stands, the Ash'ari argument fails.

Key Terms
الخلو عن الأعراض
السكون
الكون
القياس
الجامع
الاتصاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a body has an accident, it cannot be without it later without a cause, but they confuse the state before and after acquisition.
Correction: Razi clarifies that before acquisition, a body can be devoid; after acquisition, removal requires a contrary.

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw the author identifies in the Ash'ari analogy between before and after qualification?
A) There is no common principle between color and location.
B) After qualification, removal requires a contrary, but before it does not.
C) Air has no color, so the analogy is invalid.
D) The Ash'ari position leads to sophistry.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes two Ash'ari analogies by showing either lack of a common principle or a crucial difference in the conditions before and after qualification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend a belief by analogy, do you first verify that there is a true common principle between the things compared, or do you assume similarity based on surface resemblance? How will this lesson change your approach to evaluating your own arguments?
?
The Ash'aris and Mu'tazila agreed on the impossibility of a body being devoid of accidents after qualification, but for different reasons. Are there beliefs you hold that are widely accepted but for the wrong reasons? Will you examine the foundations of your convictions as Razi did, even if it means challenging consensus?
?
Razi was willing to contradict consensus when logic demanded it. Is your faith secure enough to follow evidence even when it seems to go against the majority? In what area of your life are you most afraid to think independently, and what would it take to overcome that fear?

Motion in a Medium vs. Void: Time is Essential to Motion
Razi refutes the claim that motion in void would be instantaneous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi refutes the claim that motion in a void would be instantaneous, affirming that time is inherent to motion itself.

Core Idea
Time is not accidental to motion; it is essential.

This short note (tanbih) tackles a subtle argument about motion, time, and the void. Some argued that if time were only due to resistance, then in a void motion would take zero time. Razi declares this false, affirming that time belongs to motion by its very nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a feel for the argument.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the Hypothetical Argument
Look for the 'if' clause: if motion's time were only due to an impediment, then in a void motion would be instantaneous.
This is the claim being refuted.
3
Understand Razi's Refutation
Razi states that this idea is known to be false. Why? Because time is essential to motion, not contingent on resistance.
Consider what it means for time to be inherent.
4
Connect to the Broader Debate
This refutation supports the realist view of time and motion against nominalist alternatives.
Reflect on implications for the existence of void.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه: الحركة في الملا الذى نسبة رقته إلى رقة الماء كنسبة زمان الحركة في الخلاء إلى زمانها في الماء إنما يقع لا في زمان إذا لم يكن استحقاقها الزمان لذاتها بل للعائق لكن ذلك معلوم الفساد
Note: Motion in a medium whose thickness is proportional to the thinness of water as the time of motion in void is to its time in water would only occur in no time if its entitlement to time were not by itself but due to an impediment, but that is known to be false.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a brief note (tanbih) refuting a hypothetical argument by those who deny the void. The argument posits that if motion in a medium is slower due to the medium's resistance, then in a medium as thin as water compared to an even thinner medium, motion speed would increase proportionally, eventually reaching zero time in a void if resistance were the only cause of time. Razi replies that motion intrinsically requires time, so even without resistance, motion takes time. The idea that time is only due to an impediment (the medium) is false. This refutes the nominalist view of time and defends the realist view that time is essential to motion.

Key Terms

الخلاء
الملا
العائق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion in a void would be instantaneous because there is no resistance.
Correction: Time is inherent to motion itself, so even without resistance, motion requires time.

Checkpoint

A) Motion in a void requires time.
B) Motion in a medium requires more time than in void.
C) Motion's time is only due to an impediment.
D) Motion is possible in a void.

Key Takeaway
Motion's time is inherent, not merely caused by an impediment; the void would not make motion instantaneous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
If motion in a void would still require time, then nothing in creation exists without duration. How does this truth affect your understanding of patience? Will you commit to not wishing away the time Allah has decreed for your steps?

Answering Objections to Annihilation
Al-Razi answers four objections about annihilation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author addresses four objections against the possibility of annihilation, distinguishing between the removal of a condition and direct divine action.

Core Idea
Annihilation can occur either by removal of a condition or by the agent's direct act.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi answers several objections raised against the possibility of annihilation. He explains that the permanent's persistence is due to a cause and that the permanent prevents the contingent from existing. He also refutes an argument based on the impossibility of two similar things by showing it leads to circularity. Finally, he affirms that the agent can directly annihilate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its English translation to get an overview of the four objections and answers.
Notice the concise style of argumentation.
2
Identify the four objections
List the four objections as the author addresses them: the first about the permanent's cause, the second about simultaneous existence and non-existence, the third about two similar things, and the fourth about annihilation by the agent.
The first three are answered collectively at the end.
3
Analyze the third objection's refutation
Focus on the argument that the removal of a condition leads to circularity between substance and accident, making it impossible. Understand why the condition must be an accident.
This refutation shows the logical necessity behind the position.
4
Reflect on the fourth objection
Contrast the first three objections with the fourth: the agent can directly annihilate without needing a condition. Consider how this preserves divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنا نجيب عن الأول بأنا بينا أن الباقى حال بقائه متعلق السبب، وعن الثانى أنا لا نقول الحادث يوجد ويعدم معا بل نقول الباقى يمنع الحادث من الدخول في الوجود، وعن الثالث أنه بناء على جواز اجتماع المثلين محال وإنما قلنا أنه لا يجوز أن يكون لانتفاء الشرط لأن ذلك الشرط لا يكون إلا العرض لأن الشرط الخارج عن ماهية التى، فيكون عرضا فيكون الجوهر محتاجا إلى العرض وكان العرض محتاجا إلى الجوهر فيلزم الدور وهو محال. والجواب عن الثلاثة الأول ما تقدم في مسألة الحدوث وعن الرابع أن نقول لم لا يجوز أن يعدم بإعدام الفاعل.
We answer the first [objection] by stating that we have shown that the permanent (al-bāqī) during its state of permanence is subject to a cause. As for the second [objection], we do not say that the contingent (al-ḥādith) exists and is annihilated simultaneously; rather, we say that the permanent prevents the contingent from entering into existence. As for the third [objection], it is based on the impossibility of the conjunction of two similar things; and we only said that it is not permissible [for it to be annihilated] due to the removal of the condition, for that condition is nothing but an accident (‘araḍ), because the condition external to the quiddity of the thing is an accident. Thus the substance (jawhar) would be in need of the accident, and the accident would be in need of the substance, leading to circularity (dawr), which is impossible. As for the answer to the first three [objections], it has already been presented in the question of origination (ḥudūth). As for the fourth [objection], we say: why is it not permissible that it be annihilated by an act of annihilation on the part of the agent (i‘dām al-fā‘il)?
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is an answer to four objections against the possibility of annihilation. The author addresses each: (1) The permanent during permanence is dependent on a cause, so it can cease. (2) The permanent prevents the contingent from existing; there is no simultaneous existence and non-existence. (3) The argument based on the impossibility of two similar things is rejected; the real reason is the removal of a condition, which is an accident, leading to circularity between substance and accident, which is impossible. (4) The agent can annihilate directly by an act of annihilation. The author notes that the answer to the first three is already in the discussion of ḥudūth.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Annihilation always requires a positive act of destruction.
Correction: Annihilation can also occur by the removal of a sustaining condition, as shown in the third objection's answer.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the permanent do with respect to the contingent?
A) It causes the contingent to exist.
B) It prevents the contingent from entering existence.
C) It is annihilated by the contingent.
D) It coexists with the contingent without interaction.

Key Takeaway
Annihilation can be understood either as the removal of a sustaining condition or as a direct act of the agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which areas of your life do you subtly assume that your continued existence depends on conditions (health, finances, relationships) rather than directly on God? How does al-Razi's argument that annihilation can occur by the agent's direct act change your perspective on where your true security lies? Will you consciously turn to God today as the ultimate sustainer, not the conditions?
?
Al-Razi shows that circular dependence—where each thing needs the other—is impossible and leads to absurdity. Are there any dependencies in your life that have become circular? For example, do you feel you cannot be happy without a certain person, while that person's happiness also depends on you? Reflect on how this creates an unstable cycle. Will you break that circle by relying on God alone?
?
If annihilation can happen by the mere removal of a condition, what is one condition you take for granted that, if removed, would deeply affect you? How prepared are you for that possibility? What single step can you take today to realign your heart so that your hope is not in that condition but in the One who can annihilate or preserve by His direct will?

Refuting Objections to Annihilation
Al-Razi refutes objections to annihilation via contrary, condition removal, and mutual dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi refutes multiple objections against the possibility of annihilation by proposing alternative mechanisms like occurrence of a contrary and removal of a condition.

Core Idea
Annihilation can occur without a positive act, through removal of conditions or occurrence of opposites.

Imam al-Razi tackles a series of objections that claim annihilation is impossible. The objector argues that annihilation would either require a new existent or no change at all, both problematic. In response, the author offers several alternative explanations, including the occurrence of a contrary and the removal of a necessary condition. This lesson traces his arguments and shows the sophistication of his occasionalist metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow of the debate. Note the structure of objection and response.
The passage is dense; don't worry about details yet.
2
Identify the initial objection
Find the objector's argument that annihilation cannot be a positive or non-positive act. See how the author concedes but proposes an alternative.
3
Trace the responses to further objections
Follow the author's replies to the dependence and opposition objections. Notice how he reverses causation and appeals to strength.
These show the occasionalist framework.
4
Examine the removal-of-condition argument
Understand how the author uses the indivisibility of substance and accident to show that non-creation of an accident leads to cessation of substance.
This avoids circularity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله الإعدام إما أن يكون أمرا وجوديا أو لا يكون قلنا يقتضى أن لا يعدم شيء البتة، لأنه يقال إذا عدم الشيء فهل يتجدد أمر أم لا يتجدد، فإن لم يتجدد أمر فهو لم يعدم، وإن تجدد فالمتجدد عدم أو وجود، لا جائز أن يكون عدما لأنه لا فرق بين أن يقال لم يتجدد وبين أن يقال تجدد العدم وإلا فأحد المعدمين يخالف الآخر وهو محاله وإن كان وجودها كان حدوثاً لموجود آخر لا عدما للوجود الأول، سلمنا فساد هذا القسم فلم لا يجوز أن يفنى بحدوث الصد. وقوله في الوجه الأول: حدوث الحادث متوقف على عدم الباقى قلت لا نسلم فإن عندنا عدم الباقى معلول الحادث، والعلة وإن امتنع انفكاكها عن المعلول لكن لا حاجة بها إلى المعلول. قوله في الوجه الثاني: المضادة حاصلة من الجانبين، قلنا لم لا يجوز أن يكون الحادث أقوى لحدوثه وإن كنا لا نعرف لمية كون الحدوث سلبا للقوة سلمنا فساد هذا القسم لكن لم لا يجوز أن يعدم الجسم لانتقاء الشرط، بيانه هو أن العرض لا يبقى والجوهر ممتنع الخلو عندنا، فإذا لم يخلق الله تعالى العرض انتفى الجوهر. قوله: يلزم الدور قلنا لم لا يجوز أن يقال الجوهر والعرض يتلازمان وإن لم يكن لأحدهما حاجة إلى الآخر كما في المتضادين ومعلولى العلة الواحدة فإذا لم يوجد أحد المتلازمين وجب عدم الآخر
His statement: 'Annihilation is either a positive thing or not.' We said: This necessitates that nothing is annihilated at all, because it is said: when a thing is annihilated, does something new occur or not? If nothing new occurs, then it has not been annihilated. If something new occurs, then what occurs is either non-existence or existence. It cannot be non-existence because there is no difference between saying 'nothing new occurred' and saying 'non-existence occurred'; otherwise, one of the two annihilations would differ from the other, which is impossible. And if it were existence, that would be the origination of another existent, not the annihilation of the first existence. We concede the corruption of this division, but why is it not permissible that it be annihilated by the occurrence of blackness? And his statement regarding the first aspect: 'The origination of the contingent is dependent on the non-existence of the permanent.' I say: We do not accept. For according to us, the non-existence of the permanent is an effect of the contingent, and although the cause cannot be separated from the effect, the cause has no need of the effect. His statement regarding the second aspect: 'Opposition occurs from both sides.' We say: Why is it not permissible that the contingent be stronger due to its origination, even if we do not know the 'why' of origination being a negation of strength? We concede the corruption of this division, but why is it not permissible that a body be annihilated due to the removal of a condition? Its explanation is that an accident does not remain, and the substance is, according to us, necessarily inseparable from it. So if God does not create the accident, the substance ceases. His statement: 'Circularity follows.' We say: Why is it not permissible to say that substance and accident are mutually dependent (mutalāzimān) even if neither has need of the other, as in the case of two opposites and two effects of one cause? So if one of the two mutually dependent things does not exist, the other necessarily ceases.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection against the possibility of annihilation. The objector argues that annihilation cannot be a positive act because that would imply a new existent, not non-existence; nor can it be a non-act because then nothing happens. The author concedes this division is flawed but proposes alternative: annihilation can occur by the occurrence of a contrary (e.g., blackness). He then addresses two further objections: (1) The contingent's origination dependent on the permanent's non-existence—he reverses causality: the permanent's non-existence is an effect of the contingent. (2) Opposition is symmetrical, so the permanent might be stronger—the author says the contingent might be stronger due to its newness. He then argues that annihilation can occur by removal of condition: since accidents do not persist and substances cannot exist without accidents, if God creates no accident, the substance ceases. To the objection of circularity (substance needs accident and vice versa), he suggests they can be mutually dependent without need, like opposites or two effects of one cause, so cessation of one implies the other.

الجوهر

التلازم

Misconception: Annihilation requires a positive act of destruction by God.
Correction: The author demonstrates that annihilation can be explained by the occurrence of a contrary or by the removal of a necessary condition, without requiring a positive act of destruction.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how can a body be annihilated without a positive act of annihilation?
A) By God directly destroying it.
B) By the occurrence of a contrary like blackness.
C) By the removal of a condition, specifically the accident that the substance needs.
D) By the substance ceasing to exist on its own.

Key Takeaway
Annihilation can be explained through the removal of conditions or occurrence of opposites, without requiring a positive act of destruction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider something in your life that you fear being 'annihilated'—a relationship, a skill, a state of imaan. Do you believe its loss would require a dramatic positive event, or could it slip away silently by the removal of the conditions that sustain it? What conditions have you neglected? Will you restore even one condition today?
?
Al-Razi argues that two things can be mutually dependent without one causing the other. Think of a relationship where you feel mutually dependent on someone. Are you relying on them as the cause of your stability, or are you both dependent on something greater (e.g., Allah's will)? How does that shift your reliance?
?
The passage shows that annihilation can happen simply by the absence of a condition. What 'conditions' in your spiritual life have you allowed to lapse, assuming that as long as you avoid major sins you are safe? What small, positive act of maintenance have you abandoned? Write down one condition you will intentionally revive this week.

Annihilation: Possibility, Conditions, and Refutations
The author critiques earlier responses on annihilation and defends its possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced arguments about annihilation in Islamic theology, including the possibility of annihilation through opposition or removal of a condition, and the refutation of objections like circularity and mutual dependence.

Core Idea
Annihilation is possible through opposition or removal of conditions, and substance's need for accident is not circular.

In this passage, the author critiques a prior theological response that claimed annihilation is impossible. He defends the majority view that annihilation can occur through the occurrence of a contrary or the removal of a condition, and he refutes several objections including circularity and the nature of conditions. This clarifies the positions of different schools like the Karramiyya and Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze every detail. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
This passage is dense, so take your time.
2
Identify the main arguments
List the key points: critique of the earlier answer, discussion of causing-to-exist vs. preserving, occurrence of a contrary, removal of a condition, circularity objection, and mutual dependence.
3
Understand the critique of the earlier response
Notice how the author rejects the statement that 'the non-existence of the permanent is an effect of the contingent' as insufficient and problematic.
4
Examine the condition and circularity arguments
Focus on the refutation of the claim that the condition for annihilation must be an accident, and how the author dismisses the circularity objection by differentiating between specified and unspecified needs.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this passage illustrates the depth of kalam debates and the importance of precise definitions in theological arguments.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله في الجواب: أن هذا يقتضى أن لا يعدم شيء البتة ليس بجواب إنما هو زيادة الإشكال وتأكيد أقول من يقول الإعدام غير ممكن إلا بطريان الضد وانتفاء الشرط وهو مذهب أكثر المتكلمين كما ذكره وهو أن عدم الباقى معلول الحادث وقولهم إن الحادث لا يكون أقوى من الباقي بكونه متعلق السبب لأن الباقى حال البقاء أيضا متعلق السبب ليس بصحيح، لأن الباقى عند قدماء المتكلمين مستغن عن السبب، وأما عند القائلين بأنه محتاج إلى سبب مبق، فجوا بهم أن الموجد أقوى من المبقى، لأن الإيجاد إعطاء الوجود الذي لم يكن أصلا والتبقية حفظ الوجود الحاصل ولكونه أخرى ترجح للحادث حال الحدوث لمكان موجودا معدوما معا محال، والباقى لو عدم بسبب حادث لزم منه محال ثم الجواب بأن الباقي يمنع الحادث عن أن يصير موجودا ولا يلزم منه محال ليس بمرضى، فإن الباقي لو كان بحيث يمنع لكان أقوى، وليس كذلك، والاعتراض بتجويز كون الحادث أكثر عددا من الباقي، والجواب بامتناع اجتماع المثلين ليس مما ذهب إليه ويتم جواب الوجه الثاني من إبطال الإعدام بطريان الضد، وهو أن التضاد حاصل من الجانبين على السواء بتجويز كون الحادث أقوى لترجيح الموجد على المبقى، وأما إبطال الإعدام بسبب انتفاء الشرط وأن الشرط لا يكون إلا عرضا فدعوى مجردة فإن من الجائز أن يكون شرطا هنا كغير العرض كما يكون الجوهر الذي هو المحل شرطا في إيجاد الأعراض فيه، وأيضا يجوز أن يكون الشرط لا جوهرا ولا عرضا بل أمرا عدميا، وقد مر بيان جواز الاشتراط به وزوال ذلك الأمر يقتضى انعدام المشروط، وبيان المصنف كون العرض شرطا في الإعدام بأن العرض لا يبقى والجوهر ممتنع الخلو عنه في عدم بانعدامه ليس مما يفيد مع هؤلاء الخصوم، لأن الكرامين لا يقولون بذلك كالمعتزلة، وأما إلزامهم الدور بسبب احتياج الجوهر إلى العرض فباطل لأن الدور يكون إذا كان المحتاج محتاجا إلى المحتاج فيما يحتاج فيه إليه وهنا ليس كذلك فإن احتياج الجوهر إلى عرض لا بعينه لا إلى عرض معين، والعرض المعين محتاج إلى جسم بعينه فلا يلزم منه الدور وجواب المصنف بتجويز التلازم من غير احتياج لأحدهما إلى الآخر ليس مفيدا هنا فإن المعرض محتاج في وجوده إلى الجسم واللازم وإن كان احتياج كل واحد من المتلازمين إلى عين الآخر محال لكن من غير احتياج أحدهما إلى الآخر وإلى ما يتعلق بالآخر ليس بمعقول فإن ذلك يكون مصاحبة عامة وهي لا تقتضى امتناع الانفكاك، وإيراد المثال بالمتضايفين على الوجه المشهور غير صحيح فإن إضافة كل واحد منهما مقتضية في الوجود إلى ذات الآخر لا إلى إضافته، ومعلولا علة واحدة يحتاج كل واحد منهما إلى الآخر فليس فيهما الاحتياج من غير لزوم الدور. وأما قوله: في الانتقال بالجواب إلى الجواب عن الثلاثة الأول ما تقدم في مسألة الحدوث، فتلك الثلاثة التي أوردها من جانب الفلاسفة وهو كون المؤثر موجبا وامتناع عدم الزمان بعد وجوده واحتياج ما يجوز عدمه إلى مادة قبل عدمه وقد مر الكلام فيها.
And his statement in the answer: 'This necessitates that nothing is annihilated at all' is not a proper answer; rather it is an increase of difficulty and a confirmation. I say: He who says annihilation is impossible except by the occurrence of a contrary (ṭurā al-ḍidd) and the removal of a condition (intifā’ al-sharṭ) — this is the doctrine of most theologians as he mentioned — and it is that the non-existence of the permanent is an effect of the contingent. And their statement that the contingent cannot be stronger than the permanent because it is subject to a cause — since the permanent during its permanence is also subject to a cause — is not correct, because the permanent according to the earlier theologians is independent of a cause. As for those who say it needs a preserving cause (sabab mubqin), their answer is that the causing-to-exist (mūjid) is stronger than the preserving (mubqī), because causing-to-exist gives existence which was not at all, while preservation is the maintenance of acquired existence. And because another [reason] is that the contingent at the moment of origination is stronger due to the fact that its existence and non-existence at the same time is impossible. And if the permanent were annihilated due to a contingent, an impossibility would follow. Then the answer that the permanent prevents the contingent from becoming existent and no impossibility follows is not satisfactory, because if the permanent were such that it prevents, it would be stronger, but it is not so. And the objection by allowing the contingent to be greater in number than the permanent, and the answer by the impossibility of the conjunction of two similar things, is not what is intended. And the answer to the second aspect of refuting annihilation by the occurrence of a contrary is that opposition occurs from both sides equally, with the allowance that the contingent is stronger due to the preponderance of the causing-to-exist over the preserving. As for the refutation of annihilation by the removal of a condition, and that the condition is only an accident, this is a mere claim, for it is possible that the condition here is other than an accident, just as the substance which is the locus (maḥall) is a condition for the origination of accidents in it. Also, it is possible for the condition to be neither substance nor accident but rather a non-existent (amr ‘adamī), and the permissibility of conditioning by it has already been explained; and the removal of that thing necessitates the non-existence of the conditioned. And the author's explanation that the accident is a condition for annihilation because the accident does not remain and the substance cannot be free from it, so its non-existence causes the substance's non-existence, is not useful with these opponents, because the Karramiyya do not say that, like the Mu‘tazila. As for their imposing circularity (al-dawr) due to the substance's need for the accident, it is false, because circularity occurs when the needy is in need of the needy in that which it needs; but here it is not so, because the substance needs an accident not specified (lā bi‘aynihi), not a specific accident; and the specific accident needs a specific body, so no circularity follows. And the author's answer by allowing mutual dependence (talāzum) without need of one for the other is not beneficial here, because the accident in its existence needs the body; and the necessary condition, even if the need of each of the two mutually dependent things for the very other is impossible, but the case where there is no need of one for the other and for what belongs to the other is not intelligible, for that would be a general accompaniment (muṣāḥaba ‘ānima) which does not necessitate the impossibility of disjunction. And the example of correlatives (al-mutaḍāyifān) in the usual manner is not correct, because the relation (iḍāfa) of each of them requires existence towards the essence of the other, not towards its relation. And the two effects of one cause each need the other? — so there is in them need without circularity. As for his statement: 'transitioning the answer to the answer about the first three [objections] — that was already mentioned in the issue of origination (ḥudūth)' — those three are the ones he presented from the side of the philosophers, namely that the efficient cause necessitates (kawn al-mu’aththir mūjiban), the impossibility of the non-existence of time after its existence, and the need of that which can be annihilated for matter before its annihilation; and the discussion of them has passed.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a complex critique of earlier responses regarding annihilation. The author first criticizes the statement that 'the non-existence of the permanent is an effect of the contingent' as insufficient. He then discusses the relative strength of the causing-to-exist vs. preserving cause, arguing that the contingent at origination is stronger. He refutes the idea that the permanent prevents the contingent by pointing out that if it did, it would be stronger, but it is not. He then addresses the possibility of annihilation by occurrence of a contrary and by removal of condition. Regarding removal of condition, he argues that the condition need not be an accident; it could be a substance or even a non-existent. He criticizes the author's (al-Razi's) earlier argument that the condition is necessarily an accident because the Karramiyya and Mu'tazila do not accept that substances need accidents in that way. He then dismantles the circularity objection: need for an unspecified accident does not create circularity. He also criticizes the idea of mutual dependence without need as unintelligible. He ends by referencing the three philosophical objections about necessity of cause, impossibility of time's non-existence, and need for matter.

التلازم

الإيجاد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all arguments against annihilation are settled and that the majority view is uncontested.
Correction: The author shows ongoing debate, refines responses, and addresses objections from different schools like the Karramiyya and Mu'tazila.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the claim that the condition for annihilation must be an accident not necessarily true?
A) Because the condition can be a substance, as the substance is a condition for accidents.
B) Because the condition can be a non-existent entity.
C) Both A and B.
D) Because the condition is always the agent's will.

Key Takeaway
Annihilation is possible through opposition or removal of conditions, and careful reasoning is needed to avoid circularity and maintain coherent theological positions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What condition (shart) in your life—a habit, a person, a status—are you relying on for your spiritual survival? If that condition were removed, would your faith be annihilated? Are you ready for that removal?
?
The author warns against circular reasoning in theology. Examine your own beliefs: is there a belief you hold that you accept only because it reinforces another belief you already hold, without an independent foundation? Will you break that circle today?
?
You believe God is al-Bāqī (the Permanent). Yet you cling to temporary things as if they will never end. Does this lesson convict you of idolatry in your attachments? What attachment must you annihilate in your heart right now?

Can an agent bring about non-existence?
Razi presents the objection and critiques the simplistic answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial argument against the possibility of an agent bringing about non-existence and why a common response is insufficient.

Core Idea
Non-existence cannot be simply willed; it requires a deeper causal explanation.

Imam Razi begins by addressing a core objection: some argue that an agent cannot truly 'create' non-existence because that would involve a renewal of nothingness. This lesson unpacks that objection and shows why a simple denial is not a valid answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening argument
Read how Razi presents the claim that non-existence cannot occur from an agent and the logical implication that such a view would mean nothing ever ceases.
Notice the terms 'إعدام' and 'عدم'—bringing about non-existence vs. non-existence itself.
2
Identify the insufficient answer
Razi criticizes a specific answer that declares non-existence impossible because it would lead to everything being permanent. Understand why he calls it 'not an answer but an increase of difficulty'.
3
Contrast with the majority view
The passage mentions that most theologians allow non-existence only through a contrary or loss of condition. See how this sets up the next lessons.
This is a preview of the doctrine of 'تضاد' and 'شرط'.

و اعدم صدور شيء عن الفاعل ، والقول أنه فعل العدم حكم بتجدد العدم بعد أن لم يكن وصدوره عن فاعل و تمايز العدمين يكون بانتسابهما إلى وجودين أو بانتساب أحدهما دون الآخر . وقوله في الجواب : أن هذا يقتضى أن لا يعدم شىء البتة ليس بجواب إنما هو زيادة الإشكال وتأكيد اقول من يقول الإعدام غير ممكن إلا بطريان الضد وانتفاء الشرط وهو مذهب أكثر المتكلمين كما ذكره وهو إن عدم الباقى معلول الحادث وقولهم إن الحادث لا يكون أقوى من الباقي بكونه متعلق السبب لأن الباقى حال البقاء أيضا متعلق السبب ليس بصحيح ، لأن الباقى عند قدماء المتكلمين مستغن عن السبب، وأما عند القائلين بأنه محتاج إلى سبب مبق ، فجوا بهم أن الموجد أقوى من المبقى ، لأن الإيجاد إعطاء الوجود الذي لم يكن أصلا والتبقية حفظ الوجود الحاصل ولكونه أخرى ترجح للحادث حال الحدوث لمكان موجودا معدوما معا محال ، والباقى لو عدم بسبب حادث مالزم منه محال ثم الجواب بأن الباقي يمنع الحادث عن أن يصير موجودا ولا يلزم منه محال ليس بمرضى ، فإن الباقي لو كان بحيث يمنع السكان أقوى ، وليس كذلك ، والاغتراض بتجويز كون الحادث أكثر عددا من البافي، والجواب بامتناع اجتماع المثلين لين ما ذهب إليه ويتم جواب الوجه الثانى من إبطال الإعدام بطويان الضد، وهو أن التضاد حاصل من الجانبين على السواء بتجويز كون الحادث أقوى الترجيح الموجود على المبقى، وأما إبطال الإعدام بسبب انتفاء الشرط وأن الشرط لا يكون إلا عرضا فدهوى مجردة فإن من الجائز أن يكون شرطا هن ك غير العرض كما يكون الجودر الذي هو المحل شرطا في إيجاد الأعراض فيه ، وأيضا يجوز أن يكون الشرط لا جوهرا ولا عرضا بل أمرا عدميا، وقد مر بيان جواز الاشتراط به وزوال ذلك الأمر يقتضى انعدام المشروط ، وبيان المصنف كون العرض شرطا في الإعدام بأن العرض لا يبقى والجوهر ممتنع الخلو عنه في عدم بانعدامه ليس مما يفيد مع هؤلاء الخصوم ، لأن الكرامين لا يقولون بذلك كالمعتزلة ، وأما الزامهم الدور بسبب احتياج الجوهر إلى العرض فباطل لأن الدور يكون إذا كان المحتاج محتاجا إلى المحتاج فيما يحتاج فيه إليه وهنا ليس كذلك فإن احتياج الجوهر إلى عرض لا بعينه لا إلى عرض معين ، والعرض المعين محتاج إلى جسم بعينه فلا يلزم منه الدور وجواب المصنف بتجريز التلازم من غير احتياج لأحدهما إلى الآخر ليس فيد مهنا فإن المعرض محتاج في وجوده إلى الجسم واللازم وإن كان باحتياج كل واحد من المتلازمين إلى عين الآخر محال لكنه من غير احتياج أحدهما إلى الآخر ، وإلى ما يتعلق بالآخر ليس بمعقوله فإن ذلك يكون مصاحبة عامة وهي لا تقتضى امتناع الانفكاك ، وإيراد المثال بالمتضايفين على الوجه المشهور غير صحيح فإن إضافة كل واحد منهما نتاجة في الوجود إلى ذات الآخر لا إلى إضافته ، ومعلولا علة واحدة يحتاج كل واحد منهما إلى آخر فليس فيهما الاحتياج من غير لزوم الدور . وأما قوله : فى الانتقال بالجواب إلى الجواب عن الثلاثة الأول ما تقدم في مسألة الحدوث ، فتلك الثلاثة التي أوردها من جانب الفلاسفة وهو كون المؤثر موجبا وامتناع عدم الزمان بعد وجوده واحتياج ما يجوز عدمه إلى مادة قبل عدمه وقا. من الكلام فيها .
And the non-occurrence of anything from the agent, and the statement that he did the act of non-existence judges that non-existence is renewed after it was not and its occurrence from an agent, and the differentiation of the two non-existences is by their relation to two existences or by the relation of one of them without the other. And his statement in the answer: that this requires that nothing ever cease to exist is not an answer but is an increase of difficulty and confirmation of the one who says that bringing about non-existence is not possible except by the occurrence of a contrary and the absence of a condition, and this is the doctrine of most theologians as he mentioned, and it is that the non-existence of the enduring thing is caused by the contingent. And their statement that the contingent cannot be stronger than the enduring by virtue of its being connected to a cause because the enduring during the state of permanence is also connected to a cause is not correct, because the enduring according to the early theologians is independent of a cause. But according to those who say it needs a preserving cause, they allowed that the originating agent is stronger than the preserving agent, because origination is giving existence which was not at all, and preservation is the conservation of the attained existence, and because another thing is the preponderance of the contingent at the time of origination, for it is impossible for a thing to be existent and non-existent simultaneously. And if the enduring were to cease due to a contingent, something impossible would follow. Then the answer that the enduring prevents the contingent from becoming existent and nothing impossible follows is not satisfactory, for if the enduring were such that it prevents the moving thing is stronger, and it is not so. And the objection by allowing that the contingent is more numerous than the enduring, and the answer by the impossibility of the conjunction of two similar things, softens what he went to. And the answer to the second aspect of refuting the bringing about of non-existence by the occurrence of a contrary is completed, namely that contrariety is obtained from both sides equally, by allowing that the contingent is stronger in preferring the existing over the preserving. As for refuting the bringing about of non-existence due to the absence of a condition, and that the condition is only an accident, that is a sheer claim, for it is possible that the condition be other than an accident, just as the substance which is the locus is a condition for the existence of accidents in it. Also, it is possible that the condition is neither substance nor accident but a non-existent matter, and the permissibility of conditioning by it has already been explained, and the removal of that thing necessitates the cessation of the conditioned. And the author's explanation that the accident is a condition for bringing about non-existence by saying that the accident does not endure and the substance is impossible to be free of it, so in non-existence by its non-existence, is not beneficial with these opponents, because the Karramites do not say that like the Mu'tazilites. As for their imposing of circularity due to the substance's need for the accident, it is false, because circularity occurs when the needy is need of the needy in that which is needed, and here it is not so, for the substance needs an accident not specified, not a specific accident, and the specific accident needs a specific body, so circularity does not follow. And the author's answer by allowing mutual implication without either needing the other is not beneficial here, for the accident needs the body for its existence, and even if the need of each of the mutually implied things for the self of the other is impossible, yet it is not conceivable that one does not need the other and is related to what pertains to the other, for that would be a general companionship which does not necessitate the impossibility of separation. And the citing of the example of correlatives in the well-known manner is not correct, for the relation of each of them in existence is to the essence of the other, not to its relation. And two effects of one cause each needs the other, so there is no need in them without circularity. As for his statement: in moving by the answer to the answer about the three first issues that preceded in the problem of origination, those are the three that he mentioned from the side of the philosophers, namely that the agent is necessitating, the impossibility of time's non-existence after its existence, and the need of that whose non-existence is possible for a matter before its non-existence, and there is discussion about them.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a complex refutation of arguments against the possibility of bringing about non-existence (إعدام) by an agent. It begins by stating that the claim that an agent can bring about non-existence implies a renewal of non-existence after it was not, and that the distinction between two non-existences is by relation to existences. The author then critiques a response that such a view would mean nothing can cease to exist, calling it not an answer but an intensification of the problem. He notes that many theologians hold that non-existence is only possible through the advent of a contrary or the absence of a condition. The discussion then addresses the strength of the contingent versus the enduring, clarifying that the enduring according to early theologians needs no cause, but even if it needed a preserving cause, the originating agent is stronger. The author rejects the idea that the enduring prevents the contingent from existing. Further, he refutes the objection about the contingent being more numerous, using the principle of impossibility of two similar things. He then turns to the refutation of non-existence by absence of condition, arguing that conditions need not be accidents; they could be substances or even non-existents. He criticizes the author's own explanation about the accident being a condition for non-existence as unhelpful against opponents like the Karramiyya. The next section deals with the charge of circularity between substance and accident: the substance needs an accident in general, not a specific one, so circularity does not apply. The author's suggestion of mutual implication without need is deemed insufficient. Finally, the passage mentions moving to answer three issues from the philosophers: the agent being necessitating, the impossibility of time ceasing, and the need for matter before non-existence.

Key Terms
إعدام
عدم
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think non-existence can be directly created by an agent as a simple act of will.
Correction: The passage shows that theologians require a mechanism (contrary or removal of condition) for non-existence to occur.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the insufficient answer claim?
A) Non-existence is impossible because it would require a renewal of nothing.
B) If non-existence were possible, nothing would ever cease.
C) The agent's power is limited to existence only.
D) Non-existence is identical to the absence of conditions.

Key Takeaway
The initial argument against causing non-existence is not refuted by claiming it would lead to universal permanence; a more nuanced causal explanation is needed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something you love vanishes from your life—health, a person, a possession—do you immediately search for a worldly reason, or does your heart turn to the One who truly brings non-existence? How would your reaction change if you truly believed that no thing ceases except through His wisdom and decree?
?
You have witnessed countless things cease: leaves fall, relationships end, your own youth slip away. If, as this lesson shows, non-existence cannot be casually willed but requires a profound cause, then what does that say about the gravity of every loss? Are you living with that awareness, or do you take disappearances for granted?
?
The theologians say non-existence occurs only by the arrival of a contrary or the removal of a condition. Can you identify a 'contrary' or 'condition' that has recently been removed in your life to bring about a loss? More importantly, are you actively seeking the spiritual condition that would keep what matters most—your faith, your connection with Allah—from ceasing?

Causality and Created Things
advanced
8 min
Contrariety and absence of condition
Razi elaborates on the majority view and its defenses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how most theologians explain non-existence through contrariety or loss of condition, and the debates around which is stronger.

Core Idea
Non-existence is a result of a contrary arrival or a condition's removal, not a direct act.

In this lesson, we explore the mainstream theological view that non-existence (adam) occurs only when a contrary appears or when a condition is absent. Razi analyzes this position, discussing whether the contingent or the enduring is stronger in causal terms.

Guided Path
1
Examine the doctrine of contrariety
Read the part where Razi states that non-existence by contrariety is 'obtained from both sides equally' and the debate on strength.
Contrariety (تضاد) is a key concept: two opposites cannot coexist.
2
Consider the condition argument
Read about the absence of condition: some say only an accident can be a condition. Razi rejects this, giving examples of substance as a condition.
Here 'شرط' means condition, not logical necessity.
3
Understand the critique of the author's example
Razi criticizes the view that the accident is the condition for non-existence because it doesn't convince opponents like the Karramites.
This shows the importance of addressing opponent-specific assumptions.

Commentary
This passage is a complex refutation of arguments against the possibility of bringing about non-existence (إعدام) by an agent. It begins by stating that the claim that an agent can bring about non-existence implies a renewal of non-existence after it was not, and that the distinction between two non-existences is by relation to existences. The author then critiques a response that such a view would mean nothing can cease to exist, calling it not an answer but an intensification of the problem. He notes that many theologians hold that non-existence is only possible through the advent of a contrary or the absence of a condition. The discussion then addresses the strength of the contingent versus the enduring, clarifying that the enduring according to early theologians needs no cause, but even if it needed a preserving cause, the originating agent is stronger. The author rejects the idea that the enduring prevents the contingent from existing. Further, he refutes the objection about the contingent being more numerous, using the principle of impossibility of two similar things. He then turns to the refutation of non-existence by absence of condition, arguing that conditions need not be accidents; they could be substances or even non-existents. He criticizes the author's own explanation about the accident being a condition for non-existence as unhelpful against opponents like the Karramiyya. The next section deals with the charge of circularity between substance and accident: the substance needs an accident in general, not a specific one, so circularity does not apply. The author's suggestion of mutual implication without need is deemed insufficient. Finally, the passage mentions moving to answer three issues from the philosophers: the agent being necessitating, the impossibility of time ceasing, and the need for matter before non-existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The only way non-existence occurs is by the arrival of a contrary, and the condition must be an accident.
Correction: Razi argues that conditions can be substances or non-existents, and contrariety is not the only mechanism.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the claim that the condition for non-existence must be an accident considered a 'sheer claim'?
A) Because accidents cannot endure.
B) Because substances can also be conditions, as when a substratum is necessary for accidents.
C) Because conditions are always non-existent.
D) Because the Karramiyya hold the opposite.

Key Takeaway
Non-existence is explained by contrariety or removal of conditions, and conditions are not restricted to accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that non-existence of the enduring is not directly caused but results from removal of conditions. Consider your own spiritual state: what conditions for your faith's strength have you allowed to slip, and what would it take to restore them—not by adding a new act, but by removing the barriers you have neglected?
?
The debate about conditions being accidents or substances may seem abstract, but it touches on how you understand Allah's preservation of you. If non-existence of your good deeds is due to a condition that is not an accident but perhaps a habit or intention you have abandoned, what specific condition are you failing to maintain, and what step will you take today to re-establish it?
?
Razi refutes the idea that the enduring is strong enough to prevent a contingent from coming into existence. In your life, do you rely on your own 'enduring' good deeds to prevent failures, or do you humbly acknowledge that only constant renewal of conditions keeps you in faith? How can you actively renew the conditions for your iman this week?

Causality and Created Things
advanced
7 min
Circularity and mutual implication
Razi refutes circularity and critiques mutual implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the charge of circularity between substance and accident, and why mutual implication without need is insufficient.

Core Idea
Circularity only occurs when each needs the other in the same respect; here the need is generic, not specific.

This lesson tackles the accusation that the relationship between substance and accident leads to circular causality. Razi shows that the substance needs an accident in general, not a specific one, so there is no vicious circle. He also critiques the notion of mutual implication without causal dependence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the charge of circularity
Read the accusation: the substance needs the accident, and the accident needs the substance, creating a loop. Note the context of the Karramiyya argument.
Circularity (دور) is a logical fallacy where A depends on B and B depends on A.
2
Razi's response: generic vs. specific need
Razi clarifies that the substance needs an accident in general, not a particular accident, while each accident needs a specific body. Thus no circularity.
This is a classic solution: the need is not reciprocal in the same way.
3
Critique of mutual implication without need
Razi argues that saying the two are mutually implicating without needing each other is not coherent, as that would just be general companionship, not necessary inseparability.
He provides the example of correlatives (المتضايفان) to illustrate true mutual dependence.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

لا يعدم شىء البتة ليس بجواب إنما هو زيادة الإشكال وتأكيد اقول من يقول الإعدام غير ممكن إلا بطريان الضد وانتفاء الشرط وهو مذهب أكثر المتكلمين كما ذكره وهو إن عدم الباقى معلول الحادث وقولهم إن الحادث لا
