انتفاء الشرط وأن الشرط لا يكون إلا عرضا فدهوى مجردة فإن من الجائز أن يكون شرطا هن ك غير العرض كما يكون الجودر الذي هو المحل شرطا في إيجاد الأعراض فيه ، وأيضا يجوز أن يكون الشرط لا جوهرا ولا عرضا بل أمرا عدميا، وقد مر بيان جواز الاشتراط به وزوال ذلك الأمر يقتضى انعدام المشروط ، وبيان المصنف كون العرض شرطا في الإعدام بأن العرض لا يبقى والجوهر ممتنع الخلو عنه في عدم بانعدامه ليس مما يفيد مع هؤلاء الخصوم ، لأن الكرامين لا يقولون بذلك كالمعتزلة ، وأما الزامهم الدور بسبب احتياج الجوهر إلى العرض فباطل لأن الدور يكون إذا كان المحتاج محتاجا إلى المحتاج فيما يحتاج فيه إليه وهنا ليس كذلك فإن احتياج الجوهر إلى عرض لا بعينه لا إلى عرض معين ، والعرض المعين محتاج إلى جسم بعينه فلا يلزم منه الدور وجواب المصنف بتجريز التلازم من غير احتياج لأحدهما إلى الآخر ليس فيد مهنا فإن المعرض محتاج في وجوده إلى الجسم واللازم وإن كان باحتياج كل واحد من المتلازمين إلى عين الآخر محال لكنه من غير احتياج أحدهما إلى الآخر ، وإلى ما يتعلق بالآخر ليس بمعقوله فإن ذلك يكون مصاحبة عامة وهي لا تقتضى امتناع الانفكاك ، وإيراد المثال بالمتضايفين على الوجه المشهور غير صحيح فإن إضافة كل واحد منهما نتاجة في الوجود إلى ذات الآخر لا إلى إضافته ، ومعلولا علة واحدة يحتاج كل واحد منهما إلى آخر فليس فيهما الاحتياج من غير لزوم الدور . وأما قوله : فى الانتقال بالجواب إلى الجواب عن الثلاثة الأول ما تقدم في مسألة الحدوث ، فتلك الثلاثة التي أوردها من جانب الفلاسفة وهو كون المؤثر موجبا وامتناع عدم الزمان بعد وجوده واحتياج ما يجوز عدمه إلى مادة قبل عدمه وقا. من الكلام فيها .
And the non-occurrence of anything from the agent, and the statement that he did the act of non-existence judges that non-existence is renewed after it was not and its occurrence from an agent, and the differentiation of the two non-existences is by their relation to two existences or by the relation of one of them without the other. And his statement in the answer: that this requires that nothing ever cease to exist is not an answer but is an increase of difficulty and confirmation of the one who says that bringing about non-existence is not possible except by the occurrence of a contrary and the absence of a condition, and this is the doctrine of most theologians as he mentioned, and it is that the non-existence of the enduring thing is caused by the contingent. And their statement that the contingent cannot be stronger than the enduring by virtue of its being connected to a cause because the enduring during the state of permanence is also connected to a cause is not correct, because the enduring according to the early theologians is independent of a cause. But according to those who say it needs a preserving cause, they allowed that the originating agent is stronger than the preserving agent, because origination is giving existence which was not at all, and preservation is the conservation of the attained existence, and because another thing is the preponderance of the contingent at the time of origination, for it is impossible for a thing to be existent and non-existent simultaneously. And if the enduring were to cease due to a contingent, something impossible would follow. Then the answer that the enduring prevents the contingent from becoming existent and nothing impossible follows is not satisfactory, for if the enduring were such that it prevents the moving thing is stronger, and it is not so. And the objection by allowing that the contingent is more numerous than the enduring, and the answer by the impossibility of the conjunction of two similar things, softens what he went to. And the answer to the second aspect of refuting the bringing about of non-existence by the occurrence of a contrary is completed, namely that contrariety is obtained from both sides equally, by allowing that the contingent is stronger in preferring the existing over the preserving. As for refuting the bringing about of non-existence due to the absence of a condition, and that the condition is only an accident, that is a sheer claim, for it is possible that the condition be other than an accident, just as the substance which is the locus is a condition for the existence of accidents in it. Also, it is possible that the condition is neither substance nor accident but a non-existent matter, and the permissibility of conditioning by it has already been explained, and the removal of that thing necessitates the cessation of the conditioned. And the author's explanation that the accident is a condition for bringing about non-existence by saying that the accident does not endure and the substance is impossible to be free of it, so in non-existence by its non-existence, is not beneficial with these opponents, because the Karramites do not say that like the Mu'tazilites. As for their imposing of circularity due to the substance's need for the accident, it is false, because circularity occurs when the needy is need of the needy in that which is needed, and here it is not so, for the substance needs an accident not specified, not a specific accident, and the specific accident needs a specific body, so circularity does not follow. And the author's answer by allowing mutual implication without either needing the other is not beneficial here, for the accident needs the body for its existence, and even if the need of each of the mutually implied things for the self of the other is impossible, yet it is not conceivable that one does not need the other and is related to what pertains to the other, for that would be a general companionship which does not necessitate the impossibility of separation. And the citing of the example of correlatives in the well-known manner is not correct, for the relation of each of them in existence is to the essence of the other, not to its relation. And two effects of one cause each needs the other, so there is no need in them without circularity. As for his statement: in moving by the answer to the answer about the three first issues that preceded in the problem of origination, those are the three that he mentioned from the side of the philosophers, namely that the agent is necessitating, the impossibility of time's non-existence after its existence, and the need of that whose non-existence is possible for a matter before its non-existence, and there is discussion about them.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a complex refutation of arguments against the possibility of bringing about non-existence (إعدام) by an agent. It begins by stating that the claim that an agent can bring about non-existence implies a renewal of non-existence after it was not, and that the distinction between two non-existences is by relation to existences. The author then critiques a response that such a view would mean nothing can cease to exist, calling it not an answer but an intensification of the problem. He notes that many theologians hold that non-existence is only possible through the advent of a contrary or the absence of a condition. The discussion then addresses the strength of the contingent versus the enduring, clarifying that the enduring according to early theologians needs no cause, but even if it needed a preserving cause, the originating agent is stronger. The author rejects the idea that the enduring prevents the contingent from existing. Further, he refutes the objection about the contingent being more numerous, using the principle of impossibility of two similar things. He then turns to the refutation of non-existence by absence of condition, arguing that conditions need not be accidents; they could be substances or even non-existents. He criticizes the author's own explanation about the accident being a condition for non-existence as unhelpful against opponents like the Karramiyya. The next section deals with the charge of circularity between substance and accident: the substance needs an accident in general, not a specific one, so circularity does not apply. The author's suggestion of mutual implication without need is deemed insufficient. Finally, the passage mentions moving to answer three issues from the philosophers: the agent being necessitating, the impossibility of time ceasing, and the need for matter before non-existence.

Key Terms
دور
متلازمان
عرض
جوهر
متضايفان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Substance and accident necessarily lead to circular causality because each requires the other.
Correction: Razi shows that the need is generic (substance needs any accident) vs. specific (accident needs a specific substance), so no circularity.

Checkpoint
How does Razi break the alleged circularity between substance and accident?
A) He denies that substance needs accident.
B) He says substance needs accident in general, not a specific one.
C) He claims accident does not need substance.
D) He says both need a third cause.

Key Takeaway
The charge of circularity fails because the needs are not reciprocal in the same way; mutual implication without need is not a coherent solution.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you tend to think of your dependence on Allah as a general, unspecified need (like substance needs any accident) or as a specific need for a particular cause? How might this lesson's distinction correct a shallow understanding of tawakkul?
?
Razi warns against the fallacy of assuming mutual implication requires reciprocal need. In your spiritual life, have you ever assumed that because you always perform a certain act of worship together with a feeling of piety, they must be causally linked? How can this lesson help you examine the true sources of your actions?

Causality and Created Things

Three philosopher's objections
Razi briefly lists three philosopher's objections and references earlier refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three objections from philosophers against the possibility of non-existence, and how Razi points to earlier discussions for answers.

Core Idea
Philosophers argue that an agent necessarily causes, time cannot cease, and non-existence requires pre-existing matter.

In the final part of the passage, Razi mentions that the answer to three philosopher's objections was already given in the discussion on origination (huduth). These objections concern the agent being necessitating, the impossibility of time's non-existence, and the need for matter before non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three objections
Read the very end of the passage: the three issues from the philosophers: agent's necessitation, time's permanence, and matter requirement.
These are classical objections from the falasifa against occasionalist causality.
2
Connect to earlier discussion
Razi says they were addressed in the problem of origination. Understand that he is not repeating the answers here.
This teaches the structure of the book: cross-references.

Commentary
This passage is a complex refutation of arguments against the possibility of bringing about non-existence (إعدام) by an agent. It begins by stating that the claim that an agent can bring about non-existence implies a renewal of non-existence after it was not, and that the distinction between two non-existences is by relation to existences. The author then critiques a response that such a view would mean nothing can cease to exist, calling it not an answer but an intensification of the problem. He notes that many theologians hold that non-existence is only possible through the advent of a contrary or the absence of a condition. The discussion then addresses the strength of the contingent versus the enduring, clarifying that the enduring according to early theologians needs no cause, but even if it needed a preserving cause, the originating agent is stronger. The author rejects the idea that the enduring prevents the contingent from existing. Further, he refutes the objection about the contingent being more numerous, using the principle of impossibility of two similar things. He then turns to the refutation of non-existence by absence of condition, arguing that conditions need not be accidents; they could be substances or even non-existents. He criticizes the author's own explanation about the accident being a condition for non-existence as unhelpful against opponents like the Karramiyya. The next section deals with the charge of circularity between substance and accident: the substance needs an accident in general, not a specific one, so circularity does not apply. The author's suggestion of mutual implication without need is deemed insufficient. Finally, the passage mentions moving to answer three issues from the philosophers: the agent being necessitating, the impossibility of time ceasing, and the need for matter before non-existence.

Checkpoint
What are the three philosopher's objections mentioned at the end of the passage?
A) The agent is free, time is finite, matter is eternal.
B) The agent is necessitating, time cannot cease, non-existence needs matter.
C) The agent is omnipotent, time is accidental, non-existence is pure nothing.
D) The agent is indifferent, time is subjective, non-existence is relative.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the objection that 'God must create by necessity,' does it shake your conviction that God is completely free in His actions? How do you personally respond to the idea that God's will is not bound by any external compulsion? What step will you take to strengthen your certainty in this area?
?
The philosopher claims that time cannot cease. If you truly believe that time is a created thing, what does that mean for your anticipation of the afterlife—a realm beyond time? Does your daily life reflect a consciousness of eternity, or are you trapped in the illusion that time is absolute? What will you change today to live with the here and now?

Causality and Created Things
beginner
4 min
Philosophers on Element Transformation
Philosophers enumerate transformations of elements with empirical examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that the four elements transform into each other, supported by empirical examples from nature and alchemy.

Core Idea
The four elements constantly change into one another, as seen in everyday and alchemical processes.

The philosophers claimed that the four elements undergo generation and corruption. In this passage, they provide concrete examples: fire becomes air, air becomes water, water becomes earth. This is not just theory; it is based on observable phenomena like condensation and alchemical experiments. Let's examine their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Pay attention to the examples given.
Notice the sequence of transformations and the role of alchemists.
2
Identify the transformation sequence
Observe the order: fire turns to air, air turns to water, water turns to earth. Also note the reversal mentioned by Ibn Sina.
The condensation on a cold vessel is cited as proof for air turning to water.
3
Understand the broader claim
This passage supports the philosophers' view that elements are not eternal but subject to change. This connects to their cosmology and natural philosophy.
Consider how this argument relates to the debate on causality.

I say: The expression of Ibn Sina is thus: 'And it may dissolve solid stony bodies into flowing waters, known to the practitioners of trickery, as it may praise running waters for drinking solid stones?' Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' claim that the four elements undergo generation and corruption, illustrated by examples: fire turns to air when extinguished, air turns to water when cooled (as seen by condensation on a cold vessel), and water turns to earth (as performed by alchemists). The author then adds a marginal note (أقول) quoting Ibn Sina's own words, noting that alchemists can dissolve solid bodies into liquids, and conversely, nature can solidify water into stone. The note clarifies the source and provides additional examples, showing the natural and artificial transformations.

The Breaker and the Broken: Form vs. Quality
Analyzes proper attribution of breaking and being broken to form vs. quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the quality (not the form) is properly considered the breaker, and the subject as the broken, in physical mixtures.

Core Idea
In causality, the quality breaks, not the form.

This passage tackles a nuanced problem in physics: how can one thing be both breaker and broken? The discussion revolves around attributing breaking to either the form (ṣūra) or the quality (kayfiyya). The author presents an opposing view, refutes it, and then offers the correct analysis using the example of mixing hot and cold water. By the end, you will see how this resolves an apparent paradox.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: problem, opposing view, refutation, correct view, example.
The passage is dense due to OCR issues, but the logical flow is clear.
2
Identify the opposing view
The philosophers claim that 'the breaker is the form and the broken is the quality'. Find this claim in the passage.
The phrase 'المكاسر هو الصورة والمنكسر هو الكيفية' indicates this view.
3
Follow the refutation
The author argues that if form and quality coexist, their combination cannot be both breaker and broken simultaneously. Understand why this creates a contradiction.
The key point: the same entity cannot act and be acted upon in the same respect.
4
Learn the correct view
The author states that the breaker is the quality and the broken is its subject (the underlying substance). See how the hot water example illustrates this.
In mixing, the hot quality breaks the cold quality, and the water itself is the broken subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فذاك هر ه لا مؤثر إلا الله وإن كان خصم اؤ هم يلزمونهم القول به في وجود صفات الله تعالى وأكثر القائلين بالملة والمعلول لا يقولون بالمقارنة لقولهم إن ذلك يقتضي محالا وهو تحصيل الحاصل أما الحكماء فيقولون بذلك وههنا يكون قولهم المكاسر هو الصورة والمنكسر هو الكيفية بأن الصورة تتكسر بواسطة الكيفية فيعود المحذور صحيحا فإنه إذا كانت الصورة موجودة مع الكيفية كان مجموعهما لا يمكن أن يكون كاسرا ومكسورا في حالة واحدة كما لم يمكن في الكيفيتين والحق أن الكاسر هو الكيفية والمنكسر هو محلها ولذلك يحصل التوسط بين الماء الحار والبارد وإذا امتزجا من غير حصول صورتين فيهما ولا يلزم منه محال .
So that is: there is no effect except God, and even if the opponent forces them to affirm it in the existence of God's attributes, most of those who affirm cause and effect do not affirm simultaneity, because they say that necessitates an impossibility, namely the acquisition of the acquired. As for the philosophers, they affirm it. And here their statement that the breaker is the form and the broken is the quality—that the form is broken by means of the quality—returns the difficulty validly. For if the form exists with the quality, their combined entity cannot be breaker and broken in one state, just as is impossible in the two qualities. The truth is that the breaker is the quality and the broken is its subject. Therefore mediation occurs between hot and cold water, and when they mix without two forms occurring in them, no impossibility follows.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage addresses a problem in physics: how can one thing be both breaker and broken? The context seems to be a debate about causality and attributes. The author references a view that the breaker is the form (sura) and the broken is the quality (kayfiyya). He rejects this, pointing out that if form and quality coexist, their combination cannot be both breaker and broken simultaneously, just as two qualities cannot be so. The correct analysis, he says, is that the breaker is the quality and the broken is its subject (mahall). He illustrates with the mixing of hot and cold water: the hot quality breaks the cold quality, and the water (subject) is broken, without needing two forms. This resolves the paradox.

Key Takeaway
The quality breaks, the subject is broken; this resolves the paradox of one entity being both breaker and broken.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Causation is Intuitive
Al-Razi argues that causation is intuitively known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi argues that causation is an intuitive concept, not derived from experience.

Core Idea
Causation is intuitive; we know it from simple actions like cutting.

Al-Razi begins a new investigation into cause and effect with a bold claim: the very idea that one thing can affect another is a primitive, intuitive notion. He supports this by pointing to our immediate grasp of actions like cutting and breaking. This passage establishes the foundation for his subsequent discussion on causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the flow of the argument. Notice how Al-Razi frames his claim as a 'question' (مسألة).
The text may have a variant reading 'ببداهة المعقول' instead of 'ببداية المقول'.
2
Identify the examples
Al-Razi uses two specific examples: cutting meat and breaking a pen. Ask yourself: Are these actions intuitively understood? How do they relate to the general concept of effect?
3
Understand the logical move
Al-Razi argues from the specific to the general: if specific effects are intuitive, then the general concept (which is a part of them) is even more obviously intuitive. See if you follow this reasoning.
This is a key argument for the intuitive nature of causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الثاني في العلة والمعلول. مسألة: كون الشيء مؤثراً في غيره متصور تصورا بديهيا. لانا ببداية المقول نعلم معنى قولنا قطعت اللحم وكسرت القلم، والتقطيع والتكسير تأثير مخصوص، فلما كان تصور التأثير المخصوص بديهيا، كان تصور مسمى التأثير الذي هو جزء ماهية التأثير المخصوص أولى أن يكون بديهيا.
The second consideration: on cause and effect. Question: The concept of a thing being effective in another is conceived intuitively. For we know by the beginning of speech [or by primitive intuition] the meaning of our statement 'I cut the meat' and 'I broke the pen', and cutting and breaking are specific effects. So since the conception of the specific effect is intuitive, the conception of the meaning of effect which is a part of the essence of the specific effect is more fitting to be intuitive.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on cause and effect. Al-Razi argues that the concept of one thing affecting another is intuitive (بديهي). He gives examples: we understand what it means to cut meat or break a pen; these actions are instances of a specific effect. Since we grasp these specific effects intuitively, the general concept of effect (التأثير) which is part of the definition of those specific effects is even more obviously intuitive. In other words, causality is a primitive notion.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Causality is learned only through repeated experience (empiricism).
Correction: Al-Razi argues that we have an immediate, intuitive grasp of causation, as seen in simple actions like cutting.

Checkpoint

B) By pointing to the immediate understanding of actions like cutting.
C) By citing the philosophers.
D) By logical deduction.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi holds that the concept of causality is intuitively known, not empirically derived.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Non-Existence Cannot Be a Cause nor Caused
Al-Razi argues against non-existence as cause or effect and refutes the philosophers' objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi argues that non-existence cannot be a cause or effect, and how he refutes the philosophers' counterargument.

Core Idea
Non-existence is pure negation; it can neither cause nor be caused.

Al-Razi tackles a subtle but important theological question: can non-existence play a causal role? He argues that causality requires positive attributes, which non-existence lacks. The philosophers propose that the absence of a cause can explain non-existence, but al-Razi shows this fails because non-existence is pure negation and cannot be described as 'preponderant'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let al-Razi's argument unfold naturally.
Notice that the passage includes both al-Razi's position and the philosophers' objection.
2
Identify al-Razi's two arguments
Al-Razi presents two reasons why non-existence cannot be a cause or effect. Find them in the text and notice how they are built.
The first argument relies on causality being a positive attribute; the second on the requirement of the effect actually occurring.
3
Understand the philosophers' objection
The philosophers argue that non-existence can be caused by the absence of a cause, just as existence is caused by a cause. See how they draw an analogy.
They compare the preponderance of existence and non-existence.
4
See al-Razi's response
Al-Razi rejects the philosophers' view by stating that non-existence is pure negation and cannot have preponderance. Understand why this refutes the analogy.
The key is that 'preponderance' is a positive attribute that cannot apply to non-existence.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this argument matters for Islamic theology, especially regarding the nature of creation and divine action.
This helps preserve the principle that only existent things can be causes or effects, safeguarding Allah's role as the sole Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: العدم لا يعلل ولا يعلل به. لانا إن جعلنا العلية والمعلولية وصفين ثبوتيين استحال كون المعدوم علة ومعلولا لاستحالة قيام الموجود بالمعدوم، وإن لم تقل به كان التأثير عبارة عن حصول الأثر عن المؤثر وذلك يستدعى أصل الحصول، وقالت الفلاسفة علة العدم عدم العلة لأن الممكن دائر بين الوجود والعدم، وكما يستدعى رجحان الوجود علة وجودية استدعى رجحان العدم علة عدمية. والجواب أن العدم نفى محض فيستحيل وصفه بالرجحان.
Question: Non-existence is not caused nor is it a cause. For if we consider causality and effect-hood as positive attributes, it is impossible for non-existent to be cause or effect because it is impossible for the existent to inhere in the non-existent. And if we do not hold that, then effect is the obtaining of the effect from the effecter, and that requires the obtaining [of the effect]. The philosophers said: The cause of non-existence is the absence of the cause, because the possible alternates between existence and non-existence, and just as the preponderance of existence requires an existential cause, so the preponderance of non-existence requires a non-existential cause. The answer: Non-existence is pure negation, so it is impossible to describe it with preponderance.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This mas'ala addresses whether non-existence (العدم) can be a cause or an effect. Al-Razi argues that non-existence cannot be either. First, if causality is a positive attribute, non-existence cannot have it. Second, even if we don't consider it positive, effect requires the occurrence of an effect, which non-existence cannot provide. He then presents the philosophers' view: the cause of non-existence is the absence of a cause, similar to how existence requires a cause. Al-Razi rejects this by saying non-existence is pure negation and cannot be described as 'preponderant'.

Key Terms
عدم
علة
معلول
ثبوتي
رجحان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The absence of a cause is itself a cause of non-existence.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that non-existence is pure negation and cannot be a cause or have preponderance, so absence of a cause cannot be considered a genuine cause.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Razi reject the philosophers' argument that the cause of non-existence is the absence of a cause?
A) Because non-existence is pure negation and cannot have preponderance.
B) Because absence of a cause does not exist.
C) Because non-existence is not a real thing.
D) Because the possible does not require a cause.

Key Takeaway
Non-existence, being pure negation, cannot function as a cause or be caused, contrary to the philosophers' claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes attribute your failures to 'bad luck' or 'missing opportunities'—things that are non-existent—rather than examining the real causes within your control? How can this lesson help you take responsibility?
?
Al-Razi shows that non-existence cannot have preponderance. In your life, do you give undue weight to fears of what might not happen, treating absent possibilities as real causes? What would change if you focused only on what actually exists?
?
The philosophers argued that absence of a cause causes non-existence. Are you ever quick to blame 'what is missing' (like lack of resources or support) instead of addressing the actual causes of a problem? How can you align your reasoning with al-Razi's insight?

The Impossibility of Two Independent Causes for One Effect
Al-Razi states the principle and begins a proof by contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a single effect cannot have two fully independent causes according to classical Islamic causality.

Core Idea
Two independent causes would each make the effect unnecessary.

Al-Razi presents a fundamental principle of causality: a single effect cannot be produced by two entirely independent causes. The reasoning, though cut off in our source, begins by showing that if it were possible, the effect would be necessary with each cause, making dependence on the other superfluous. This lesson explains the principle and the start of his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice that the proof is incomplete.
The text ends mid-sentence; we have only the first half of the argument.
2
Identify the principle
The passage states that a single individual effect cannot have two independent causes. Mark this as the main claim.
Independent causes means each alone would suffice to produce the effect.
3
Analyze the reasoning
Al-Razi gives a conditional proof: if there were two independent causes, the effect would be necessary with each. This leads to a contradiction.
The proof is cut off, but the logic is that each cause would make the effect independent of the other.
4
Consider a common misunderstanding
This principle does not rule out cooperative causes that together produce an effect. It only applies to fully independent causes.
Cooperative causes are dependent on each other and thus are not independent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: المعلول الواحد بالشخص يستحيل أن يجتمع عليه علتان مستقلنان. وإلا لكان مع كل واحد منهما واجب الوقوع فيمتنع استناده إلى الآخر فيستغنى بكل واحد منهما عن
Question: A single effect in person cannot have two independent causes. Otherwise, it would be necessary of occurrence with each one of them, so its dependence on the other would be impossible, and it would be independent of each one of them from
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This brief mas'ala states the principle that a single effect cannot be produced by two completely independent causes. The reasoning: if it were caused by two independent causes, then with each cause the effect would be necessary, and thus it would not need the other cause, leading to independence from each. The text is cut off, but the argument is clear: independent causes would each be sufficient, making the others superfluous.

Key Terms
معلول واحد
علتان مستقلنان
واجب الوقوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This principle means that an effect cannot have any two causes, even if they work together.
Correction: The principle only rules out independent causes that are each sufficient on their own. Cooperative causes that depend on each other are not included.

Checkpoint

A) The effect would occur twice.
B) The effect would be dependent on both.
C) The effect would be unnecessary with each.
D) The effect would be impossible.

Key Takeaway
A single effect cannot have two independent causes because each would make the effect necessary and independent of the other, leading to a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Why Non-Existence Cannot Be a Cause or Effect
Razi proves that non-existence is neither a cause nor an effect, and refutes the philosophers' view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why non-existence cannot be a cause or an effect according to Imam Razi, and how he refutes the philosophical objection that non-existence can be caused by the absence of a cause.

Core Idea
Non-existence is pure negation; it cannot cause or be caused.

In this passage, Imam Razi tackles a fundamental question about causality: can non-existence (al-`adam) act as a cause or be produced as an effect? Some philosophers argued that just as existence requires a cause, non-existence can also be caused—for example, darkness caused by absence of light. Razi systematically refutes this view, establishing that non-existence is pure negation and thus cannot enter into causal relations at all.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic carefully, then the translation. Notice the structure: statement of the issue, two arguments, objection, answer.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify Razi's two arguments
First, if causality is a positive attribute, non-existence cannot have it. Second, even if causality is not positive, effect requires actual occurrence, which non-existence lacks.
3
Analyze the philosophers' objection
They claim non-existence can be caused by the absence of a cause, using the principle of preponderance.
4
Understand Razi's final answer
Razi replies that non-existence is pure negation, so it cannot be described as 'preponderant'—preponderance is a real attribute.
5
Reflect on the implication
This passage establishes that only existing things can be causes or effects, preserving the integrity of causal reasoning in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: العدم لا يعلل ولا يعلل به. لانا إن جعلنا العلية والمعلولية وصفين ثبوتيين استحال كون المعدوم علة ومعلولا لاستحالة قيام الموجود المعدوم، وإن لم تقل به كان التأثير عبارة عن حصول الأثر عن المؤثر وذلك يستدعى أصل الحصول، وقالت الفلاسفة علة العدم عدم العلة لأن الممكن دائر بين الوجود والعدم، وكما يستدعى رجحان الوجود علة وجودية استدعى رجحان العدم علة عدمية. والجواب أن العدم نفى محض فيستحيل وصفه بالرجحان.
Issue: Non-existence is not a cause and is not caused. Because if we make causality and effectuality two positive attributes, it is impossible for non-existent to be a cause and an effect due to the impossibility of the existent subsisting in the non-existent. And if we do not say that, then effect is nothing but the occurrence of the effect from the cause, and that requires the principle of occurrence. The philosophers said: The cause of non-existence is the non-existence of the cause, because the possible oscillates between existence and non-existence, and just as the preponderance of existence requires an existential cause, so the preponderance of non-existence requires a non-existential cause. The answer: Non-existence is pure negation, so it is impossible to describe it with preponderance.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage refutes two positions on non-existence and causality. First, Razi argues that if causality is a positive attribute (as some claim), non-existence cannot possess it. Second, even if causality is not a positive attribute, the concept of effect requires an actual occurrence, which non-existence lacks. Then the philosophers object that non-existence can be caused by the absence of a cause. Razi's answer is that non-existence is pure nothingness; it cannot be described as 'preponderant' because preponderance is a real attribute.

Key Terms
عدم
علة
معلول
رجحان
نفي محض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence can be caused by the absence of a cause (e.g., darkness caused by absence of light).
Correction: Razi argues that non-existence is pure negation; it has no real attributes and cannot be described as preponderant, so it cannot be caused.

Checkpoint
What is Razi's final answer to the philosophers' claim that non-existence can have a cause?
A) Non-existence can have a non-existential cause
B) Non-existence is pure negation and cannot be described as preponderant
C) Non-existence is a positive attribute
D) Non-existence depends on the possible

Key Takeaway
Non-existence is pure negation and cannot be a cause or effect; only existing things enter causal relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The lesson teaches that non-existence cannot cause anything. What non-existent thoughts or illusions are you letting control your actions today? Will you renounce their imagined power and act only in light of what truly exists?
?
The philosophers thought that absence of a cause can cause non-existence. Do you ever assume that your lack of good deeds is simply due to absence of effort, as if that absence itself is a cause? But the lesson says non-existence is pure negation—it does not cause anything. Your inaction does not 'cause' failure; it is merely the absence of action. What one existing thing can you choose to do right now, regardless of how you feel?
?
Razi concludes that non-existence cannot be described as preponderant. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes feel that your sins or shortcomings 'outweigh' your good deeds, as if your non-existence of virtue has a weight? But non-existence has no weight. What existing good deed can you perform today to tip the scale towards existence, rather than dwelling on the negation of what you lack?

A Single Effect Cannot Have Two Independent Causes
Razi proves by contradiction that one effect cannot have two independent causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's proof by contradiction that a single individual effect cannot have two independent causes.

Core Idea
No effect can have two independent causes—each would make the other superfluous.

This lesson presents a foundational principle of Islamic theology: causation is not arbitrary. Razi uses a logical proof to show that if one effect had two independent causes, each cause would alone necessitate the effect, making the other cause unnecessary. This leads to a contradiction, proving that such a scenario cannot occur.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of Razi's concise argument.
Notice how Razi uses the phrase 'وإلا' to introduce the proof by contradiction.
2
Identify the hypothesis
Razi assumes for argument's sake that two independent causes for one effect are possible. Understand what 'independent' means here.
Independent causes are those that are each sufficient to produce the effect, not requiring the other.
3
Follow the contradiction
If each cause makes the effect necessary, then the effect would not need the other cause. Razi concludes this leads to impossibility.
The contradiction arises because the effect would be independent of each cause, yet each is claimed to be a cause.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The original claim that two independent causes can coexist for one effect is false. Therefore, it is impossible.
This principle is used to argue that only Allah can be the ultimate cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: المعلول الواحد بالشخص يستحيل أن يجتمع عليه علتان مستقلنان. وإلا لكان مع كل واحد منهما واجب الوقوع فيمتنع استناده إلى الآخر فيستغنى بكل واحد منهما عن كل واحد منهما وهو محال.
Issue: A single individual effect is impossible to have two independent causes. Otherwise, with each one of them its occurrence would be necessary, so its dependence on the other would be impossible, and it would be independent of each of them, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage gives a concise proof by contradiction. If a single effect had two independent causes, each cause alone would make the effect necessary. Then the effect would not need the other cause, contradicting its dependence on both. Thus, two independent causes for one effect are impossible.

Key Terms
معلول
علة
مستقل
واجب الوقوع
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two independent causes can work together to produce a single effect.
Correction: Razi shows that if each cause is independent and each alone necessitates the effect, then one cause would make the other superfluous, leading to contradiction. True cooperation requires interdependence, not independence.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi say that two independent causes for one effect are impossible?
A) Because the effect would have to be dependent on both equally
B) Because each cause by itself would necessitate the effect, making the other cause superfluous
C) Because two causes cannot exist at the same time
D) Because the effect would be eternal

Key Takeaway
A single individual effect cannot have two independent causes because each would alone necessitate the effect, making the other superfluous and leading to a contradiction.

Similar Effects Can Have Different Causes
Razi defends permissibility of two similar effects having different causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that similar effects can have different causes, and his distinction between the effect's need for a cause in general and the specification of the cause.

Core Idea
Similar effects don't require identical causes; the specific cause is determined by the cause itself.

Razi takes on a common assumption in causal reasoning: that similar effects must have similar causes. Against the majority of Ash'aris, he argues otherwise. This passage presents his proof, the objection, and his answer that distinguishes between the effect's need for any cause and the determination of which specific cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Let the argument unfold in its original structure.
The passage is short but dense; read it twice.
2
Identify Razi's proof and the objection
Find Razi's proof (the example of blackness and whiteness) and the opponent's objection based on the effect's essence needing a specific cause.
3
Understand Razi's answer
Razi's answer introduces a crucial distinction: the effect depends on a cause in general, but the specific cause is determined by the cause's own nature, not the effect's. Reflect on why this resolves the objection.
This distinction is key to the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: المعلولان المتماثلان يجوز تعليلهما بعلتين مختلفتين خلافا لأكثر أصحابنا. لنا: أن السواد والبياض مع اختلافهما يشتركان في المخالفة والمضادة. احتجوا بأن افتقار المعلول إلى العلة المعينة إن كان لماهيته أو لشىء من لوازمها وجب فى كل ما يساوى ذلك المعلول أن يفتقر إلى مثل تلك العلة، وإن لم يكن لشيء من لوازم تلك الماهية كانت تلك الماهية غنية عن تلك العلة، والغنى عن الشيء يستحيل تعليله به. والجواب: أن المعلول إنما لماهيته مفتقر إلى مطلق العلة وتعين العلة إنما جاء من جانب العلة لا من جانب المعلول.
Issue: Two similar effects are permissible to be caused by two different causes, contrary to most of our companions. Our proof: Blackness and whiteness, despite their difference, share in opposition and contradiction. They objected: If the effect's need for the specific cause is due to its essence or something of its necessary concomitants, then everything equal to that effect must need a similar cause. And if it is not due to something from the necessary concomitants of that essence, then that essence is independent of that cause, and independence from the thing makes its being caused by it impossible. The answer: The effect, by its essence, is in need of the absolute cause, and the specification of the cause only comes from the side of the cause, not from the side of the effect.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage argues that similar effects can have different causes. Razi's proof gives an example: blackness and whiteness share the property of being opposites, yet they are different. The opponent objects that if the effect needs a specific cause due to its essence, then any similar effect would need that same cause; otherwise the essence would be independent. Razi's answer distinguishes between the need for a cause in general (which is due to the effect's essence) and the specific cause, which is determined by the cause's own nature, not the effect's.

Key Terms
متماثل
علة معينة
مخالفة
مضادة
لوازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that similar effects must have similar causes because the effect's essence determines the cause.
Correction: Razi shows that the effect's essence only requires a cause in general; the specific cause is determined by the cause itself, not the effect's essence.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what determines the specific cause for an effect?
A) The essence of the effect
B) The cause itself, not the effect
C) The similarity between effects
D) The opposition between effects

Key Takeaway
The specification of a cause for an effect is determined by the cause's own nature, not by the effect's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you assumed that two similar outcomes in your life must have the same cause, and how did that assumption lead you to misunderstand God's wisdom or make a poor decision? Will you now pause to ask God for the true cause rather than assuming?
?
Do you sometimes feel that your own actions (the effects) are the only thing that matters, forgetting that the specification of the cause (God's will) is what truly determines the outcome? How will you remind yourself daily that the cause comes from God, not from your effort alone?
?
Are there areas in your life where you have been trying to replicate a good outcome by copying its external form, while ignoring that the underlying cause may be different for you? What is one specific area where you will seek the unique cause God has appointed for you instead of imitating others?

A Single Cause Can Produce Multiple Effects
Razi argues that one cause can produce multiple effects, refuting the composition objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that a single cause can produce multiple effects and how he refutes the objection that this would imply composition in the cause.

Core Idea
Different effect-concepts do not require composition in the cause.

Razi confronts a central objection against the Ash'ari position on causality: if a cause is simple, how can it produce multiple distinct effects? The philosophers and Mu'tazila argue that multiple effects would force composition. Razi responds with a clever conceptual analysis, showing that the apparent multiplicity is in our concepts, not in the reality of the cause. This lesson walks through his argument and the example of a point on a circle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three parts of the passage
The passage has a clear structure: statement of position with proof, objection, and answer. Read it once to get the overall flow.
The proof is short: corporeality necessitates location and accident-reception.
2
Understand Razi's proof
Razi offers the example of corporeality (jismiyya) as a single cause producing two effects: being in place and receiving accidents. This is his evidence for the possibility.
3
Analyze the opponent's trilemma
The opponent argues that if a cause is a source of multiple effects, the concepts of being a source for each effect must either both be inside the essence, both outside, or one inside and one outside — each leading to composition or regress.
Understand each horn of the trilemma.
4
See Razi's answer: effectiveness is not a real attribute
Razi denies that effectiveness (mu'thiriyya) is a positive attribute. Thus the conceptual multiplicity does not affect the essence itself. This blocks the trilemma.
He refers to his earlier explanation that effectiveness is not a real attribute.
5
Reflect on the analogies
Razi gives two analogies: a point on a circle being opposite different points, and negations like 'not B' and 'not C'. In both cases, different concepts do not make the subject composite. Use these to solidify your understanding.
Consider why these analogies are effective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: العلة الواحدة يجوز أن يصدر عنها أكثر من معلول واحد عندنا خلافا للفلاسفة والمعتزلة. لنا أن الجسمية تقتضى الحصول في المكان وقبول الأعراض. احتجوا بأن مفهوم كونه مصدرا لأحد المعلولين غير مفهوم كونه مصدراً للأخرى، فالمفهومان المتغايران إن كانا داخلين في ماهية المصدر لم يكن المصدر مفرداً بل يكون مركبا، وإن كانا خارجين كانا معلولين فيكون الكلام في كيفية صدورهما عنه كالكلام فى الأول فيفضى إلى التسلسل، وإن كان أحدهما داخلا والآخر خارجاً كانت الماهية مركبة لأن الداخل هو جزء الماهية وما له جزء كان مركباً، وكان المعلول أيضاً واحداً لأن الداخل لا يكون معلولا. والجواب: أن مؤثرية الشىء فى الشيء ليست صفة ثبوتية على ما بيناه، وإذا كان كذلك بطل أن يقال إنه جزء الماهية أو خارج عنها. والذى يدل عليه وهو أن مفهوم كون النقطة محاذية لهذه النقطة من الدائرة غير مفهوم كونها محاذية للنقطة الأخرى، ولم يلزم من تغاير هذه المفهومات كون النقطة مركبة وكذا مفهوم كون الألف ليس ( ب ) مغايراً لمفهوم ليس ( ج ) ولم يلزم من تغير هذه السلوب وقوع الكثرة في الماهية فكذا ههنا.
Issue: A single cause is permissible to produce more than one effect, according to us, contrary to the philosophers and the Mu'tazila. Our proof: Corporeality necessitates being in place and receiving accidents. They objected: The concept of it being a source for one of the two effects is not the same as the concept of it being a source for the other. If the two different concepts are included in the essence of the source, then the source would not be simple but composite. If they are outside, then they become effects, so the discussion about how they proceed from it would be like the first discussion, leading to infinite regress. If one is inside and the other outside, then the essence is composite because the inside is part of the essence, and what has a part is composite, and the effect would also be one because the inside is not an effect. The answer: That a thing is effective in a thing is not a positive attribute, as we have explained. If so, it becomes invalid that it is said to be part of the essence or outside it. And what indicates this is that the concept of a point being opposite this point on a circle is not the same as the concept of it being opposite another point, and yet from the difference of these concepts it does not follow that the point is composite. Similarly, the concept of 'not B' is different from the concept of 'not C', and from the difference of these negations the occurrence of multiplicity in the essence does not follow. So also here.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage defends the Ash'ari position that one cause can produce multiple effects. Razi's proof gives the example of corporeality (jismiyya) which causes both location and accident-reception. The opponent objects using a conceptual argument: if the cause is simple, its being the source of different effects would introduce composition. Razi's answer is that effectiveness is not a real attribute, so multiple effect-concepts do not imply composition in the cause. He illustrates with a point on a circle: being opposite point A and being opposite point B are different concepts, but the point remains simple. Similarly, negations like 'not B' and 'not C' are different but don't multiply the essence.

Key Terms
علة واحدة
معلول
مؤثرية
صفة ثبوتية
نقطة
محاذاة
سلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a cause produces multiple effects, it must have multiple distinct aspects or parts in its essence.
Correction: Razi shows that the multiplicity is conceptual, not real. Effectiveness is not a real attribute, so different effect-concepts do not imply any composition in the cause.

Checkpoint
What example does Razi use to show that different concepts do not imply composition in the subject?
A) Fire producing heat and light
B) A point on a circle being opposite two different points
C) A father having multiple children
D) The sun illuminating and warming

Key Takeaway
A single cause can produce multiple effects because effectiveness is not a positive attribute, so the conceptual distinction of effects does not imply real composition in the cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of the countless blessings in your life—each one distinct—do you ever doubt that one God alone can be their source? This lesson teaches that conceptual diversity does not imply composition in the Cause. Search your heart: Do you truly entrust every need to Allah alone, or do you subconsciously assign different parts of your life to different imagined sources? Commit today to see His hand in everything, without dividing His oneness.
?
The philosophers argued that a simple cause cannot produce multiple effects without becoming composite. Have you ever imposed this kind of logical limitation on Allah, thinking that His ability to do many things somehow requires a complex nature? Examine your assumptions: Do you believe that Allah's power is absolute, or do you mentally restrict it by human reasoning? Repent of any subtle reduction of His majesty and affirm that He creates all things by a single, simple will.
?
Razi uses a point on a circle as an analogy: a single point can be opposite many points without being composite. Consider your relationship with Allah. Do you relate to Him as a single, simple Reality, or do you compartmentalize His attributes—His mercy here, His justice there? How can you cultivate a heart that sees all His actions as flowing from one undivided essence? Ask Allah to grant you a unified vision of His oneness in every aspect of your life.

Two Similar Effects May Have Two Different Causes
Imam Razi argues for the permissibility of two different causes for two similar effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's argument that two similar effects can be caused by two different causes, and his response to the objection that similarity would require identical causes.

Core Idea
Similar effects do not necessarily share the same cause; specification comes from the cause side.

Imam Razi addresses a key issue in causality: Can two similar effects be produced by two distinct causes? He challenges the majority Ash'ari view by using the example of blackness and whiteness, which share the property of being opposites yet arise from different causes. This lesson unpacks his proof, the objection, and his decisive answer.

Guided Path
1
Understand the issue
Recognize that Imam Razi asserts the permissibility of two different causes for two similar effects, contrary to the majority of his companions.
This is a mas'ala (doctrinal issue) in kalam.
2
Examine the proof and objection
Razi uses blackness and whiteness as an example. The opponents argue that dependence on a cause either follows from the essence or its concomitants, leading to a dilemma.
Note how the objection attempts to force a necessary link between effect and cause.
3
Grasp the answer
Razi distinguishes between the effect's need for an absolute cause and the cause's specification by its own side, rebutting the objection.
This distinction is key to understanding Razi's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : المعلولان المتماثلان يجوز تعليلهما بعلتين مختلفتين خلافا لأكثر أصحابنا
لنا : أن السواد والبياض مع اختلافهما يشتركان في المخالفة والمضادة
احتجوا بأن افتقار المعلول إلى العلة المدينة إن كان لماهيته أو لشىء من لوازمها وجب في كل ما يساوى ذلك المعلول أن يفتقر إلى مثل تلك العلة، وإن لم يكن لشيء من لوازم تلك الماهية كانت تلك الماهية غنية عن تلك العلة، والغنى عن العلة يستحيل تعليله به.
والجواب : أن المعلول لماهية مفتقر إلى مطلق العلة وتعين العلة إنما جاء من جانب العلة لا من جانب المعلول (۲)
(1) أقول: هذا صحيح إذا كان المراد من الاجتماع والاستقبال كون كل واحد من العلتين تاما وبالفعل أى مشتملا هذا على العلل الأربع وشرائطها
Issue: Two similar effects may be caused by two different causes, contrary to most of our companions. Our proof: Blackness and whiteness, despite their difference, share in opposition and contrariety. They objected: If the dependence of the effect on the specific cause is for its essence or for something from its necessary concomitants, then it is necessary that everything equal to that effect depend on the like of that cause; and if it is not for something from the necessary concomitants of that essence, then that essence is independent of that cause, and independence from the cause makes it impossible to be caused by it. The answer: The effect, by its essence, is dependent on the absolute cause, and the specification of the cause comes only from the side of the cause, not from the side of the effect (2). (1) I say: This is correct if what is meant by conjunction and confrontation is that each of the two causes is complete and actual, i.e., comprising the four causes and their conditions.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents a mas'ala (doctrinal issue) where Imam Razi maintains that two similar effects may legitimately be caused by two different causes, opposing the majority of his Ash'ari companions. He begins with a proof (lana): blackness and whiteness, though different colors, share the property of being opposite or contrary to something else, yet they are caused by distinct causes. The opponents (احتجوا) argue that if an effect depends on a specific cause for its essence or its necessary concomitants, then any identical effect must depend on the same cause; if not, the effect is independent, and an independent thing cannot be caused. Imam Razi's answer (الجواب) distinguishes between the essence of the effect, which requires only an absolute cause, and the specific determination of that cause, which arises from the cause itself, not from the effect. The footnote (أقول) clarifies that this argument holds when both causes are complete and actual, encompassing all four causes (material, formal, efficient, final) and their conditions.

Key Terms
المعلول
العلة
الماهية
المخالفة
المضادة
اللوازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that similarity of effects necessitates similarity of causes.
Correction: Razi shows that specification depends on the cause, not the effect, so two similar effects can have different causes.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, why do blackness and whiteness not require the same cause?
A) Because they are completely different in essence.
B) Because they share only accidental properties.
C) Because the effect only needs an absolute cause, and specification comes from the cause.
D) Because they are not really effects.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi argues that two similar effects can have different causes because the effect's essence only requires an absolute cause, while the specific cause is determined by the cause itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily judgments, how often do you assume that similar outcomes (like two people's successes or failures) must have identical causes? Where in your life are you failing to see the unique, cause-side specification that Razi insists upon?
?
When you look at your own sins and good deeds, do you tend to attribute them to your own essence or to the specific causes Allah has placed? How does Razi's distinction between absolute dependence on Allah and specification from the cause side challenge your view of personal responsibility?
?
If the specification of causes comes entirely from the cause side, not from the effect, how does that reshape your understanding of Allah's absolute sovereignty over every detail of your life? Are you truly willing to accept that nothing in your character or circumstances is self-determined, but rather wholly specified by its cause?

intermediate
8 min
One Cause May Produce Multiple Effects
Imam Razi argues that one cause can produce multiple effects and refutes the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that a single cause can produce multiple effects, and how he responds to the objection of infinite regress.

Core Idea
One cause can produce many effects because effectiveness is not a real attribute.

In this lesson, we encounter a key dispute in Islamic theology: can a single cause produce more than one effect? Imam Razi argues that it can, using the example of corporeality, and he responds to a sophisticated objection about composition and infinite regress. This passage clarifies the nature of causality and the simplicity of the divine essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it, then read the full translation. Let the author's own argument structure come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the trilemma presented by the opponents.
2
Identify the main proof
Razi's proof (lana) is that corporeality (الجسمية) necessitates both being in place and receiving accidents—two distinct effects from one attribute. Recognize how this demonstrates his claim.
3
Understand the opponent's trilemma
The philosophers and Mu'tazila object with three possibilities: if the concepts of being a source for each effect are part of the cause's essence (compositeness), external (regress), or one internal/one external (compositeness and only one effect). Make sure you can articulate each horn.
This is a classic kalam objection using composition and regress.
4
Analyze Razi's answer
Razi denies that effectiveness (مؤثرية) is a positive attribute; hence it cannot be part of the essence or external. He supports this with analogies: a point on a circle can be aligned with multiple points without being composite, and negations like 'A is not B' and 'A is not C' don't make A composite.
This answer shows that multiplicity of conceptual relations does not imply multiplicity in the essence.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this matters: if a single cause—especially God—could only produce one effect, that would limit divine power. Razi's position upholds God's ability to create multiple things directly.
This connects to tawhid and divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : العلة الواحدة يجوز أن يصدر عنها أكثر من معلول واحد عندنا خلافا للفلاسفة والمعتزلة
لنا أن الجسمية تقتضى الحصول في المكان وقبول الأعراض .
احتجوا بأن مفهوم كونه مصدرا لأحد المعلولين غير مفهوم كونه مصدراً للأخرى ، فالمفهومان المتغايران إن كانا داخلين في ماهية المصدر لم يكن المصدر مفرداً بل يكون مركبا ، وإن كانا خارجين كانا معلولين فيكون الكلام في كيفية صدورهما عنه كالكلام في الأول فيفضى إلى التسلسل ، وإن كان أحدهما داخلا والآخر خارجاً كانت الماهية مركبة لأن الداخل هو جزء الماهية وما له جزء كان مركباً ، وكان المعلول أيضاً واحداً لأن الداخل لا يكون معلولا .
والجواب: أن مؤثرية الشىء فى الشيء ليست صفة ثبوتية على ما بيناه ، وإذا كان كذلك بطل أن يقال إنه جزء الماهية أو خارج عنها (٣) . والذى يدل عليه وهو أن مفهوم كون النقطة محاذية لهذه النقطة من الدائرة غير مفهوم كونها محاذية للنقطة الأخرى ، ولم يلزم من تغاير هذه المفهومات كون النقطة مركبة وكذا مفهوم كون الألف ليس ( ب ) مغايراً لمفهوم ليس ( ج ) ولم يلزم من تغير هذه السلوب وقوع الكثرة في الماهية فكذا ههنا (4)
(3) أقول : الأشعرية قالوا الصفة الواحدة لا تقتضى أكثر من حكم واحد أما الذات الواحدة فلم يقولوا ذلك فيه إذ لم يقولوا بعلية عدا الضارب، والمعتزلة والفلاسفة قالوا بذلك في الذوات أيضاً وصاحب الكتاب خالف الكل والحصول في المكان وجودى و معلول للجسمية من باب التأثير وقبول الأعراض ليس بوجودى هنده وإن كان وجودياً لكنه من باب التأثر وهم لا يمنعون كون العلة الواحدة مع كونها فاعلة كونها منفعلة فليس هذا الدليل بصحيح ودليلهم غير مبنى على كون المؤثرية ثبوتية بل مفهومه أن مؤثرية المؤثر الواحد في أثر لا يكون من جهة مؤثريته فى غير ذلك الأثر ثم الجهتان إما داخلين أو غير داخلتان إلى آخره (٤) أقول : الإضافة والسلب لا يعقلان في شيء واحد وعندهم إن العلة الواحدة لا يصدر عنها شيئان من حيث أنها واحدة ولا يمنعون صدور شيئين يقبلهما قابلان عنها فلا يتوجه النقض بالإضافة والسلب عليهم
Issue: One cause may produce more than one effect according to us, contrary to the philosophers and Mu'tazila. Our proof: Corporeality necessitates being in place and receiving accidents. They objected: The concept of its being a source for one effect is not the concept of its being a source for another; if the two differing concepts are included in the essence of the source, the source would not be simple but composite; if they are external, they would be effects, and the discussion about how they proceed from it would be like the first discussion, leading to infinite regress; if one is internal and the other external, the essence is composite because the internal is a part of the essence, and what has a part is composite, and the effect would also be one because the internal is not an effect. The answer: The effective-ness of a thing in another is not a positive attribute as we have explained; if so, it is false to say it is part of the essence or external to it (3). What indicates this is that the concept of a point being aligned with this point of the circle is not the concept of its being aligned with another point, and the difference of these concepts does not necessitate that the point is composite; similarly, the concept of 'A is not (B)' is different from the concept of 'is not (C)', and the difference of these negations does not entail multiplicity in the essence; so here (4). (3) I say: The Ash'aris said that a single attribute necessitates no more than one judgment; but as for a single essence, they did not say that, since they did not affirm causality except for the Agent; the Mu'tazila and philosophers said that also for essences, and the author disagreed with all. Being in place is existent and caused by corporeality on the mode of efficacy, and receiving accidents is not existent according to him, though if it were existent it would be on the mode of passivity; they do not deny that one cause, while being an agent, can be passive, so this proof is not sound. Their argument is not based on the positivity of effectiveness; rather its meaning is that the effectiveness of one agent in one effect is not from the aspect of its effectiveness in another effect; then the two aspects are either internal or external, etc. (4) I say: Relation and negation are not intelligible in one thing; according to them, one cause does not produce two things from the aspect of its being one, but they do not prevent two things that are received by two recipients from proceeding from it, thus the objection by relation and negation does not apply.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is another mas'ala where Imam Razi argues that a single cause can produce more than one effect, opposing the philosophers and Mu'tazila (who generally held that from one cause only one effect can proceed). His proof (lana) is that corporeality (الجسمية) alone necessitates both location in place and reception of accidents—two distinct effects. The opponents object with a trilemma: if the concepts of being a source for each effect are part of the cause's essence, the cause is composite; if external, the concepts themselves become effects (leading to regress); if one internal and one external, the cause is composite and only one effect results. Razi's answer is that the property of being effective (مؤثرية) is not a positive attribute; therefore, it cannot be said to be part of the essence or external. He supports this with analogies: a single point on a circle can be aligned with multiple points simultaneously without being composite; similarly, the negation of different predicates (e.g., 'A is not B' vs 'A is not C') does not make the subject composite. The editorial notes clarify the positions of Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, and philosophers, and note that the author's view here is novel.

Key Terms
العلة الواحدة
المعلول
المؤثرية
الجسمية
الماهية
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Effectiveness is a positive attribute that, if multiple, would make the cause composite.
Correction: Imam Razi argues that effectiveness (مؤثرية) is not a positive attribute; therefore, having multiple conceptual relations to effects does not entail composition in the cause.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Imam Razi's proof for a single cause producing multiple effects?
A) Blackness and whiteness require different causes.
B) Corporeality necessitates both location and accident reception.
C) A point cannot be aligned with two points.
D) Effectiveness is a positive attribute.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi demonstrates that a single cause can produce multiple effects because the ability to produce effects is not a real attribute that would cause composition or infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's power, do you ever subconsciously limit it by imagining it like a human tool that can only do one thing at a time? How can Razi's argument that effectiveness is not a real attribute free you from that limitation? Will you today repent for any doubt you've had about God's ability to handle all your affairs simultaneously?
?
The opponents argued that one cause cannot produce multiple effects because it would require composition in God. In your own life, have you ever felt that God's actions in different areas of your life seem to contradict His unity? How does Razi's answer help you trust that God's simplicity does not conflict with His comprehensive action? Commit to not using your limited logic to judge God's actions this week.
?
Razi uses the example of a point aligned with multiple points to show that conceptual multiplicity does not require real multiplicity. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes confuse conceptual distinctions (like God's mercy and justice) for real separation? How can you train yourself to see them as aspects of one simple reality? This week, when you face a situation where you feel God's mercy and justice conflict, will you remember Razi's point and trust in God's simplicity?

Rational Cause May Require a Separate Condition
Imam Razi argues that rational causes may need separate conditions; editor clarifies Ash'ari views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's argument that a rational cause can depend on a separate condition for its effect, and how the Ash'ari position on attributes (essential vs. meaningful) relates to this issue.

Core Idea
A rational cause may need an external condition to produce its effect.

Imam Razi presents a challenge to his Ash'ari companions: even if a substance necessitates receiving all accidents, each accident requires the absence of its opposite—a separate condition. The editor then explains that Ash'aris who affirm states hold that meanings necessitate judgments unconditionally, and they distinguish essential from meaningful attributes. This lesson unpacks both the proof and the broader theological context.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp Razi's argument and the editor's clarification.
The editor's note (1) is part of the source.
2
Identify Razi's proof
Find the claim that substance necessitates receiving all accidents, and the condition that each accident is valid only when its opposite is absent.
3
Understand the Ash'ari response
The editor explains that Ash'aris who affirm states have a limited set of meanings; corporeality is not a meaning, so Razi's example doesn't refute them.
4
Distinguish essential vs. meaningful attributes
Essential attributes (like spatiality) remain as long as the thing exists; meaningful attributes (like knowledge) are caused by a meaning. This is key to understanding the Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : العلة العقلية يجوز أن يتوقف إيجابها لأثرها على شرط منفصل خلافا لأصحابنا
لنا أن الجوهر يوجب قبول الأعراض بأسرها لكن صحة كل عرض مشروط بانتفاء ضده عن المحل
(1) أقول : نفاة الأحوال من الأشاعرة لا يقولون بالعلة والمعلول ومثبتوها يقولون بالمعاني الموجبة للأحكام في محالها وهى عندهم علل تلك الأحكام وإيجابها لا يتوقف على شرط ، والجوهرية عندهم ليست من المعاني ولا يرد عليهم بها نقض بل المعاني عندهم محصورة وذلك أن الصفات عندهم إما صفات نفسية وإما صفات معنوية أما النفسية فهى ما يلزم نفس الموصوف ويبقى معها ما بقيت كالتحيز للجوهر والمعنوية ما تكون معللة بمعنى كالحياة المعللة بالعلم والعلم عندهم معنى هو علة ليكون محله عالما ، ومخالفة صاحب الكتاب إياهم من غير تعرض لموضع الخلاف
Issue: A rational cause may depend for its causing its effect on a separate condition, contrary to our companions. Our proof: Substance necessitates the reception of all accidents, but the validity of each accident is conditional upon the absence of its opposite in the subject. (1) I say: Among the Ash'aris, those who deny states (ahwal) do not affirm cause and effect, while those who affirm states speak of meanings that necessitate judgments in their loci, and those meanings are for them the causes of those judgments, and their causing does not depend on a condition. Corporeality for them is not among the meanings, so no objection applies to them from it; rather meanings for them are limited. For attributes are either essential (nafsiyya) or meaningful (ma'nawiyya): essential attributes are those that necessarily accompany the described thing and remain as long as it remains, like spatiality for substance; meaningful attributes are those caused by a meaning, like life caused by knowledge; and knowledge for them is a meaning that is the cause for its locus to be knowing. The author's disagreement with them is without addressing the point of disagreement.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This mas'ala addresses whether a rational cause (like a substance's nature) can require an external condition to produce its effect, contrary to most Ash'ari companions. Razi's proof: a substance (الجوهر) necessarily enables reception of all accidents, but each accident is only valid when its opposite is absent from the subject—this absence is a separate condition. The editorial note explains that Ash'aris who affirm states (مثبتو الأحوال) hold that meanings (معان) necessitate judgments unconditionally; corporeality is not such a meaning, so Razi's example does not refute them. The note also clarifies the distinction between essential and meaningful attributes.

Key Terms
العلة العقلية
الشرط المنفصل
الجوهر
الأعراض
الأحوال
الصفات النفسية
الصفات المعنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A rational cause always produces its effect immediately and unconditionally.
Correction: Imam Razi argues that even a substance's nature to receive all accidents depends on the condition that the opposite of each accident is absent from the subject.

Checkpoint
What is the condition mentioned for an accident to be valid in a substance?
A) The substance must be corporeal.
B) The opposite of the accident must be absent from the locus.
C) The accident must be caused by a meaning.
D) The cause must be simple.

Key Takeaway
A rational cause may require a separate condition for its effect, as seen in substance requiring absence of opposite for accidents, though Ash'aris who affirm states limit meanings to those that necessitate judgments unconditionally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the causes in your life—your health, your provision, your knowledge—do you assume they produce their effects automatically? How does Razi's point that even rational causes may need a separate condition shatter your assumption of self-sufficiency? Will you now look for the conditions Allah has placed, and turn to Him as the ultimate condition-giver?
?
The Ash'ari distinction tells you that your own attributes (like knowledge) are not independent causes but meanings created by Allah. Do you live as if your knowledge, power, or life are your own, or as if they are continually dependent on His will? What would change in your prayer or reliance if you truly felt this dependence?
?
If the absence of an opposite is necessary for an accident to exist, then every good in your life is preserved only by Allah's preventing its opposite. When was the last time you thanked Allah not just for what you have, but for what He has kept away? Will you start today to count the hidden conditions of grace?
