A Rational Cause May Be Composite
Imam Razi argues for composite rational causes and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's argument that a rational cause can be composite and how the Ash'ari perspective reduces the disagreement to a verbal one.

Core Idea
A composite cause can produce an effect that its parts alone cannot.

Imam Razi presents a key issue in causality: can a rational cause be made up of multiple components? He argues yes, against his Ash'ari companions. In this passage, you will see his proof using two examples, the objection raised, and a brief refutation. An editorial note clarifies that the apparent disagreement may be merely verbal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: claim, proof, objection, answer, and editorial comment.
The passage includes both Razi's original text and a later editorial note.
2
Identify the claim and proof
Razi claims a rational cause can be composite. His proof: knowledge of each premise alone does not give the conclusion, but both together do. Similarly, each unit does not give 'tenness', but the collection does.
3
Understand the objection and answer
The opponents argue: if a single part does not cause, then the whole also cannot, because the essence of the parts remains unchanged. Razi's answer is a simple 'refutation' (naqd) – he rejects the analogy without elaboration here.
The refutation is left unexplained, but the editorial note provides context.
4
Analyze the editorial clarification
The note explains that Ash'aris deny real causality; they see knowledge of the conclusion as directly created by God. Hence, Razi's examples do not apply to them. Also, the units 'tenness' example is actually identity, not composition.
This shows the disagreement may be about definitions, not substance.
5
Consider the doctrinal implications
Reflect on how this issue relates to the broader debate on causality in kalam. Does the verbal nature of the dispute mean there is no real conflict? What does this tell us about the role of composite causes in rational arguments?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : العلة العقلية يجوز أن تكون مركبة عندنا خلافا لأصحابنا
لنا أن العلم بكل واحد من المقدمتين لا يستلزم العلم بالنتيجة والعلم بهما يوجب العلم بالنتيجة ، وكذا كل واحد من آحاد العشرة لا يوجب صفة العشرية ومجموع تلك الآحاد يوجب العشرية . واحتجوا عليه بأن واحداً لما لم يوجب فالمجموع لا يوجب أيضاً لأن الماهية باقية كما كانت . والجواب النقض - وبالله التوفيق (۳)
(۲) أقول : قد مر أن الأشاعرة لا يقولون بعلية العلم بالمقدمات للعلم بالنتيجة بل يقولون إن الله يخلق العلم بالنتيجة على سبيل إجراء العادة وكل ما يورده المصنف في هذا الموضع مثالا للعلة ليس عندهم علة وأما مجموع الآحاد فهو نفس العشرة ، والأمثال عندهم المعاني المذكورة وليس شيء منها بمركب ، فإذا هذا الخلاف يرجع إلى اللفظ .
Issue: A rational cause may be composite according to us, contrary to our companions. Our proof: Knowledge of each of the two premises does not necessitate knowledge of the conclusion, but knowledge of both together necessitates knowledge of the conclusion. Similarly, each single unit of ten does not necessitate the property of tenness, but the collection of those units necessitates tenness. They objected: Since one does not necessitate, the collection also does not necessitate, because the essence remains as it was. The answer is by refutation – and with God is success (3). (2) I say: It has already passed that the Ash'aris do not affirm that knowledge of premises is the cause of knowledge of the conclusion; rather they say that God creates knowledge of the conclusion as a customary occurrence. And everything the author presents in this place as an example of a cause is not a cause according to them. As for the collection of units, it is the very tenness; and the examples for them are the mentioned meanings, none of which is composite. Thus this disagreement reduces to a verbal one.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This mas'ala argues that a rational cause can be composite, i.e., multiple items together produce an effect that none individually produce. Razi's proof: knowledge of both premises together gives knowledge of the conclusion, but each premise alone does not. Likewise, individual units do not give 'tenness', but the collection does. The opponents object that if each part fails to necessitate, the whole also fails because the essence of the parts remains. Razi's answer is a refutation (النقض), meaning he rejects the analogy. The editorial note explains that Ash'aris reject that knowledge of premises is a real cause; they see the conclusion's knowledge as a direct creation by God. Therefore, the example of premises doesn't apply. Similarly, the collection of units is identical to tenness, not a composite cause. Hence the disagreement is merely verbal.

Key Terms
العلة المركبة
المقدمتين
النتيجة
العشرة
النقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the parts of a cause do not individually produce an effect, then the whole cannot produce it either.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that a composite cause can produce an emergent effect that its parts do not, as in knowledge of premises and tenness. The objection is refuted by recognizing that the whole is not merely the sum of parts causally.

Checkpoint
What example does Imam Razi use to show that a composite cause can have an effect not produced by its parts?
A) Substance and accidents.
B) Two similar effects with different causes.
C) Knowledge of two premises leading to the conclusion.
D) A single point aligned with two points.

Key Takeaway
A rational cause can be composite, but the apparent disagreement with the Ash'aris may be rooted in differing definitions of causality rather than a substantive theological divide.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that combining separate truths can produce a conviction that neither alone can. What two truths about Allah have you held separately but never truly combined in your heart? What conclusion would that combination force you to act on today?
?
Razi's argument that a composite cause has an effect beyond its parts mirrors how our deeds can combine for spiritual outcomes. Which spiritual 'units' (like prayer and charity) have you kept apart, and what 'tenness' have you missed by not combining them? Will you combine them this week?
?
The editorial note suggests the disagreement between Razi and the Ash'aris may be merely verbal – different words for the same reality. Are there arguments you hold onto because of pride in a label rather than truth? Can you humbly re-examine a theological position you assume is substantive but might be just wording?

The Intellectual Cause May Require a Condition
Razi states that an intellectual cause may need a separate condition for its effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that an intellectual cause can require a separate condition for its effect, and why this matters for causal reasoning in kalam.

Core Idea
An intellectual cause may need a separate condition to produce its effect.

In this brief but pivotal passage, Imam al-Razi articulates a key principle about intellectual causes: they do not always necessitate their effect unconditionally. He provides a concrete example from the relationship between a substance and its accidents to demonstrate that a separate condition (absence of an opposite) can be required. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for aqeedah.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
Read the opening sentence carefully: Razi states his position that an intellectual cause may require a condition for its effect, contrary to some opponents. This is the thesis.
Note that 'cause' here is 'illa 'aqliyya, not a physical cause.
2
Examine the proof
Read the proof: a substance (jawhar) necessitates receiving all accidents, but each accident's soundness depends on the absence of its opposite. This shows the condition's role.
The substance is the cause, and the condition (absence of opposite) is separate from it.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters: it refines the concept of causality in kalam, showing that effects can depend on additional conditions even when the cause is present.
This principle affects arguments about divine attributes and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : العلة العقلية يجوز أن يتوقف إيجابها لأثرها على شرط منفصل خلافا لأصحابنا. لنا أن الجوهر يوجب قبول الأعراض بأسرها لكن صحة كل عرض مشروط بانتفاء ضده عن المحل.
Issue: The intellectual cause may have its effect's entailment contingent upon a separate condition, contrary to our companions. Our proof: The substance necessitates the reception of all accidents, but the soundness of each accident is conditioned by the absence of its opposite in the locus.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a mas'ala (issue) stating a position about intellectual causes (al-illa al-aqliyya). It begins with the claim that an intellectual cause may require a separate condition for its entailment of its effect, contrary to the view of the author's opponents (أصحابنا, likely the Ash'ari school). Then it provides a proof: a substance (jawhar) necessitates the acceptance of all accidents, but each accident's validity depends on the absence of its opposite in its locus. This illustrates that the cause (the substance) does not entail its effect unconditionally, thus supporting the possibility of a condition.

Key Terms
العلة العقلية
شرط منفصل
الجوهر
الأعراض
ضد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that an intellectual cause always necessitates its effect without any condition.
Correction: Razi shows that even an intellectual cause (like substance) can require a separate condition (like absence of opposite) for its effect to occur.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue about the intellectual cause (العلة العقلية)?
A) It never requires a condition for its effect.
B) It may require a separate condition for its effect.
C) It is always composite.
D) It is identical to its effect.

Key Takeaway
An intellectual cause does not necessarily entail its effect unconditionally; separate conditions may be required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a good deed must bring immediate reward, only to feel disappointed when it didn't? How does this lesson's teaching about causes requiring conditions reshape your expectation of God's response?
?
Think of a situation where you relied on a means (like effort, skill, or prayer) and expected a certain outcome. How might a separate condition—unknown to you—have been necessary for that outcome, and how does this lesson affect your reliance on God alone?
?
Do you live as if God's commands are unconditional causes of success, or do you acknowledge that His decrees may depend on hidden conditions? What condition might you be neglecting right now that is preventing an effect you seek?

intermediate
5 min
The Intellectual Cause May Be Composite
Razi argues for composite intellectual cause with examples and rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that an intellectual cause can be composite because the whole has properties beyond the parts.

Core Idea
The composite cause has an effect greater than the sum of its parts.

Imam Razi presents a brief but powerful argument in defense of composite intellectual causes. He uses two analogies—premises and the number ten—to show that a combination of elements can produce an effect that none of the parts alone could. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and the objection he answers.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the original Arabic text once. Try to identify the main claim, the two examples, the objection, and the response.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the proof
Notice that the proof consists of two parallel examples: knowledge from two premises vs. one, and the number ten vs. its units. How do these examples show the same idea?
Both demonstrate that a composite can have a property that its parts lack.
3
Examine the objection
The opponent says that since each part individually does not cause, the combination also cannot cause because the essence of the parts remains unchanged. Why is this objection plausible?
Think about the principle 'the whole is nothing but the sum of its parts.'
4
Grasp the rebuttal
Razi answers with al-naqd (rebuttal). He points out that the essence of the whole is not simply the essence of the parts; a new relational property emerges. Can you think of an example where the whole has a property not in any part?
Consider a circle: individual points have no curvature, but the circle does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : العلة العقلية يجوز أن تكون مركبة عندنا خلافا لأصحابنا. لنا أن العلم بكل واحد من المقدمتين لا يستلزم العلم بالنتيجة والعلم بهما يوجب العلم بالنتيجة، وكذا كل واحد من آحاد العشرة لا يوجب صفة العشرية ومجموع تلك الآحاد يوجب العشرية. واحتجوا عليه بأن واحداً لما لم يوجب فالمجموع لا يوجب أيضاً لأن الماهية باقية كما كانت. والجواب النقض - وبالله التوفيق.
Issue: The intellectual cause may be composite according to us, contrary to our companions. Our proof: Knowledge of each single premise does not entail knowledge of the conclusion, but knowledge of both entails knowledge of the conclusion. Similarly, each single unit of ten does not entail the property of ten-ness, while the sum of those units entails ten-ness. They argued that since a single thing does not entail, the sum also does not entail because the essence remains as it was. The answer is rebuttal (al-naqd). And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This is another mas'ala about the permissibility of a composite intellectual cause. The author argues that a cause can be composed of multiple parts, because individual premises alone do not give knowledge of the conclusion, but together they do. Similarly, individual units do not give the property of ten, but the sum does. The opponents object that if each part does not cause, the whole cannot cause because the essence is unchanged. The author answers with a rebuttal (al-naqd), meaning he points out that the opponents' reasoning fails because the composite has a different property than the parts.

Correction: The composite introduces a new relational property (composition) that is not present in any part alone, so the whole can have causal powers beyond the parts.

?
The author's rebuttal is swift: the composite changes everything. Think of your relationships—are you treating them as mere assemblies of separate interactions, or do you recognize that the union creates something entirely new that demands a new response from you?

Critique of the proof against infinite regress
Imam Razi critiques the standard proof against infinite regress using the application argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical gap Imam Razi identifies in the standard proof against infinite regress.

Core Idea
Razi shows that an infinite series could be self-causing, undermining the need for an external cause.

Imam Razi critically examines the classic argument that an infinite regress is impossible because the collection of infinite things would need an external cause. His analysis reveals a subtle logical flaw: the proof fails to exclude the possibility that the infinite individuals themselves could collectively serve as the cause. This passage is a masterclass in precise logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical steps.
2
Identify the original argument
Identify what the original proof claimed: that the collection of non-finite things requires an external cause because each individual is not a cause for itself.
3
Follow Razi's counter
Notice Razi's response: from the fact that individuals are not causes alone, it does not follow that they collectively cannot be the cause. The cause could be internal.
4
Understand the conclusion
Razi concludes that the original argument does not prove its aim (an external cause). The case remains open.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) أقول : في إبطال التسلسل موضع نظر ، وذلك أنه أثبت لمجموع الأمور الغير المتنزهية مؤثرا بسبب احتياج المجموع إلى آحاده وإنما يجب من ذلك أن يكون للمجموع مؤثرات لانهاية لها هي الآحاد ، وإذا لم يكن كل واحد من تلك الآحاد علة لنفسه ولا للعلة يلزم أن لا يكون علة بانفراده المجموع ، ولا يلزم أن لا يكون سائر الآحاد علة بل الحق ذلك وحينئذ يكون عامل المجموع داخلة فيه، ولا يلزم من ذلك أن يكون هو مع علة المجموع خارجة فلا يتم مطلوبه هو
(1) I say: In refuting infinite regress there is a point of consideration. Because he proved for the collection of non‑finite things an efficient cause due to the collection’s need for its individuals, and from that it only follows that the collection has infinite efficient causes which are the individuals. And if each one of those individuals is not a cause for itself nor for the cause, it follows that it is not a cause alone for the collection, but it does not follow that the rest of the individuals are not a cause—rather that is true. Then in that case the cause of the collection is included in it, and from that it does not follow that it [the cause] is external to the collection, so his aim is not achieved.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a critical note by Imam Razi on the proof against infinite regress presented earlier. He points out a flaw: the earlier proof argued that the collection of non‑finite things needs an external cause because each individual is not a cause for itself or for the entire collection. But Razi responds that this only shows that no single individual is the cause of the collection, not that the individuals collectively cannot be the cause. In fact, the cause of the collection could be the infinite series of individuals themselves, which would be internal, not external. Therefore, the conclusion that there must be an external cause does not follow from that reasoning alone.

Key Terms
تسلسل
علة
مؤثر
غير متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Razi is rejecting the conclusion that an infinite regress is impossible, but he is only pointing out a logical gap in that particular argument.
Correction: Razi is critiquing the reasoning, not necessarily the conclusion. He shows that the argument as presented does not logically force acceptance of an external cause.

Checkpoint
What flaw does Imam Razi find in the earlier argument about the collection of non‑finite things?
A) The collection cannot be infinite
B) The individuals might collectively be the cause of the collection, so it need not be external
C) The cause must be prior in time
D) The proof relies on a false premise

Key Takeaway
Razi's critique reveals that the standard argument against infinite regress does not necessarily prove an external cause, because the infinite individuals themselves could be the cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a proof for God's existence that seems convincing, do you ever pause to examine it critically like Razi did, or do you accept it because it feels safe? What could be at stake if you found a flaw in that proof?

Razi was willing to challenge a well-known argument. Is there an intellectual or spiritual belief you hold that you have never truly questioned? Will you commit to examining it honestly, even if it makes you uncomfortable?
?
The idea that the universe might be self-causing can feel threatening. When a truth you rely on is challenged, does your heart react with fear or with trust that truth can withstand scrutiny? How will you respond the next time your beliefs are tested?

Objection: Possibility and the Need for a Cause
Objection and initial response about causal need

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a key objection to the kalam argument for a necessary being and the initial responses that restrict the need for a cause to temporal things.

Core Idea
The need for a cause arises from temporal origination, not from mere possibility.

This passage presents an objection to the standard Ash'ari position that every possible needs a cause. The objector proposes that because existence is more fitting for the possible being, it may not need a cause. The author begins his response by distinguishing between need due to origination and need due to possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first sentence of the Arabic passage: the objector proposes that the manager of the world, though possible, is better suited for existence, so it dispenses with a cause.
The objector uses the term 'أولى' (more appropriate) to argue for sufficiency.
2
Notice the first concession
The author concedes that necessity is like non-existence in relation to the possible being, but questions why it needs a cause. This sets up the dialectic.
'سلمنا' indicates a concession, but the author is not fully agreeing.
3
Understand the explanation
The author provides the explanation (بيانه) that the need for an efficient cause is due to temporal origination (حدوث), not possibility (إمكان). If the cause is eternal, it does not require a cause.
This is the core response: distinguishing between need due to origination vs. possibility.
4
See the second concession and challenge
The author concedes that some cause is necessary, but then asks why circularity (دور) is invalid, hinting at a deeper issue.
The full response continues in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال مدير العالم ممكن الوجود لكن الوجود به أولى فالأجل هذه الأولوية تستغ عن المؤثر ، سلمنا أن الوجوب بالنسبة إليه كا لعدم لكن لم قلت إنه يفتقر إلى السبب بيانه : أن علة الحاجة إلى المؤثر هو الحدوث لا الإمكان، فإذا كان ذلك المؤثر قديما لم يحتج إلى المؤثر سلمنا أنه لابد من سبب فلم قلت أن الدور باطل
It was said: Why is it not permissible to say that the manager of the world is a possible existent, but existence is more appropriate for it, and because of this appropriateness it dispenses with the efficient cause? We grant that necessity is like non‑existence in relation to it, but why do you say that it needs a cause? Explanation: The cause of the need for an efficient cause is occurrence, not possibility. So if that efficient cause is eternal, it does not need an efficient cause. We grant that there must be a cause, so why do you say that circularity is invalid?
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and begins the response. The objector suggests that the manager (supposed necessary being) is actually possible, but existence is more fitting for it, so it does not need a cause. The objector concedes that necessity is like non‑existence in relation to it (i.e., it is not necessary). Then the objector argues that the need for a cause arises from occurrence (huduth), not from possibility (imkan). Since the manager is eternal, it does not need a cause. The objector then tentatively accepts that some cause is needed but questions why circularity (dawr) is impossible. The author's response begins here but is cut off; the full response continues in the next lesson.

مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the need for a cause arises from possibility itself, regardless of temporal origination.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the need is due to temporal origination (حدوث), not mere possibility (إمكان).

Checkpoint
What reason does the objector give for the manager of the world not needing an efficient cause?
A) It is necessary in itself
B) Existence is more appropriate for it, so it dispenses with a cause
C) It is eternal and occurrence requires a cause
D) It is impossible

Key Takeaway
The objector attempts to remove the need for a cause by appealing to appropriateness, but the response shows that the need is grounded in temporal origination, not mere possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You were brought into existence after non-existence. Do you truly recognize that your existence is not self-sufficient? How does that awareness affect your reliance on the Creator today? Will you consciously attribute your next breath to Him, or do you assume it as a given?
?
The objector tried to bypass the need for a cause by appealing to 'appropriateness'. In your life, do you sometimes treat your blessings as 'appropriate' outcomes of your own efforts, forgetting the true cause? Confess a specific instance where you attributed success to your own merit. What would it mean to genuinely acknowledge the cause behind it?
?
If the need for a cause is due to origination, then every moment you exist is a continuous origination. How does that make you feel about your dependence on Allah? What practical step will you take today to act on that dependence?

Refuting Circularity: Cause and Priority
Razi refutes the circularity objection by distinguishing types of priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's refutation of the circularity objection by distinguishing temporal and essential priority.

Core Idea
Mutual causation is impossible because it would require each to be prior to itself.

In this passage, Imam Razi addresses an objection against the principle that cause is prior to effect. The objector argues that if two things were mutual causes, each would be prior to itself, implying absurdity. Razi clarifies that priority need not be temporal; it can be essential, meaning one is the efficient cause of the other. He challenges the objector to define what they mean by priority, showing that on any coherent definition, mutual causation leads to contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Notice the objector's claim: if cause is prior to effect, mutual causation would make each prior to itself.
2
Identify Razi's first response
Razi asks whether priority is meant in time or in essence. He argues temporal priority is false because cause and effect are simultaneous.
3
Understand essential priority
If priority means being the efficient cause, then mutual causation means each is cause of the other, which is impossible.
4
Consider the alternative
If the objector means another kind of priority, he must define it before the argument can proceed.
5
Conclusion on circularity
Razi grants circularity is invalid but challenges the objector to explain why infinite regress is also invalid.
This transitions to the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ولأن العلة قبل المعلول فيلزم أن يكون كل واحد منهما قبل نفسه قلنا : تدعى القبلية بالزمان أو بالذات أو بمعنى آخر فإن عنيت به الأول فهو باطل لأنه لا معنى لكون الشيء .ؤثرا فى الغير إلا صدور الأثر عنه على ما تقدم ، فقبل صدور الأثر عنه يستحيل أن يكون مؤثرا وإذا كان كذلك استحال تقدم العلة على المعلول بالزمان ، وإن عنيت به التقدم بالذات فنقول تعنى بالقدم بالذات كونه مؤثرا فيه، أو تعنى به أمرا آخر، فإن عنيت به المؤثر كان قولك لو كان كل واحد منهما مؤثرا في الآخر لكان كل واحد منهما متقدما على الآخر إلزاما للشيء على نفسه ، وإن عنيت به أمرا آخر فلابد من بيان ماهية ذلك التقدم ليتمكن من إقامة الدلالة على أن الشيء يستحيل أن يكون متقدما على نفسه بذلك المعنى، سلمنا فساد الدور فلم قلت أن التسلسل باطل . قوله : ذلك المجموع مفتقر إلى كل واحد من تلك الآحاد. قلنا: لا نسلم أنه يصح وصف تلك الأسباب والمسيبات بأنه مجموع وكل ، لأن هذه الألفاظ مشعرة بالتفاهي إطلاقها إلا بعد ثبوت التناهى وهو أول المسألة ، سلمنا أنه يصح وصفها بذلك لكنا نقول إن دل ما ذكرته على فساد التسلسل فههذا ما يدل على صحته فلا يصح بيانه : وهو أن هذه الحوادث المحسوسة لا بدلها من مؤثر فالمؤثر فيها إما أن يكون محدثا أو قديما فان كان مجدها فالسكلام فيها كالكلام فى الأول ، فإما أن يتسلسل فيكون ذلك اعترافا بصحة التسلسل أو ينتهى إلى قديم وذلك هو القسم الثاني من القسمين المذكورين . فنقول: تأثير ذلك القديم في ذلك الحادث إما أن يتوقف على شرط حادث أو لا يتوقف ، فإن لم يتوقف على شرط لزم من قدم المؤثر قدم هذا الحادث وإلا لكان نسبة صدور الأثر كنسبة لاصدوره عنه ، وإن لم يفتقر صدوره عنه إلى مرجح منفصل فقد ترجح الممكن لا عن سبب وذلك ليسد باب إثبات الصانع ، وإن افتقر لم يكن المؤثر التام قبل حصول ذلك المنفصل المرجح مؤثر! تاما هذا خلف ، وأما أن يتوقف على شرط فذلك الشرط إن كان قديما عاد الإشكال ، وإن كان محدثا فإما أن يكون مقارنا لذلك الحادث أو سابقا عليه فإن كان مقارنا في الكلام في حدوثه كالكلام فى الأول ، فإن كان شرط حدوثه هو الحادث الأول لزم الدور ، وإن كان شرط حدوثه حادثا آخر لزم التسلسل وأما إن كان شرط حدوث ذلك الحادث حادثا سابقا عليه فنقول حال حدوث ذلك .. ابق لم يكن القديم مؤثرا بالفعل فى الحادث اللاحق وعند فنائه يصير مؤثرا فيه بالفعل فتلك المؤثرية حكم حادث لا بد لها من مؤثر ، فإن كان هو الحادث الذى عدم الآن لزم تعليل الوجود بالعدم وهو محال ، الحادث الذي حدث به لزم الدور ، وإن كان حادثا آخر لزم التسلسل ، فظهر أنه لابد من التزام الفسلسل
His statement: Because the cause is prior to the effect, so it would follow that each of them is prior to itself. We say: Do you mean priority in time or in essence or another meaning? If you mean the first, it is false because there is no meaning to a thing being an efficient cause in another except the effect proceeding from it as previously mentioned. So before the effect proceeds from it, it is impossible for it to be an efficient cause. If that is so, it is impossible for the cause to be prior to the effect in time. If you mean priority in essence, then we say: Do you mean by priority in essence its being an efficient cause in it, or do you mean something else? If you mean the efficient cause, then your statement that if each of them were efficient in the other, each would be prior to the other is an imposition of a thing upon itself. If you mean something else, then you must explain the nature of that priority to be able to adduce evidence that a thing is impossible to be prior to itself in that sense. We grant the invalidity of circularity, so why do you say that infinite regress is invalid? His statement: That collection is dependent on each one of those individuals. We say: We do not accept that it is valid to describe those causes and effects as a collection and a whole, because these words imply finitude; they are not applied except after establishing finitude, which is the starting point of the issue. We grant that it is valid to describe them thus, but we say: If what you mentioned indicates the invalidity of infinite regress, then here is what indicates its validity, so it is not valid. Explanation: These sensible events must have an efficient cause; that efficient cause in them is either originated or eternal. If it is originated, then the discussion about it is like the discussion about the first. So either it leads to infinite regress, and that is an admission of the validity of infinite regress, or it terminates in an eternal, and that is the second of the two mentioned divisions. We say: The effect of that eternal on that event either depends on an originated condition or does not depend. If it does not depend on a condition, then from the eternity of the efficient cause follows the eternity of this event; otherwise the ratio of the effect's issuance would be like the ratio of its non-issuance from it. And if its issuance from it does not require a separate preponderator, then the possible would be preponderated without a cause, and that closes the door to proving the Maker. And if it does require, then the complete efficient cause is not a complete efficient cause before the occurrence of that separate preponderator—this is a contradiction. As for its depending on a condition, that condition, if eternal, the problem returns. If it is originated, then either concomitant with that event or prior to it. If it is concomitant, then the discussion about its occurrence is like the discussion about the first. If the condition of its occurrence is the first event, circularity follows. If the condition of its occurrence is another event, infinite regress follows. As for if the condition of the occurrence of that event is an event prior to it, we say: At the moment of the occurrence of that prior, the eternal is not actually efficient in the later event, and upon its passing it becomes actually efficient in it. That efficiency is an originated attribute, and it must have an efficient cause. If that is the event that has now perished, then it is explaining existence by non-existence, which is impossible. If it is the event that occurred by it, circularity follows. If it is another event, infinite regress follows. So it becomes clear that infinite regress must be accepted.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is an extended refutation by Imam Razi of two objections: circularity (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul). The objector first challenges the claim that cause must be prior to effect, suggesting that if two things were mutual causes, each would be prior to itself. Razi responds by distinguishing types of priority: temporal priority is not required because cause and effect are simultaneous in time; essential priority means the cause is the efficient cause of the effect. If that is what priority means, then mutual causation would lead to each being the cause of the other, which is absurd. If the objector means something else, he must define it. The objector then argues that the collection (of causes) is dependent on each individual, so the series has no external cause. Razi replies that the terms 'collection' and 'whole' presuppose finitude, which is what is being questioned. Even if we grant the description, Razi argues that the objector's reasoning can be turned around: from the existence of sensed events, we can argue that either the series is infinite or it ends in an eternal cause. If it is infinite, that is admitted; if it ends in an eternal, then we must examine how the eternal acts on originated events. Razi then goes through the scenarios: whether the eternal's action depends on a condition or not, whether the condition is eternal or originated, whether it is simultaneous or prior, and so on. In each case, he shows that either circularity or infinite regress follows, concluding that infinite regress is unavoidable for those who deny the first cause. The aim is to force the opponent to accept a first eternal cause without needing to reject infinite regress absolutely.

Key Terms
دور
علة
معلول
تقدم
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that cause must always be temporally prior to effect.
Correction: Razi argues that priority can be essential, not temporal; cause and effect can be simultaneous.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, if 'priority' is meant in the temporal sense, what follows?
A) The cause must be prior to effect in time
B) Cause and effect are simultaneous
C) Mutual causation is possible
D) Priority is impossible

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the circularity objection by distinguishing temporal and essential priority, showing that mutual causation is impossible on any coherent definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi insists on defining terms precisely before debating. When you argue about faith, do you often assume shared definitions? What misunderstanding in your own understanding of tawheed might be clarified by defining 'cause' or 'priority'? Commit to looking up one theological term this week.
?
The objector believed cause must be prior to effect, but Razi showed it's not necessary. In your life, do you assume that good deeds must precede reward, or that knowledge must precede action? What if they can happen together? How might that change your urgency in practicing Islam?
?
Razi's argument requires you to examine your own reasoning for circularity. Are there beliefs you hold that you justify by themselves (e.g., 'I know this because it's in the Qur'an, and I trust the Qur'an because it's from God, and I know it's from God because it says so')? Can you identify one potential circular reasoning in your own faith and commit to resolving it?

Refuting Infinite Regress: The Necessity of a First Cause
Razi refutes the infinite regress objection by showing it forces acceptance of infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's argument that rejecting the first cause leads to accepting infinite regress, and his analysis of conditions that inevitably leads to circularity or regress.

Core Idea
If you deny the first cause, you must accept infinite regress.

Imam Razi now turns to the objection that the collection of causes depends on each individual, implying no external cause. Razi counters that the very notion of 'collection' presupposes finitude. More importantly, he argues that if one does not accept a first eternal cause, then every scenario for how an eternal cause could act on originated events leads either to circularity or to infinite regress, forcing the opponent to admit infinite regress as necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the objector's statement
The objector says the collection of causes depends on each individual, so no external cause is needed.
2
Razi's first reply
Razi says that describing the series as a 'collection' or 'whole' implies finitude, which is what is disputed.
3
Razi's counter-argument
Even if we accept the terminology, Razi argues that we can use the same reasoning to prove infinite regress is necessary: from observed events, we must posit either an infinite chain or an eternal cause.
4
Analyzing the eternal cause
If we posit an eternal cause, its action either depends on a condition or not. Both lead to problems.
This is the core of the argument.
5
The condition scenarios
Razi examines each possibility: condition eternal or originated, simultaneous or prior, showing each leads to circularity or infinite regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ولأن العلة قبل المعلول فيلزم أن يكون كل واحد منهما قبل نفسه قلنا : تدعى القبلية بالزمان أو بالذات أو بمعنى آخر فإن عنيت به الأول فهو باطل لأنه لا معنى لكون الشيء .ؤثرا فى الغير إلا صدور الأثر عنه على ما تقدم ، فقبل صدور الأثر عنه يستحيل أن يكون مؤثرا وإذا كان كذلك استحال تقدم العلة على المعلول بالزمان ، وإن عنيت به التقدم بالذات فنقول تعنى بالقدم بالذات كونه مؤثرا فيه، أو تعنى به أمرا آخر، فإن عنيت به المؤثر كان قولك لو كان كل واحد منهما مؤثرا في الآخر لكان كل واحد منهما متقدما على الآخر إلزاما للشيء على نفسه ، وإن عنيت به أمرا آخر فلابد من بيان ماهية ذلك التقدم ليتمكن من إقامة الدلالة على أن الشيء يستحيل أن يكون متقدما على نفسه بذلك المعنى، سلمنا فساد الدور فلم قلت أن التسلسل باطل . قوله : ذلك المجموع مفتقر إلى كل واحد من تلك الآحاد. قلنا: لا نسلم أنه يصح وصف تلك الأسباب والمسيبات بأنه مجموع وكل ، لأن هذه الألفاظ مشعرة بالتفاهي إطلاقها إلا بعد ثبوت التناهى وهو أول المسألة ، سلمنا أنه يصح وصفها بذلك لكنا نقول إن دل ما ذكرته على فساد التسلسل فههذا ما يدل على صحته فلا يصح بيانه : وهو أن هذه الحوادث المحسوسة لا بدلها من مؤثر فالمؤثر فيها إما أن يكون محدثا أو قديما فان كان مجدها فالسكلام فيها كالكلام فى الأول ، فإما أن يتسلسل فيكون ذلك اعترافا بصحة التسلسل أو ينتهى إلى قديم وذلك هو القسم الثاني من القسمين المذكورين . فنقول: تأثير ذلك القديم في ذلك الحادث إما أن يتوقف على شرط حادث أو لا يتوقف ، فإن لم يتوقف على شرط لزم من قدم المؤثر قدم هذا الحادث وإلا لكان نسبة صدور الأثر كنسبة لاصدوره عنه ، وإن لم يفتقر صدوره عنه إلى مرجح منفصل فقد ترجح الممكن لا عن سبب وذلك ليسد باب إثبات الصانع ، وإن افتقر لم يكن المؤثر التام قبل حصول ذلك المنفصل المرجح مؤثر! تاما هذا خلف ، وأما أن يتوقف على شرط فذلك الشرط إن كان قديما عاد الإشكال ، وإن كان محدثا فإما أن يكون مقارنا لذلك الحادث أو سابقا عليه فإن كان مقارنا في الكلام في حدوثه كالكلام فى الأول ، فإن كان شرط حدوثه هو الحادث الأول لزم الدور ، وإن كان شرط حدوثه حادثا آخر لزم التسلسل وأما إن كان شرط حدوث ذلك الحادث حادثا سابقا عليه فنقول حال حدوث ذلك .. ابق لم يكن القديم مؤثرا بالفعل فى الحادث اللاحق وعند فنائه يصير مؤثرا فيه بالفعل فتلك المؤثرية حكم حادث لا بد لها من مؤثر ، فإن كان هو الحادث الذى عدم الآن لزم تعليل الوجود بالعدم وهو محال ، الحادث الذي حدث به لزم الدور ، وإن كان حادثا آخر لزم التسلسل ، فظهر أنه لابد من التزام الفسلسل
His statement: Because the cause is prior to the effect, so it would follow that each of them is prior to itself. We say: Do you mean priority in time or in essence or another meaning? If you mean the first, it is false because there is no meaning to a thing being an efficient cause in another except the effect proceeding from it as previously mentioned. So before the effect proceeds from it, it is impossible for it to be an efficient cause. If that is so, it is impossible for the cause to be prior to the effect in time. If you mean priority in essence, then we say: Do you mean by priority in essence its being an efficient cause in it, or do you mean something else? If you mean the efficient cause, then your statement that if each of them were efficient in the other, each would be prior to the other is an imposition of a thing upon itself. If you mean something else, then you must explain the nature of that priority to be able to adduce evidence that a thing is impossible to be prior to itself in that sense. We grant the invalidity of circularity, so why do you say that infinite regress is invalid? His statement: That collection is dependent on each one of those individuals. We say: We do not accept that it is valid to describe those causes and effects as a collection and a whole, because these words imply finitude; they are not applied except after establishing finitude, which is the starting point of the issue. We grant that it is valid to describe them thus, but we say: If what you mentioned indicates the invalidity of infinite regress, then here is what indicates its validity, so it is not valid. Explanation: These sensible events must have an efficient cause; that efficient cause in them is either originated or eternal. If it is originated, then the discussion about it is like the discussion about the first. So either it leads to infinite regress, and that is an admission of the validity of infinite regress, or it terminates in an eternal, and that is the second of the two mentioned divisions. We say: The effect of that eternal on that event either depends on an originated condition or does not depend. If it does not depend on a condition, then from the eternity of the efficient cause follows the eternity of this event; otherwise the ratio of the effect's issuance would be like the ratio of its non-issuance from it. And if its issuance from it does not require a separate preponderator, then the possible would be preponderated without a cause, and that closes the door to proving the Maker. And if it does require, then the complete efficient cause is not a complete efficient cause before the occurrence of that separate preponderator—this is a contradiction. As for its depending on a condition, that condition, if eternal, the problem returns. If it is originated, then either concomitant with that event or prior to it. If it is concomitant, then the discussion about its occurrence is like the discussion about the first. If the condition of its occurrence is the first event, circularity follows. If the condition of its occurrence is another event, infinite regress follows. As for if the condition of the occurrence of that event is an event prior to it, we say: At the moment of the occurrence of that prior, the eternal is not actually efficient in the later event, and upon its passing it becomes actually efficient in it. That efficiency is an originated attribute, and it must have an efficient cause. If that is the event that has now perished, then it is explaining existence by non-existence, which is impossible. If it is the event that occurred by it, circularity follows. If it is another event, infinite regress follows. So it becomes clear that infinite regress must be accepted.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is an extended refutation by Imam Razi of two objections: circularity (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul). The objector first challenges the claim that cause must be prior to effect, suggesting that if two things were mutual causes, each would be prior to itself. Razi responds by distinguishing types of priority: temporal priority is not required because cause and effect are simultaneous in time; essential priority means the cause is the efficient cause of the effect. If that is what priority means, then mutual causation would lead to each being the cause of the other, which is absurd. If the objector means something else, he must define it. The objector then argues that the collection (of causes) is dependent on each individual, so the series has no external cause. Razi replies that the terms 'collection' and 'whole' presuppose finitude, which is what is being questioned. Even if we grant the description, Razi argues that the objector's reasoning can be turned around: from the existence of sensed events, we can argue that either the series is infinite or it ends in an eternal cause. If it is infinite, that is admitted; if it ends in an eternal, then we must examine how the eternal acts on originated events. Razi then goes through the scenarios: whether the eternal's action depends on a condition or not, whether the condition is eternal or originated, whether it is simultaneous or prior, and so on. In each case, he shows that either circularity or infinite regress follows, concluding that infinite regress is unavoidable for those who deny the first cause. The aim is to force the opponent to accept a first eternal cause without needing to reject infinite regress absolutely.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the argument for a first cause requires rejecting infinite regress entirely.
Correction: Razi here argues that if you reject the first cause, you must accept infinite regress; he does not reject infinite regress as impossible but as a consequence of denying the first cause.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, if the eternal cause's action depends on an originated condition that is prior to the effect, what follows?
A) The eternal cause is complete before the condition
B) The condition must be eternal
C) The efficiency attribute itself is originated and requires a cause, leading to regress or circularity
D) The effect is eternal

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi demonstrates that denying a first eternal cause forces one to accept infinite regress, as every alternative leads to contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The proof shows that the eternal cannot act without conditions. How does this deepen your understanding that everything depends on Allah's will and power? Are there areas in your life where you act as if things happen automatically without His direct involvement?
?
Razi's argument forces you to accept infinite regress or a first cause. In your heart, have you really settled on the existence of a First Cause that is necessarily existent? Or do you sometimes entertain an agnostic position? What does this argument demand of you?

Refuting the Objection on 'Total' and Proving Infinite Regress
Refutes the claim that a total requires parts and proves infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection against infinite regress based on the concept of a total is refuted, and how the causal analysis leads to a proof of infinite regress.

Core Idea
The opponent's own premises force an infinite regress of conditions.

This passage presents a sophisticated dialectical argument. The opponent claims that if there is an infinite chain of causes and effects, then the total of that chain would require each part, implying finitude. The responder first questions the applicability of the term 'total' in an infinite context, then shows that even if we accept the terms, the opponent's logic backfires and proves infinite regress. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage once to get the overall flow. Notice the dialectical structure: the opponent speaks, then the responder replies.
2
Understand the initial objection and response
The opponent says the total depends on each unit. The responder first denies that 'total' applies before finitude is established, then concedes for argument and shows it leads to regress.
The key move: the responder argues that the opponent's premise actually supports infinite regress.
3
Follow the causal analysis
The responder then provides a detailed proof: if the cause of events is originated, regress; if eternal, then its influence either depends on a condition or not, and each option leads to regress or absurdity.
Trace through the branches: eternal with no condition → event eternal; eternal with condition → condition analysis leads to regress, circularity, or impossible.
4
Recognize the dialectical purpose
The responder is not asserting infinite regress as true; rather, he is showing that the opponent's own reasoning commits him to regress, which the opponent rejects. This refutes the opponent's objection.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ذلك المجموع مفتقر إلى كل واحد من تلك الآحاد . قلنا: لا نسلم أنه يصح وصف تلك الأسباب والمسيبات بأنه مجموع وكل ، لأن هذه الألفاظ مشعرة بالتفاهي إطلاقها إلا بعد ثبوت التناهى وهو أول المسألة ، سلمنا أنه يصح وصفها بذلك لكنا نقول إن دل ما ذكرته على فساد التسلسل فههذا ما يدل على صحته . فلا يصح بيانه : وهو أن هذه الحوادث المحسوسة لا بدلها من مؤثر فالمؤثر فيها إما أن يكون محدثا أو قديما فان كان مجدها فالسكلام فيها كالكلام فى الأول ، فإما أن يتسلسل فيكون ذلك اعترافا بصحة التسلسل أو ينتهى إلى قديم وذلك هو القسم الثاني من القسمين المذكورين . فنقول: تأثير ذلك القديم في ذلك الحادث إما أن يتوقف على شرط حادث أو لا يتوقف ، فإن لم يتوقف على شرط لزم من قدم المؤثر قدم هذا الحادث وإلا لكان نسبة صدور الأثر كنسبة لاصدوره عنه ، وإن لم يفتقر صدوره عنه إلى مرجح منفصل فقد ترجح الممكن لا عن سبب وذلك ليسد باب إثبات الصانع ، وإن افتقر لم يكن المؤثر التام قبل حصول ذلك المنفصل المرجح مؤثرا تاما هذا خلف ، وأما أن يتوقف على شرط فذلك الشرط إن كان قديما عاد الإشكال ، وإن كان محدثا فإما أن يكون مقارنا لذلك الحادث أو سابقا عليه فإن كان مقارنا في الكلام في حدوثه كالكلام فى الأول ، فإن كان شرط حدوثه هو الحادث الأول لزم الدور ، وإن كان شرط حدوثه حادثا آخر لزم التسلسل وأما إن كان شرط حدوث ذلك الحادث حادثا سابقا عليه فنقول حال حدوث ذلك الحادث لم يكن القديم مؤثرا بالفعل فى الحادث اللاحق وعند فنائه يصير مؤثرا فيه بالفعل فتلك المؤثرية حكم حادث لا بد لها من مؤثر ، فإن كان هو الحادث الذى عدم الآن لزم تعليل الوجود بالعدم وهو محال ، وإن كان هو الحادث الذي حدث به لزم الدور ، وإن كان حادثا آخر لزم التسلسل ، فظهر أنه لابد من التزام التسلسل .
He said: That total is dependent on each one of those units. We said: We do not concede that it is proper to describe those causes and effects as a total and each, because these terms indicate that their application is [only] after the establishment of finitude – and that is the very issue of the question. We grant that it is proper to describe them as such, but we say: If what you mentioned indicates the corruption of infinite regress, then this indicates its validity. Then its proof is not sound? [Actually:] That these sensible events must have an effect; the effect in them is either originated or eternal. If it is originated, then the discussion about it is like the discussion about the first. So either there is infinite regress, which is an admission of the validity of infinite regress, or it ends with an eternal – and that is the second of the two mentioned divisions. Then we say: The influence of that eternal upon that originated event either depends on an originated condition or does not depend. If it does not depend on a condition, then from the eternality of the effect follows the eternality of this event – otherwise the ratio of the emanation of the effect would be like its ratio of not emanating? And if its emanation from it does not require a separate preponderator, then the possible would be preponderated without a cause, and that closes the door of proving the Maker. And if it requires, then before the occurrence of that separate preponderator, the complete effect was not a complete effect – this is a contradiction. And if it depends on a condition, then that condition: if it is eternal, the problem returns; if it is originated, then either it is simultaneous with that event or prior to it. If it is simultaneous, then the discussion about its origination is like the discussion about the first. If the condition of its origination is the first event, then circularity follows; if the condition of its origination is another event, then infinite regress follows. And if the condition of that event's origination is an event prior to it, then we say: At the time of the occurrence of that event, the eternal was not actually an effect in the later event, and upon its annihilation it becomes an effect in it actually. So that effect-ness is an originated state; it must have an effect. If it is the event that has just ceased, then the existence would be caused by non-existence, which is impossible. If it is the event that occurred by it, then circularity follows. If it is another event, then infinite regress follows. So it appears that one must commit to infinite regress.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by a proof. The opponent claims that the total (majmu') of causes and effects is dependent on each individual unit. The responder initially rejects this, arguing that terms like 'total' and 'each' imply finitude, which is the very point in dispute. However, he then concedes for argument that it is proper to describe them as such, but points out that the opponent's premise actually proves infinite regress rather than refuting it. The responder then provides a detailed proof: sensible events require an effect; that effect is either originated or eternal. If originated, we repeat the question, leading to either regress or stopping at an eternal. Regarding the eternal's influence on an originated event, it either depends on a condition or not. If no condition, the event would be eternal (contradiction) or require no cause (absurd). If it depends on a condition, analysis of that condition (simultaneous, prior, etc.) leads to infinite regress, circularity, or explaining existence by non-existence (all absurd). Thus, infinite regress is unavoidable – but note this is presented as a proof that the opponent's own reasoning leads to regress, so the respondent is highlighting a problem for the opponent.

Correction: The responder is not affirming infinite regress; rather, he is showing that the opponent's premises lead to regress, which the opponent rejects. This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Checkpoint
According to the responder, if the eternal's influence on an originated event depends on a condition that is originated and simultaneous with the event, what follows?
A) Infinite regress.
B) The condition's own origination requires the same analysis, leading to regress.
C) Circularity.
D) The event would be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's objection that an infinite total requires parts is refuted by showing that the opponent's own causal reasoning leads to infinite regress, a conclusion the opponent rejects.

?
Consider a chain of conditions in your own life—perhaps your career depends on a series of events and decisions. If you trace it back far enough, do you genuinely believe it ends with an uncaused First Cause, or do you avoid thinking about the ultimate origin? What does your answer reveal about your trust in Allah as the Al-Mu'aththir (the Effective Cause)?
?
This lesson shows that assumptions lead to conclusions one may reject. In your faith, are there any assumptions you hold—about God's actions or about causality—that, if followed logically, might lead to an undesirable conclusion? Are you willing to examine those assumptions honestly, or do you prefer to avoid the argument for the sake of comfort?

Clarifying the Objection against Infinite Regress
Clarifies a dialectical point in an objection to the infinite regress proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a key dialectical move in the debate about infinite regress: the charge that a certain objection would entail explaining existence by non-existence, which is impossible.

Core Idea
Existence cannot be explained by non-existence.

This short editorial note clarifies a precise point in an objection against the proof of infinite regress. It shows that if the opponent's reasoning is accepted, it would lead to the absurd conclusion that existence is caused by absence. Understanding this move helps you follow the subtle back-and-forth of kalam arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Note the compressed style: it refers to an earlier statement and then draws an impossible consequence.
This is a comment on an argument, not a standalone proof.
2
Identify the logical structure
The note presents a conditional: if (premise) then (consequence). The consequence is ruled out as impossible (muhāl). Thus the premise is false.
3
Understand the key term 'ta'līl al-wujūd bi-l-'adam'
This phrase means 'explaining existence by non-existence.' It is considered impossible because non-existence is nothing, and nothing cannot be a cause.
4
Recognize the dialectical role
This note is not a new argument but a clarification of why a certain objection fails. It shows that the objection leads to an absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : قوله في معارضته دليل إبطال التسلسل بإثبات صحة إن كانت المؤثرية في الحادث اللاحق موقوفة على عدم الحادث السابق لزم تعليل الوجود بالعدم وهو محال .
I say: His statement in opposing the proof of the invalidity of infinite regress by establishing its validity: If the effect-ness in the later event depends upon the non-existence of the prior event, then the existence would be explained by non-existence – which is impossible.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This short editorial note comments on a specific part of the previous argument. It refers to the objection against the proof that invalidates infinite regress (dalīl ibṭāl al-tasalsul) by claiming that such proof actually establishes its validity. The note emphasizes the point: if the effect-ness (mu'aththariyya) of the eternal in a later event depends on the non-existence of a prior event, that would mean that existence is caused by non-existence (ta'līl al-wujūd bi-l-'adam), which is impossible. This underscores an absurdity in the opponent's reasoning.

Misconception: This note is a new, independent argument against infinite regress.
Correction: This note is a clarification of a point within an existing objection; it does not present a new proof but refutes a counterargument.

Checkpoint

A) Infinite regress of events.
B) Existence being explained by non-existence.
C) Circular causality.
D) Dependence of the eternal on a condition.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of explaining existence by non-existence is used to refute a counterargument against the proof of infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Critique of the Tasalsul Argument
Editorial note critiques a previous argument on invalidating tasalsul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a key objection to the proof against infinite regress is itself criticized, and why explaining existence by non-existence is considered impossible.

Core Idea
The impossibility of explaining existence by non-existence undermines a common objection to the proof of tasalsul.

This lesson examines an editorial note that critiques a previous argument against the invalidity of an infinite regress (tasalsul). The note highlights a logical flaw: if the causal effect of a later event depends on the non-existence of an earlier event, then existence would be explained by non-existence, which is impossible. Understanding this critique deepens your grasp of causal reasoning in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words come through before you interpret.
The passage is a short editorial comment, so read it carefully.
2
Identify key technical terms
Find the terms 'tasalsul', 'mu'thiriyya', and 'ta'lil al-wujud bi al-'adam'. Note their meanings as given in the key terms.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Analyze the logical structure
The passage presents a conditional: if effectivity depends on non-existence, then existence is explained by non-existence. The conclusion labels this as impossible. Identify the premise and the conclusion.
This is a critique of an objection, so the author is pointing out a flaw.
4
Reflect on why the conclusion is impossible
Consider why explaining existence by non-existence is logically impossible. Think about causality: non-existence cannot be a cause of existence.
This reflects a core principle in kalam: existence cannot originate from non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : قوله في معارضته دليل إبطال التسلسل بإثبات صحة إن كانت المؤثرية في الحادث اللاحق موقوفة على عدم الحادث السابق لزم تعليل الوجود بالعدم وهو محال .
I say: His statement in opposing the proof of invalidating the chain (tasalsul) by establishing its validity: if the effectivity in the later event is dependent on the non-existence of the prior event, then it would necessitate explaining existence by non-existence, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is an editorial comment (likely by a later scholar) on a previous argument. The argument claimed that if the causality of a later event depends on the non-existence of an earlier event, then existence would be caused by non-existence, which is absurd. The note critiques the reasoning, suggesting a flaw in the opponent's objection. The comment uses technical terms: 'tasalsul' (infinite regress), 'mu'thiriyya' (causal effect), and 'ta'lil al-wujud bi al-'adam' (explaining existence by non-existence).

Key Terms

B) Explaining existence by non-existence.

Key Takeaway
The editorial note warns against explaining existence by non-existence, which would be a logical absurdity in causal reasoning.

Refutation of Infinite Regress of Conditions
Razi refutes the objection of infinite regress of conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the objection that divine influence depends on an infinite chain of originated conditions by reducing it to the impossible concept of events with no beginning.

Core Idea
Infinite regress of conditions collapses into the impossible: events with no beginning.

In this brief but critical passage, Imam Razi tackles a common objection to the argument for the temporal origination of the world. The objector concedes that if God's influence were unconditional, the world would be eternal, but then suggests that the condition itself might be originated, leading to an infinite chain. Razi skillfully redirects the discussion to the already-refuted notion of an infinite series of events.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the structure: concession, objection, redirection.
2
Identify the three parts
Break the passage into its components: the concession, the objection (infinite regress), and the redirection to 'events with no beginning'.
This logical structure is common in kalam argumentation.
3
Understand why the refutation works
Reflect on why infinite regress is impossible according to earlier proofs. Razi does not repeat the proof but references it.
Refer back to the lesson on حوادث لا أول لها.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أنه لو لم يتوقف تأثيره في العالم على شرطه لزم من قدمه قدم العالم، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال تأثيره في وجود العالم كان موقوفا على شرط حادث وحدوث ذلك الشرط على شرط آخر لا إلى أول ، والكلام فيه يرجع إلى مسألة حوادث لا أول لها
We grant that if his influence on the world were not dependent on a condition, then from his eternity follows the eternity of the world. So why is it not permissible to say that his influence on the existence of the world was dependent on an originated condition, and the origination of that condition on another condition without a first? The discussion about that reverts to the issue of events with no beginning.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is an objection and indirect response. The objector concedes the earlier point but proposes that the condition for the agent's influence might be an originated condition, which in turn depends on another condition ad infinitum. Razi responds that this reverts to the issue of 'events with no beginning' (حوادث لا أول لها), which he considers impossible (as established earlier). Thus the infinite regress is invalid.

Correction: Razi shows that an infinite regress of conditions is equivalent to an infinite series of events, which has already been refuted as impossible.

Answer to Objection: Why the World Was Not Created in Eternity
Al-Razi responds to the claim that the world's non-existence in eternity was due to impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's refutation of the claim that the world's non-existence in eternity was due to impossibility, and the distinction between voluntary and necessitating causation.

Core Idea
Impossibility applies only to free choice, not to necessitating cause.

In this passage, Al-Razi tackles a key objection: if the world's non-existence in eternity was due to impossibility, then the same impossibility would apply at any time, so why wasn't it created later? He distinguishes between two types of causation—voluntary (by power and choice) and necessitating—and shows that only the first is impossible in eternity. He then argues that even if we grant impossibility, the world should have been created at the earliest possible moment, which would still be before its actual creation, leading to an infinite regress or an unwarranted gap. This argument reinforces the Ash'ari position that the world is temporally originated by divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: Al-Razi first states the objection, then gives his response with several lines of reasoning.
Pay attention to the key terms: أزل, علة موجبة, قدرة, اختيار, واسطة, حوادث لا أول لها.
2
Understand the distinction between two types of causation
Al-Razi says impossibility applies only to the world's existence by power and choice (قدرة واختيار) in eternity, not to its existence by a necessitating cause (علة موجبة). Why does this distinction matter?
This undermines the objection because the objector assumed all causation is alike.
3
Analyze the temporal argument
Even if we grant impossibility in eternity, if the world came into existence at some finite time, then it could not have been impossible at that moment. But if the cause is present, why wasn't it created earlier? This leads to either an infinite past of possible creation moments or an arbitrary gap (واسطة).
Al-Razi refers to 'events with no beginning' (حوادث لا أول لها) which he claims have been refuted earlier, and the 'intermediate' (واسطة) which Muslims reject.
4
Connect to the broader theological context
This passage is part of the debate between philosophers (who advocate a pre-eternal world via necessitation) and theologians (who hold that the world is created ex nihilo by divine will). Al-Razi defends the theological view by showing that the objector's position leads to unacceptable consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : قوله إنما لم يوجد العالم في الأزل لإستحالة وجوده أولا . قلت وقوع العالم بالقدرة والاختيار فى الأزل محال ، أما استناده إلى العلة الموجبة غير محال فلم يصلح هذا عن صدوره عن الملة القديمة في الأزل ، سلمنا كونه محالا فى الأزل لكن لو وجد قبل أن وجد بمقدار يوم لم يصر بسبب ذلك أرليا ، فكان يجب أن يوجد قبل أن وجد ، لأن العلة قائمة والمانع المذكور مفقود وأما حوادث لا أول لها فقد تقدم إبطالها، وأما الواسطة فقد أجمع المسلمون على إبطالها .
The answer: [Their] statement: 'The world did not come to be in eternity only because of the impossibility of its existence then.' I reply: The occurrence of the world by power and choice in eternity is impossible. As for its being based on a necessitating cause, it is not impossible, so this does not suffice to prevent its emanation from the eternal cause in eternity. Grant that it is impossible in eternity, but if it existed before it existed by a day, it would not thereby become eternal. So it should have existed before it existed, because the cause is present and the mentioned obstacle is absent. As for events with no beginning, their refutation has preceded. As for the intermediate (al-wasita), the Muslims have agreed on its refutation.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage answers an objection that the world was not created in eternity because its existence then was impossible. Al-Razi distinguishes: impossibility applies only to creation by choice, not by necessitating cause. If the cause is necessitating, the effect must be eternal. Even if we grant impossibility, the world should have been created at the earliest possible moment, which would still be before its actual creation, leading to an infinite past or an unwarranted gap (wasita), both of which are refuted.

Key Terms

اختيار
واسطة
حوادث لا أول لها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the world's non-existence in eternity proves its origination is impossible at any time.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that impossibility only applies to voluntary creation, not necessitating causation, and even if it were impossible, the world would still have to exist at the earliest possible moment, leading to absurdities if not.

Checkpoint

A) Because impossibility applies only to voluntary causation, not necessitating causation.
B) Because the world actually existed in eternity.
C) Because the objector misquoted the Quran.
D) Because the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway

Possibility Cannot Be Time-Dependent
Objection and answer on time-dependence of possibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why time cannot be the factor that determines whether something is possible or impossible, and how the author resolves the objection by showing that either time is non-existent or itself requires a determiner.

Core Idea
Time cannot determine possibility; either it is nothing or it needs its own determination.

This lesson examines a short but important objection: Could possibility depend on time? The author presents the objection and then refutes it with a simple disjunction. By analyzing this exchange, you will grasp a key principle in kalam: that time, like any other contingent, cannot be the ultimate specifier.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the objection proposes that something can be possible at one time and impossible at another.
2
Understand the objector's claim
The objector suggests that possibility (imkan) is time-relative. This view challenges the idea that possibility is an intrinsic property.
3
Analyze the author's disjunctive response
The author splits the case: if time does not exist, it cannot have an effect; if it exists, the same problem of specification arises as before.
Notice the logic: time itself would need a determiner, leading to infinite regress.
4
Reflect on the implication
Conclude that time cannot serve as a criterion for possibility. This reinforces that only an eternal, self-sufficient being (Allah) can ground possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : يجوز أن يكون ممكنا في وقت ممتنعا في وقت آخر . قلت : الوقت إن لم يكن موجودا استحال أن يكون له أثر ، وإن كان موجودا كان الكلام كما في الأول .
He said: It is permissible that something be possible at one time and impossible at another. I said: If time is not existent, it is impossible for it to have an effect; and if it is existent, the discussion is as in the first case.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This is a short objection and answer. The objector suggests that possibility (imkan) could be relative to specific times: a thing might be possible at time A and impossible at time B. The author (Imam Razi) responds with a disjunction: either time does not exist or it does. If time does not exist, it cannot have any effect (athar) on what is possible. If time does exist, then the same problem arises: that time itself would need specification by something else (like will or another time), leading to infinite regress as before. Thus, the notion of time-grounded possibility does not solve the problem of specification.

Key Terms
الممكن
الممتنع
الوقت
الأثر
الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that time can determine when something becomes possible.
Correction: The author shows that time either is nothing or itself requires a determiner, so it cannot be a genuine criterion for possibility.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the claim that something can be possible at one time and impossible at another?
A) Time is non-existent and thus has no effect.
B) Time is eternal and unchanging.
C) Possibility is independent of time.
D) If time exists, it needs another time to specify it, leading to infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
Time cannot be a determining factor for possibility because either it is non-existent and thus has no effect, or it exists and itself requires a specifier, leading to the same problem.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Celestial Conjunctions and Divine Power
Al-Razi refutes celestial causation by distinguishing preponderance from effective causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why celestial conjunctions are not true causes and how the distinction between preponderance and effective causation defends the uniqueness of divine power and will.

Core Idea
Celestial conjunctions do not create; only Allah's power is the true cause.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi addresses an objection that temporal events are caused by celestial conjunctions. He argues that the rational proof that possibilities are actualized by divine power remains sound. He then refutes a related objection about the role of divine will, drawing a crucial distinction between preponderance and effective causation. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its implications for understanding God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation.
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the literal translation. This gives you the author's flow.
2
Identify the objection and initial response.
Find the objection about celestial conjunctions and the author's first reply. Notice that he affirms the validity of divine power as the cause.
3
Understand the objection about will and the answer.
The second objection claims will is redundant if power suffices. The answer makes a key distinction: preponderance (tarjih) vs. effective causation (ta'thir).
This distinction is central to the lesson.
4
Examine the parallel argument about knowledge.
The author draws a parallel: if knowledge of each thing is distinct, God would have multiple knowledges. This supports the distinction between attributes.
This reinforces the reasoning.
5
Reflect on the significance.
Consider why this argument matters for Islamic theology: it safeguards divine omnipotence and the uniqueness of God's creative power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : هذه الحوادث مستندة إلى الاتصالات الفلكية. قلنا تستقيم الدلالة على الممكنات واقع بقدرة الله تعالى ، أما المعارضة بنفس الإرادة فقوية أن جميع وجوابها أن مفهوم كون الشيء مرجحا غير مفهوم كونه مؤثرا وذلك يوجب الفرق بين القدرة والإرادة ويتوجه عليه أن المفهوم من كونه عالما بهذا السواد غير المفهوم من كونه عالما بذلك فيلزم أن يكون له بحسب كل معلوم علما ، وقد التزمه الأستاذ أبو سهل الصعلوكى منا وهو الوجه ليس إلا .
He said: These events are dependent on celestial conjunctions. We said: The indication upon possibilities being actualized by the power of God is valid. As for the objection by means of will itself, it is strong that all [؟], and its answer is that the concept of something being preponderant is not the same as the concept of it being effective, and that necessitates a difference between power and will. And it is objected against him that the concept of His being knowing of this blackness is not the same as the concept of His being knowing of that, so that would require Him to have knowledge corresponding to every known thing, and this has been held by the master Abu Sahl al-Su'looki from us, and it is the only aspect.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and an answer. The objector claims that temporal events (hawadith) are based on celestial conjunctions (ittisalat falakiyya). The author responds that the evidence (dalala) for possibilities being actually brought about by the power of God is sound. Then he addresses a related objection involving will itself: the objector might argue that if divine power is sufficient, will is redundant. The answer is that the concept of something being a 'preponderant' (murajjih) is not identical to the concept of it being an 'effective cause' (mu'aththir). This distinction necessitates a difference between power and will: power is the effective cause, while will is the preponderant that specifies. Furthermore, the author notes that if one argues that knowledge of each specific thing is distinct, then God would have multiple knowledges, a position adopted by Abu Sahl al-Su'looki. This supports the distinction between attributes.

Correction: Al-Razi clarifies that such conjunctions are not true causes; only divine power is the effective cause of all events.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
